Preface The present volume seeks to enquire into the drivers of permacrisis which has lately engulfed the world order, endangering international secu- rity through a cocktail combination of geoeconomics and geopolitical instrumentalities increasingly deployed in both narratives and practices of international relations of the nation-states. Most of the chapters in this volume have been developed by the authors out of the papers presented by them in an international confer- ence on Geoeconomics and International Security: Interrogating Contem- porary Trends and Manifestations at the University of North Bengal in January 2023. Some of the chapters, however, have been contributed by invited authors to add a few crucial aspects and issues not well covered in the conference proceedings. The authors were encouraged to update their writing in the light of the still raging Russian-Ukraine war and the newer international armed conﬂicts such as the Israel-Hamas war. The return of international war in the dynamic processes of world politics is reshaping inter-state relations and presenting acute challenges to the maintenance of the liberal international order. This is further endangered by the weaponi- sation of interdependence in the information, technology, commodity and energy spheres. Instead of contributing to positive engagement, facilita- tion and dispute resolution, the various arches of interdependence among nations are serving as instruments of pressure and coercion, of sanctions and restrictions. v

CHAPTER 1 Introduction: Context and Drivers of Permacrisis Omprakash Mishra and Souradeep Sen Out of the crooked timber of humanity no straight thing was ever made. 1 The various chapters contained in this volume attempt to understand the contemporary landscape of international politics and how interac- tions and international relations of the countries have transitioned to the present complexity and crises. Being sceptical of mono-causal explana- tions, the volume incentivises analysis as an intellectually viable means to the realisation of the ends of security. A myriad of crises in the contem- porary world are gnawing away at the status quo. The point where the present differs from the past is perhaps with regard to the effects and manifestations of revision, whereby protracted crises are not necessarily 1 The quotation, originally in German, comes from Immanuel Kant. The customary English translation is by Isaiah Berlin (Berlin 2013: viii). O. Mishra (B) Jadavpur University, Kolkata, India e-mail: professor.mishra@gmail.com S. Sen University of North Bengal, Siliguri, India © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_1 1

2 O. MISHRA AND S. SEN culminating towards any commonly accepted notion of progress. Recog- nising the protean and crisis-prone nature of international politics—where crises at the unit level often coalesce to entrench the inescapable structural anarchy of the system—the volume attempts to locate, explain and predict the contemporary drivers and manifestations of permacrisis. 2 In tune with the perpetual relevance of the above quoted adage, it is argued that the contemporary context of international politics can be encapsulated within a neat continuum: from complexity to permacrisis. A brief inquiry into international history since the end of the Cold War may sufﬁce to inform the historical moorings of this volume. The end of the Cold War heralded a brief period of optimism, pivoting upon the ideological triumphalism of the Western bloc that was alleged to have won its global geopolitical contest against the Soviet Union-led Eastern bloc (Fukuyama 1992). As the realities of post-Cold War international politics have adduced, however, this triumphant euphoria generated its own set of contradictions. It was soon surpassed by systemic complexity and a consequent global dystopia of its own creation—often dubbed as the ‘return of geopolitics’ (Mead 2014). Various aspects of the post-Cold War era were directly responsible for the resultant complexity and the concomitant dystopia. First, the end of the Cold War did not necessarily imply the end of the clash among contending ideologies. In the unipolar moment, the US as the sole superpower or global rule-maker attempted to preserve the global status quo through the promotion of liberal democracy to its geopolitically relevant theatres (Mearsheimer 2018). The concomi- tant unleashing of globalisation processes powered by information and communication technology was expected to promote worldwide inte- gration at several levels. However, globalisation processes contained a strong element of fragmentation as well, complicating state-formation and nation-building projects in Africa, Balkans and the Middle East. Despite unipolarity, the post-Cold War era witnessed insurmountable challenges to international security, in the form of violent ethnic clashes, territo- rial and border disputes and intermixes of state interests and extremist violence. The state borders became fragile and externalisation of intra- state disputes and violent clashes brought to the fore massive forced 2 Permacrisis is a neologism denoting “an extended period of instability and insecurity, especially one resulting from a series of catastrophic events” (Collins English Dictionary 2023).

1 INTRODUCTION: CONTEXT AND DRIVERS OF PERMACRISIS 3 displacement, necessitating international attention and both unilateral and multilateral interventions. The developing unanimity in collective decision-making at the United Nations for addressing the myriad prob- lems of international security soon gave away to new set of disputes and contestation. Chieﬂy these stemmed from liberal international order’s antagonism with revisionist ideologies, such as ethno-nationalism, conser- vatism and authoritarianism (Cox 2017). The breakdown of international consensus on addressing the newer challenges to international security and maintenance of peace further led to the rise of authoritarian, state- led capitalism in Russia and, most importantly, in China (Allen 2023). The implications of these aspects become stark through unpacking several shifts in the contemporary balance of power. To begin with, the Russian Federation, although initially dependent upon American aid, not least, pliant—as evinced by its willingness to join the North Atlantic Treaty Organization (NATO)—towards the West, took an unexpected turn towards a revanchist foreign policy by the late 1990s. This complicated its relationship not only with the Common- wealth of Independent States (CIS) and the newly independent states of Eastern Europe (Galeotti 2022) but also with the West. Russia’s histor- ical ‘insecurity complex’—which increased its threat perception of NATO, thereby impelling a resort to revanchism since 2007—led to the creation of its resources-based ‘fortress economy’ in the country and to the projec- tion of a hyperrealist foreign policy entirely based on ‘spheres of inﬂuence’ in its neighbourhood and beyond. Russian revanchism, thus, engendered a chain reaction of threat perception within the Eastern European states, which impelled them to seek the military shield of the NATO and the prospective beneﬁt of associating with the European Union (Windsor 2006;A b e l o w 2022). Coupled with Russian revanchism in world affairs, the failure of state building and the formation of dysfunctional states in Africa, West and Southern Asia have led to the resurgence of two distinct, yet interre- lated, threats to international security and humanitarian sphere: non- international armed conﬂict and the return of international war. The ﬁrst has been witnessed across Africa, and in Afghanistan, Lebanon, Syria and Yemen, and of late, in the Israel-Hamas irregular warfare in West Asia. The second returned brieﬂy with the Russian invasion of Georgia (2008) and the dispute between Armenia and Azerbaijan over the Nagorno- Karabakh region in the South Caucasus; and then, massively with the protracted ‘war of attrition’ between Russia and Ukraine, since February

4 O. MISHRA AND S. SEN 2022. Apart from the perpetuation of antagonistic collective security organisations, the international system has been rife with geopolitical competition among great powers that resulted in the Russian occupation of Crimea, followed by nuclear proliferation in Iran and North Korea, and consequential nuclear force modernisation in the US, Russia and China (Cox 2020). Further, contemporary violent extremism and civil wars in pockets of Asia and throughout Africa, coupled with the parallel divisions of warfare into nuclear, conventional, asymmetric and hybrid genera- tions, have entrenched global refugee crises and complex humanitarian emergencies (Stephens and Baker 2006; Dyer 2023). As regards the global political economy, the ﬁfth wave of globalisa- tion fomented multilateralism among countries of the Global North and the Global South as well as further cooperation within the Global South (Chakrabarti 2016). Complex interdependence, engendered by globalisa- tion, enabled poorer states to experience high rates of growth that were further accelerated by the Global Financial Crisis (Zakaria 2011). The states in Latin America, Africa and Asia began to have, for the ﬁrst time, some say in global governance as a result of their economic heft. This ‘rise of the rest’—from India and China to South Africa and Brazil— and South-South cooperation also entailed a shift in the post-Cold War balance of power that culminated, through multipolarity, into the ‘rise of China’. With the largest GDP by purchasing power parity (Arends 2014), China has become increasingly assertive, and often, irredentist in world affairs; concomitantly, its ‘wolf warrior diplomacy’ has fomented conjec- tures of reinvigorating the ‘Thucydides Trap’ of belligerence with the US (Allison 2018; Martin 2021). Furthermore, the compulsions to sustain economic globalisation through unfettered supply chains have also propelled regional and global ﬁnancial instabilities, and attendant geopolitical competitions in devel- oping regions. The so-called post-American world order (Zakaria 2011) has thereby witnessed the creation of new narratives—such as the Asia– Paciﬁc and Indo-Paciﬁc—to designate potential zones of economic coop- eration and competition (Karnad 2015). It has also necessitated the emergence of plurilateral or minilateral economic groupings in the face of the seemingly insoluble antagonisms between revisionist Russia and China, on the one hand, and the pro-status quo Western multilateral institutions, on the other. Global political-economic shifts have gradually impelled the formation and expansion of new ﬁnancial institutions and regional organisations such as the G20, the BIMSTEC and the BRICS

1 INTRODUCTION: CONTEXT AND DRIVERS OF PERMACRISIS 5 to either increase the bargaining strength of the developing countries vis- à-vis the extant Bretton Woods Institutions or reduce their dependence thereupon (Stott 2023). Relatedly, China’s proclamation of its globally ambitious ‘Belt and Road Initiative’ (BRI) in 2013 has endeavoured to unhinge globalisa- tion and its attendant ﬁnancial institutions from their American moorings shape these in its own image and present these for the acquiescence of other states through an admixture of coercion and persuasion (Breslin and Xiao 2018). Concomitantly, since 2017, there has been the resur- gence of the Quadrilateral Security Dialogue (QUAD) to contain China’s economic inﬂuence in the Indo-Paciﬁc and the formation of the AUKUS in 2021 to check its geostrategic ambitions therein. Such formations have reignited debates on their role in fomenting the ‘competition dilemma’ of China vis-à-vis India and the liberal democratic extra-regional powers in the Indo-Paciﬁc, as well as their efﬁcacy in ‘congaging’ China (Elliott 2018; Chatterjee Miller 2021). These shifts in the contemporary balance of power impel the recog- nition that the liberal international order, created in the initial years of post-Cold War era, has been interminably assailed by several drivers of crises appertaining speciﬁcally to the domains of global political economy, geopolitics and international security. These drivers of crises have, concur- rently, entrenched global complexities to a point of no return, where- from—at least, retrospectively—the only signpost suggested a further slide from one crisis to the other. The curious fact, however, remains that despite being assailed by multifaceted and often seemingly insoluble crises, the liberal international order continues to persist. If indeed the imperfec- tions of international politics are but the surface reﬂections of the crooked timber of human nature, the present volume attempts to make sense not only of such imperfections, but also the functions of systemic persistence in the face of immanent and dystopian crises. Moving on, by 2019, various crises reached their critical points with further worldwide ecological alerts which culminated in the novel coro- navirus pandemic. Since before the pandemic, however, a new balance of power was gradually emerging that was informed by the enmeshed manifestations of the politico-economic, geopolitical and security drivers of international crises. For understanding the complexity of this balance and the concomitant nature of crises, scholars of IR were obliged not only to revisit dated theories, but also towards further conceptual inno- vations. In 1992, M. Mitchell Waldrop developed ‘complexity theory’

6 O. MISHRA AND S. SEN which has since then assumed major importance in the study of systems. For Waldrop, the components of the …complex systems are more spontaneous, more disorderly, more alive… [and] have somehow acquired the ability to bring order and chaos into a special kind of balance… often called the edge of chaos… where new ideas and innovative genotypes are forever nibbling away at the edges of the status quo, and where even the most entrenched old guard will eventually be overthrown… where seventy years of Soviet communism suddenly give way to political turmoil and ferment (Waldrop 1992: 12). Since Robert Jervis ( 1997) applied complexity theory to international relations, scholars of IR have gradually come to analyse strategic reali- ties of the post-9/11 era through its rubric. According to the votaries of ‘systemic complexity’, the context for strategies of war and peace has become more complex in the new security environment after 9/11, thereby impelling an urgent reformulation of strategic assumptions and choices. Arguably, permacrisis is the logical outcome of systemic complexity. The word, most widely understood as a portmanteau of ‘permanent’ and ‘crisis’, represents a contemporary inversion of ‘crisis’—as a necessary and beneﬁcial corrective towards progress. Conceived at the backdrop of Brexit and popularised in 2022, in the aftermath of Russia’s invasion of Ukraine, permacrisis represents a narrative embodying “the dizzying sense of lurching from one unprecedented event to another”, with the trepida- tion of new horrors around the corner. It describes the feeling of living through an extended period of instability and insecurity in the forms of inﬂation, war and political upheavals (Turnbull 2022). Indeed the world has been through permacrisis before—as the global history of the previous centuries would adduce (Tooze 2022)—however, the permanent state of crisis that mankind has been collectively hurtling along, for the last few years, is perhaps more rapid, complex and insidious than those of any previous epoch for technological and ecological reasons. The description of the volume’s conceptual moorings is herein warranted. Following Edgar Morin ( 2008), it can be argued that since the tech- nological revolution, mankind has been residing within a network of interlocking systems and any crisis in one of those systems will engender a crisis in all the others. Previously, Morin and Kern had used the word ‘polycrisis’ to describe this situation where the most ‘vital’ problem of the

1 INTRODUCTION: CONTEXT AND DRIVERS OF PERMACRISIS 7 day was not any single threat but the “complex intersolidarity of prob- lems, antagonisms, crises, uncontrollable processes, and the general crisis of the planet” ( 1999: 74). Of late, the polycrisis has regained currency with such global shocks as the pandemic, diverse and worldwide ecological alerts, and threats of a third world war. For one observer, “[A] problem becomes a crisis when it challenges our ability to cope and thus threatens our identity. In the polycrisis the shocks are disparate, but they interact so that the whole is even more overwhelming than the sum of the parts” (Tooze 2022). What makes permacrisis distinct from polycrisis—albeit the former being a sublation of the latter—is its permanency despite the collective efforts towards the amelioration of speciﬁc crisis in well-deﬁned arenas. For addressing the immanent crisis of the international order, the present volume, therefore, suggests a multidimensional conceptual approach that synthesises the implications of the three key drivers of permacrisis, being at once mindful of the indeterminacies at the systemic level of inter- national politics. In other words, it argues that a system in permacrisis exudes ‘structural indeterminacy’. In a previous literature, Paul ( 2009: 8) has indicated that three features of the contemporary global security environment point at its ‘structural indeterminacy’: small events produce massive and unpredictable changes; the complex effect of multiple and uncoordinated interactions among a plethora of components causes insta- bility and the global structure; and its processes are ever in ﬂux—evolving, adapting and unfolding over time in a highly contingent manner. The contemporary validity of this formulation can be put to test. Since 2019, for example, the world has witnessed a resurgence of violent extremism and an unprecedented increase in cyber-attacks and an upsurge in the weaponisation of non-military instruments of coercion by states—Western sanctions against Russian oligarchs and Chinese digital- infrastructure ﬁrms, to wit. Furthermore, ‘structural indeterminacy’ has manifested through the COVID-19 pandemic, Russian inﬂuence opera- tions against the US and Western Europe, Chinese revanchism in South and Southeast Asia, Brexit and the US-China trade war, the US retreat from Afghanistan after two decades of failed counterinsurgency, the Russian invasion of Ukraine and the renewal of irregular warfare in West Asia. It is further argued that the global nodes of permacrisis point at the implications of systemic complexity, which emanate from a curious admixture of geopolitical templates and geoeconomic instruments of

8 O. MISHRA AND S. SEN statecraft. The traditional understanding of geopolitics—denoting the political implications of geography with some universal and some contin- gent elements—is based on certain principles which inexorably intertwine geography with peace and conﬂict, as perhaps their singular causative element. Classical geopolitics is thereby “concerned with questions of inﬂuence and power over space and territory”; it uses geographical frames or templates to make sense of world affairs; and, perhaps most importantly, traditional or classical geopolitics is future oriented, offering “insights into the likely behaviour of states because their interests” are considered to be fundamentally unchanging (Dodds 2019). This tradi- tional understanding of geopolitics has, since the waning years of the Cold War, manifested within the domains of global political economy in the form of geoeconomics. Since the 1990s, geoeconomics entered into the lexicons of interna- tional politics and strategic studies as a neologism denoting the “admix- ture of the logic of conﬂict with the methods of commerce—or … the logic of war in the grammar of commerce” (Luttwak 1990). It has been argued, over the years, that since the post-Cold War—unipolar moment in international politics—the military underpinnings of the concept of balance of power have been abruptly uprooted, only to be replaced with a new logic combining the economic and the military, with one feeding off the other or the economic often outweighing the military. This new balance of power, or geoeconomics, pivots upon the manipula- tion of economic instruments ranging from trade and investment policy, economic and ﬁnancial sanctions, to cyber, aid and energy commodities for achieving geopolitical goals. Western liberals had brieﬂy toyed with the claim that the geoeconomics of complex interdependence and global- isation would lead to the fragmentation and decentralisation of global networks of power relations, which would, in turn, make wars quite unnecessary (Blackwill and Harris 2016; Allison 2018). The other side of geoeconomics, however, has remained unexplained within the standard liberal template of complex interdependence: how the US, along with other Western states, has since the 1990s increas- ingly ‘weaponised’ interdependence for strategic advantage (Farrell and Newman 2019). This has engendered the systemic complexity perpet- uating insecurities and has ultimately snowballed into permacrisis. With the decline in US hegemony and the rise of China, the same geoeconomic instruments are now being deployed by the latter for coercive ends, which has only entrenched the self-same crisis (Allison 2018; Spalding 2022).

1 INTRODUCTION: CONTEXT AND DRIVERS OF PERMACRISIS 9 The instruments of geoeconomics never function in isolation, requiring as they do a state’s unique mastery of the hard instruments of soft power and a signiﬁcant level of military capability. In determinate ways, states’ geopolitical objectives stimulate the deployment of synthetic coercive strategies that may blur the distinction between military and economic instruments. As the contemporary conditions of global complexity and permacrisis could not diminish the pressures that lead to wars, interde- pendence has instead become the new battleground (Galeotti 2021). The weaponisation of geoeconomic instruments is having far-reaching impli- cations for international security—where the lines between war and peace may seem to have been blurred into near-irrelevance in a world of perma- nent low-level conﬂict—often undeclared, where the actors may keep changing sides. Insofar as international security is concerned, systemic complexity and permacrisis have synthesised an array of non-traditional threats with tradi- tional ones. Synthetic threats to international security have now been presenting insurmountable challenges not only to the sovereignty of states, but also to the viability of non-state actors and civil societies. As outright state-on-state warfare of the old kind has become less useful and less affordable—evinced by the ongoing Russian invasion of Ukraine is now assuming an unprecedented hybridity: resulting from the symbiosis between its military and economic aspects. We may have entered a time when there is talk of the weaponisation of information and ICT tools, global supply chains of strategic materials and energy resources, labour, private military contractors (PMCs), not least, platforms of games and sports, cultural symbols and practices, as well as the strategies to counter climate change. Although the list is endless, not all states are equipped to juggle geoeconomic and security imperatives seamlessly. Nor is every state or region particularly adept at sanctioning or rewarding detractors and supporters, respectively, for lack of hard power capabilities. Such states in the peripheral regions of the world economy are often at the receiving end of the coercive spectrum of great power politics. Ergo, there are stark regional variations vis-à-vis state capacities of wielding geoeconomic and geopolitical instruments to further the ends of national security. The treatment of permacrisis as the context informing contemporary international politics, as at once the cause and the effect of interna- tional politics—through the historical and conceptual inquiry into its provenance—yields three speciﬁc questions. These questions have been variously addressed within the 17 contributions to the present volume:

10 O. MISHRA AND S. SEN 1. Given the contextual particularities and cyclical causality of perma- crisis, what theoretical interventions/innovations are necessary to understand the phenomenon, as well as the causes and functions of the persistence of the international system? 2. What are the constituents of the contemporary global political- economic, geopolitical and international-security drivers of perma- crisis, and how are these causally related to one another as well as to their effect? 3. How can the regional/state-wise variations and implications of the drivers of permacrisis be accounted for, especially vis-à-vis their effect on the global distribution of its consequences? Concomitantly, the volume is divided into three sections: the ﬁrst, entitled ‘Theoretical Interventions into Permacrisis’—ranging from Chap- ters 2 through 5—comprises the theoretical interventions into the drivers and implications of systemic complexity and permacrisis. The section comprises four distinct interventions that are primed towards abstrac- tions, generalisations and predictions vis-à-vis the nature of contemporary international politics. These are informed variously by the realist, eclectic, liberal and political-economic approaches to international politics. The section begins with Anindya Jyoti Majumdar’s theoretical explo- ration into the nature and signiﬁcance of geopolitical constructs vis-à-vis permacrisis. The chapter underscores how geopolitical narratives intri- cately shape the approach and decisions of human being and interna- tional politics within the framework of what he considers the KARMA Loop. Majumdar discusses the cyclical causality of permacrisis by empha- sising the growing importance of oppositional narratives and counter- hegemonic perspectives as challenges to the liberal world order. The author suggests that decision-making on security within geopolitical constructs may not rely solely on reason, but is inﬂuenced by emotional responses and perpetuated through evolving narratives. Shibashis Chat- terjee and Abhinandan Kumar view the contemporary international order as an intricate fusion of geoeconomics and geopolitics and elucidate it through three logics of order, control and exceptionalism. The authors employ this eclectic framework to analyse the constitution and function- ality of the international order, with each of the logic representing the geopolitical landscape, geoeconomic aspect and the impact of reliance on state structures during uncertain times. In a different vein, Gazala

1 INTRODUCTION: CONTEXT AND DRIVERS OF PERMACRISIS 11 Fareedi contends that globalisation has paradoxically led to the weaponi- sation of interdependence. Fareedi analyses the persistence and indispens- ability of contemporary systems of interdependence, multilateralism and global governance institutions vis-à-vis the preservation of liberal values, exploring thereby the possibility of reconceptualising and encapsulating these within the narrative of Global South Liberalism as the means to preserve the liberal international order. Finally, from the perspective of global political economy, Saibal Kar hypotheses the internal economic drivers of international conﬂicts, exploring the conditions that prompt states to engage in border aggression despite the risk of economic sanc- tions, signiﬁcant losses and other international repercussions. His analysis underscores the complex interplay of economic woes, resource access and geopolitical decisions that shape the crisis-ridden state of contemporary international politics. The second section or ‘Understanding the Key Drivers of Permacri- sis’—from Chapters 6 through 12—builds on the theoretical interven- tions in the ﬁrst. The section comprises the key empirical studies of the volume into the nature and instruments of the three key drivers of contemporary permacrisis—geoeconomics, geopolitics and international security. It builds the narrative of permacrisis, based upon the analysis of the interrelationships and interactions among its key drivers. The ﬁrst three chapters of this section, respectively by Apurba Kumar Bardalai, Supreeta Mehta and Shivani Gupta, build on the recognition of the present complexity vis-à-vis non-international armed conﬂicts and the return of international war. Bardalai posits the impact of the contemporary internationalisation of armed conﬂicts on national and regional security. He illustrates the intricate relationship between international security and geoeconomics in extant theatres of conﬂict, thereby inferring that conﬂicts have become the means to pursue the strategic interests of states that have very little interest in conﬂict resolution. Furthering the discussion on conﬂict, Supreeta Mehta begins her inquiry by recognising the negative impact of permacrisis on the legitimacy of the liberal international order. Mehta opines that as the existing major powers try to hold on to their spheres of inﬂuence and new ones try to seize control, a tug of war, with multiple rope ends, has ensued. She attempts to explain the key drivers of permacrisis through pointed analyses of the Russia-Ukraine War and the looming crisis in the Indo-Paciﬁc region. The complex interplay of conﬂict and diplomacy in the previous entries ﬁnds further expression in

12 O. MISHRA AND S. SEN Shivani Gupta’s chapter, where she incentivises an in-depth analysis of the Russia-Ukraine War and irregular warfare between Israel and Hamas to explicate the ramiﬁcations of these on international humanitarian law. Gupta highlights the uncertainty induced by these conﬂicts, inﬂuencing geopolitical alliances, economic crises, energy alternatives and mass migra- tion. To address these challenges, she advocates a targeted overhaul of the existing regime of international humanitarian law. From a different empirical standpoint, Chapters 9 and 10 focus on the geoeconomic drivers of permacrisis, especially the contemporary weapon- isation of economic sanctions and energy architecture. As regards the ﬁrst, Souradeep Sen and Zaad Mahmood explore the popularity of sanctions as instruments for preserving the liberal world order. Arguing that utility and efﬁcacy of sanctions are not to be taken on face value, the authors propose—through a systematic case study of sanctions regime on North Korea, Venezuela and Egypt—a ‘triangular credo of utility and efﬁcacy’. Emphasising the uniqueness of each sanctions case in the light of domestic and international factors, the authors conclude that no universal criteria can strictly determine sanctions’ efﬁcacy in today’s interdependent world. Cauvery Ganapathy assumes a similar tack in her contribution. She argues that strategies for energy self-sufﬁciency are now urgent and yet complex due to multiple pressures. The author unravels the ways in which the pursuit of energy security amid permacrisis fosters international tension. She advocates for a new energy architecture that addresses the issue of fossil fuel inequalities and the indispensability of renewable and clean energy in reshaping the international energy security paradigm. Through her analysis of energy as a geoeconomic driver of permacrisis, Ganap- athy reimagines energy imperatives and urges for a collective response to transform the global energy landscape. The section concludes with two contributions that explore the rami- ﬁcations of permacrisis on human security. Speciﬁcally, the argument in Sabahat Ambreen’s contribution highlights the intricate relationship between security issues and irregular migration, where each exacerbates the other. Additionally, the absence of lasting solutions for protracted refugee situations ampliﬁes the ongoing migrant crisis, thereby snow- balling into permacrisis. Ambreen infers that this perpetual state of crisis intertwines security challenges with migration issues, underscoring the complexities faced by states, non-state actors and individuals amid a land- scape dominated by non-military conﬂicts and threats to human security. Omprakash Mishra and Amartya Ray notch up the discussion by analysing

1 INTRODUCTION: CONTEXT AND DRIVERS OF PERMACRISIS 13 the ubiquity of Complex Humanitarian Emergencies (CHE) in the post- Cold War era, attributing the causes thereof to the ﬂuid and fragmented nature of state formation since the 1980s. The authors examine the inter- national community’s role in mitigating CHE, by analysing existing legal frameworks and institutional arrangements, and underline the doctrine of ‘responsibility to protect’ (R2P). Mishra and Ray also explore various intervention strategies in CHE and analyse the potential impacts of such interventions. The third section, or the ‘Regional Variations and Implications of Permacrisis’—from Chapters 13 through 18—builds on the thought- composite of the ﬁrst and the second sections. It informs the empirical interventions and debates—from a strategic and area studies angle— into the regional/state-wise variations and implications of the drivers of permacrisis. The causes and implications of the composite drivers of permacrisis have found systematic treatment though the analysis of geopo- litical regions such as the Indo-Paciﬁc, South and Southeast Asia and Africa. Explicit within these analyses is a consistent focus on the impact of a triadic great power competition among the US and its allies, on the one hand, China and its client states on the other, and a resurgent and strategically autonomous India as the third pole in the competition on the stability of the fragile strategic balances of these regions. The challenges posed by China to the liberal international order and the implications of Chinese foreign policy for permacrisis have received comprehensive treatment in the contributions of Priyadarshini Ghosh, Tilottama Mukherjee and Ishani Naskar. Ghosh examines China’s ascent and assertiveness, particularly in Southeast Asia and the South China Sea. Chronicling the transition of Chinese foreign policy from the cautious era of Deng to the ‘Chinese Dream’ of Xi, Ghosh focuses on the latter. The Xi Jinping era represents an overt challenge to the global status quo through various attempts to revise geopolitical narratives. Mukherjee shifts the focus to the impact of the ‘Chinese dream’ on South- east Asia. Emphasising Chinese geoeconomic strategies—especially post the South China Sea imbroglio—she argues that these approaches have only heightened Southeast Asian states’ concerns about China and the consequent recalibration of their ties with China’s global competitors. Mukherjee suggests that China could build trust through geoeconomic instruments, drawing parallels with the earlier approach of the US, if it wishes to retain regional support for its ‘Chinese dream’. Naskar reaches similar inferences, in her contribution, albeit following a different tack.

14 O. MISHRA AND S. SEN Understanding geoeconomics as the ﬂipside of neoliberal interdepen- dence which has been transformed by the Chinese foreign policy under Xi, Naskar infers that Asia’s complex geopolitical and geoeconomic factors, including multilateralism, are shaped by China’s pivotal role in balancing regional stakeholders. The author explores this dynamic process through examples of regionalism and sub-regionalism in Southeast Asia. Moving from the east to the west, the focus in the three concluding chapters of the section is variously on Indo-Paciﬁc, South Asia and Africa. Sayantani Sen Mazumdar contributes further to the discussion of China’s geoeconomic inﬂuence over and geopolitical incursions into the Indo- Paciﬁc. She also brings to the forefront India’s balancing role in the region. Elucidating the complex interplay among the three key drivers of permacrisis, Sen Mazumdar argues that despite its economic potential, a plethora of security risks pose signiﬁcant challenges to the Indo-Paciﬁc maritime realm, thereby addressing a few crucial questions vis-à-vis the emerging crisis in the Indo-Paciﬁc region. The author emphasises the necessity for a collaborative dialogue among the major stakeholders which may pivot on India and China’s geo-centrality to the region. Also emphasising the strides in India’s foreign policy, Abhishek Mishra explores India’s strategic approach towards Africa within the context of the African Continental Free Trade Area (AfCFTA). In the process, he underscores India’s multifaceted strategy vis-à-vis Global Value Chain (GVC) integration, infrastructure development, and trade and ﬁnance facilitation to realise the potentials of what would constitute the world’s largest free trade area. Mishra advocates for a durable partnership between India and the countries of Africa, stressing India’s interest in the conti- nent as both economic and geopolitical. Sampurna Goswami argues that since contemporary international politics leans towards integration, India-China relations are increasingly shaped by efforts in regional and sub-regional contexts to foster comprehensive development and reduce the dependence of the Global South on the great powers. The author highlights Myanmar as a case where both India and China are engaged in development initiatives, attempting cooperation but simultaneously trying to contain and outdo each other. She further explores the intricate dynamics of India-China interactions in Myanmar within the framework of regional integration. Given the brief description of the chapters comprising the volume, it comes as no surprise that a phenomenon as extensive and entrenched as permacrisis cannot be fathomed within the conﬁnes of a single volume.

1 INTRODUCTION: CONTEXT AND DRIVERS OF PERMACRISIS 15 Although the volume has attempted to encapsulate the theoretical, tactical and processual aspects of permacrisis within the practicable limits imposed by spatial constraints, certain gaps or limitations vis-à-vis its research foci can be identiﬁed. Such gaps emanate not from the complete absence of a nodal issue or region vis-à-vis the volume’s central theme, but from the parsimony of references to such issues or regions within its conﬁnes. Such limitations can, in the obverse, inform the future research agenda on permacrisis, its key drivers and circular causality and the regional vari- ations and implications thereof. In this regard, the following themes are relevant: the impact of Artiﬁcial Intelligence on systemic complexity and permacrisis; the role of cyber as a geoeconomic instrument of statecraft; the implications of rapidly growing Chinese-owned and operated global digital infrastructure; the interplay among geoeconomics and the emer- gent ﬁfth generation warfare and the environmental drivers of permacrisis. As regards area studies, future research on permacrisis and its practicable correctives shall pivot on interdisciplinary studies of regions in the Global South, from Latin America to Africa, since these regions may become the future testing grounds for the application of diverse geopolitical narratives and geoeconomic instruments. PART I Theoretical Interventions into Permacrisis

CHAPTER 2 Geopolitical Narratives and Decisions in Permacrisis: Reason, Emotion and Security Anindya Jyoti Majumdar Human existence is besieged with a plethora of newer challenges brought forth by every passing day. Very frequently, the challenges turn into crises and threats of different nature at various levels—from individual through national to global. The feeling that ordinarily persists therefore is hardly upbeat and most of the time, pessimist thoughts pervade actions. The immediate task becomes—not really to aspire for resolving issues perma- nently—but to somehow manage the instantaneous challenges, in order to delay the eventual catastrophic future. However, people cannot live without hope, and therefore, optimistic narratives of the future, based on the protection of certain fundamental values like freedom, justice, equality, peace and sustainable development among others, are perceived and projected as desirable, even essential, goals of mankind. A combina- tion of hope and despair shapes the global environment, or to be more A. J. Majumdar (B) Jadavpur University, Kolkata, India e-mail: anindyajyoti.majumdar@jadavpuruniversity.in © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_2 21

22 A. J. MAJUMDAR speciﬁc, elements of hope in despair drive the mankind towards relentless experiments for, at least, perceived near-perfect arrangements. While the experiments continue for fault-free or better alternatives, pending the emergence of such a perfect world, survival and security of core interests predominantly guide the policies of states thereby forming the foundational substance of international politics. At the international level, dynamic and fragile power arrangements often change and novel challenges emerge necessitating attempts to understand the new reality, and fresh assessments usually focus on areas hitherto ignored or viewed as merely tangential. As a result, the geopolitical perceptions of the world change frequently. New terms and new vocabulary emanate from the quest to explore the changing realities and a conglomeration of amended theories becomes the order of the day. This process is essential, natural and fashionable at the same time. This contribution seeks to understand the nature and signiﬁcance of geopolitical constructs and endorses the hypothesis that geostrategic, geoeconomic and geo-cultural strands of policy, though separated for academic convenience, are in effect the manifestations of a combination of different strategies designed and utilised to fulﬁl the goals of geopolitics, i.e., security, survival and interest-promotion, within a framework of suit- able narratives. While hegemonic narratives are usually projected by the powerful, counter-hegemonic narratives too are formed by the rest, often branded as anti-geopolitics. In the process, the chapter explicates (a) how the interconnections between the opposites increasingly assume greater signiﬁcance; (b) how the geopolitical narratives (and counter-narratives) subsume every aspect of our existence; and (c) how we tend to move within a loop, identiﬁed as the KARMA Loop herein which postulates that making decisions on security might not be entirely based on reason. It is a make-believe world of geopolitical constructs that operates by evoking emotional response and uses new terms and jargons to remain relevant and contemporary. Geopolitics and Geopolitical Narratives Conventionally, geopolitics pre-supposes geography’s conditioning inﬂu- ence upon history and politics as geographical settings ordinarily set the limit upon a state’s strategic destiny. The perceptible impact of a country’s geographical settings on its policies is easily discernible and such attributes may result in either advantage or disadvantage for that country. A state

2 GEOPOLITICAL NARRATIVES AND DECISIONS … 23 can easily identify its obvious physical attributes and try to adopt policies to either reduce disadvantages or use those attributes to its advantage. It is quite apparent that the continental and the maritime countries, or the island nations and landlocked countries would have different orienta- tions in policy-making conditioned by geographical limitations or access to strategic resources or routes. However, over-emphasis on geographical conditions leads to geographical determinism—a mono-causal explana- tion that fails to explain state behaviour in totality. Nevertheless, the attempts to formulate grand designs based on worldwide geographical lay-out require sweeping generalisations and labelling vast areas with particular characteristics, tags and identities. In order to re-interpret the world suitable to the preferences of the interpreter, imagination steps in and the ﬁne line between reason and emotion tends to get blurred. Geopolitics is, therefore, “an intriguing concoction of reality and imagination” (Majumdar and Bose 2021: 8 4 ) .A sar e s u l t ,g e o p o - litical explanations often take the shape of narratives. And the moment we move into the realm of geopolitical narratives, geopolitics acquires larger-than-life dimensions. In simple terms, The world is actively spatialized, divided up, labeled, sorted out into a hier- archy of places of greater or lesser ‘importance’ by political geographers, other academics and political leaders. This process provides the geograph- ical framing within which political elites and mass publics act in the world in pursuit of their own identities and interests (Agnew 2003:3). Indeed, places are identiﬁed and labelled using geographical descriptions, metaphors and templates generating a simple model of the world guiding security and foreign policies of states, and in the process, such labels generate particular understanding of places, communities and accompa- nying identities (Dodds 2007:4). ‘North’, ‘South’, ‘East’ and ‘West’ are not merely cardinal geographical directions. These are used as tags, each having underlying immensely powerful imageries in international rela- tions. For example, labelling a few countries as ‘failed’ states, ‘rogue’ states (O’Reilly 2007), ‘torn’ states or ‘swing core’ states and explaining their behaviour by such yardsticks (Huntington 1996: 244) is common in international practice. Knowing how the notions, perceptions and imagi- nation inﬂuence policy prescriptions may also reveal deliberate attempts to impose identities for strategic manipulation. None of these terms however have any validity in international law but prove convenient for political

24 A. J. MAJUMDAR strategising. In effect, we deal with imagined geographies and imposed identities, as pointed out by various scholars—not unlike Anderson’s ‘imagined communities’ (Anderson 1983) as socially constructed ideas. These are essentially elements of narratives and not of systemic theories. Geopolitical narratives emerge because of the attempts to understand, explain, theorise and interpret the interplay of time, space and actor dimensions of international transactions. A narrative can be deﬁned as a way of establishing inter-linkages among events in order to present a unique account that is attractive, easy to understand and which often invokes emotional attachment. In the process, identities are invented or manufactured, and in most cases, identities are artiﬁcial constructs giving birth to myths. Reality then is a perceived reality that depends on what one intends to believe or accept. In other words, multiple realities coexist at the level of cognisance. Geographical representations inﬂuence people’s perception of this world and as Dodds argues, “in that sense we are all geopolitical theorists” (Dodds 2007:11) but our understandings might differ radically for ethnic, religious, political and other reasons. More than facts, the interpretation of facts contributes to making of perception (or misperception) and very often, perceptions become more powerful than facts though perceptions may generate a false sense of reality. A geopo- litical narrative can be an intoxicating cocktail combining imagination, emotion, myth and subjective interpretation of facts which is skilfully presented as rational explanation of events thereby justifying policies. Policies are, in fact, cocooned within narratives. Espousing the notion of ‘cognitive dissonance’ Robert Jervis explains, when one set of beliefs clash with another, a person tries to minimise dissonance by ﬁnding new reasons or by rearranging his beliefs to support the original actions rather than making a policy change. According to him, one of the common misperceptions is ‘overestimating one’s importance as Inﬂuence or Target’ (Jervis 1976: 343). Once coupled with geopolit- ical narratives, cognitive dissonance and misperceptions can very well lead to miscalculations and result in slip-ups in policies. Here, emotion over- powers the rational actor, imagination clouds reason. Rationality suggests that emotion should be subservient to reason but very often they coexist, correlate and converge in policy-formulation. Over time, new world order arrangements take shape forcing change in perceptions and attitudes inﬂuencing policies. “The geopolitical perspec- tive is dynamic”, argues Cohen, “it evolves as the international system and its operational environment change” (Cohen 2009: 3) and “radical

2 GEOPOLITICAL NARRATIVES AND DECISIONS … 25 geopolitical restructuring is a continuous process” (Cohen 2009: 29). A particular narrative focuses on particular space, time and actors. The transition from Cold War to post-Cold War arrangements has spawned multiple narratives including that of ‘War on Terror’ or more recently, of a tussle between the ‘Authoritarian Regimes and the League of Democracies’. Consequently, certain geographical places acquire strategic signiﬁcance and are transformed into imagined spaces—be it the micro ‘Af-Pak region’ in the ‘war on terror’ or the macro ‘Indo-Paciﬁc’ in a tussle between authoritarian regimes and the democratic states. Obvi- ously, leadership variables provide directions to policies and help build new narratives. In the last few years, Xi Jinping’s Chinese Dream— i.e., making China great and a strong country with world class forces through an assertive ‘wolf warrior’ diplomacy—has formed the image of a belligerent China. India’s approach, on the contrary, is to exclaim faith in V asudhaiva Kutumbakam (i.e., the world is a family) and thereby on the one hand, to maintain independent relations with each and all and on the other, pushing multilateralism as the preferred option. “Most geopolit- ical visions portray the world as divided between two systems, with one’s own nation in a strategic position either to defend the cause of right or to act as an intermediary”, argues Dijkink. In fact, “The self-deﬁnition as a bridge between East and West is very popular on the Eurasian continent (Germany, Russia and India)” (Dijkink 1996: 141). Not merely between the East and the West during the Cold War period, self-deﬁnition as a bridge between two extremes continues to be dominant in India even in the post-Cold War phase, predominantly between the North and the South. One can think of several popular phrases that emerged immediately after the end of Cold War. For example, the ‘end of history’ thesis proclaimed that all ideological conﬂicts have now been resolved because of supremacy of liberalism (Fukuyama 1992). Again, the idea of ‘clash of civilizations’ charted out possible course of future conﬂicts (Huntington 1997). A catchy phrase would indicate a narrative, be it the ‘new world order’ or ‘war on terror’. Even the so-called strategies like the ‘string of pearls’ or the ‘necklace of diamonds’ are not more than narratives; the ideas have never been ofﬁcially endorsed by either China or India. Whether a theoretical explanation, policy/strategy formulation or expo- sition on perceived reality—indeed there is no dearth of narratives and each narrative has rational and emotional elements roiled into it.

26 A. J. MAJUMDAR Geopolitics, it may appear, is less about peace and more about conﬂicts—actual or potential. While dominant geopolitical constructs are often motivated and biased, they represent the existing patterns of the world order as projected by the major powers of the day. Perhaps from being essentially Eurocentric or ‘Western’ in nature, contemporary geopolitics would have to adjust and adapt to changing power equations in Asia. However, it is the contest between the narratives that determines the turns and twists of history and the propensity of conﬂicts and feasi- bility of durable peace. Despite the assertion that over time, the impact of geography on state behaviour might not be even important, much less decisive and the ﬂawed research programmes of political geographers would slide further into irrelevance (Fettweis 2015: 248), geopolitics continues to bounce back in different forms. Again, while often criticised as misrepresentations that fail the test of empirical analysis—‘inherently unfalsiﬁable, unscientiﬁc’ (Fettweis 2015: 237)—some such geopolitical formulations, like the cultural–geographical model of ‘the clash of civi- lizations’, frequently resurrected, garner support by appealing to fear psychosis. A simple reason why geopolitics fails in rigorous scrutiny is that geopolitical expositions are essentially narratives and not theories based on coherent logic. Call it geopolitical vision or mere imagination based on re-interpreting geographical dimensions, once the basic undertone of the geopolitical narrative is set, policies tend to follow and these geopolitical narratives subsume geoeconomic, geo-cultural and geostrategic issues—in effect, all policies stem from the grand narrative and get automatically justi- ﬁed. Realist goals of accumulation of power, security and dominance are realised in the garb of liberal principles of democracy promotion, market access and regimes. Each segment of human transaction is ‘weaponised’ to achieve maximum advantage. Geoeconomics, therefore, is not about replacing geo-strategy, i.e. economic power is not replacing the mili- tary power, nor it signiﬁes merely benevolent commercial transactions bringing nations together. Geoeconomic strategies including weaponisa- tion of trade, value chains, tariffs, concessions, sanctions and debt-traps are all in effect instruments to fulﬁl geopolitical goals, i.e. in simple terms, security, survival and dominance. Concentration of global supply chain facilitated rise of China. Apprehension about China’s growing belliger- ence has led the West towards de-risking by locating other investment destinations. Geoeconomics is an important component of all-embracing

2 GEOPOLITICAL NARRATIVES AND DECISIONS … 27 extensive geopolitics; the same may be said about geo-cultural endeavours as soft power weaponries. Geopolitics is often deﬁned loosely and the popularity of the term has made it almost synonymous with international politics. As a result, geopolitics becomes much like an umbrella term that is continuously spinning stories about this world incorporating new issues, establishing linkages with events and setting new targets and tools. Fresh geopolitical narratives are formulated to subsume newer elements into the main- stream—ethnic assertions and identity, climate change, forced migration, pandemic—in short, whatever one can think of. It usurps all emerging issues and re-moulds them into its own framework. In this sense, the extent or the scope of geopolitics is limitless and exceptionally extensive. The COVID-19 pandemic can actually provide a fascinating case study of extensive geopolitics. A communicable disease attaining a virulent form killing people worldwide demanded a collective response from the comity of nations. Instead, it soon got mired in geopolitical narratives and counter-narratives. While suspicions over its origin and nature and the debate on whether a biological weapon was released deliberately or accidentally reﬂected the mistrust states retain against each other, the competition to acquire medicines and protective kits and consequent vaccine diplomacy followed the usual self-help approach. Bringing in a virtual stop to movements and connectivity during the period when COVID-19 was at its peak, the pandemic established once again the fact that international organisations often fail to come up with collective response when needed, while states look for relative gains, even at a time of common crisis and shared challenge to mankind. In this connection, one needs to take note of critical geopolitics and counter-narratives as opposed to conventional geopolitical narratives. Critical geopolitics emphasises that orthodox geopolitics is hazy, erro- neous, distorted and simplifying. Geography is about power. It is not a product of nature but a product of histories of struggle over power to occupy, organise and administer space. To the dominant narratives, resis- tance and contestations are obvious. The counter-hegemonic narratives, termed as ‘geopolitics from below’ or ‘anti-geopolitics’, come from the (strategies and tactics of) civil society movements and the (oppositional discourses of) dissident intellectuals (Ó Tuathail, Dalby and Routledge 1998: 245). How the geopolitical entities are constructed, how different alternative geopolitical arrangements can be imagined and how dissi- dent arguments contest ofﬁcial versions contribute to critical geopolitics

28 A. J. MAJUMDAR (Ó Tuathail1996; Tuathail, Dalby and Routledge 1998:312). Reference can be made to Michel Foucault’s view of geopolitics as a metaphor for the operation of power in general and geography as a discourse is form of power/knowledge; or to Yves Lacoste’s idea of geography as a strategic form of knowledge closely linked to a set of political and military prac- tices; even to Edward Said’s views of Orientalism that reveals how knowl- edge of other cultures is created and disseminated (Ó Tuathail1996). In the process however, better arrangements for individuals are visualised under various categories like alternative, post-structural or subaltern. Reality is contextualised and socially constructed. The obvious outcome is formation of other narratives that are counter-hegemonic in nature. Following Foucault, there are no relations of power without resis- tances, with contestation by those who have faced forms of domination, exploitation and subjection. This counter-hegemonic resistance (against the state) takes the form of ethical and cultural force challenging the idea that state’s interests are identical to the community’s interests. It challenges (1) the material geopolitical power of states and (2) the domi- nant representations imposed by political elites. Therefore, a distinct set of ideas that focus upon peace and not power projections, nego- tiated settlement of disputes and not use of force, sustainable devel- opment and not economic inequalities form the crux of the substance of ‘anti-geopolitics’, opposing the traditional geopolitical assumptions and justiﬁcations. While state behaviour sustains and endorses tradi- tional geopolitics, anti-geopolitics is sustained by international networks of groups, organisations, civil society and social movements. It presents a narrative of hope and aspiration against the security-centric narratives of fear and frustration (Ó Tuathail, Dalby and Routledge 1998). As a result, we have hegemonic discourses forged by the powerful as well as counter-hegemonic discourses of the marginalised leading to spin- ning of narratives of different kinds. For both groups, their respective positions are rational and correct as per their convictions. This moulds the attitude of the protagonists and shapes their approaches to issues of security, the referent object being either the state or the individual. The zeal to prove each other wrong and expose the fallacies of each other’s positions also pre-supposes a signiﬁcant extent of emotional commitment to respective narratives. One can always visualise unrestrained ﬂow of goods, services, tech- nologies and investments creating jobs for millions and resulting in mutual economic gains, regional and civilisational harmony leading to

2 GEOPOLITICAL NARRATIVES AND DECISIONS … 29 the absence of inter-state conﬂicts. However, suspicions and obstruc- tions on ground prevent any worthwhile movement towards these goals. The counter-narratives of peace, equality, cooperation and mutual beneﬁt that may pose serious opposition to the entrenched geopolitical ideas are sought to be appropriated by the state as well. These counter-narratives ﬁnd their expressions at the declaratory level of the state but very little is actually done at the ground level creating a mismatch between what is said and what is done (Majumdar and Bose 2021) leading to habitual double-speak of most politicians. Despite the nationalist narratives harping on protecting and promoting national interests, common global concerns are fast becoming integrated with national interests. The binaries—geopolitical narratives and counter- narratives of anti-geopolitics—represent the opposites. “More than the contest between geopolitics and anti-geopolitics, a dynamic combination of the two shapes contemporary global politics; and conﬂicts and peace remain dependent upon a balanced blend of both” (Majumdar and Bose 2021:95). The interconnections repeatedly manifest themselves and can hardly be ignored. When the interconnection is missed, the perspective becomes more emotional in content than rational. T he Element of Emotion and the Interconnections of Opposites Approaches to perceived (or misperceived) existential threats are usually inﬂuenced by a combination of instinctive response, emotional reac- tion and attempted rational decision-making. There are always formal/ informal, systematic/unsystematic attempts to interpret issues and thereby to inﬂuence opinion of a larger body, and in the process, narra- tives come into being often abridged in innovative words and phrases. Throughout history, new words have emerged (each carrying with it a particular narrative) and become a part of our perceptions. Recent addi- tions to the lexicon to understand the conditions of present-day human existence are no exceptions to geopolitical constructs. For example, the sense of living through an extended period of instability and insecurity caused by social, economic and political turmoil is termed as permacrisis. It is a new addition to the vocabulary to explain the world we live in but hardly there has been a period of lasting stability and enduring peace at the global level in the past. In fact, peace and stability are essentially

30 A. J. MAJUMDAR sought after by all but the process usually results in a favourable arrange- ment for the big powers often by subjugation of the weak or by ignoring their aspirations. Even the very structured bipolar arrangement of the Cold War days, that put a plethora of disparate challenges under a cloak of intense ideological competition, was not free from crises. We live and will continue to live in permacrisis. The word permacrisis could be a novel addition to the lexicon but the spirit has been living with us for centuries. In other words, permacrisis is the constant state of affairs for the mankind. Likewise, following Adam Tooze, another such term in vogue now is polycrisis. It refers to the complex, disparate and cross-cutting set of challenges. Though the French theorist Edgar Morin used the term in the 1990s, Tooze popularises the term and explains that “the shocks are disparate but they interact so that the whole is even more overwhelming than the sum of the parts” (Tooze 2022). Both these terms—permacrisis and polycrisis—are examples of attempts to understand the contempo- rary complexities. These terms visualise a complex matrix of challenges of various types requiring multifarious responses and try to convey that practically nothing can be viewed in isolation. All these terms are however hazy and have deeper meanings—in fact, many meanings—than what they prima facie convey. Further, the meanings are often distorted because of the degree of intensity of emotional attachment we have with them. This has one interesting implication. Single-cause explanations become increasingly irrelevant making ﬁxed solutions untenable. Broadly, we cannot say any more, for example, that capitalism is the root cause of all evils and communism is the solution; or realist approaches make the world unsafe and intense liberalism is better suited to solve problems. Interconnections are increasingly becoming important in understanding continuity and change and the tussle between opposite positions or extremities. Interconnections between opposites; between realism and liberalism; reason and emotion; theories and narratives often present themselves as pragmatic choices and binaries are necessary only to high- light possible linkages. In fact, geopolitics identiﬁes interconnections, subsumes those into a construct and projects a suitable narrative for circulation. Geopolitical narratives create space for emotion to be accommodated in rationally thought-out schemes. In this sense, it is marginally different from post-truth. Post-truth is deﬁned as a political culture in which debate is framed largely by appeals to emotion and perceptions and repeated assertion of beliefs ignoring factual data. Geopolitical narratives however

2 GEOPOLITICAL NARRATIVES AND DECISIONS … 31 may use factual data but interpret those in ways that evoke emotion and help create desirable perceptions. Counter-hegemonic geopolitical narratives are not different so far as these narratives seek to promote a cause. T he KARMA Loop: Combining Reason and Emotion There are many approaches to understand the process of decision-making with different kinds of variables being adhered to ranging from rational actor model to leadership idiosyncrasy, from cost–beneﬁt calculus to bio- political subjectivities. We often however take decisions on the basis of the amount of knowledge that we might have—extensive or inadequate, as the case may be—and also the degree of our attachment to a particular issue. Prioritisation that is made is not always based on reason. In fact, these days, yet another term—Emotional Intelligence—has gained huge popularity. If one goes by the book by Daniel Goleman, emotional intel- ligence has reconciled what had been seen as two opposites: our feelings and our reasoning (Goleman 2021: ix). Once again, we ﬁnd a set of inter- connections between two apparently diverse platforms. We need not go deep into the psychological aspects that the term emotional intelligence suggests, but taking a cue from the basic concept and applying it to inter- national politics, it is perhaps possible to argue that to manoeuvre the environs in the interest of meaningful survival one should be abreast of the changes and must measure the extent of one’s association or emotion with the change as a pre-condition for decision-making. In other words, it is possible to think of an almost universal common process that in successive stages seeks to respond to the environment. Such a process involves a few constituting elements: (1) Knowledge: It is crucial to acquire near-total knowledge of the existing geopolitical environment (hegemonic and counter- hegemonic) and the process primarily includes data collection or trends identiﬁcation (information) and subjective interpretation of collected data of various types (implications). Interpretation of data is done by the leadership with the help of the team of experts. Insti- tutions developed over time are also geared to perform such tasks with elaborate arrangements of agencies dedicated to data collec- tion and expert advice. Usually applying a cost–beneﬁt calculus and necessary trade-off between policies, decisions are made and these

32 A. J. MAJUMDAR decisions, though termed as rational, often manifest attitudinal elements of the interpreters. (2) Association: Identifying and prioritising linkages with various segments of geopolitical environment and how the actor is linked or associated and the extent of emotive attachment with partic- ular issues inﬂuence decision-making. Without emotive association, one remains indifferent to an issue, which explains selective engage- ment and different treatment on the part of the states with regard to same types of issues in different parts of the world. Based on beliefs and convictions, perceptions and preferred interpreta- tions, association with a particular space or issue is formed and the degree of attachment/association determines the intensity of efforts towards a positive outcome. This re-endorses attitudinal elements into decision-making. (3) Recognition: This signiﬁes understanding and recognising the limits and potentials of the actor’s tangible and non-tangible capabilities and the extent of skill of materialising desirable delivery or inﬂu- encing the probable outcome. This may involve utilising hard, soft or smart power instruments and unless the goals correspond to the abilities of the state, any design or plan would remain a mere dream and not be counted as the interest of the state. Recognition of abili- ties in the context of desired outcome remains a crucial component in decision-making. (4) Manoeuvre/Modulation: Based on the above three, choosing the most plausible path considering the constraints, challenges, permitted opportunities, scope available and the intensity of attach- ment at a given point of time leads to applicable strategies. At this stage, the narratives are formed, modiﬁed and circulated as appropriate tools to manoeuvre or modulate the environment in the context of which the decisions are applied. (5) And ﬁnally, Application: This may be regarded as the ﬁnal stage of pursuing the path/policy with suitable vocabulary (spearheading particular narratives) assumed or designed to be acceptable to a larger audience and fulﬁl the goal of desired outcome. Since the application of decisions (on the part of various actors) impacts on the existing state of affairs and reshapes it and because response to change is a form of change itself and introduces new elements, new knowledge base is created and once again we go back to the ﬁrst

2 GEOPOLITICAL NARRATIVES AND DECISIONS … 33 premise. It is a kind of loop that endlessly continues. It is to be kept in mind that at each step, there is ample scope for the interplay of human ingenuity or human error, mistakes and follies. The process does not guar- antee optimum efﬁcacy of decisions. And unlike the virtual world, there is no provision of backspace or delete keys and it is impossible to undo what has already been done. If one picked up the ﬁrst letters of the aforemen- tioned steps of decision-making in permacrisis—Knowledge, Association, Recognition, Manoeuvre/Modulation and Application—it would turn out as K-A-R-M-A in its abbreviated form. The process therefore can be marked as the KARMA Loop that combines the crises and response to crises in cyclical order. Indeed, it is the KARMA then that determines the outcome. Conclusion In ﬁne, it may be said that geopolitical constructs help explain the percep- tion of the political environment in which we live in, and every other aspect of human life—broadly economic, cultural or social—becomes component of grand geopolitical narratives, hegemonic or counter- hegemonic. These are subsidiaries though they may acquire prominence depending on the demands of time but the geopolitical goals of security remain supreme and keep inﬂuencing policies and actions in other ﬁelds. New terms like ‘permacrisis’ or ‘polycrisis’ in effect denote a constant state of affairs that is unstable and dynamic in nature, which in turn enhances the scope, enriches the content and keeps extending the geopolitical horizons. Multiple geopolitical narratives can simultaneously coexist leading to multiple realities. More than the binaries, it is crucial to identify inter- connections between the opposites. The opposites do not lose their distinctiveness as far as ideas are concerned, but for effective policies, a judicious combination of different perspectives needs to be followed. Since decisions cannot be entirely based on reason but also depend on intensity of attachment, narratives necessarily aim at invoking attachment while projecting the decision as rational. The decision-making process operates within a loop as decisions them- selves are important enablers of change impacting on existing situations. It is therefore an endless process that keeps responding to the changing envi- ronment. The KARMA Loop explains how such decisions are made. It is pertinent to locate the interconnections between reason and emotion to

34 A. J. MAJUMDAR understand the motives behind decisions. The KARMA loop ﬁts into the geopolitical framework of decision-making as in search of security, both combine reason and emotion to deal with a world where reality is open to multiple interpretations and deliberate manufacture of suitable narra- tives. The end result often turns out to be an unsatisfactory compromise of what is desired, what is feasible and what is prudent. References Agnew, J. 2003. Geopolitics: Re-visioning world politics.
