CHAPTER 3 Geoeconomics, Geopolitics, and the Contemporary International Order: Some Reﬂections Shibashis Chatterjee and Abhinandan Kumar Geoeconomics is never autonomous. It is ultimately predicated on the political and military balances across the world. As the international system has steadily moved away from the US-led unipolar one to an unpredictable multipolar order, the attendant risks have also multiplied alarmingly. The ongoing war in Ukraine and the Chinese assertions in the Asia–Paciﬁc bear ominous portends for global stability and order. The military and political power of the US and the EU is declining; the norma- tive goals underpinning the present order are being contested; major powers are increasingly becoming uncompromising in maintaining domi- nance in their respective spheres of inﬂuence; exclusionary politics and protectionist policies are on the rise; and, most signiﬁcantly, conﬂicts are increasing in many parts of the world. On top of this, we witness massive S. Chatterjee (B) · A. Kumar Jadavpur University, Kolkata, India e-mail: shibashis.chatterjee@gmail.com © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_3 35

36 S. CHATTERJEE AND A. KUMAR existential challenges over the decay and decline of global commons. The climate crisis and the threat of pandemics threaten human existence as never. This mosaic of complexities requires that we read the economic, the geopolitical, and the social not in isolation but in interrelations. This contribution delineates the contemporary international order as an insep- arable intertwining of the texts of geoeconomics and geopolitics. We understand the contemporary order in terms of three logics. The ﬁrst is the logic of order, where we ﬁnd the simultaneous articulations of networks and territorial spacing. The second is the logic of control where the state has reworked its regulatory functions since markets have increas- ingly steered into crises. This has led to muscular nationalist tendencies along with a rise of right-wing populist forces worldwide. Third, we witness a logic of exceptionalism that builds on the uncertainties of the pandemic. It is found that science has once again strengthened the cause of sovereignty at a critical hour. The more we depend on the state, the easier it is for the state to bend us for our good (or bad). And the more we think like the state, the lesser is the extractive cost of the state to imple- ment demanding measures that would at some stage prove costly to the people. A theory is, as Kenneth Waltz wrote famously, ‘a picture, mentally formed, of a bounded realm or domain of activity. A theory is a depic- tion of the organization of a domain and the connections among its parts’ (Waltz 1979: 8). Hence, we contend that the logics of order, control, and exception are deployed to make sense of the way the international order is constituted, its functionality and modes. In our conception, the logic of order reﬂects the geopolitical landscape, the logic of control represents the geoeconomic aspect, and the logic of exceptionalism underscores how reliance on state structures during uncertain times can lead to an increase in state power. By examining these three logics in conjunction with each other, we aim to blend geopolitics, geoeconomics, and political theory in making sense of these tectonic shifts upon social structures that reproduce the recursive logics of existence across time and space. I Orders are central to the way states live. In IR theory, there is a long-established tradition that distinguishes between systems and orders. Hedley Bull’s contribution is seminal in this regard. In Bull’s words, “Where states are in regular contact with one another, and where in addi- tion there is interaction between them sufﬁcient to make the behaviour

3 GEOECONOMICS, GEOPOLITICS … 37 of each a necessary element in the calculations of the other, then we may speak of their forming a system” (Bull 1977: 10). On the other hand, “A society of states (or international society) exists when a group of states, conscious of certain common interests and common values, form a society in the sense that they conceive themselves to be bound by a common set of rules in their relations with one another, and share in the working of common institutions” ( 1977: 13). The contrast is based on the idea of an instrumental association of states based on mutual needs and a deeper communion raised on shared purposes. This reﬂects the German sociologist Ferdinand Tönnies’s celebrated differentiation between the two ideal types that he named the Gemeinschaft (communal society) and the Gesellschaft (associational society) (Tönnies and Harris 2001). Cooperation seems easier when states share values and cultural norms, although the violence of the modern European societies that culminated in the brutalities of the two World Wars put a big question mark on this association. Geopolitical rivalries and conﬂicts have existed simultaneously alongside economic cooperation, trade, global businesses, and the ritual- istic agreement among most states on creating a peaceful order based on democratic norms and the rule of law. Most states, like individuals, prefer predictability to uncertainties and fuzziness, patterns over randomness, order over disorderly conduct and stability over instability. The desir- ability of order is therefore not in doubt. States, nonetheless, fail to unite on what kind of order is preferable. Moreover, states disagree as to the degree of permissible inequality in constructing the order, the ways of order making, and the need to sustain or transform orders (Lascurettes and Poznansky 2021). Any description of the present order would have to pay attention to these four dimensions. The ﬁrst formulation we develop is that the present epoch, like all periods in the past, is constituted by the logic of order, where we ﬁnd the simultaneous articulations of networks and territorial spacing. It is essen- tial to note that while we refer to the ‘logic of order’ in this context, it should not be confused with the broader concept of international order, which encompasses a more comprehensive set of norms, rules, and power structures governing the global arena. In the sense of logic, order is more akin to the process of ordering, the way a totality comes into being through the interactions of agency and structure (Wendt 1987; Klotz et al. 2006). We develop this formulation by concentrating on two works, the ﬁrst by Anne-Marie Slaughter and the second by John Mearsheimer, as these are contrasting illustrations of orders based on the

38 S. CHATTERJEE AND A. KUMAR logic of networked spaces (Anne-Marie Slaughter) and structural anarchy- driven geopolitical rivalries (John Mearsheimer). This would lead us to describe the four axes of the present order—what it means; who are the architects; how is it built; and whether it is in crisis? Anne-Marie Slaughter ( 2004) argues that governments are “aggrega- tions of distinct institutions with separate roles and capacities” ( 2004: 13) that make the international networks absolutely pivotal. In her words, “appreciating the extent and nature of existing government networks makes it possible to envision a genuinely new world order” ( 2004: 15). The pluralistic reading is essential since “the analytical lens of the unitary state obscures the very existence of these different government institu- tions” ( 2004: 13). She shows that governmental networks constitute “a pattern of regular and purposive relations among like government units working across the borders that divide countries from one another and that demarcate the ‘domestic’ from the ‘international’ sphere” ( 2004: 14). Normatively, she upholds “a system of global governance that insti- tutionalizes cooperation and sufﬁciently contains conﬂict such that all nations and their peoples may achieve greater peace and prosperity, improve their stewardship of the earth, and reach minimum standards of human dignity” ( 2004: 15). This would happen through networks, which she describes as the “foundation of a full-scale disaggregated world order” ( 2004: 132). The treatment here is both descriptive and prospec- tive. Moreover, the allusion to the order is both empirical and normative, married imaginatively ( 2004: 133). Networks, both within states and in world affairs, would be the catalyst to the change in the international order—the ushering in of an interconnected market-order that would be controlled exclusively neither by the state nor by the market. This is surely not a new imagination. From Karl Deutsch to Robert Keohane and Joseph Nye, the liberals have consistently argued for a transformed reality predicated on the culture of deep interdependence successfully socialised through the intergenerational elites. This happens in spite of the geopolit- ical divide. The need for cooperation in increasingly complex domains of collective actions, some of which are existential to our survival, ultimately trumps the competitive dynamics of the anarchical order. The realist position, articulated most notably by John Mearsheimer, highlights the futility of cooperation under the perpetually anarchical environment. This logic is based on the premise that “international relations is not a constant state of war, but it is a state of relentless secu- rity competition, with the possibility of war always in the background”

3 GEOECONOMICS, GEOPOLITICS … 39 (Mearsheimer 1994: 9). The prospect of states cooperating to thwart the negative effects of this constant security competition is highly unlikely primarily for two reasons: considerations of relative gains as opposed to absolute gains, and concerns of cheating by other states. He critiques the theories that argue that institutions can effectively promote peace, namely liberal institutionalism, collective security, and critical theory, for their ﬂawed causal logic and the inadequate empirical evidence in their support. According to him, order “is an organized group of international institutions that help govern the interactions among the member states” (Mearsheimer 2019: 9). He recognises the vitality of orders in managing interstate relations in an economically and militarily interdependent world but makes a critical distinction between bounded and international orders. While bounded orders have limited membership, are regional in scope, and provide a framework for security competition among major powers, international orders are characterised by universal membership and aid in fostering cooperation among major powers. However, the institu- tions that constitute international orders lack autonomous authority. They serve as tools of powerful states and are designed to inﬂuence weaker states’ behaviour in alignment with the great powers’ interests. More- over, whether the prevalent order will be realist, agnostic, or ideological depends on the distribution of power among the great powers and not on their individual ideological preferences ( 2019: 12). In other words, even if the two major powers happen to be liberal themselves, the resultant international order born out of the security competition between them will be realist. The order can be agnostic or ideological (e.g., liberal) only under unipolarity depending on the ideological inclination of the unipole. Thus, in Mearsheimer’s view, distribution and balance of power are independent variables that determine the nature of order, while insti- tutions and ideological preferences are merely intervening variables in the process. These contrasting perspectives of Anne-Marie Slaughter and John Mearsheimer have continued to shape discussions about the evolving nature of the international order. However, the fact is that while political realism correctly indicates at the structural dissonance among states in an anarchical setup bereft of any central regulatory authority, the liberal insis- tence on interconnections between states (and societies) based on trade and commerce, cultural exchanges, shared social norms, institutional link- ages, and the need to abide by commonly recognised legal standards have, on balance, moderated the impacts of structural anarchy to a considerable degree.

40 S. CHATTERJEE AND A. KUMAR We understand order as any recurrent patterns of interactions sustained over a period of time. The complexity of any given order betrays many permutations and combinations that cannot be reduced to any singu- larity. It is always a combination of varied patterns. The contemporary order is multipolar in terms of the distribution of military power, capitalist but increasingly inward-looking, ideologically divergent, and politically volatile. It is loosely committed to multilateral institutions and practices. However, many emerging powers contest the rules and framework of this multilateral order and want a transformation of many key elements of it (Lake et al. 2021; Bajpai and Laksmana 2023). The contestations are both internal and external (Adler-Nissen and Zarakol 2021). There are states like China and Russia that contest the order militarily and in terms of geo-strategy. Yet, China is committed to an open trading system and a globalised economy that is consistent with its economic interests. States like Russia, Iran, and North Korea, on the other hand, are high- risk states. They are capable of military mobilisations if their vital interests are compromised. What constitutes these interests is tricky. For Russia, these are mostly territorial, demographic, and strategic; for Iran, these stem more directly from its political identity; for North Korea, it is about regime survival through aggressive regional policies. The Western states, the traditional pillars of the contemporary global order, are increasingly divided over the issues of globalisation and protectionist policies, the imperatives of welfare and immigration, liberal values, and strong polit- ical regulations of borders and societal norms (Vuorelma 2020). The US, in particular, is caught in a bind. On the one hand, it stands ﬁrmly to protect the present order. On the other, it seems ambivalent on what needs securing. Is order automatically set by invisible structural dynamics or is it consciously created by powerful states? Again, there is no easy answer to this question (Lascurettes and Poznansky 2021: 9). Aspects of the order are the results of macro-sociological changes over long periods that include ideas, values, and expectations. Institutions and national attributes are largely created by the elites through conscious policy interventions. Geopolitical rivalries are always set in an environment that cannot be reduced to discreet national choices. Similarly, economic policies largely depend on structural factors like levels of technology, knowledge, specialisation, aspirations, and the nature of social classes. Individual choices undeniably matter, but without favourable structural factors these seldom materialise in the ﬁrst place. We, therefore, witness

3 GEOECONOMICS, GEOPOLITICS … 41 both macro-transformation and agential action in the making and contes- tations of the present order. How the order is made is a political question. The established powers blend structural dynamics and concerted collec- tive action as triggers to order making. The revisionist states usually rely on the arguments of national action. Finally, while the contemporary conﬁguration is claimed as quintessen- tial and unchangeable by the traditional proponents of the liberal inter- national order, it is believed to be irreversibly altered by its detractors. The stability argument takes many forms. First, realists are in any case loath to change—they see no possibility of any systemic transformation of the international system. They claim structural anarchy to be irreversible. Only the distribution of power or the polarity of the system changes from one period to another (Waltz 1977). Liberals of course are committed to change. But, as the works of Alexandre Kojève in the past and Francis Fukuyama in our times have shown, liberals can also freeze transforma- tions by arguing the ideological triumph of liberalism over all its rivals (Roth 1985; Fukuyama 1992). The neoliberals make similar arguments based on the unshakeable sovereignty of the market (Keohane and Nye 1989). This perspective allows technological changes but negates polit- ical transformation. All changes thus become rudimentary and superﬁcial; they do not usher in anything fundamentally novel. The critiques of the liberal order, in sharp contrast, see the order crumbling in front of our eyes. This is partly because of the inherent ﬂaws of the order itself (Mearsheimer 2019); but it is also the result of revolutionary changes in technologies of communication arising out of globalisation and the simul- taneous rise of economic nationalism that has undermined the advances of the same globalisation process (Kornprobst and Paul 2021). The economic and political consequences of such rapid transformation in trends cannot be predicted accurately. Much of this argument is unde- niably based on conventional nationalist sentiments and favours changing the given power structure by democratising the prevailing order based on Western dominance. However, the claims of power transition apart, there is little engagement with the wider conﬁgurations of the given order, its purposes, and its direction. The contemporary order, like orders of the past, remains open ended on the possibility of change.

42 S. CHATTERJEE AND A. KUMAR II Our second formulation concerns the logic of control which relates to the changes in the role of the nation-state. The contemporary times have shown an extraordinary attraction to the authoritarian and strong leader, often accompanied by the resurgence of masculine orientations and hierarchical political control even within formal democracies. This largely involves the reworking of identities through populist politics, prompting the need for fresh reﬂections. The rise of right-wing populist forces is partly the result of states losing their traditional control of borders and a remarkable escalation in competitive regulatory protec- tionism the world over. It has also gained strength due to the growing emotional insecurities of the people resulting from the ruthless competi- tion of a merit-based system of material prosperity that fails to insulate people against alienation and loneliness (Rhodes-Purdy et al. 2021). It is characterised by a realignment of identities to resonate with the common masses, often leveraging shared sentiments of disenfranchise- ment or discontent with the existing order—an order that has been created and is being led by the established elite groups (Bang and Marsh 2018; Noury and Roland 2020). Beyond mere political manoeuvring, this process also denotes a transformation in societal norms and values with far-reaching consequences. It often involves the use of symbols, narra- tives, and rhetoric that resonate with the targeted group’s identity. This can include appeals to tradition, history, and heritage, as well as the use of divisive language that emphasises differences between ‘us’ and ‘them’. The polarisation and fractiousness engendered by such identity-driven politics have led to social fragmentation, undermining the prospects for consensus and compromise. While the politics of identity remains central to this drama, the signif- icance of the role of neoliberal economics as providing its material foundations can hardly be exaggerated. The tension between the promises of neoliberalism and its potential societal repercussions is a deﬁning feature of contemporary political discourse. To begin with, neoliberalism has led to a perceived democracy deﬁcit, as it often prioritises market forces over democratic decision-making (Joppke 2023). This has created a sense of disenfranchisement among certain groups, who feel that their interests are not being represented. Neoliberal policies such as deregula- tion and limited social spending have led to increased income inequality and job insecurity among the middle class. Moreover, austerity measures

3 GEOECONOMICS, GEOPOLITICS … 43 and cutbacks in welfare programmes have created a sense of insecurity among vulnerable populations (Inglehart and Norris 2016; Rodrik 2021). Tapping into the anxieties generated by such policies, right-wing populists have blamed immigration and globalisation and have advocated for strict immigration controls and protectionist economic policies. Thus, while neoliberalism has indeed spurred economic growth in certain regions, it has simultaneously marginalised those sections of the population who perceive themselves as alienated and dispossessed. Right-wing populist leaders often exploit this discontent, presenting themselves as champions of the silent majority, promising to rectify economic imbalances and restore a sense of security through protectionist measures and nationalist policies. While populist governments are not identical to dictatorial or fascist regimes, their reliance on the leader’s will and their tendency to exclude those deemed outside the ‘good’ or ‘right’ people pushes constitutional democracy to its limits and potentially paves the way to authoritarianism. It blurs the lines that make representative democracy function and merges the notion of sovereignty with the voting majority while side-lining delib- eration and opposition (Urbinati 2019). Thus, even though populism is not anti-democratic by deﬁnition, it tends towards illiberal and authori- tarian practices. It is pertinent to note that the post-1945 world order, commonly known as the liberal international order (LIO), is actually an amalgamation of twin order building projects: the Westphalian project which gives primacy to state sovereignty and the liberal international project which encourages cooperation among states based on the values of liberal democracy, pluralism, and individual rights (Ikenberry 2011). As Robert Patman ( 2019) points out, populists worldwide “yearn for a world ruled by independent nation-states that brook no constraints on their sovereignty by multilateral institutions or international rules” ( 2019: 295). Thus, they prefer to retain only the Westphalian strand of the order building project while undermining the liberal strand. According to the detractors of the LIO, these tendencies were inherent in the policies required to uphold that very order. For instance, Mearsheimer’s scepticism about its staying power leads him to proclaim the demise of the LIO, which, according to him, was destined to fail because it contained the seeds of its own destruction. He points out three inherent ﬂaws in the LIO ( 2019: 30). First was the ambitious attempt to spread liberal democracy worldwide. This endeavour faced signiﬁ- cant obstacles, including resistance from nationalistic forces within target

44 S. CHATTERJEE AND A. KUMAR countries often resulting in conﬂicts and wars and ultimately undermining the order’s legitimacy. Second, LIO’s strong preference for interna- tional institutions and open borders clashed with the powerful forces of nationalism, which prioritise national sovereignty and identity. Promo- tion of open borders and refugee acceptance stirred tensions in a world where national identity is highly valued. Finally, the pursuit of hyper- globalisation, aimed at reducing trade barriers globally led to job losses, stagnant wages, and increased income inequality within liberal states. It also made the international ﬁnancial system less stable, causing recur- ring ﬁnancial crises. These economic challenges fuelled discontent with the liberal order and encouraged protectionist policies. These three ﬂaws, often interrelated, played a pivotal role in undermining the liberal inter- national order, contributing to political divisions, populist movements, and shifts towards nationalism in various Western democracies. Moreover, these vulnerabilities of the LIO have highlighted the difﬁculty in neatly distinguishing between trade and commerce on the one hand and national security on the other into separate registers. This entanglement of geoeco- nomics with geopolitics in contemporary times has necessitated, in some views, the retention of greater control over the market by the state for the sake of comprehensive security (Farrell and Newman 2023). The intermeshing of geopolitics and geoeconomics makes it difﬁcult to deploy ‘pure’ theoretical categories. Apparently, our account reads like a standard realist take on the international order. However, the shifts towards nationalism, proclivity to economic protectionism, and the emphasis on Westphalian sovereignty should not lead us to confuse the ‘logic of control’ with the standard realist position because unlike conventional realism, which views states as unitary actors in an anarchic international system, the ‘logic of control’ recognises the complex inter- play between domestic politics and international relations as well as the pivotal role networks and supply-chain economies play in the contem- porary order. It is an ‘intermestic’ perspective that takes into account both domestic and international factors and recognises states as active agents rather than passive responders. The rise of right-wing populist forces worldwide, for instance, is not just a reaction to global phenomena such as immigration and globalisation, but also a response to domestic issues such as income inequality, job insecurity, and welfare cutbacks.

3 GEOECONOMICS, GEOPOLITICS … 45 III All orders provide for emergency situations, some more formally than others. Since the international order is structurally decentralised but inter- meshed, institutionalisation is a major challenge. However, the longevity of the present order, in spite of the many challenges it has faced, is primarily explained by its capacity to go beyond the normal, to name existential threats and securitise domains, and, most crucially, to normalise exceptions (Schweller 2001;H u y s m a n s 2006). The third logic we propose is the ‘logic of exceptionalism’, which comes into play during extraordinary circumstances that disrupt the normal operations of order and control. These are situations that require exceptional measures, often leading to an expansion of state power and a reconﬁguration of soci- etal norms. All orders have logic of exception built into them to deal with emergencies and states leverage these emergencies to re-imagine and strengthen their sovereignty. While we use the COVID-19 pandemic as an illustrative case study for this logic, this is not limited to pandemics but applies to any crisis that disrupts the status quo and necessitates exceptional responses. As the world witnessed the COVID-19 pandemic, attention was legit- imately hijacked by epidemiologists and healthcare professionals, whose diagnosis and prognosis shaped how governments and societies responded to this crisis. As things stood, there was a wide consensus among the scientiﬁc community that social distancing and lockdown were essential as they helped disrupt the chains of contamination and thereby slowed down the rate of infection within communities. While some countries, like Vietnam, did quite well in containing the threat, most African states, and many European and Asian states struggled. Their initial carelessness in controlling the spread of the disease by easy communication proved very costly. Still others like the US also reacted late at least partly because of the government’s unwillingness to appreciate the gravity of the situa- tion owing to a variety of reasons, including business considerations and poor cognition. Despite these variations, one thing was crystal clear: the states fought the disease and seemed to be our best guarantee in this war against the contagion. Yet, the crisis was not a state-based one. In fact, as the UN Secretary- General António Guterres commented, “We are at war with a virus” and normal rules would not apply (United Nations 2020). While the idea of the human family has undergirded a part of the UN system right from its

46 S. CHATTERJEE AND A. KUMAR inception, as reﬂected in the steady work done by its many less glamorous specialised agencies and non-governmental organisations that work with it, it has never been a political lodestar in the past. This pandemic was an opportunity to test its political, as against moral, viability, though trends ultimately failed to provide very encouraging signs. There was hardly any global upscaling to this new normal. As border closing and shutdowns were hailed as effective and necessary measures to ﬁght the virus, the free movement of health workers and volunteers to various parts of the world was restricted or curbed in the interest of the containment of the threat itself (Flinders 2021). This meant that no matter how much we emphasised the global nature of the threat and the need to develop responses in keeping with it, science itself seemed to be working against the logic of standard human- itarian assistance models that are based on movement and mobilisation across frontiers. In a bizarre and paradoxical way, solidarity seemed to demand isolation rather than mixing, distance rather than connectivity, and self-discipline rather than interventionism. This seriously complicated the making of global strategies as we have traditionally understood them, their efﬁcacy or past success notwithstanding. More importantly, science had once again strengthened the cause of sovereignty at a critical hour. A cursory glance into what happened across the globe revealed that states were desperately trying to wage the war as they understood it, deploying resources available to them, in terms of political strategies and cultural representations that were thought to be relevant. Rather than the nature of the political system, the stock of social and political capital became far more important here (Sandset and Villadsen 2023). Some states found distancing and lockdown socially difﬁcult and politically costly, as the arithmetic of proﬁt got adversely affected by such moves. Others saw it easier to adopt restrictions as these societies were more dependent on their states for a wide range of services and beneﬁts. Society’s rela- tive autonomy vis-à-vis the state was therefore a good measure of the success or failings of state-dictated hard measures in two senses. First, the more we depend on the state, the easier it is for the state to bend us for our good (or bad). And the more we think like the state, the lesser the extractive cost of the state to implement difﬁcult measures that would at some point prove costly to the people. Second, science is the modern hand-maiden of sovereign politics. When science becomes the language, politics, at least in the existential sense, gets invisible. This invisibility is a priceless possession of sovereign states.

3 GEOECONOMICS, GEOPOLITICS … 47 In a nutshell, the COVID-19 pandemic afforded yet another oppor- tunity for the state to re-imagine itself and buttress sovereignty by way of performance rather than by control alone. The strengthening has happened in three ways. First, as Pratap Bhanu Mehta ( 2020) has argued, it allowed the state to improve its surveillance of the population on scien- tiﬁc grounds. No one will complain if the state wants to put in place mechanisms to track the movements of people, map their travels and stays, and ﬁnd out who they are talking to, all for their physical safety and well- being. These mechanisms deployed a wide array of technologies that are likely to stay as the threats from the COVID-19 virus may have subsided but a new pandemic may arise in time soon. If the threats remain, the deterrents are our best bets. If people think that regulations are needed for their safety and security they seldom resist. Good science told them that pandemics needed isolation. Isolation was not voluntary but required political action. Our collective action problem was overcome by the regu- latory state (Eck and Hatz 2020). We have let the state intrude on our ways and those who resisted were obscurantist who needed to be coerced. Second, the pandemic helped the state to shore up its defects and governmentality gaps. It became clear that sovereignty was the precondi- tion to effective service delivery. Its failings only called for strengthening the powers of governments for states that gave more autonomy to their society were precisely the ones that failed to save lives. As the Italian example shows, healthcare quality is not the solitary variable at play. There has to be political resolve and hard muscles in equal measures to steer them properly in the right direction. The state, therefore, got more active in binding the community closely by arguing that the lack of tightness counts heavily in crisis situations. The more close-knit as a people we are, the easier it is for the state to ﬁght enemies, be it a virus or an untrust- worthy ‘other’ (Dionne and Turkmen 2020). Sovereignty as practice is a habit of mind. The apparent performative success of a sovereign dispen- sation in treating demographics as nameless entities standardised for the effective delivery of services only nurtured that habit. Finally, the state gained by securitising the disease (Harrington 2021; Gozdecka 2021). The Secretary-General’s use of the word ‘war’, no matter his real intentions, is instructive here. Faced with an unprece- dented threat, which is claimed as the severest since World War II (again the parallel is germane), the states found it opportune to deploy the emergency mode of operation in its basic civilian functions that called for unquestioned legitimacy and full public support behind its moves.

48 S. CHATTERJEE AND A. KUMAR In any such event, if the state proves it can not only save citizens against real battles but also in wars against deadly diseases, it arrogates to itself such moral and political capital that challenging it for misdeeds and excesses would sound hollow and mischievous to even the most level-headed citizens (Greitens 2020; Huang 2021). Irrespective of the ultimate outcome of the state’s war against the virus, neither science nor humanity emerged as the winner. Žižek ( 2020) wrote that the emergency highlighted issues that had always remained with us. Issues like ‘extreme inequality, the commodiﬁed digitisation of our lives, and institutional disregard for the environment’ were brought forth in public imagination due to the normalised exceptions induced by the pandemic. It showed that the politics of life was also the politics of death—many lives had to be sacriﬁced for saving the sacrosanct ones. It also aggravated the growing gig economy of footloose labour who serve an increasingly anonymous capitalist order all over the world. Latour ( 2021) reads the crisis as a reminder of the things to come—an inevitable consequence of the much larger ecological disaster that awaits us. Both Žižek and Latour in their own ways have called for a fundamental reordering of our relations with life—be it the environment or the global economy. While these philo- sophical interventions are rich and nuanced, they miss out on the reality of the contemporary political order that is marked by the dual tensions arising out of several geopolitical and economic faultlines. Conclusion The theoretical positions on order demand discrimination of relevance and some aspects become invariably more important than others. Thus liberals ﬁnd the order to be based on capitalism, liberal democratic insti- tutions, rule of law, the idea of science and progress, and the possibility of transformation. Realists, in sharp contrast, read orders by the distribution of power and polarity. Constructivists deﬁne orders based on ideational imagination, materiality, and identity. In reality, however, systems of order are never univariate. They are innately complex and include many elements, parameters, justiﬁcations, and rules that govern relations among units. The present order is largely built on the political capital of the post-1945 period. Hence, the order remains by and large liberal, notwith- standing the deep contestations against an allegedly unequal distribution

3 GEOECONOMICS, GEOPOLITICS … 49 of privilege resulting from the same. It is broadly committed to democ- racy, free trade and exchanges, rule of law, multilateralism, incremental changes, and stability. Its overarching commitment is to the idioms of modernity predicated on the sovereignty of science and technology. This means, despite many political, social, and economic variations, states and societies remain by and large tethered to the forces of modernisation and industrialisation. While the world continues to witness the ascension of indigenous culture and traditional ways of life, along with a politics of de- globalisation and manifest rejection of liberal values, there is no apparent deviation from the material attractions of modern life. Nevertheless, the institutional architecture surrounding this order seems under a lot of stress. It is deeply contested along both strategic and economic lines. The Western alliance led by the US is in favour of retaining the status quo, although their capacity to preserve the given mili- tary balance is declining. The revisionist states are increasing in number but they do not present a united front. China, Russia, Turkey, and Iran, among others, are revisionist powers but their policies are not entirely in sync. The Russia-Ukraine War has undoubtedly brought these states together but a countervailing coalition to the US-led NATO alliance is yet to be formed. To mitigate the challenge of the revisionist states, espe- cially in the Indo-Paciﬁc, the US has entered into bilateral and minilateral strategic partnerships based on the ‘hub and spokes’ model. However, countries like India, Brazil, South Africa, and many Asia–Paciﬁc countries continue to be the classical swing states with a broad strategic portfolio that shares values with both the West and the revisionist powers (Wilkins 2022; Ciorciari and Haacke 2019). On the economic front too, there is a deep-set crisis. The after-effects of the global recession are still strong. The Chinese economic model stresses supply chains based on Chinese manufacturing and capital inﬂows that are increasingly getting contested across the world. The West theoretically remains committed to a capitalist economy but protectionism is on the rise in many parts, which is primarily linked with domestic political compulsions. The developing economies navigate the middle ground. There is no dominant narrative that unites these tendencies and the claims of equity and justice cut across the mili- tary and economic domains. Thus geoeconomics assumes signiﬁcance as liberal, open, and globalised economic policies are increasingly contested by inward, protectionist, and regionalised tendencies where the intricate networks of corporate capital are getting instrumentalised in the inter- ests of geopolitical interests. Investments and ﬁnancial instruments are

50 S. CHATTERJEE AND A. KUMAR progressively drawn into geopolitical strategies that weaponised economic capabilities for political gains. Second, the rise of right-wing populism as a response to the perceived loss of state control over borders and increased regulatory protectionism has somewhat transformed the role of the nation-state in contempo- rary times. Populist leaders leverage identity politics, using symbols and narratives to resonate with disenfranchised groups and emphasising divi- sions between ‘us’ and ‘them’. Neoliberal economics plays a signiﬁcant role here, as it prioritises market forces over democratic decision-making, contributing to income inequality and job insecurity. Populist leaders exploit these grievances, promising protectionist and nationalist policies. While not inherently anti-democratic, populism tends towards illiberal and authoritarian practices, ultimately challenging the liberal international order. Third, the COVID-19 pandemic has exacerbated these trends which were already underway. The pandemic occurred alongside the long global recession that has proved to be remarkably obstinate. The regulatory state is the answer to our failure to solve problems collectively through volun- tary actions. We have continuously let the state intrude on our beneﬁt and the state has gained by securitising its survival. This has led to the democratisation of the condition of exception that normalises the use of coercion in virtually all spheres of life. We argue that the present order, like all orders, is made up of mecha- nisms of control, stability, and rules of exceptionalism. The distinctiveness of the present international order lies in its inseparable duality of geoe- conomicsand geopolitics and the unmistakable turn towards a politics of populism that poses severe challenges to the constitutive norms of the given order. Still, the time has not come yet to write the cenotaph of the liberal order. The growing pervasiveness of the logic of exceptionalism notwithstanding, the present order is predicated upon the shared norms of modernity that may ultimately withstand the recent predicament as it has weathered many crises in the past. CHAPTER 4 In Search of Global South Liberalism: Prospects and Challenges Gazala Fareedi The dichotomy between security issues on the one hand and economic issues on the other in the study of international politics is increasingly becoming blurred in contemporary times. Traditionally security issues were considered to be in the realm of ‘high-politics’ and socio-economic- cultural issues in the domain of ‘low-politics’. However, this evolving complex relationship between international security and international economy seems to be taking on new dynamics in the contemporary global landscape. This phenomenon is demonstrated by the term ‘geoeconomics’ which refers to the use of economic means to achieve political ends which may also be security related. Economic sanctions, trade restrictions, devel- opment aid, investments and other economic carrot and stick policies may be used to coerce states into compliance with political and security-related G. Fareedi (B) Southﬁeld College, University of North Bengal, Darjeeling, India e-mail: gazalafareedi@gmail.com © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_4 55

56 G. FAREEDI demands. The greater the interdependence, especially asymmetrical inter- dependence, the more states become willing to use and make other states vulnerable to the use of such geoeconomic tactics. The term ‘geoeconomics’ was used by Edward Luttwak in an article published in 1990 titled, ‘From Geopolitics to Geoeconomics: Logic of Conﬂict, Grammar of Commerce’. In the context of the post-cold war international system, he argued that “the methods of commerce are displacing military methods- with disposable capital in lieu of ﬁre- power, civilian innovation in lieu of military-technical advancement, and market penetration in lieu of garrisons and bases” (Luttwak 1990: 17). The emphasis was on the point that “economic regulation is as much a tool of statecraft as military defences ever were” and that the logic of war and conﬂict is increasingly being played out through the grammar of commerce (Luttwak 1990:18). According to him, although “under whatever name, ‘geoeconomics’ has always been an important aspect of international life”, in the past, strategic priorities always over-shadowed commercial priorities. However, in the post-cold war period, there has been a “devaluation of military strength as an instrument of statecraft in the direct relations of the greatest powers” (Luttwak 1990: 20–21). This further leads to a scenario where both the causes and instruments of war are increasingly going to be economic in nature (Luttwak 1990: 21). Following the popularisation of the term since the publication of this article, contemporary scholars have argued that there has been a shift from geopolitics to geoeconomics in international politics. Sören Scholvin and Mikael Wigell have argued that “States increasingly practice power politics by economic means”. Hence the Western countries increasingly favour negative geoeconomic policies like economic sanctions to deal with cases like Iran’s nuclear weapons programme or the Russian annexation of Crimea. Other emerging powers like China, Brazil and South Africa use positive geoeconomic tools such as investments to build inﬂuence and garner alliances. Through alliances such as the BRICS, there is an effort to soft balance the Western powers economically (Scholvin and Wigell 2019:1–2). Blackwill and Harris have written that “Russia, China and others now routinely look to geoeconomics means, often as a ﬁrst resort, and often to undermine American power and inﬂuence” while America has increasingly forgotten the use of this traditional method (Blackwill and Harris 2017: 1). Starting with the Johnson and Nixon administration, the use of geoeconomic tools had lessened in the overall American foreign

4 IN SEARCH OF GLOBAL SOUTH LIBERALISM: PROSPECTS … 57 policy, making America more prone to using military means (Blackwill and Harris 2017: 3). In the 1990s, American policymakers became scep- tical of using geoeconomic tools to strengthen American power due to many factors. First, since the ‘unipolar moment’ lacked any serious chal- lengers to the system, economic liberalism prevailed. Second, American economists strictly adhered to the separation of positive sum realm of economy and negative sum realm of power politics (Mandelbaum 2014: 6). During this period, America’s hegemony prevailed over both the spheres of geopolitics and geoeconomics (Baracuhy 2019: 18). However, as has been argued by Braz Baracuhy, with the start of the new millen- nium, a noticeable shift occurred in the global landscape, revealing a distinct separation in the distribution of economic and political inﬂu- ence. This led to the detachment of geoeconomics from geopolitics. A multipolar economic framework emerged, encompassing innovation, investment, production, trade and, notably, economic growth. The tradi- tional centres of economic power and signiﬁcant geoeconomic players, such as France, Germany, Great Britain, Japan and the United States, now existed alongside emerging centres of economic inﬂuence, including Brazil, China and India, which have become increasingly active in the realm of geoeconomics (Baracuhy 2019: 18). Hence, an increasing number of nations are engaging in geopolitics using various economic instruments to pursue goals that were tradi- tionally associated with military pressure or conquest. These include not only great powers like Russia and China, but also the Gulf States and others (Blackwill and Harris 2017: 4–5). The major shift that has taken place is that this new application of geoeconomics by other coun- tries is without any hesitation with regard to the preservation of the global liberal economic order which US foreign policy protected and promoted during its heyday. Blackwill and Harris argue that rising powers “pledge no particular allegiance to these same liberal economic under- standings, do not take any such disciplinary divides between geo-politics and economics in their own policy making, and are thoroughly comfort- able with harnessing economic tools to work their strategic will in the world” (Blackwill and Harris 2017:7).

58 G. FAREEDI Challenges to Complex Interdependence The theory and practice of geoeconomics therefore raises a fundamental challenge to the theory and practice of liberalism in international politics, which broadly argues that interdependence and trade among states may lead to further cooperation and reduce the chances of conﬂict. Concep- tually, ‘geoeconomics’ aligns itself to the realist theory of international politics (Blackwill and Harris 2017: 9) which explains state behaviour on the basis of concepts like survival, self-help, statism, zero sum game and competition for relative power. Geoeconomics has highlighted the limi- tations of the liberal economic and political order. With the increasing economic interdependence among states and various other non-state actors, the risks and vulnerabilities are also on the rise. Henry Farrell and Abraham L Newman have outlined “how states increasingly ‘weaponize interdependence’ by leveraging global networks of informational and ﬁnancial exchange for strategic advantage” (Farrell and Newman 2019: 42–79). They argue that instead of a decentralised version of complex interdependence taking place globally, there has emerged an unequal ‘hub and spoke’ version of interdependence where power is magniﬁed in the central nodes. Focusing on international ﬁnancial system and global internet communications, they argue that the central nodes for both these systems lie predominantly within the advanced industrial countries like the United States and United Kingdom. These systems can be weaponised by these countries through two methods: ‘panopticon’ and ‘chokepoint effects of networks’ (Farrell and Newman 2019: 42–79). They argue that, “In the former, advantaged states [having territorial and legal jurisdiction over network hubs] use their network position to extract informational advantages vis-à-vis adversaries, whereas in the latter, they can cut adver- saries off from network ﬂows” (Farrell and Newman 2019:42–79). From the ‘weaponization of interdependence’, Mark Galeotti has moved a step further and argued that contemporarily the world is going through ‘the weaponisation of everything’ and we live in a state of permanent crisis or permacrisis (Galeotti 2021). He argues that although traditional warfare as in the twentieth century still exists, such a kind of warfare has become rarer as it has become both socially (body bags) and economically expen- sive. Warfare has therefore gone on to be fought in other realms of economy, politics, legality and culture, which he titles twenty-ﬁrst century warfare. According to Galeotti, “What is more, these conﬂicts are often not only distant but covert, and as a result undeclared, unacknowledged,

4 IN SEARCH OF GLOBAL SOUTH LIBERALISM: PROSPECTS … 59 even unrecognized…[creating] a potential world of permanent, subli- mated conﬂict, of the political struggle of all against all” (Galeotti 2021: 209). However, what needs to be underlined is that conﬂict and war in different forms have always existed in human life. As Andrew Linklater has mentioned, “No such exercise in political imagination can ignore Freud’s safe prediction that societies may never reach the point where they no longer need to guard against those who will seize any opportunity to exploit the vulnerable and to inﬂict harm in other ways” (Linklater 2011: 2). This does not however preclude the striving for establishing a collec- tive understanding of the manner in which conﬂict can be reduced and cooperation might be broadened and deepened globally. Linklater has further emphasised that in contrast to predatory animals, humans have the capacity for cultural development, allowing them to structure their interactions in a way that restrains aggressive tendencies and manages the ability to cause harm (Linklater 2011: 3). Even though the terms geoe- conomics, weaponised interdependence, hybrid warfare and permacrisis have come to re-organise the international security and political dynamics, different versions of the same Machiavellian strategies have always been at play throughout human life. This does not in any manner undermine human beings’ capacity for cooperation and progress. Use of Geoeconomics and Nature of Permacrisis The use of geoeconomics, even though not under its present nomen- clature, has always existed in human interactions. In fact, in the under- standing of power, one can say that ‘A’ has power over ‘B’ if ‘A’ can make ‘B’ do what it wants it to do (Menon 2008: 148). This can be done by ‘A’ in three ways: ﬁrst, by the threat of physical retaliation if disobeyed (use of force/military use/traditional warfare/stick policy); second by giving or withdrawing certain inducements (carrot policy/ giving-taking away of economic beneﬁt/other incentives); and third by soft power where ‘B’ will automatically do what ‘A’ wants it to do due to its afﬁnity with ‘A’. Here, geoeconomics comes under the second category of the way in which power is exercised. With regard to inter- national politics, if push comes to shove, states have a proclivity to use all three versions of power in their interactions with other states. Braz Baracuhy has argued that “geoeconomics and geo-politics should be seen together under the framework of geostrategy” (Baracuhy 2019: 14).

60 G. FAREEDI According to him, “Geoeconomics and geo-politics are distinct yet related and complementary types of geostrategy” (Baracuhy 2019:18). Amer- ican geostrategy in East Asia under the Obama administration included both the concepts of geopolitics and geoeconomics. Although the United States under the Trump administration withdrew from the TPP (Trans- Paciﬁc Partnership), but this was the geoeconomic aspect to complement and supplement the ‘Pivot to Asia’ geopolitical strategy. This was done in order to overall balance the rise of China in the region (Baracuhy 2019: 15). With regard to the timeline, although the term came into prominence only after the 1990s, Baracuhy has outlined that America’s containment strategy after the end of the Second World War included both geopolitical and geoeconomic aspects, with the formation of the North Atlantic Treaty Organization as the former aspect and the Marshall Plan as the latter aspect of the strategy (Baracuhy 2019: 21). What also needs to be underlined is that geoeconomics as a strategy has a greater probability to be used by those countries with a greater economic capacity. It cannot be used by most of the developing coun- tries in the Global South, especially smaller countries like small island states. ‘Global South’ is itself not a homogenous category and there are great variations within the category itself. However, most of the coun- tries within this category would still be categorised under the zone of developing economies. Hence it can be said that there is a greater proba- bility that these countries will be at the receiving end of the geoeconomic policies of the economically stronger industrialised developed countries. Also, it needs to be highlighted that the nature of the permacrisis for these countries might be of a very different kind than other countries. With regard to permacrisis, Mark Galeotti outlined a scenario of a war of everyone against everyone with mixed methods of attack and subversion through “Hybrid War. Grey Zone Warfare. Asymmetric War. Tolerance Warfare. Unrestricted War. Non-Linear Warfare” (Galeotti 2022:9 ) . All such overt and covert tactics requires an attacker/conspirator and a victim/attacked. However, for the many economically weaker and geographically smaller island states, the permacrisis is in the form of either abject poverty and extremely low human development index among its population, or existential crisis in the form of severe climate change impacts like submergence of entire territories under water due to the rise in the sea levels. These latter challenges do not require an active conspir- atorial enemy tactics behind the scenes planning subversion through various traditional and non-traditional means. The security concerns of

4 IN SEARCH OF GLOBAL SOUTH LIBERALISM: PROSPECTS … 61 the island states are non-traditional in nature, like climate change, unreg- ulated ﬁshing, pollution and oil spills. This is in stark contrast to the traditional security threats of the bigger countries. For example, one of the major concerns for United States, India and Australia is regarding the increasing strategic use of economic power by China (Baruah 2022). In order to send a message to the world, Maldives had in 2009 held a cabinet meeting underwater. Since rising sea levels can submerge the archipelago, Maldives literally faces a complete territorial existential threat (Maryam 2009). Highlighting the same issue in the ‘Oceans Nations: An Indo Paciﬁc Islands Dialogue Forum’ held in 2021, Ahmed Khaleel, minister of state for foreign affairs of Maldives, said, “For a lot of other countries, the threats [such as IUU ﬁshing and climate change] are on the margins. But for a country like the Maldives, its life or death itself” (Baruah 2022). Reforming Multilateralism This does not mean that island nation states cannot be subjected to or are free from the dangers of traditional military security threats and geoe- conomic strategies or a mix of both. That threat is always looming large over all sovereign states. However, both in the context of global problems like climate change which require global solutions and in the context of traditional military security threats, one of the solutions is multilateralism. In fact, for many developing countries which are not only geographically small but also militarily and economically weak, multilateral platforms play a very important role in both international political and economic governance. While the importance of the United Nations can be debated for developed countries, the same cannot be purported for developing/ underdeveloped countries. Not only does the United Nations give all countries an equal footing in the General Assembly, but it provides a very important platform to voice their concerns internationally. Perma- nent Representative and Ambassador to the UN for Fiji Satyendra Prasad points out: “We look to multilateralism for global governance….and we look to the multilateral system to provide us with the rules through which we negotiate and mediate our interests and protect our interest in global systems” (Baruah 2022). Based on the premises of the realist theory of international politics, the role of international organisations, regional organisations and non- governmental organisations come under scepticism. Apart from giving

62 G. FAREEDI international organisations an unimportant secondary role, it is even argued that such organisations are only a tool in the hands of the powerful countries. During the deadly corona virus pandemic, another specialised agency of the UN, the WHO (World Health Organization), came under heavy criticism. It was pointed out that the “talk of the town is that the state has become the sole provider of security and stability and that glob- alization is in retreat” (Fareedi 2020). But what needs to be realised is that states are worse off dealing with such global problems alone. The pandemic “has truly questioned the limiting theories of global politics which posit self-regarding/egoistic behaviour by states as the only natural behavior” (Fareedi 2020). In such crisis situations, the question is not one of whether or not states are willing to cooperate but rather what would be the catastrophic consequences of non-cooperation. This is not only true for problems in the non-traditional realm like climate change but also traditional problems like nuclear weapons and nuclear energy uses. The same can be held right for protection against emerging geoeconomic tactics. In fact, Galeotti who has outlined the concept of ‘the weaponisation of everything’ and a dismal international scenario of permacrisis has written that, “[I]t may appear that there is no place for international institutions such as the UN, for example, but arguably the opposite is true. It is, rather, that the focus needs to shift away from a concentration on ‘big war’ and towards setting and enforcing the new ‘etiquette’ of international rela- tions. Established international organizations of global governance like the International Court of Justice, Interpol, the World Health Organisation, International telecommunication Union and others will have to enhance their capabilities to address the emerging challenges. They need to be strengthened and broadened to play a more positive and constructive role in world affairs (Galeotti 2021: 210-212). The explicit need for the continued presence and play of varied insti- tutions of the international order has to be re-enforced with the need to reform these institutions to make these more democratic and equal. The power of the developed countries that underlined the establishment of these institutions has manifested itself in reserving a special place for themselves, whether it be regarding the membership and the veto power of the permanent ﬁve members of the Security Council or the voting powers in the International Monetary Fund, among others. One of the

4 IN SEARCH OF GLOBAL SOUTH LIBERALISM: PROSPECTS … 63 efforts towards establishing a more equitable multilateralism was made through the South-South Cooperation. The origins of this cooperation go back to the historic Asian-African Conference held in Bandung in 1955, where “the newly decolonized countries of the global south emphasized economic and cultural cooperation, human rights and the promotion of world peace” (Gray and Gills 2016: 558). This further went on to become a precursor to the formation of the Non-Aligned Movement. This became the political dimension of the South-South Cooperation. The economic dimension was put forward by the formation of the Group of 77 and the UNCTAD (United Nations Conference on Trade and Development) which called for the formation of the NIEO (New Inter- national Economic Order) in 1974 to end the economic domination of the Global North on the Global South. Unfortunately, by the 1980s with the rising debt crisis of the Global South countries coupled with the strict resistance by the Global North, the NIEO project could not take off. However, with the changing global scenario, Kevin Gray and Barry K. have argued that “There is a widespread sense today, however, that the time is ripe for moving SSC once again onto the centre stage of world politics and economics, and a renewed interest in its historic promise to transform world order” (Gray and Gills 2016: 558). Here, they focus on the role of India, Brazil, South Africa Dialogue Forum (2003) and BRICS (2001 and 2010) and their role in the construction of an alternative to the hegemonic and neo-colonial politics of the Global North. Whether the South-South Cooperation will be able to attain the objec- tives of promoting a more equitable economic and political world order is a question whose answer is surrounded by a lot of debates. The impor- tant principle is the maintenance of a rules-based international order where countries refrain from advancing short-term geoeconomic tools for protecting the rules-based system. The system or rules of the game might have been tilted towards powerful, but the larger objective is not to do away with the rules altogether but to reform them. Considering that the Global South is not a homogenous category, question arises as to the varied intentions and approaches of these countries as well. There are scholars who are of the view that the elite and the powerful countries in the Global South like China, India and Brazil will just go on to repro- duce the same unequal structures. On the other hand, there are scholars who are more hopeful about cooperation and its potential to transform the world. But the context has deﬁnitely changed. In contrast to earlier

64 G. FAREEDI times, when no countries from the Global South had the economic poten- tial, the renewed demands for restructuring of the unequal global trade and international organisations are taking place in a different context. Here the bargaining power of the Global South has increased with the rise of the economic potential of some of its countries. As Gray and Gills have argued, this “has led them to acquire the capacity for greater and more sustained economic cooperation with developing countries” (Gray and Gills 2016: 570). Secondly, instead of rejecting the already existing institutions of global trade which are dominated by the countries of the Global North, developing countries have taken an active part in them. For example, the WTO might have its problems, but developing countries are still a part of it, because not to be so would call for global trade marginal- isation (Gray and Gills 2016: 560). At the same time, they also highlight that now countries have got a new alternative with regard to loans espe- cially with the setting up of the BRICS Bank—New Development Bank in 2015. Hence emerging developing countries have now taken on the role of becoming signiﬁcant donors as well (Gray and Gills 2016: 562). Blackwill and Harris have argued that newly emerging countries, especially China, have no regard towards maintaining the global liberal economic order that the United States has preserved since establishment. Hence these new countries are more prone to using geoeconomic tactics in a ruthless manner (Blackwill and Harris 2017: 7). The same is reit- erated by the terms ‘debt trap’ and ‘rogue donor’. However, Gray and Gills have argued that, “while the ideologies deployed may be different from those of the West, both use political rhetoric to conceal their own interests. At the same time, as with the interventions of rising powers in other ﬁelds of SSC, there remain key questions as to whether emerging donors are capable of challenging the dominant conditionality-driven aid architecture, and whether a new aid paradigm can be established that moves beyond the pursuit of the national interest and focuses on the developmental needs of recipients” (Gray and Gills 2016: 563). However, with these changes and new structures in place, at least the recipient countries have a choice as to which institution would better serve their interests. They have been able to loosen their complete dependence on the countries of the Global North.

4 IN SEARCH OF GLOBAL SOUTH LIBERALISM: PROSPECTS … 65 Global South Liberalism An equitable form of multilateralism is one of the most important pillars on which to promote a system in which countries look towards maintaining long-term rules-based order instead of using geoeconomic strategies which can only be beneﬁcial in the short term. Multilateral forums can be used by various groupings, especially of the weaker states to strengthen fair and sustainable trade, economy, polity and justice. Economic expansionism has to be balanced with sustainable development, environmental protection and for maintaining basic human rights and welfare of the people. Narratives play a very important role in politics in general, whether domestic or international. Theories are one of the most important ways on which narratives are based. Policies, both domestic and international, are cocooned within the dominant narratives. The narra- tive of permacrisis globally can set the stage for extreme measures to be taken by the state actors. It might even lead to the rise of authoritar- ianism in states as leaders would justify the centralisation of powers to deal with emergency situations, thereby leading to a democratic back- sliding in many countries. In this context, Ole Waever has emphasised the dangers of securitisation of everything. He writes, “…addressing an issue in security terms still evokes an image of threat-defense, allocating to the state an important role in addressing it. This is not always an improve- ment” (Weaver 2007: 66). Securitisation essentially includes increasing the role of state negatively which can be very dangerous for individual rights and freedom. The state might even justify spying on its own citi- zens and setting up a panopticon like system for purposes of maintenance of security and stability. This narrative of permacrisis is well intentioned but it might lead to unintended negative consequences. One of the ways in which the narrative of the permacrisis, ‘weapon- isation of everything’ and ‘weaponisation of interdependence’ can be balanced and approached dialectically, is by posing it along with the narra- tive of the liberal international order. Since the liberal international order has come to be viewed in terms of domination by the West on the rest, there is a need for it to be reclaimed and remodelled. In this context, J. Luis Rodriguez and Christy Thorton have argued that most accounts of liberalism in the Global South follow either two trajectories: one is expansionism of the liberal project from the West to the rest where the Global North is essentially the rule maker and the Global South is merely the rule takers. The other trajectory is that of the critical approaches

66 G. FAREEDI which underline the West’s imperial project as the bedrock of the liberal order. However, Rodriguez and Thorton, based on case studies from Latin America, have instead argued that Global South countries have not been merely rule/norm takers in the whole liberal international order project. It has been pointed out that “They [Latin American states] have insisted that the rules of a rules-based order should apply not just to the poor, weak or indebted but to the rich, strong, and powerful as well” (Rodriguez and Christy 2022: 627). They further argue that instead of merely being rule takers, leaders and diplomats from Latin America have actively involved themselves in international processes that have gone on to shape the varied contours of the Liberal International Order (LIO) . More importantly these processes continue even today and should not be ignored by the political and mainstream academic discourse surrounding the LIO (Rodriguez and Christy 2022: 627). There needs to be a de- linking of the Liberal International Order and the West/Global North as countries from the Global South have been as much a part of this order as any other country. They also point out that it might be assumed that many countries of the Global South having been at the receiving end of the spectrum of great power economic and political processes and would have refused the liberal international order but they have repeatedly sought to reform and even strengthen the institutions that have governed that order over time” (Rodriguez and Christy 2022: 627). Even the discourse surrounding the rising powers, which have beneﬁted from the liberal order both economically and politically, focuses on their resistance against this order. Based on the realist notions of balance of power, “…scholars argue that rising powers might attempt to build a new order- even through war- if the order does not adapt to accommodate their demands” (Rodriguez and Christy 2022: 630). However, the reality is quite different especially based on the study of countries from Latin America. “Latin America was the region where the United States ﬁrst experimented with the practices and policies of hegemony.... It is precisely because of their familiarity with the vicissitudes of hegemonic behaviour, however, that Latin Americans have advocated for institutions and frameworks in the international order that can address problems collectively and can counter domination by constraining the unilateral prerogatives of great powers… Thus, Latin America offers examples of how Global South actors might seek to modify some aspects of the international order while defending

4 IN SEARCH OF GLOBAL SOUTH LIBERALISM: PROSPECTS … 67 others that might beneﬁt similar countries” (Rodriguez and Christy 2022: 630). Role of new international and inter-governmental organisations is vital in this context. The recent enlargement of the BRICS with the addition of the six new members in the Johannesburg Summit in August 2023 goes on to show the possibility of reclaiming and remodelling the international order. Although BRICS might have its own share of problems, but it has remained steadfast for 15 years and is increasingly playing greater role in economic governance with the BRICS New Development Bank. This is a modest beginning, but a beginning to provide an alternative nonethe- less. Similarly, from the perspective of India, it is a rising power and it has consistently tried to shape the Liberal International Order through various means, especially with its active role in United Nations and its many organs. Conclusion Interdependence is being weaponised but the protection lies in coopera- tion itself. Countries have to move towards more partnerships, alliances and groups to solve not only global problems like climate change but also to protect themselves against such negative geoeconomic strategies. This is true not only for economically weaker countries but also the rising powers that have a stake in preserving the rules-based order that has led to their rise in the ﬁrst place. Hence the countries from the Global South need to be actively preserving this order. The fact that these rising powers are in a position to use geoeconomic strategies in the ﬁrst instance is because their economies have strengthened by being an active part of the liberal order in the ﬁrst place. This order as of now has its prob- lems and needs reform and remodelling, but it also needs reclaiming as well to make it more equal and sustainable. Also, a re-deﬁnition does not mean that its core principles and foundations are being challenged. The new reformed system needs to be built on the same positive rules- based foundations. Longer-term interests of preserving and claiming a reformed version of this order should be prioritised over geoeconomic strategies that would be more beneﬁcial only in the short term. There is no denying that global institutions need reform and restructuring so that more countries can have an equal power in the decision-making bodies. At the same time, we need to know that the future of the world lies

68 G. FAREEDI in a rules-based order strengthened by global institutions that promote values such as the human rights and liberal democracy. These values need continuous protection and promotion both in the domestic sphere and in the international arena. References Baruah, M. D. 2022.
