CHAPTER 7 International Diplomacy in the Era of Permacrisis: Competing Worldviews Supreeta Mehta The world did not have a chance to settle down properly after the end of the Cold War. From genocide in one part of the world to the countries ﬁrst experiencing globalisation, liberalisation, and privatisation, on the other end, the world has been in upheaval at each moment in time after the 1990s. Increased acceptance of globalisation, the spread of the market economy and subsequent economic opportunities, and the technological revolution led to two things: interconnectedness and emergence of non- traditional challenges. As multilateral institutions increased in number, the slogans for inclusivity were heard louder. Most interestingly, we witness a divergence of interests and opinions in the West, which has called the efﬁcacy of the Western-created and Western-led international institutions into question. From a ﬁnancial crisis to invasions, to massive migration inﬂux, to a pandemic, and now a war, the West has faced one blow after another while the developing world deals with its own political, social, S. Mehta (B) Khudiram Bose Central College, University of Calcutta, Kolkata, India e-mail: supreetamehta19@gmail.com © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_7 113

114 S. MEHTA and economic challenges as regional power centres emerge, ﬁghting for inﬂuence, and nationalistic sentiments running high at home. The Collins Dictionary Word of the Year, ‘permacrisis’, sums up the situation in international relations, where the world is going through one crisis after another (Shariatmadari 2022). Although they referred particu- larly to the crises that Europe is lurching from one to the other, it cannot be denied that a similar situation has enveloped international politics. The pandemic revealed how easily a virus can interrupt the day-to-day func- tioning of the entire world, as well as the potential of the same world, particularly some countries, to jump back up and ‘re-normalise’ the situ- ation. At the same time, the recurrent crises at the global level are posing signiﬁcant challenges for peace and security in the world. To put it simply, permacrisis refers to a set of circumstances in global politics that are deﬁned by recurring conﬂicts of varied nature without any end to them in sight. In other words, such a situation brings about a deep sense of instability and volatility in international relations. How did we arrive at this destination? The answer to this is even more complex and truthfully quite predictable when one looks at the recent history of international politics. The complexity of the matter at hand owes itself to the proliferation of philosophies and technologies, emanating from the Western liberal world order. The predictability of the present situation can be ascertained from the way power politics has played out since the latter half of the twentieth century, or even before that. One detail, however, that is highly characteristic of the current situation of permacrisis is that countries are no longer blindly forming themselves into camps. Instead, countries are carefully choosing their stances and strictly avoiding forming any enmities, unless there is an overwhelming political/security threat. The two distinct worldviews that existed during the period of the Cold War, as well as a non-aligned third, is a feature of a bygone era. This in turn is due to the overarching interconnectedness in the world today. It is a vicious circle. Competing worldviews, including multiple distinct strategies of the West, are perhaps the most important attributes of this situation of permacrisis. The present chapter seeks to explain the current environment of permacrisis through two examples, the Russia-Ukraine war and the conﬂictual situation in the Indo-Paciﬁc region. It also incorporates an understanding of how India deals with these same situations via its foreign policy standing and policies, to provide an angle of how developing

7 INTERNATIONAL DIPLOMACY IN THE ERA … 115 regional powers are responding to global politics in turmoil. The contri- bution discusses how there is essentially a transition in the world order itself and how the liberal international system is falling short of the requirements of the time. That along with the world being interconnected closely has called for a look outside Western security arrangements. The Russia-Ukraine war and the crisis in the Indo-Paciﬁc are two such conﬂicts that reveal the problems and challenges of current international politics, the response by the West, and the attitude of the developing countries/emerging powers to the same. This essentially brings out the points of divergence and the variations that have occurred in the global regimes of developed and developing countries. They also demonstrate how present disputes tend to be intractable and long-term in nature. Due to the interdependence in the world and the recurring nature of conﬂicts, that have been given the nomenclature of permacrisis, navi- gating multilateralism has become a complex task. It calls the Western-led and Western-created liberal order into question and presents multiple, competing worldviews. Although problems of terrorism, climate change, health-related crises, and migration have not been discussed in the present endeavour, they offer another angle of the present situation of permacrisis. These issues are more global in nature yet they provide deeper insights into domestic politics and opinions rather than international relations. However, it is outside the scope of this contribution to discuss these and as such we limit our focus on two exclusive geopolitical and geoeconomic discords of this decade. T he Russia-Ukraine War: V aried Responses Russia’s invasion of Ukraine and the consequent war has emerged as the most deﬁning conﬂict of the current times. It is also representative of and exempliﬁes the present environment of permacrisis in international rela- tions. In February 2022, Russia launched a full-scale invasion of Ukraine, after having sent some of its troops to Donetsk and Luhansk regions, which had broken away from Ukraine just a month before, to quell unrest. The invasion shocked the world, as some of the major interna- tional players came forward to condemn this act of aggression. One year later and Russia-Ukraine war continues to persist, in what seems to be another protracted conﬂict of the present times. The reasons for the inva- sion, the judgement on who is right and who is wrong, the historical events that have led up to this, and decisions taken by a myriad of state

116 S. MEHTA actors are factors that are out of the scope of the present chapter, as it does not further its objective. The pertinent considerations, however, are the global spill-over effects of the Russia-Ukraine war, in general, and partic- ularly on India’s political and economic situations, and what the conﬂict portrays about the present world order. The two most obvious impacts that stand out are political and economic in nature. Politically, Russia has received a massive barrage of criticism that is not mere rhetoric but also solidiﬁed by sanctions from some of the leading power centres. The United States came out as the biggest critic of Russia, along with several countries from the liberal-democratic political order. India, while not supporting the inva- sion, and maintaining that conﬂicts are best solved through dialogue, did not condemn, or vote against Russia on any international platforms. This is not exactly peculiar to Indian behaviour when it comes to sanctioning Russia’s acts of aggression. In a Lok Sabha session where a question was posed on the impact of the Russia-Ukraine war on India, in March 2022, the Minister of State in the Ministry of External Affairs, Smt. Meenakashi Lekhi stated: We have expressed deep concern at the worsening situation and called for immediate cessation of violence and end to all hostilities. Our state- ments at the Security Council and General Assembly have urged an urgent ceaseﬁre and ensuring safe passage for stranded civilians. We have also highlighted the humanitarian assistance extended by India to Ukraine and its neighbours at this hour of crisis … there is no choice but diplomacy and dialogue. We have emphasised … that the global order is anchored in international law, UN Charter, and respect for territorial integrity and sovereignty of states (Question No.3771 Impact of Russia-Ukraine War on India n.d.). On previous occasions, during the height of the Cold War, India had refrained from censuring Russian acts of aggression in Hungary (1956), Czechoslovakia (1968), and even when it invaded Afghanistan in 1979. For India, certain strategic reasons have pushed it to take the stance. Primarily, India does not want to sanction or cut off relations with Russia, as the countries of the Western liberal-democratic order have, and push it towards China as the only alternative and as a result further strengthening their relations (Tellis 2022). Geopolitical threats that India faces from China and Pakistan are quite real and India does not want Russia to be pushed towards that axis, even more so when Russia shares good relations

7 INTERNATIONAL DIPLOMACY IN THE ERA … 117 with China and Pakistan. So, while India is a democracy and supports a liberal-democratic order, it is the geographical proximity of political and security threats that has shaped India’s stance towards the war situation. India’s past and present foreign secretaries and diplomats have maintained time and again that India is not and will never be part of any military alliance. When it comes to international events, India perceives them and then makes a decision that is best suited to its interests (India’s Response to Russia’s War in Ukraine 2022). It does not blindly support any narra- tive or is not part of any camp. Hence its worldview does not represent that of any bloc or camp and may even seem contradictory at any point in time. This stance may be seen as being pragmatic in positive connotations and displays an attitude of strategic ambivalence if considered negatively. The economic effect worldwide of any crisis during present times resembles a ripple. The highly integrated and interdependent nature of the world has manifested these circumstances. The Russia-Ukraine war has led to a stark rise in the price of crude oil (as the US and Europe placed bans on multiple industries of Russia), volatility in the stock market, peaked inﬂation in many economies, and increased prices in food products as supply chains was dealt a major blow. India too faced high inﬂation, growth in food prices, devaluation of the rupee, and stress on foreign reserves. Indian exports declined as trade declined in its major export destinations (US and Europe). However, India’s strong ﬁscal policy and the economic outlook it adopted, braving the challenges of the pandemic, enabled it to withstand serious damage (The Times of India 2023). However, India has also come out of this scenario as a winner of sorts or at least, accrued certain proﬁts for the country itself. The ban on Russian crude oil led to a decline in the prices, India saw an opportunity to buy crude oil at a cheap rate in massive volumes (The Times of India 2023) as Russia jumped positions to become one of the largest trading partners of India during this time. At present India and Russia are set to become signiﬁcant trade partners as both coun- tries have identiﬁed areas of complementarities and joint efforts, albeit, with necessary tweaks and adjustments (Kulik 2023). One of the major impacts of the war on the global economic order has been the large-scale damage done to supply chains. However, it would not be incorrect to mention that global supply chains have demon- strated the probability of disruption in the event of a crisis, even before the Russia-Ukraine war. One of the biggest exports to be affected by the war has been food and raw materials. Russia and Ukraine are the

118 S. MEHTA biggest exporters of grains, such as wheat and barley. While Russia has damaged agricultural infrastructure in Ukraine and has been sanctioned, the major source of food grain import–export has been disrupted, leading to an increase in food prices as demand continues to increase in the face of a shortage of supply. Developing countries in Asia and Africa have been particularly affected. The main resources whose interruption in supply has particularly affected the West are oil and natural gas. Energy resources, along with an interruption in the supply of crucial metals have also created hindrances in the infrastructure development in the US and Europe (Hamilton 2023). Supply routes between China and Europe, which became quite active during the coronavirus pandemic, have been jammed as Russia is part of the said route (Stackpole 2022). The signif- icance of the maintenance of ﬂexible, independent, and neutral supply chains has been exempliﬁed by the Russia-Ukraine war in particular and geopolitical crises in general. The nature of supply chains in most cases is such that there is multiples of them that source, reﬁne, modify, and then ﬁnally deliver them. Hence, if any one of the components faces disruption or hindrances, then the entire process gets hampered. Russia and Ukraine are some of the main exporters of wheat and barley. The war has not only created global food shortages but has also severely impacted the economies of both the countries involved in the war, partic- ularly Ukraine. Ukraine’s Black Sea port has been blockaded by Russia and the former faces the real possibility of severe economic vulnerability and a humanitarian crisis (Naz and Kear 2022). Even the COVID-19 crisis has revealed the dangers of and challenges to the global supply of resources. The steps taken then were disrupted once again by the next geopolitical crisis. The perception of this apparent trend of disrup- tions due to conﬂicts/calamities, the recurrence of these situations, and the identiﬁcation of resultant problems in international relations have prompted the need for solutions. Even before the pandemic, the United States followed by the United Kingdom, the European Union, Australia, and South Korea had introduced economic policy changes in the form of export control and screening of foreign direct investment processes. The focus of these countries has also been the advancement of tech- nological processes in artiﬁcial intelligence, biotechnology, and the like (Goodman, Reynolds, and Fittipaldi 2022). Subsequently, these countries are looking at emerging economies for alternative supply chain routes, which in turn beneﬁt the latter as they seek to enhance their national political, economic, and strategic power.

7 INTERNATIONAL DIPLOMACY IN THE ERA … 119 Hence, just like the political order, the global economic order is also demonstrating a situation of ﬂux and transition. One of the answers to the complications of over-dependence and interconnectedness of global economies, during times of crises, has been to look inward. In other words, the ideas of self-reliance and sustainability have gathered momentum. However, there still need to be solutions to the system of transportation of resources that are already in place. Moreover, not all countries are equipped to sustain themselves on the basis of self-reliance alone. Thus, while certain established powers are facing setbacks, espe- cially European countries, the same situation has presented itself as an opportunity for others. The Western countries have well-developed supply routes, much wealthier economies, and technologically advanced indus- trialised zones, while developing countries fall short in these aspects. Moreover, the developing countries also require well-established and larger production capabilities and increased levels of foreign investments. A strong and stable government, with a healthy culture and environ- ment are prerequisite to gain global economic heft. Thus, emerging centres of power like India, Vietnam, Indonesia, and some others are now rapidly transforming themselves into potential manufacturing hubs and presenting alternative supply routes. China has already developed alternative trade routes to quite an extent (the Belt and Road Initiative was launched in 2013) as a substitute and set up banks like the Asian Infrastructure Investment Bank (AIIB) as another substitute to existing international ﬁnancial institutions. This is China’s strategy to counter Western ﬁnancial inﬂuence. However, during COVID-19, the world lost faith and started searching for a replacement for Chinese trade, goods, and supply chains, hence now we are witnessing the rise of potential supply chain routes in emerging economies like India. The ‘Make in India’ scheme (2014) and the ‘Self-Reliant India Scheme’ (2020) are examples of India trying to transform itself as a manufacturing hub. Furthermore, India is working multilaterally to diversify and strengthen new supply chain systems like the ‘Supply Chain Resilience Initiative’ with Australia and Japan (Goodman, Reynolds, and Fittipaldi 2022). Thus, we are now looking at an international economic order that is not exclusive to Western dominance. While there are at least a couple of major economic powers, regional economic power centres are also evolving and making their marks on global economic trends.

120 S. MEHTA Politics in the Indo-Pacific: T he Eye of the Storm The region of the Indo-Paciﬁc has emerged as an area of crucial strategic importance in international relations today. In terms of the current global political and economic order, which is in a state of ﬂux and transfor- mation, the Indo-Paciﬁc represents an arena where major and emerging powers are set to converge. The obvious competition is between the US and China. The former is trying to hold on to and increase its sway and weight in the region while the latter wants to create its alternate insti- tutions and is aggressively and proactively taking steps to establish its authority in the Indo-Paciﬁc. The Indo-Paciﬁc region offers huge reserves of oil and natural gas, important sea lanes of communication for the inﬂow and outﬂow of trade trafﬁc, and strategic raw materials among the littoral countries of Africa like tin, iron ore, uranium, diamond, gold, and some others (Kumar 2023). In political terms, it is part of the US’ national strategy to maintain a rule-based order in the Indo-Paciﬁc. In other words, it perceives the present condition of the area to be arbi- trary and problematic, particularly due to the presence and actions of the People’s Republic of China (PRC). According to the US, the PRC has a bullying character towards the countries in the Indo-Paciﬁc, referring to the varied examples of China’s problems with Taiwan, India, and in South China Sea. The US has raised a ﬁve-pronged approach to this area: 1. Advance a free and open Indo-Paciﬁc; 2. Build connections within and beyond the region; 3. Drive regional prosperity; 4. Bolster Indo-Paciﬁc security; and 5. Build regional resilience to transnational threats (The White House, Washington, and Executive Ofﬁce of the President: National Secu- rity Council, Washington DC 2022). For this, the US has heavily relied on building relations with the regional powers and is encouraging multilateral institutions to gather support for the achievement of a rule-based order in the region. From working on joint technological advancements to promoting an economic framework, to supporting India’s sustained regional leadership in South Asia, US involvement in the region demonstrates the signiﬁcance it attaches to it (The White House, Washington, and Executive Ofﬁce of the

7 INTERNATIONAL DIPLOMACY IN THE ERA … 121 President: National Security Council, Washington DC 2022). Undoubt- edly, it is the rapid ascendancy of another power centre that has stimulated US motivations to block its attempt by creating a counterforce. China’s stimulation for a dominating presence in the Indo-Paciﬁc stems from its ambitions of becoming a global power. The Indo-Paciﬁc holds the key to major resources and supply routes. In geopolitical terms, a good measure of sway in this region can translate into greater authority in international politics. Furthermore, it is not only the access to resources/raw materials but also that China’s majority of exports travel in this area to its destinations in South Asia, Southeast Asia, Africa, and West Asia. China’s understanding of the magnitude of ensuring safe and secure maritime access in the Indo-Paciﬁc has thus existed for a long time. Its Navy, the People’s Liberation Army Navy (PLA-N) has been revamped and refocused over the years to ﬁt the needs of China in this region. However, the country is also aware that its ambitions here can be easily thwarted by other maritime powers like the US and India. Hence China has come forward to act as the security power of the region and has made heavy inroads into the countries of South Asia and Africa to gain leverage. Infrastructural development has been the main mantra of the Asian heavyweight, as countries in South Asia and Africa started falling for the attractive development opportunities ﬁnanced by China. The BRI was launched in 2013 with the aim to help China in creating an alterna- tive trade route, by land and sea, connecting the countries from Asia to Europe and those in between. This has enabled the country to not only demonstrate itself as the main guarantor of infrastructural development in the region but also to gain a foothold as well as deal with traditional and non-traditional maritime threats in the vast region (Kumar 2023). India is extremely concerned with the need for a secured maritime environment in the Indo-Paciﬁc, particularly in the Indian Ocean Region (IOR). It is widely accepted among scholarly circles that India has been lax when it came to its maritime strategy in the Indian Ocean, until recently. India believes the Ocean and its adjoining areas are its natural sphere of inﬂuence. Hence it views itself as the main security provider in the region. However, it is only with China’s increasing footprint in the same area that India has strengthened its maritime policy in the IOR and the Indo-Paciﬁc. Particularly regarding the Indian Ocean, India has rapidly advanced various policy initiatives and has attempted to increase its presence and assistance to countries, particularly island nations. Such policy initiatives are multilateral as well as unilateral, with

122 S. MEHTA concerns ranging from infrastructure development to humanitarian assis- tance. The Security and Growth for All in the Region (SAGAR) initiative, launched in 2015, is the Indian policy of maritime engagement and coop- eration and independence and security of the Ocean. The Sagarmala Project, launched in 2021, aims to boost Indian infrastructure, partic- ularly the development of ports and connectivity along its coastline. India is part of the QUAD grouping, which has attracted so much controversy. At the same time, India maintains that it is not part of any alliance as such and seeks to play an important role in promoting and preserving the security of the Indo-Paciﬁc. Its interest in the Indo-Paciﬁc is not to counter Chinese inﬂuence even though India’s primary intention in the areas closer home regarding China’s presence, particularly in its neigh- bourhood, is different. Although India is aware that it cannot replace Chinese investment in these areas, it seeks to push itself as a viable alter- native and is aggressively advancing its image. India’s Neighborhood First policy is also important in this regard, as it includes the island countries of Sri Lanka and Maldives. They are also vital components of the Indian Ocean strategy. Thus, even while nurturing great power ambitions, India does not want to get caught in the line of ﬁre between the US and China. Its primary aim appears to secure its regional power status. Thus, both the US and PRC have legitimate claims in the region, and the Indo-Paciﬁc is an important theatre where a possible twenty-ﬁrst- century war is brewing. Both countries have acknowledged the challenges coming from either side and recognised each other as competitors. Both countries have also presented their respective worldviews regarding the region. While some scholars maintain, that the US policy in the Indo- Paciﬁc is much wider than just countering China (Pant and Mann 2023), some others believe it is only due to increasing Chinese inﬂuence in the same region that the US has launched its Indo-Paciﬁc strategy (Hu and Meng 2020). This has resulted in roughly two opposing blocs operating in a climate like that of the Cold War era. The biggest difference between the era before 1991 and post-2010s is the nature of international relations and diplomacy. Interconnectedness, technological advancements in society, economy, and politics, the emer- gence of multiple centres of power, and the hegemony of the market have transformed the world order. It is for the same reasons that the repercussions of the Russia-Ukraine conﬂict are felt around the world, not just limited to the US and Europe, although in different magnitudes and understanding. European Union’s stand regarding the Indo-Paciﬁc

7 INTERNATIONAL DIPLOMACY IN THE ERA … 123 is relatively new (the EU released the ‘EU Strategy for Cooperation in the Indo-Paciﬁc’ in 2021) and quite ambivalent. For the EU countries, the concept of the Indo-Paciﬁc and maritime security are novel concepts. While they do understand the US strategy to ‘contain’ China in the Indo-Paciﬁc, they have maintained that they are looking for a cooper- ative approach, particularly with China, to increase and maintain good conditions of trade (EEAS Press Team 2023). Just like the US, they also talk about a rule-based order in the region, although not neces- sarily treating China as a belligerent. In this aspect, it is closer to India’s approach towards the Indo-Paciﬁc rather than that of the US. For the EU, the Indo-Paciﬁc has associated economic concerns rather than secu- rity ones. It believes that the area is crucial for trade and is keen on building a free trade regime in the area, including with China. It is for this reason that it looks towards a safe and secure Indo-Paciﬁc maritime area. Hence, the transformed nature of international relations and the increased dependence on Chinese economic engagements have resulted in ambivalent attitudes, even from Europe, as they do not see a rival in either camps. As the conﬂict in the Indo-Paciﬁc gathers storm, the EU will be required to take on a more speciﬁc stand (EEAS Press Team 2023). India had earlier taken the path of non-alignment, to witness and reﬂect on conﬂicts independently, without conforming to any speciﬁc camp. India is more likely to be let off and accepted by the US than the EU’s unde- cided outlook. Thus, even though the Indo-Paciﬁc is not exactly Europe’s game to play, countries like France and Germany have formulated national policies regarding the Indo-Paciﬁc. World Politics in an Era of Incessant Conflicts The Russia-Ukraine war and the situation in the Indo-Paciﬁc, both exem- plify the nature of international relations in present times. While one has blown out in a full-scale war, the other is symbolic of a storm brewing and gathering clouds. Both conﬂicts are archetypical to the current global status quo, with multiple centres of power, connectivity, technological advancement, and economic interconnectedness playing signiﬁcant roles. Both are also intractable conﬂicts, with no apparent real solution to them. These two situations are only two examples of a range of other conﬂicts present in global politics now, including region-based and issue-based conﬂicts.

124 S. MEHTA Whenever an international order goes through a process of transforma- tion, regular and persisting conﬂicts become characteristic of that kind of status quo. The most recent example is that of the Cold War. However, even though the situation of permacrisis has been referred to Europe since the 2000s, surprisingly, global politics has been going through changes, contestations, and conﬂicts since the end of the Cold War. In both the cases of the Russia-Ukraine war and the crisis in the Indo-Paciﬁc, one can witness that the Western liberal order is a main point of contention, with one side backing it and the other resisting it. But neither Russia nor China is trying to completely overturn the status quo. Russia will come out of the present war with Ukraine weaker than it was before going in. In economic forums China has been referred to as the US’ main challenger. China has even tried setting up alternate international institutions when it could not get enough leverage in the Western-created and Western-led ones. For example, it launched the Asian Infrastructure Investment Bank (AIIB), which seeks to act as an alternative to the IMF and WB (Hallaert and Messerlin 2020). Indeed, the West has even been accused of widening the contours of the liberal order to justify their actions on several occa- sions, for example with the making of and the invasion by the ‘Coalition of the Willing’ in Iraq (Patrick 2015). Hence, it can be said that the world did not settle down after the end of the Cold War. Challenges to the liberal order, or its exclusive tendencies, are coming from not only Russia, China, North Korea, and Iran, but also from emerging countries like India, Brazil, and South Africa which conform to the existing order (Flockhart 2018). The focus of these countries is inclusivity and changes that need to be made in the existing political and economic liberal order. Today market economy and technological knowledge are the two main drivers of power. They are also the main areas of attack. There are also several non-traditional political security threats like climate change and terrorism that have emerged and gained strength as a by-product of the globalisation process. Globalisation and the development of a multipolar world, with several regional power centres, have exacerbated the compe- tition, with old powers trying to hold on to the regions of prior inﬂuence and new powers are attempting to garner authority. This has in part led to the persistent nature of conﬂicts. In the developing world, the emergence of regional powers due to the prior withdrawal of major powers has created a vacuum that has led to competition today. Neither do major powers like the US want to spread themselves too thinly over every region and hence have called on

7 INTERNATIONAL DIPLOMACY IN THE ERA … 125 emerging powers like India to take on global responsibility. In this era of persistent crises ranging from ﬁnancial to humanitarian, alternate centres of power are emerging. Indeed, this phenomenon is more powerful than the confrontation of the big powers. Emerging countries like India are taking advantage of this opportunity by developing themselves as invest- ment and manufacturing hubs as well as playing an important role in strategic supply chain management. Politically, India has chosen to judge international events independently, according to its perceived national interest. We have discussed above how India chose to remain silent on the Russia-Ukraine war due to the geopolitical threats and felt inse- curity from its north. The European Union has insisted more on a cooperative economic stand on the Indo-Paciﬁc, rather than a confronta- tionist attitude at a time when the American security umbrella over the EU has become questionable. Hence what the world might witness, in the coming decades, is a bigger role being played by emerging powers determined to bargain best from the existing system. Conclusion International relations are in a state of transformation. The recurrent conﬂicts—political, social, and economic—are demonstrative of the trans- formation. The crux of permacrisis is the challenges and resistance facing the existing liberal world order. Some of these situations are created by natural causes, like the pandemic in 2020. Nevertheless, in either its source or consequences, the conﬂicts reveal the shortcomings of the Western-led rule-based regime. Supporters of this regime do not support it blindly, but only to the extent to which it is in line with and supportive of their own national strategies. The rise of regional centres of power has acted as alternative sources of support, both in political and ﬁnancial terms. There is also a discernible difference in opinion and motivations between the US and the EU in terms of foreign policy and national requirements. As a result, the divisive lines have grown sharper. Soon, a complete Western-led liberal world order seems unlikely without changes, inclusions, and adjustments. The emerging powers have gained enough strength that they cannot be ignored and must be included in multilateral decision-making.

CHAPTER 8 The Changing Nature of Conﬂict and Ramiﬁcations on International Humanitarian Law Shivani Gupta February 24, 2022, is likely to go down in history as a watershed moment in the contemporary world. Russia’s war against Ukraine was a global shock revealing a massive security threat that has shattered Europe’s peace. Thousands of Ukrainian civilians have been killed and countless buildings have been destroyed in the ongoing conﬂict. Tens of thou- sands of troops on both sides have also been killed or seriously injured. Almost two years after the seismic shock of Russia’s full-scale invasion, the Ukraine conﬂict has transformed into a protracted war of attrition. The initial assumptions of swift victory and decisive outcomes have dissolved, replaced by a complex and evolving stalemate where geopolitical ambi- tions clash with the stark reality of human suffering. Beyond Ukraine’s borders, the invasion shattered European security, reshaped inter-state S. Gupta (B) International Centre for Parliamentary Studies, London, UK e-mail: shivanigupta9000@gmail.com © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_8 129

130 S. GUPTA relations, and frayed a tightly knit global economy. This conﬂict is a reminder of the Cold War era, with Russia on one side, and NATO and the European Union on the other, with fears of the situation escalating into a bigger crisis, one not limited to the territory of Ukraine. These conditions are casting shadows of uncertainty over international alliances and partnerships. Nestled within the tumultuous folds of history, the Russia-Ukraine conﬂict stands as a stark reminder of the fragile balance between peace and conﬂict in an interconnected world. The conﬂict’s economic and social ramiﬁcations, which extend far beyond the battleﬁeld, will test the limits of international cooperation. This conﬂict is and will continue to alter the contours of the international order. The global economy, already reeling from the pandemic’s aftershocks, faces further headwinds, as disrupted supply chains and skyrocketing energy prices threaten interna- tional stability and prosperity. Meanwhile, the humanitarian crisis unfolds with millions displaced, cities reduced to rubble, and the human cost of war etched onto every war-torn face. As we wade through this murky landscape, it becomes clear that the Ukraine conﬂict is not merely a regional dispute, but a harbinger of a new and perilous chapter in global affairs. T he Russia-Ukraine Conflict: An International Armed Conflict? The foremost question in any international humanitarian law (IHL) anal- ysis is whether an armed conﬂict is taking place, and if so, of what kind. The primary goal of IHL is to protect civilians and regulate conduct of the parties during armed conﬂict. The rules that apply to a conﬂict are determined by its classiﬁcation and this is dependent upon the parties involved. International armed conﬂict (IAC) involves hostilities between states, whereas non-international conﬂict (NIAC) occurs when a certain level of violence occurs between a state and an organised armed group or between organised armed groups. The current conﬂict in Ukraine is an international armed conﬂict governed by the four Geneva Conventions and Additional Protocol I (API), to which both Ukraine and Russia are parties. There are signiﬁcant differences between international armed conﬂict and non-international armed conﬂict. The 1907 Hague IV Conven- tions, the four 1949 Geneva Conventions, and Additional Protocol I to

8 THE CHANGING NATURE OF CONFLICT … 131 those Conventions apply during an international armed conﬂict. During a non-international armed conﬂict, the majority of applicable treaty law is provided by Common Article 3 of the 1949 Geneva Conventions and the Additional Protocol II. The latter only applies to Parties to the instru- ment and in speciﬁc types of non-international armed conﬂicts. Overall, the body of customary international law applicable in international armed conﬂict is far more extensive than that applicable in non-international armed conﬂict. International armed conﬂict exists under the following circumstances: 1. when there are hostilities between the armed forces or other organs of States; 2. during belligerent occupation of one State by another; 3. when a State is in “control” of an organised armed group ﬁghting another State; and 4. ﬁnally, the last type is limited to Parties to Additional Protocol I and involves groups ﬁghting for self-determination (ICRC). Between February 2014 and February 2022, there were multiple NIACs between Ukraine and separatist groups backed by Russia. Russia’s “special military operations” (UNSC 2022) in Ukraine began in February 2022, ushering in a new phase of the conﬂict. Military operations also trans- formed previously existing NIACs into IACs as Russia began to exert overall control over such groups. As a result, all parties are now subject to the IHL rules that govern such conﬂicts. The two parties have also acknowledged that they were engaged in an international armed conﬂict governed by IHL. T he Resurgence of International Armed Conflicts The nature of conﬂict has changed dramatically since the end of the Cold War. The majority of conﬂicts were fought between domestic groups rather than states. Conventional wars between two or more states were becoming less common. What had replaced them was what Mary Kaldor referred to as “new wars” (Kaldor 1999). Non-state actors such as polit- ical militias, criminal gangs, and international terrorist organisations are frequently involved in these conﬂicts. Regional tensions, a weak state of the rule of law, lack of accountability of state institutions, illegal economic

132 S. GUPTA gain, and the scarcity of resources intensiﬁed by climate change, have become dominant drivers of such conﬂicts. It was thought that the era of conventional inter-state wars was as good as over. However, the inter- national armed conﬂict in Ukraine has brought back a type and scale of armed conﬂict that the international community has long pledged to erad- icate. It was out of this fear of a resurgence of state-on-state conﬂicts that the Chief of the General Staff of the UK compared the current crisis to their “1937 moment” that ravaged Europe 80 years ago (Pszczel 2022). Three months before the invasion of Ukraine by Russia, the then- British Prime Minister Boris Johnson had rejected the suggestions of increasing heavy weapons for the British army. “The old concepts of ﬁghting big tank battles on European landmass are over” (Johnson 2021), he said, however only a couple of months later, he was urging the UK to send more battle tanks to assist Ukraine in its ﬁght against Russian forces. Moreover, the conﬂict has also started a new arms race that reminds one of the 1930s build-up to World War II. Russia has mobilised hundreds of thousands of conscripts and plans to increase its military strength from 1 million to 1 and a half million troops. The United States has increased weapons production to replace the stock- piles sent to Ukraine. France intends to increase military spending by one-third by 2030, while Germany has lifted its longstanding prohibi- tion on sending weapons to conﬂict zones and shipped missiles and tanks to Ukraine (Aljazeera 2023). Prior to the start of the war, it was largely believed that military operations would shift towards more advanced tech- nology and cyber warfare, with less dependence on tanks or artillery. However, the Ukraine war has seen a convergence of both old and new war technologies. The Russia-Ukraine conﬂict has thus shown the world that conventional warfare, state-on-state, has returned. While Russian President Vladimir Putin had hoped the invasion would weaken NATO, instead, the military alliance originally formed to counter the Soviet Union has found a new purpose. Finland and Sweden, two neutral countries that had previously avoided becoming a part of NATO joined it as protection against Russia. The European Union also retaliated against Russia with tough sanctions and sent Ukraine billions in support. Moreover, the war also drew the US back into European politics after decades of inactivity there. Some gaps, however, can be identiﬁed in this Western unity. For instance, the Hungarian Prime Minister Viktor Orban, a close ally of Putin in the EU, lobbied against sanctions on Moscow and

8 THE CHANGING NATURE OF CONFLICT … 133 refused to send weapons to Ukraine. The longer the conﬂict will last, the greater will be the strain on Western unity. Application of the International Humanitarian Law in the Russia-Ukraine Conflict Since both Russia and Ukraine are signatories to the four Geneva Conven- tions of 1949 and their Additional Protocol I of 1977, the current armed conﬂict in Europe is an international armed conﬂict to which the full range of relevant international humanitarian law treaties applies. Russian operations have resulted in a vast number of civilian deaths as well as widespread damage to or destruction of residential and medical infrastruc- ture, and historical monuments among other things. This demonstrates that the fundamental tenets of the conduct of hostilities in accordance with IHL have in many cases not been followed. There have also been deliberate attacks on critical civilian infrastructure such as electrical grids and water processing plants (Pearson 2023). The objective of such attacks is to break civilian morale. This would be unlawful as per the IHL. Most of the violations of IHL have occurred against civilians and civilian objects in Ukraine. This is mainly due to the fact that most of the hostilities are taking place within Ukrainian territory. Patterns of abuse have also been reported concerning the treatment of persons captured by the enemy, the execution of civilians, mistreatment in detention facilities, deportations of children among others. According to the UN Human Rights Moni- toring Mission in Ukraine, both sides of the conﬂict are allegedly guilty of abusing POWs that could amount to war crime (HRW 2022). Despite the use of modern technology, the current hostilities between Russia and Ukraine have in a lot of ways resembled the World War era warfare. The Ukraine-Russia conﬂict, as an International Armed Conﬂict, taking place on a large scale after a long period of time, has brought to light legal and practical issues that are reminiscent of the World War periods. During the initial period of humanitarian operations in Ukraine, ICRC struggled to cope and provide efﬁcient service. Top ICRC ofﬁcials had little recent and direct experience with international armed conﬂict involving major powers of the type seen in the Ukraine and Russia war. This is because majority of ICRC activities in recent times had focused on intra-state conﬂicts with changing levels of violence in “fragile societies” in non-Western areas. Organisational history, precedents and “doctrine” concerning major wars were not always visible in Geneva (Forsythe 2023).

134 S. GUPTA The war has also highlighted some gaps in international humanitarian law. Important among them include the uncertainty relating to the exact protection afforded to civilians during a foreign invasion, the lack of clarity and consensus with regard to the meaning of mercenaries, the application of the nationality criterion in case of dual or multiple nationals in terms of Prisoner of War status, the crime of forced recruitment of civilians, and the absence of a universally accepted prohibition on certain types of weapons, such as cluster munitions. Another signiﬁcant gap in the application of IHL is the role of cyber operations in the conduct of the war. This is a problem exacerbated due to the unavailability of facts as the States have a tendency to try and keep the extent and methods of cybercrimes under wraps. Russia-Ukraine War and the Changing World The events in Ukraine have served as a sobering reminder of the costs of war. Since Russia invaded Ukraine in February 2022, millions of people have been forced to ﬂee their homes; vast acres of valuable land have been left destroyed or rendered unusable; and unfortunately, thousands of people have been killed as a consequence of the ongoing violence. The crisis has demonstrated how intrinsically connected the world and its risks are. At a time when countries around the world had just started to recover from the lingering effects of an unexpected global pandemic, the events in Ukraine have added another layer of complication to an already hostile security landscape. What began as a regional conﬂict has quickly evolved into a political and economic crisis with extensive world- wide implications. Unfortunately, it is evident that the conﬂict is far from over. The crisis has left the world in a signiﬁcantly altered state than it was prior to Russia’s invasion. These changes will continue to be felt for a long time in the future. These changes also include macro ones with disruptions in the world order. It has become evident that the crisis has ramiﬁcations beyond the European security architecture and the main adversaries. The use of sanctions against Russia and counter sanctions by Russia on the West has deepened the gulf between the East and the West. The war has also had major economic implications as economies around the world had only begun to recover from the repercussions of COVID- 19. This war has thus been a disaster for Ukraine as well as Russia and a crisis for the rest of the world. The world has become a more unstable and fearful place since Russia invaded its neighbour on February 24, 2022.

8 THE CHANGING NATURE OF CONFLICT … 135 Some of the major ways in which the Russia-Ukraine crisis has changed the world are discussed below: Return of European Wars and Shifting Alliances The war between Russia and Ukraine has been referred to as a “war against the West” (Tenzer 2023). It is held that Putin’s regime has chosen confrontation with the “collective West”, irrespective of the costs for Russia itself (Zakharova 2023). Russia maintains that, “the 2014 coup was stage-managed by Western countries to make Ukraine anti-Russia. The interest of the West lies in pitting two fraternal peoples against one another in the ‘colonial tradition’ to prevent the Russian Federation’s re- emergence as a global Power and delay a new multipolar world” (UNGA 2023). Such a narrative has resulted in the West calling Russia “the most signiﬁcant and direct threat to Allies’ security” (NATO 2023). Moreover, this has made Russia’s neighbours fear that Russia may in future indulge in aggression against other countries. The decision by Finland and Sweden to join the NATO is a case in point. The war has accelerated actions which were unthinkable before, an example being Ukraine’s EU candi- date status being granted within three months. Indeed, most European countries have signiﬁcantly increased their defence budgets. Russia’s invasion has also strengthened NATO and provided it with a renewed vigour with an increased presence in Eastern Europe. Due to a major war breaking out in Europe after decades, countries around the world are now divided into three major groups. These include ﬁrstly, the ones who sided with Russia, secondly, the ones who vowed support to Ukraine, and lastly, a group of non-aligned nations avoiding involve- ment. This trend indicates a partial resurgence of what used to be many countries’ original positions during the Cold War. The European Union states were quick to respond with major sanc- tions and action against Russia. Most of the European countries depended upon the protective security umbrella of NATO, backed by American military capabilities. However, there is another group of countries that have openly supported Russia. For instance, Putin’s old allies, such as Syria’s Bashar al-Assad and Belarus’s Aliaksandr Lukashenka have extended their support to him. Russia also entered into a closer strategic relationship with Iran.

136 S. GUPTA In Asia, the region’s major powers, China and India refused to take a side and get entangled in the conﬂict. Thus these countries have princi- pally rejected the Western narrative of the conﬂict as a war between good and evil and have chosen to sustain their relationships with Russia for a combination of economic, military, and diplomatic beneﬁts. While the United Nations Security Council has not been able to play a vital role in the conﬂict, mainly owing to the paralysing effect of Russia’s veto, the UN General Assembly passed a resolution condemning Russia’s aggres- sion with an overwhelming majority. However, solidarity with Ukraine is evidently not universal among all UN members as the resolution saw thirty-ﬁve abstentions (UNGA 2022). Most of these abstentions came from African countries. This shows that most African states mirror the sentiments shared by some of their Asian counterparts. Increased Use of Private Military Companies (PMCs) in War Operations Russian private military companies (PMCs) have already been active in Syria and Africa in recent years (Mishra 2023). The most prominent Russian mercenary group is the Wagner group which ﬁrst made an appear- ance in Crimea in 2014. Russia’s war against Ukraine has seen an upsurge in the use of such companies. The purpose of using such PMCs is to provide deniability of Russian involvement in war crimes. This has become possible because of the ambiguous legal status of private mili- tary companies around the world. However, Russia’s use of such PMCs poses a serious threat to international security because they are changing the meaning of mercenary. Companies such as the Wagner group do not ﬁt into the traditional “soldier of fortune” meaning of mercenaries. The 1977 Additional Protocols I and II of the Geneva Conventions banned mercenaries. This was mainly because they were warriors without a state, who fought for proﬁt rather than an ideology. Article 47 of Protocol I to the Geneva Convention provides the most widely accepted deﬁnition of a mercenary in international law. However, the Article has a lot of loopholes which has allowed private military companies to operate comparatively easily. Russia has been using these legal ambiguities, surrounding these companies in terms of International Law to conduct its operations against Ukraine.

8 THE CHANGING NATURE OF CONFLICT … 137 The Future of Russia in International Relations The present war between Russia and Ukraine has broken the myth of Russian invincibility. The original goal of the invasion has evidently not been achieved yet. Russian forces had to withdraw from the vicinity of Kyiv and had to retreat from many other places. Ukraine’s strategic use of limited resources, reinforced by foreign assistance has provided an unexpected resistance to Russia’s military strength. Russia suffered huge human losses as is evident by its extension of the age limit for volun- teers to join the war. Additionally, Russia has paid a high price for the war. Sanctions are starting to pinch and will most likely set the Russian economy back for decades. This is particularly so as Russia is unable to produce quite a lot of essential goods without the import of foreign technology or parts. Apparently unemployment is at a decline, however, it is mainly because a large number of the people in Russia have been mobilised in war efforts. It is estimated that in 2022, Russia’s gross domestic product (GDP) dropped by 2.1% (Council of the European Union 2023). Thousands of companies, from Apple to McDonald’s, that have either ceased or reduced their operations in Russia have effectively added to the pressure. This exodus, combined with a national conscrip- tion drive in 2022, has resulted in a large number of Russian citizens ﬂeeing the country. This exacerbates the already existing “brain drain” upon a weak economy. Thus the war has made the Russian Federation an international pariah and the country is not likely to recover its reputation for a long period of time. However, this does not mean that Moscow is entirely without friends. Russia has strengthened economic relations with China. Nevertheless, China has avoided getting directly entangled in the conﬂict by refusing to send weapons in Russia’s support. The outcomes of this war are likely to serve as either an encouragement or a warning to China which might harbour the hope of reclaiming Taiwan by force. Moreover, Putin has strengthened military ties with North Korea and Iran, which provides armed drones that Russia uses against Ukraine’s infrastructure. No matter how weakened or secluded by the West, Putin’s Russia continues to bolster its inﬂuence in Africa and the Middle East through the use of its economic and military strength.

138 S. GUPTA The Return of the Nuclear Threat The end of the Cold War and the establishment of the US-Russian arms control regime had made the threat of nuclear weapons less relevant. However, ever since the invasion of Ukraine by Russia, the world has seen regular nuclear weapons threats from Russia, though most of them have been indirect. Such war strategies that use nuclear threats have resulted in a return of nuclear arms power competition on a global level. It is owing to this that the current President of the United States, Joe Biden stated that the risk of a “nuclear Armageddon” is at its highest level since 60 years (Biden 2022). In addition, the Zaporizhzhia nuclear power plant which happens to be Europe’s largest nuclear power plant is located in an active conﬂict zone, raising the fear of spectre of a new Chernobyl. The Use of Modern Technology in Warfare With the widespread use of open-source intelligence (OSINT) during the Russia-Ukraine conﬂict, a new type of information warfare has emerged. By making use of the advantages offered by satellite imagery, smart- phones, and social media, Ukrainian forces have modiﬁed the traditional kill chain. By outsourcing parts of it to their civilians reporting Russian movements, it has been able to create a more extensive and resilient network. This war is also an example of a ﬁrst sustained conﬂict which for the ﬁrst time saw the use of all the currently available uses for drones which are integrated into combined operations on both sides. Energy and Food Crisis Both Russia and Ukraine being major suppliers of energy, food, and fertilisers, the present conﬂict has resulted in a disruption in their supply for both developing and developed countries. These effects have further globalised the consequences of war. The most signiﬁcant shift has been Europe’s move away from reliance on Russian gas. With a record of keeping the gas supply on even during the Cold War, Soviet invasion of Afghanistan, and the nuclear crisis of the 1980s, Russia assumed Europe would get desperate for gas. However, Europe responded to the supply disruption in two ways: by looking for gas elsewhere and by hastening its progress to renewable energy. Before the war broke out, the EU had committed to reduce emissions by 40%

8 THE CHANGING NATURE OF CONFLICT … 139 and attain 32% renewables. In the aftermath of the Russian invasion, those targets were raised to 57% and 45%, respectively (European Commission 2022). A number of African countries including Mozambique and Nigeria are now being wooed by powerful countries of both the West and East for energy supplies. This has the potential to be a game-changer in geopol- itics with Europe looking towards African energy to help in the energy transition, giving African states leveraging power. Thus evidently Putin’s aim of using gas as a weapon has so far failed to serve the purpose. With Europe’s vast pipelines standing idle, Russia was at the risk of having stranded assets, further weakening its economy. Owing to this it had to look elsewhere for new customers. It has found some success. Along with India, China is now the equal largest importer of Russian oil and has signed a new 30-year gas deal. Moreover, since both Russia and Ukraine are major exporters of agri- cultural fertilisers, the disruption to global food chains increased essential goods prices to a lifetime high. This resulted in a cost-of-living crisis in both the developed as well as developing economies. Furthermore, the disruption of both harvests and a Russian grain blockade made the world susceptible to a humanitarian disaster. This is particularly true in the case of developing countries many of which are dependent on imported wheat. The World Food Programme (WFP) exclaimed that the current crisis remains at “unprecedented proportions” with as many as 783 million people facing chronic hunger (World Food Programme 2023). Addition- ally, high fuel prices also result in increased fertiliser prices and transport costs, which lead to further increases in the price of food for consumers. An Ailing World Economy The current war between Russia and Ukraine has hampered global trade that was still recovering from the effects of the global pandemic. As pointed out above, since Russia and Ukraine are major suppliers and producers of wheat and fertiliser, food prices have risen exponentially. The effects of the war have been felt far and wide from chilly homes in Europe during winters to soaring prices in food markets in Africa. Before the war, the European Union nations imported almost half of their natural gas and a third of their oil from Russia. The Russian invasion of Ukraine resulted in sanctions against Russia that led to an energy price shock, the likes of which the world had not seen since the 1970s.

140 S. GUPTA Although, over time food prices have reduced from the record highs, food continues to be used as a geopolitical weapon. Russia has laid the blame for the food price increase upon the West. Ukraine and its supporters on the other hand accuse Russia of using hunger and famine as a weapon of war. Thus the present war has highlighted the fragility of an interconnected world, just as the pandemic did. The war also set back attempts to ﬁght climate change, increasing Europe’s use of coal. However, Europe’s search for alternatives to Russian oil and gas may result in an accelerated transition to renewable energy sources. A Large Refugee Crisis One of the most visible consequences of the Russian invasion has been the internal and external displacement of millions of Ukrainians. According to the United Nations High Commission for Refugees (UNHCR), there are nearly 5.1 million internally displaced people in Ukraine (as of May 2023), while more than 6.2 million refugees from Ukraine have been recorded globally (as of July 2023). Approximately 17.6 million people are in the need of humanitarian assistance in 2023, the vast majority of them being women and children (UNHCR 2023). The highest propor- tions of these displaced people can now be found in the neighbouring countries of Poland, Germany, and the Czech Republic. According to Beata Javorcik, the chief economist at the European Bank for Reconstruc- tion and Development, “this is the fastest forced displacement of people since the 1940s” (Javorcik 2022). The loss of human capital will also impact Ukraine’s economic recovery and its ability to rebuild the country when the war is ﬁnally over. T he Future of Non-international Armed Conflict Armed conﬂicts continue to affect every region across the globe. Each conﬂict gives rise to unique humanitarian challenges and poten- tially signals new trends in geopolitics. The Ukraine-Russia conﬂict has given the international community an obvious reason to be concerned about a resurgence of armed conﬂict between states after decades of primarily non-international armed conﬂicts. The international commu- nity is watching with fear of a potential Chinese invasion of Taiwan following the heels of the Russian invasion of Ukraine. Overall, recent events have compelled the world to prepare for more international

8 THE CHANGING NATURE OF CONFLICT … 141 conﬂicts in the near future. Yet, regardless of whether these develop- ments signify the return of state-to-state conﬂict, the trends observed over the last two decades also continue. There is no sign of an end to the protracted non-international armed conﬂicts continuing in Syria and Yemen. Despite a worrying resurgence of international armed conﬂicts, the vast majority of conﬂicts in the world continue to be non-international armed conﬂicts. These conﬂicts often involve a myriad of non-state armed groups, spill across borders, and typically involve foreign states inter- vening on the side of territorial states or non-state armed groups. The fact that non-international armed conﬂicts are here to stay and will continue to have major repercussions on the political and economic scene cannot be more evident than from the outbreak of the Israel-Hamas conﬂict. The Russia-Ukraine war demonstrated how intricately interconnected the global geopolitical economic and landscapes are. The consequences of the conﬂict between Israel and Hamas, which began on October 7, 2023, are already being felt around the world. Economic disruption, growing military vulnerabilities, increased political polarisation, new strategic chal- lenges, and the changing nature of warfare are among the identiﬁable global threats. The Israel-Palestine conﬂict is the latest chapter in the decades-long cycle of violence in the Middle East region that regrettably appears to have no end in sight. While the reasons behind these events are complex, the conﬂict’s immediate and potential long-term political and economic repercussions are difﬁcult to dismiss. The war in Gaza reﬂects the chal- lenges of conﬂicts that involve non-state armed groups. Israel has fought as many as four conventional state-on-state wars with its neighbouring countries between 1948 and 1973. However, in many ways, ﬁghting non-state armed groups have proven to be more complicated. Regard- less of the outcome of Israel’s war in Gaza, the current war has made it evident that Israel will have to rethink its entire strategy towards the failing Middle East peace process if it wants to invest in sustainable peace for itself. Moreover, conﬂicts in the Middle East tend to lead to an increase in oil prices. Additionally, if the Israel-Hamas war escalates, the impact on oil prices could make it more expensive for the West to maintain its sanctions on Russian oil and gas. If the conﬂict extends to major oil- producing nations in the region, an already ailing global economy could face further severe jolts as energy costs for businesses and households could increase substantially. This is most likely so as the energy supply

142 S. GUPTA is usually disrupted during such conﬂicts. Higher energy prices would also impede national banks’ efforts across the world to bring inﬂation under control in both developed as well as developing economies. Thus in less than three months after Hamas breached Israel’s security wall in more than 20 locations, the war has already had a global impact. The longer the Gaza War continues, the greater the possibility of far-reaching consequences beyond the Israeli or Palestinian borders. The Israel-Hamas war has thus demonstrated how non-international armed conﬂicts continue to have a signiﬁcant political and economic impact on the global geopolitical scene. The Geneva Conventions of 1949 and their Additional Protocols of 1977 were largely drafted with interna- tional armed conﬂicts in mind. However, in recent decades, contemporary armed conﬂicts have differed in many ways from “traditional” inter-state wars. As a result, it has been often stated that this legal framework is no longer relevant or provides insufﬁcient regulation for contemporary armed conﬂicts. However, the current geopolitical scene is witnessing both, a major international armed conﬂict as well as a non-international armed conﬂict. Both conﬂicts have outlined to the international commu- nity that international humanitarian law has, in fact, endured over time. However, it is also evident that the primary legal issues associated with the conduct of modern warfare remain the same in both cases. Technology continues to advance at a rapid pace, with cyber operations, autonomous weapons, and the use of outer space raising concerns about the appli- cation and interpretation of IHL. IHL needs to develop further into a comprehensive protective legal framework through jurisprudence, state practice, the development of weapons treaties, and soft law interpreta- tions and guidance. The challenges to IHL exposed by the recent conﬂicts need to be addressed to help it develop and adapt to the evolving nature of warfare and new technologies. Concluding T houghts Since February 2022, there has been an ongoing international armed conﬂict between Russia and Ukraine. The Bolshevik revolutionary Vladimir Lenin is believed to have stated that “there are decades where nothing happens, and there are weeks where decades happen”. While the quote’s veriﬁability is questionable, its assertion cannot be truer when it comes to Russia’s international armed conﬂict against Ukraine.

8 THE CHANGING NATURE OF CONFLICT … 143 The world has changed dramatically in the two years since Russian President Vladimir Putin launched his “special military operation”, against Ukraine. These changes are perhaps in some way irrevocable. Russia’s war in Ukraine is a continuing tragedy, ruining lives and livelihoods in Ukraine and leading to changes in the global economy. This war, which broke out soon after the global pandemic, has brought about a new economic era. The present times are reminiscent of other major historical events including the immediate aftermath of World War II (1944–46), the oil crisis (1971–73), and the disintegration of the Soviet Union (1989–92). Each of those events altered the global landscape owing to the release of powerful underlying forces that had been accumulating over a fault line. Each marked the beginning of a new era. The changes being brought about by the Russia-Ukraine international armed conﬂict are being rein- forced by another major non-international conﬂict between Israel and Hamas. If these conﬂicts continue to escalate, the devastation will have an accelerating affect upon every aspect of people’s lives across the world. The repercussions of these wars are particularly worrying in the light of an already bleak picture of an ailing global economy. In such a situa- tion of permacrisis, the prominence and importance of the international humanitarian law is increasing like never before. However, with majority of these crises posing newer challenges that are either outside IHL’s protective scope or is very vaguely dealt with, respect for IHL and compli- ance with its priorities is endangered like never before. It is therefore of crucial importance for the international humanitarian law to develop and expand so that it can adapt to the evolving nature of warfare and new technologies.
