CHAPTER 9 ‘Idealist’s Dream or Realist’s Cudgel’: Examining Sanctions as Instruments of Statecraft Souradeep Sen and Zaad Mahmood Sanction, broadly understood as a non-military response to conﬂicts among nations, is a popular instrument of statecraft. As a supplement to war leveraging interdependence, sanctions have the potential to serve as a powerful weapon to achieve diplomatic and foreign policy objectives particularly in period of permacrisis. Conceptually, permacrisis refers to a condition of permanent low-level conﬂicts due to the systemic complexity of the international order. The origins and continuation of permacrisis can be attributed to the ways in which the United States of America, along with other Western states, have S. Sen (B) University of North Bengal, Siliguri, India e-mail: souradeepsen18@gmail.com Z. Mahmood Presidency University, Kolkata, India e-mail: zaad.polsc@presiuniv.ac.in © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_9 147

148 S. SEN AND Z. MAHMOOD increasingly ‘weaponised’ interdependence by leveraging global networks of informational and ﬁnancial exchange for strategic advantage (Farrell and Newman 2019), thereby engendering the systemic complexity. The global nodes of permacrisis point are best fathomed at the domain of global political economy or geoeconomics as conditions of global complexity have transformed interdependence into the new battleground (Galeotti 2021). With the decline in US hegemony and the rise of China, the same geoeconomic instruments of interdependence are now being deployed by other countries for coercive ends, which has only entrenched the self- same crisis. Thus the weaponisation of geoeconomic instruments has far- reaching implications for international security—where the lines between war and peace appear to have been blurred into near-irrelevance in a world of permanent low-level conﬂicts. In this context, sanctions as geoeconomics instrument have acquired increased relevance which is evident in the pervasiveness thereof. The use of sanctions has become ubiquitous since the end of the Cold War, thereby enabling scholars to label the 1990s as the ‘sanctions decade’ (Kovach 2015). Despite the popularity as a tool of statecraft, there exist signiﬁcant debate and uncertainty about the efﬁcacy of sanctions. The present chapter shall attempt to evaluate the basis of the utility and efﬁcacy of sanctions. Based on a systematic case study of sanc- tions episodes, it puts forward a conceptual model of the ‘triangular credo of utility and efﬁcacy’. We suggest that the utility and efﬁcacy of sanctions depends on speciﬁc political settlements engendered by the international system marked by ‘systemic complexity’; the characteristics of target countries such as nature of regime and economic condition; and the commitment and consensus of the sanction senders. In this triad, the systemic, domestic and sanction characteristics create unique ‘sanc- tion settlement’ that determine their efﬁcacy and utility. Intuitively, the authors emphasise that no strict set of universal criteria can be applied to infer efﬁcacy, as the international system is in a state of permacrisis, not least, as all reported cases of sanctions are intrinsically different due to domestic political variables, also because of the issues of motives and intents of states locked in sanctions contests. Discussion in the chapter shall evolve through the following sections: the ﬁrst would concern the history, competing deﬁnitions of sanctions, followed by a brief literature review of the debates surrounding their typologies, utility and efﬁcacy that culminate in the statements of the

9 ‘IDEALIST’S DREAM OR REALIST’S CUDGEL’: EXAMINING … 149 present inquest’s theoretical and methodological moorings; in the second section, the entry shall delve into the descriptive case studies of three speciﬁc sanctions cases since the 1990s to tease out particular inferences regarding their efﬁcacy; ﬁnally, in the third section, the inferences of the previous would be analysed comparatively to form abstractions and gener- alisations on the utility and efﬁcacy of sanctions in a world immanently at crisis. Understanding and Debating Sanctions Sanctions can simply be deﬁned as actions by one or more legal entities to limit or end their economic relations with a target entity in order to persuade the latter to change one or more of its policies (Morgan, Bapat, and Kobayashi 2014). The idea is based on a general theory of economic sanctions that encapsulates the following steps: economic punishment leads to economic disintegration, which leads to political disintegration, and which, in turn, leads to compliance (Baldwin and Pape 1998). Sanc- tions can take many forms such as tariffs, export controls, embargoes, import bans, travel bans, freezing of target assets, ceasing foreign aid and blockades. As geoeconomic instruments, sanctions have been considered both the idealist’s dream and the realist’s cudgel. These instruments promise the powerless a world free of war and discrimination and give the powerful tools for domination (Coates 2019). The earliest instance of economic sanctions can be traced back to ancient Athens, around 432 B.C., when Pericles issued the Megarian Decree to blockade Sparta’s allies. Historians contend that the decree helped ignite the Peloponnesian War (Kolhatkar 2022). In the modern era, more speciﬁcally, in 1806, Napoleon imposed his Continental System, forbidding European nations from trading with the United Kingdom (Kelerchian 2014). The contemporary iteration of sanctions can be traced to the League of Nations with the idea of a non-military coercive instrument to change state behaviour and restrain interstate war. The twentieth century normalised the practice of using sanctions as an ‘economic weapon’ in the hope that it would act as an antidote to war (Mulder 2022). Provisions for sanction measures were retained under Article 41 of the UN Charter, thereby perpetuating its legality (Charter of the United Nations 1945: 9, 11).

150 S. SEN AND Z. MAHMOOD Even though sanctions have been historically used as a tool of state- craft, their relevance increased exponentially in the post-Cold War era. The 1990s witnessed a stupendous increase in the promulgation of sanc- tions measures, particularly by the US as liberal optimism about the post-Cold War international order reigned supreme. As Fig. 9.1 shows, on average the world witnessed 10 sanctions imposed every year between 1965 and 1980, which increased slightly in the next decade. The marked increase in instances of sanctions occurred between 1990 and 2000, as on average the world witnessed nearly 50 sanctions every year. Despite the increase in popularity, the record of sanctions has been mixed at best which may have contributed to their decreasing numbers in recent years. Historically, there have been some cases of successful sanctions like the US threat of sanctions against Britain in 1956 that contributed to the end of the Suez Canal Crisis (Aldrich 1967; Peevers 2014). Another instance of ‘successful sanction’ was the US ‘invisible Fig. 9.1 Instance of Sanctions between 1965 and 2005. Source Hufbauer, Schott, and Elliott 2009

9 ‘IDEALIST’S DREAM OR REALIST’S CUDGEL’: EXAMINING … 151 blockade’ on Chile that exacerbated the economic crisis during Allende Presidency (Sigmund 1974) and created the conditions for a military coup under Pinochet. An international sanction against Apartheid South Africa (1977–91) is also considered among the few success stories in the major policy change category (Hufbauer and Oegg 2001). The instances of successful sanctions are however overwhelmed by cases of failure. The literature on sanctions presents a number of cases like the ‘ABCD’ (Americans, British, Chinese, and Dutch) Encirclement of Japan in 1940–41 to prevent the sale of crucial oil, iron, and steel to Japan as a response to its aggression in China and Indochina, US sanctions against Cuba to bring down the regime of Fidel Castro as instances of failed sanctions (Sanchez 2003; LeoGrande 2015). Similarly sanctions against North Korea since its foundation in 1948 have neither been successful in regime change nor in preventing nuclear programme. The most discussed instances of failed sanctions involve China and Iran (Carswell 1981). Evidently the discussion on cases of sanctions provides competing narrative and contested versions about the utility and efﬁcacy of sanctions. Sanctions debates The literature presents contestations about the utility and efﬁcacy of sanc- tions. The contemporary sanctions debates owe heavily to Hufbauer, Schott, and Elliott ( 1985) who examined instances of sanctions globally to conclude that success ratio of sanctions to be low but somewhat effec- tive. Based on a study of 115 cases of sanctions, they ﬁnd that sanctions were successful in 34 per cent of the cases. In a revised study, Hufbauer et al. ( 2009) having expanded the number of cases to 170, show that the success rate of sanctions have fallen over time, from about 50 per cent before 1970 to about 33 per cent in the 1990s. The measurement of the efﬁcacy of sanctions, however, has faced almost insurmountable paradoxes since then. To wit, the differences in evaluation of sanctions have stemmed from difﬁculties in operationalising and measuring success or failure of sanc- tions, as well as theoretical conceptualisations. Re-evaluating the 1990 edition of the Hufbauer, Schott, and Elliott (hereafter HSE) dataset, Robert A. Pape ( 1997) argues that the HSE study is ﬂawed as none of the claimed 40 successes stand up to scrutiny. Out of HSE’s 115 cases, only 5 are appropriately considered successful by Pape. He further argues

152 S. SEN AND Z. MAHMOOD that modern states are willing to incur high costs in pursuit of their objec- tives and multilateral cooperation is unlikely to improve effectiveness of sanctions (Baldwin and Pape 1998). Therefore, in most cases, it is exceptionally difﬁcult to deﬁne what constitutes success and failure for sanctions policies, considering the myriad of instrumental and non-instrumental reasons for which they can be imposed (Early and Cilizoglu 2020). Further, simply identifying when coercive sanctions episodes begin can be challenging because the mere threat of sanctions can change the target state’s behaviours. Such challenges are matched by the complexity of accounting for the varied economic, political, and humanitarian costs for the states that impose them, their target state(s), and even third parties. Importantly, the efﬁcacy of sanctions as a non-violent means to resolve disputes has also been questioned. Allen ( 2008) points out that sanc- tions increase poverty, raise unemployment, and result in poorer health for citizens in the affected nation. Sanctions also increase the likelihood of anti-government activity, which may include an uptick in violent action such as rioting (Peksen 2009). Sanctions may also have counterproduc- tive effects when targeted against particular actions that a target state may perceive to be indispensable for its survival, such as the proliferation of WMDs (Early 2012). Relatedly, there is extensive debate on the necessary qualities or criteria for successful/unsuccessful sanctions. Theoretically, sanctions are imposed on a target country by a sender to bring about some form of change in policy or action. The issues that may trigger sanctions can range from geopolitical disputes to the improvement of human rights (Morgan, Bapat, and Kobayashi 2014). The pathway of sanction traverses from economic cost to political acquiesces. Sanctions inﬂict economic damage on the target state usually by restricting trade, ﬁnancial transactions, and freezing assets of that state. Contemporarily, travel bans and personal sanctions to speciﬁc individuals in the target state have been issued to avoid collateral damage on the whole population in the target country (Hufbauer et al. 2009). Intuitively, the success of sanctions depends on the extent of economic cost imposed on the target, which in turn depends immediately on the size and scope of the sanction, and non- proximally, on factors such as economic structure, social and political conditions. Further, sanctions are relational in nature and the power differential between sender and target are crucial to their success. Despite the unanimity on the broad characteristics relevant for sanction success,

9 ‘IDEALIST’S DREAM OR REALIST’S CUDGEL’: EXAMINING … 153 there is a signiﬁcant disagreement on individual characteristics or quali- ties of sanctions, as well as the economic, social, and political conditions which are necessary for their success. To begin with, the HSE dataset (1985) infers that sanctions which are comprehensive with clear objectives, supported by a coalition of sender states have better odds of success (Mudler 2022). However the argument that comprehensive sanctions are more likely to succeed has been ques- tioned, time and again, on ethical, economic, and political grounds which is why in recent years instead of comprehensive sanctions with negative externalities, the idea of smart sanction has been advocated (Kirshner 1997;W e i s s1999;D r e z n e r2011; Biglaiser and Lektzian 2011; Blackwill and Harris 2016). In a later work, Hufbauer et al. ( 2009) suggest that sanctions tend to be most effective when the goals are relatively modest, imposed quickly and decisively, the target is economically and politically weaker than the sender and there exist substantial trade relations between the two. Clearly there is lack of unanimity on the conditions which have evolved over time. In other dimensions, the idea of sanctions backed by a coalition as being more powerful and effective is well accepted. Theoretically, sanc- tions imposed unilaterally may leave room for the target state to reduce their impact by turning to alternative markets and by using counter- strategies ranging from import substitution to smuggling (Hovi, Huseby, and Sprinz 2005; Smeets 2018). It has been argued by some scholars that sanctions supported by multiple nation-states have better chances of success (Bapat and Morgan 2009). However, the condition of multina- tional sanctions runs opposite to the need for quick and decisive sanctions (Early 2011) as the ability to conjure a sanction alliance depends on the nature and type of sanctioning states, the issues of sanction, not least, the vagaries of the international order. Evidently, the effectiveness of sanctions depends on two very basic variables: domestic political-economy conditions and external reliance for trade and ﬁnance of both the target and sender states (Blackwill and Harris 2016; Mulder 2022). Economic sanctions are usually unsuccessful if targeted against another powerful state. Conversely, sanctions are more likely to be successful when the cost is signiﬁcant for the target state (Drezner 1999) or when the sanction issue is of low importance therein (Ang and Peksen 2007). Aside from economic conditions, the politics of the states in a sanctions case is also relevant in determining outcomes.

154 S. SEN AND Z. MAHMOOD Sanctions are likely to succeed when target states are friendly or demo- cratic, rather than inimical or autocratic—as the latter tend to be more resilient and can absorb economic pressure by simply transferring it to their populations. Such states either suppress any consequent discontent or use it to rally nationalist sentiments (Hovi, Huseby, and Sprinz 2005). Thus, sanctions can both destabilise targeted regimes or strengthen them, depending on regime types, domestic dynamics, and the sanctions designs (Ozdamar and Shanin 2021). Furthermore, insofar as the international system of states is concerned, the relationship between sanction and interdependence is not unidirec- tional (Mulder 2022). On the one hand, sanctions are likely to be more effective in an interdependent world, whereby trade and exchange among national economies create vulnerabilities that can be exploited by sanc- tions regimes. The end of the Cold War and the renewal of ﬁnancial globalisation have structurally transformed the erstwhile bipolar inter- national system from brief unipolarity to multipolarity, thereby altering the very context of sanctions. Sanctions in a networked and interde- pendent world have become more precise and sophisticated (Early and Cilizoglu 2020). However, an interdependent and networked world constrains the powers of the nation-state, as the primary actors in sanctions. Globalisation expands the scope for private actors such as multinational and transnational corporations—that control vast majority of global production, dominate global trade, and determine the distri- butional consequences of international trade—that may not be driven by states’ political agenda (Mahmood 2017). Ergo, interdependence can also, on the obverse, increase the difﬁculties and costs of imposing sanctions, not least, those associated with the possibility of conjuring a sanctions alliance. The discussion on sanctions reveals that intensity and impact of sanctions vary across senders and targets, depend on the type and scope of sanctions, sender-target relationship, and the degree of power disparity (Ozdamar and Shanin 2021). Thus analysing the utility and efﬁcacy of sanctions as geoeconomic instruments requires a contextual reading. Following J. David Singer’s (1961) well-known schema of International Relations, it may be possible to contextualise sanctions at the level of the international system, the national state level, and the individual level. Certain historical and geopo- litical facts pertaining to all three levels may be brought together to form the political context of sanctions. For reasons of the technolog- ical revolution, globalisation, and the consequent shrinking of the world,

9 ‘IDEALIST’S DREAM OR REALIST’S CUDGEL’: EXAMINING … 155 the international system has, of late, been marked with tremendous complexity. The context of the present global strategic environment informs the present inquest into the utility and efﬁcacy of sanctions. Examining sanction success and failure Any effort to identify and evaluate the bases of contemporary utility and efﬁcacy of sanctions warrants a theoretically informed and methodologi- cally robust framework. A word on methodology is thereby warranted. First, the post-Cold War epoch informs the temporal framework of the inquiry, since the very political context of sanctions have undergone irreversible changes. The end of bipolarity and emergent multipolar world has meant that veto points in the world decreased for a brief period post- 1990 but then increased with the assertion of countries like China and Russia. Second, since the 1990s, there have been three chief sanctioning enti- ties in global politics that comprise the bulk of global sanctions—the US, the European Union, and the United Nations. The inquest shall nomi- nally focus on these entities as the ‘sanctioner’ or ‘sender’, for they have variously and repeatedly resorted to comprehensive and/or targeted sanc- tions on speciﬁc regimes and/or activities to elicit changes ranging from particular policies to entire regimes; not least, a few of the ‘sanctioned’ entities/activities overlap the domains of unilateral, multinational, and international sanctions that have emanated respectively from the US, the EU, and the UN. Finally, as regards the choice of approach and the bases for evaluating the utility and efﬁcacy of sanctions, the inquest shall resort to interpre- tivism, chieﬂy for adhering to the spatial limitations of the present volume. In this regard, the case study method may be followed, with the choice of limited cases from which inferences can be drawn, and generalisation reached on the utility and efﬁcacy of sanctions. Bearing in mind the choice of sanction senders, a plethora of cases open up from which selection can be made based on the overlaps of sanction types, and geographical or issue-based representation across the spectrum of geopolitics. Using this logic, North Korea may serve as an Asian case of unilateral and international sanctions where all the three sanctioning entities— mentioned above—have participated simultaneously against the target state and/or its leadership; secondly, Venezuela may serve as a Latin American case of multinational sanctions, where the US and the EU have

156 S. SEN AND Z. MAHMOOD simultaneously sanctioned the Venezuelan state and/or its leadership; and third, Egypt may serve as an African case of multinational sanctions, where the EU had sanctioned its former President and several other entities and had to drop the sanctions regime only recently, in the light of its abject failure. Furthermore, since following a standardised set of assumptions and criteria for evaluating efﬁcacy has often led to negative consequences— adduced previously—the present inquest shall remain agnostic regarding these, and instead, advocate a modicum of methodological individualism. It shall attempt to understand whether success in the following sanctions cases is to be evaluated based solely on particular outcomes appertaining to the speciﬁc case in point, or if certain tangible generalisations are possible, by means of abstraction. Whatever the inferences, the present inquest does not aim to arrive—within its limited scope—at a general theory of sanctions utility and efﬁcacy. Analysing Sanctions: Select Case Studies North Korea The Democratic People’s Republic of Korea (DPRK) has, over the years, faced sanctions from the US, the EU, and the United Nations. North Korea has faced international sanctions primarily due to its pursuit of nuclear weapons and ballistic missiles ostensibly to ensure security from perceived threats emanating from the US military bases in the region and joint military exercises conducted by the United States and its allies. Initially ratifying the Nuclear Non-proliferation Treaty (NPT) in 1985, but withdrawing in 2003—citing American aggression—North Korea conducted its ﬁrst nuclear test in 2006. Efforts to denuclearise North Korea through bilateral and multilateral negotiations since the 1990s have largely failed. The Singapore Summit (2018) between President Donald Trump and Kim Jong-un led to the suspension of high-proﬁle military exercises but did not yield substantive steps towards denuclearisa- tion or sanctions relief. Subsequent negotiations in 2019 ended without agreement, and missile testing escalated (Nishizawa, Duan, and Albert 2022). The UN has imposed the most signiﬁcant sanctions on North Korea, obligating all UN member states to enforce them. These sanctions aim to deter North Korea’s weapons development, restrict access to resources

9 ‘IDEALIST’S DREAM OR REALIST’S CUDGEL’: EXAMINING … 157 and technology, block prohibited activities, and leverage negotiations. They cover various aspects of North Korea’s WMD programme, including nuclear, missile, biological, and chemical weapons. Financial sanctions involve closing North Korean correspondent accounts, prohibiting ﬁnan- cial services, bulk cash transfers, and halting investments and joint ventures. Sectorial sanctions target North Korean exports, including coal, iron, textiles, and seafood. Transportation and shipping restrictions include vessel inspections and impoundments, bans on North Korean- ﬂagged vessels, and restrictions on landing rights and overﬂight rights for North Korean aircraft. Humanitarian assistance is allowed under the sanctions. The United States has imposed additional unilateral sanctions on North Korea, targeting a wider range of individuals and businesses, as well as entities in China and Russia supporting North Korea’s weapons programmes, cyber-attacks, human rights abuses, censorship, and money laundering (U.S. Department of State n.d.). North Korea was designated a state sponsor of terrorism from 1988 to 2008 and was relisted in 2017. The European Union, along with Australia, Japan, and South Korea have also variously imposed sanctions on North Korea, having expanded these in response to North Korea’s missile testing and other provocations. These measures include asset freezes, trade restrictions and limitations on entry, investment ban, and remittance caps (Stangarone 2021). While sanctions aim to curb North Korea’s weapons programme, their effectiveness remains a subject of debate, as it continues missile testing. The UN sanctions have signiﬁcantly impacted North Korea’s economy, leading to a sharp decline in GDP and trade since 2016. Despite this, some indicators like stable commodity prices and exchange rates suggest that the sanctions’ economic effect may not be as robust as anticipated. Although the pandemic had further worsened the humanitarian situa- tion, North Korea could also manage to evade some sanctions through illicit trading routes—especially with China, exceeding imposed quotas for reﬁned petroleum imports and ﬁnding ways around bans on coal, iron, and seafood exports (Frank 2018; Stangarone 2021; Nishizawa, Duan, and Albert 2022). The unilateral non-proliferation sanctions by the US have so far had a negligible impact on the North Korean nuclear programme. Critics argue that the unilateral sanctions need stronger enforcement—which is not possible given North Korea’s closed economy and polity, also for Chinese and Russian intervention as ‘sanctions busters’—hurt ordinary families

158 S. SEN AND Z. MAHMOOD instead of elites, and embolden the regime to continue nuclear devel- opment based on the interpretation that additional sanctions are a threat to regime survival. The latter ordinarily entails further belligerence, such as increasing missile testing (Nishizawa, Duan, and Albert 2022). Overall, experts believe sanctions alone may not effectively deter North Korea’s nuclear programme. Some argue that North Korea, due to its relations with China and South Korea, sustains foreign trade, thereby alleviating the pressure from UN sanctions (Kim and Martin-Hermosillo 2013). Additionally, North Korea’s command economy weakens the impact of these sanctions, and it is also unlikely for South Korea—since it shares borders with North Korea—to impose harsh sanctions due to concerns about a refugee inﬂux. Ineffectual sanctions have, therefore, led to escalated tactics, potentially escalating tensions. Thus, using sanc- tions that are likely to fail may increase risks and costs for coercing states, pushing them towards more forceful measures. Others contend that North Korea evades international sanctions by establishing state trading companies (STCs). These entities, operating within legal and illegal realms, procure goods on behalf of the regime. The STCs serve as the linchpin in North Korea’s efforts to bypass inter- national sanctions. Following signiﬁcant events such as the sanctioning of Banco Delta Asia (BDA) in Macau, Premier Wen Jiabao’s visit to North Korea in 2009, and the succession of Kim Jong-un in 2011, North Korea, Inc. underwent a transformation, deepening its integration into China and regional economic centres. Consequently, with China as its ‘sanc- tions buster’, North Korea sought new partners and ﬁnancial instruments beyond the reach of the US Treasury Department. Thus, North Korea’s procurement methods have evolved over time, adapting to new barriers and market conditions. The regime has turned to private Chinese ﬁrms and middlemen, expanded its operations within China, and diversiﬁed procurement pathways through regions like Hong Kong and Southeast Asia. These adjustments have allowed North Korea to maintain a degree of resilience in the face of sanctions (Park and Walsh 2016). Venezuela The primary entities imposing sanctions on Venezuela include the United States, the European Union, and Canada, with some Latin American nations also implementing restrictions on high-ranking ofﬁcials from Venezuela’s government. The United States initiated its sanctions more

9 ‘IDEALIST’S DREAM OR REALIST’S CUDGEL’: EXAMINING … 159 than a decade ago, initially as a response to non-compliance with poli- cies related to drug trafﬁcking, illegal gold mining, terrorism, and human trafﬁcking. The US justiﬁed its sanctions by citing a multitude of factors, including the authoritarian regime led by Nicolas Maduro, allegations of extrajudicial killings by security forces, and widespread corruption. As protests continued and the Venezuelan government’s repression escalated, the US expanded its sanctions under President Trump. These measures included limiting the Venezuelan government’s access to US debt and equity markets, prohibiting transactions involving digital currency, and restricting purchases of Venezuelan debt. In 2019, the US banned inter- actions between American individuals and companies with the Maduro government, imposing secondary sanctions on third-country parties (Seelke 2023). The EU joined the sanctions regime in November 2017 by implementing an arms embargo, freezing assets, and imposing travel bans on individuals linked to repression (Roy 2022). The impact of sanctions on Venezuela can be observed across various sectors, including hydrocarbons, manufacturing, agribusiness, and gold mining (Rodríguez 2022). In 2017, ﬁnancial sanctions exacerbated disparities in ofﬁcial and black-market exchange rates, leading to a decline in oil production and exports. The subsequent trade restrictions in 2019 further burdened the country as the US market closed its doors to Venezuelan production, causing a signiﬁcant loss of revenue. The consequences of these sanctions extended to the widely expropriated agri- cultural sector, which was already grappling with hyperinﬂation, currency scarcity, military control, and corruption. Manufacturing sector had a history of dependence on oil-related activities and the sanctions led to difﬁculties in accessing credit from abroad. The sanctions also restricted access to raw materials which further impacted production. Not least, the sanctions’ impact extended to the emerging gold mining industry, as the government sought to expand it in response to economic challenges, but paradoxically fostered an alliance between itself and criminal organisations and guerrilla groups. Overall, the sanctions, in conjunction with pre- existing economic challenges, exacerbated Venezuela’s economic crisis. They prompted shifts in economic behaviour, from the use of alterna- tive currencies to the emergence of informal channels, adding complexity to the nation’s economic landscape (Rosales 2019; Rodriguez 2022). To understand the impact and the possible success of the sanc- tions regime, it is important to view Venezuela as a rentier economy, where revenue from oil extraction is redistributed through subsidies and

160 S. SEN AND Z. MAHMOOD currency overvaluation to various sectors (Bull and Rosales 2020). This inﬂux of rent income from abroad is pivotal for the nation’s political insti- tutions and social organisations. By the time sanctions were imposed, Venezuela had regressed into a defensive authoritarian rentierism, util- ising oil rents primarily to sustain the ruling government and suppress opponents. The sanctions have disrupted established avenues of rent appropriation, thereby, diminishing the capacity to leverage oil rents due to severed external ﬁnancing, hindering the transfer of oil and gold into rents, and impeding the ability of individual actors to accumulate rent. The Venezuelan case underscores several critical points highlighted in the literature on sanctions. Firstly, sanctions have led to the informali- sation and criminalisation of the Venezuelan economy (Bull and Rosales 2020). Secondly, sanctions have been imposed in isolation, sometimes conﬂicting with other diplomatic strategies such as negotiations and multilateral diplomacy. This fragmented approach has limited their effec- tiveness, as countries like Cuba, Russia, Turkey, and China have exploited opportunities as ‘sanctions busters’ (Smilde and Ramsey 2020). Thirdly, while the causal link is complex, sanctions have coincided with a deep- ening of authoritarianism. Sanctions are generally less effective against authoritarian regimes, and they often contribute to more autocratic governance (Rosenberg, Goldman, Drezner, and Julia 2016). Finally, in this case, the distinction between targeted and comprehensive sanc- tions is blurred due to Venezuela’s heavy dependence on oil and state control. While both sanctions and mismanagement likely contributed to the economic collapse, it is crucial to recognise the multi-causal nature of these issues. Furthermore, in practice, sanctions have rarely resulted in regime change, as their effect on the Maduro administration in Venezuela could adduce. The primary goal of some of the targeted sanctions has been to induce political change and restore the functioning of democratic institu- tions. However, achieving consensus on the legitimacy of the Maduro government and the extent of international intervention has proved challenging. The speciﬁc demands for lifting sanctions on the Maduro government remain unclear: after initially demanding Maduro’s resig- nation, the US policy took a backﬂip with the Biden administration’s statements emphasising the need for negotiations and democratic solu- tions (Rodriguez 2022). Thereafter, the governing elite have adapted by transferring control to key constituents—particularly military ofﬁ- cials—in sectors with access to foreign currency and mineral extraction.

9 ‘IDEALIST’S DREAM OR REALIST’S CUDGEL’: EXAMINING … 161 This has fostered loyalty among these constituents, thereby raising the costs of defection. The informalisation and criminalisation of operations as a result of prolonged sanctions have, therefore, provided alternative means of survival for both the Venezuelan government and business enti- ties, diminishing the incentives for the constituents to turn against their principles. Egypt The European Union’s targeted sanctions on pre-Arab Spring Egyp- tian state authorities is a particularly interesting case, for it reveals the contradictions of the complex web of targeted, multinational sanctions, and the domestic political will aimed at recovering ﬁnancial assets that were systematically expropriated from the national exchequer. Restrictive measures were initially adopted in 2011—at the backdrop of the Arab Spring—and aimed notably at assisting post-Arab Spring Egyptian author- ities with the recovery of misappropriated state assets. The measures consisted of an asset freeze imposed on the assets of the listed individuals located in the EU. In addition, nationals from the EU member states and legal entities incorporated in the EU were forbidden from making funds available to the listed individuals, either directly or indirectly. Since 2011, the targeted sanctions have been reviewed on an annual basis, with some individuals having been delisted over time (Council of the EU Press Release 2021). In the aftermath of the revolution, Egyptians were hopeful of bringing back the billions of Euros purportedly stolen by President Hosni Mubarak and his family, to invest them in building a new Egypt (CIFAR 2017). President Mubarak was investigated for allegedly misappropriating public funds and was convicted by the Egyptian authorities for the same. His wealth was reported to be concealed in France, Germany, and Spain. The EU had deployed targeted sanctions against the former president and his family, thereby disbarring them from accessing the funds which were reportedly stashed in European banks (Transparency International 2022). As the post-revolution political landscape began to change throughout the Middle East and North Africa (MENA) region, the EU’s targeted sanctions against Mubarak as well as those against former Tunisian pres- ident Zine al-Abidine Ben Ali were due to expire on 31 January 2016. These were subsequently renewed upon the premise that despite early promises, post-revolution governments in the MENA region had made

162 S. SEN AND Z. MAHMOOD little or no progress, as their efforts focused largely on regime survival rather than reform. As a result, corruption had thrived, despite the best intentions of transitional governments which, the advocates of EU sanc- tions surmised were therefore in need of the Union’s sanctions regime to overcome domestic ﬁnancial malpractices (Transparency International 2016). The EU’s sanctions regime related to the misappropriation of Egyptian state funds was aimed at promoting the objectives of the EU- Egypt partnership in the context of the Common Foreign and Security Policy (Council of the EU Press Release 2021). Notwithstanding the EU’s high rhetoric in justifying the sanctions, the political will in Egypt to prosecute members of the Mubarak circle— to prove that corruption had indeed led to asset theft—was very weak. Although by mid-2015 the transitional government had created the so- called truth-ﬁnding committees to that effect, and some of Mubarak’s assets were indeed frozen, none of these were conﬁscated and returned to Egypt. These committees, moreover, were not independent from the Egyptian government, and were soon mired in the political turmoil and interferences facing the latter. Since 2017, it had also become stark that the basis upon which restrictive measures were slapped on the Mubarak family were provided by the transitional Egyptian government in a hurry—without the necessary preparation of resources and legal mate- rials requested by the European states—which brought to a rejection of most of the asset recovery requests (CIFAR 2017). As regards the prosecution and sentencing for corruption, the neces- sary requirement of most foreign jurisdictions is to prove that the assets held in their countries were stolen through crime. Although Mubarak and his sons were convicted in the notorious ‘presidential palaces case’ for embezzling USD 17.9 billion, those cases could not be linked to the asset recovery legal assistance request, since investigations on how the vast wealth of the Mubarak family was funnelled abroad never took place. Moreover, under President Al-Sisi, Egypt’s anti-corruption legisla- tion took an unexpected turn through the amendment of the illicit gains law, whereby, the government allowed reconciliation with the purport- edly corrupt elements of the Mubarak administration in exchange for returning some of the stolen assets (CIFAR 2017). Meanwhile, Mubarak and other sanctioned entities had already appealed to the EU General Court in 2011. After a decade-long legal battle—culminating in a judgement only after Mubarak’s death in 2020— the EU General Court (Ninth Chamber) ruled in favour of the applicants.

9 ‘IDEALIST’S DREAM OR REALIST’S CUDGEL’: EXAMINING … 163 The Court conﬁrmed the unlawfulness of the EU sanctions against Mubarak and his family, allowed his living heirs access to the funds in European banks, and ordered the Council of the European Union to pay back any costs the family incurred in the proceedings (Judgment of the General Court 2022). Pre-empting the judgement of the General Court, the EU revoked the Egyptian sanctions regime and delisted nine people, citing paradoxically, in its ofﬁcial press release that ‘following the most recent review of the nine listings still in force, the Council concluded that the regime had served its purpose’ (Council of the EU Press Release 2021). The experts, however, have had a completely different take on the issue: ﬁrst, that the EU Council’s inability to pursue suspected cases of misappropriation of Egyptian public funds with due process has set a worrying precedent; second, that the setback has come when klep- tocracy has been plundering public resources, undermining democracy, and provoking instability globally; and ﬁnally, that the proposed EU reforms must address its asset recovery legislation to improve trans- parency, thereby ceasing to be an attractive destination for dirty money (Hatter in Transparency International 2021). Whatever may be the suggestions, this failed case of restrictive measures on Egyptian enti- ties reveal at once the vacuity of targeted, multinational sanctions when issued in the dearth of consensus among the sanctioning states, as well as the reduced efﬁcacy of these measures when posited on a political system that lacks the necessary will for ensuring ﬁnancial transparency and accountability. Conclusion: Whether or Whither Sanctions? The brief discussion on three different sanction episodes illuminates the utility and efﬁcacy of sanctions and in this section we draw on the lessons. Examining the experiences of sanctions in three different situations, in the context of permacrisis lends us towards a conceptual model of ‘polit- ical settlement of sanctions’. The utility and efﬁcacy of sanctions depends on speciﬁc political dynamics engendered by the systemic complexity of the international system; characteristics of target countries’ regime and economic condition; and the commitment and clarity of the sanction and the senders. It is the triad of systemic, domestic and sanction characteris- tics that create unique ‘sanction settlement’ that determines their efﬁcacy and utility. Intuitively, we emphasise that no strict set of universal criteria

164 S. SEN AND Z. MAHMOOD can be applied to infer efﬁcacy, as the international system is in a state of permacrisis, not least, as all cases are intrinsically different due to domestic political variables, also issues of motives and intents of states locked in sanctions contests. As the North Korea case study shows sanctions have battered its economy and prevented its prospects of ever joining the mainstream of international politics, however, these have failed to prevent nuclearisa- tion and/or institute regime change, especially due to the presence of ‘sanctions busters’ and existential threat to the regime which makes it reti- cent to international concerns. The Venezuela case shows another form of sanction buster. Sanctions have impacted the Venezuelan economy, but the consequent informalisation and criminalisation of operations as a result of prolonged sanctions have provided alternative means of survival for both the Venezuelan government and business entities, diminishing the efﬁcacy of sanctions. The Egyptian case reveals the vacuity of targeted, multinational sanc- tions when issued in the dearth of consensus among the sanctioning states, as well as the reduced efﬁcacy of these measures when posited on a political system that lacks the necessary will for ensuring ﬁnancial transparency and accountability. The US non-proliferation sanctions on North Korea and other nuclear aspirants have either completely failed or were revoked, not least, all of these have fallen short of achieving their desired objectives. It reveals that aside from the level of commitment and consensus among senders, the reason, type of sanctions, attribution, and desired objectives are critical to the success of sanctions. The cases also highlight the importance of domestic conditions in the target country’s economy and politics. States possess the capacity to navi- gate the repercussions of non-compliance, provided they have the means to do so. In the selected instances, sanctioned states had the ability to withstand the economic impacts. In the case of Venezuela, sanctions were accompanied by the informalisation and criminalisation of the national economy, while North Korea’s relatively stable economy, combined with a stable dollar-won exchange rate, enabled it to maintain domestic prices for essential commodities like rice, corn, and petroleum. The lack of domestic opposition strength in the respective sanc- tioned countries prevented political pressures on the regime. In some countries, opposition lacked the necessary popularity or strength (as in the case of the US-backed Juan Guaidó in Venezuela), and in North Korea, opposition does not exist or is unable to sustain itself due to the

9 ‘IDEALIST’S DREAM OR REALIST’S CUDGEL’: EXAMINING … 165 overwhelmingly autocratic nature of the government. Further sanction objectives of regime change involve considerations of nationalistic fervour. Even in the face of repression, economic collapse, and corruption, citi- zens may ‘rally around the ﬂag’ to maintain leadership in the hands of existing leaders, rather than allow a leader inﬂuenced by a foreign inter- vening state to take power. It is imperative to note that the act of sanction involves subjective connotations shaped by subjective interpretation and meaning-making practices, for the countries imposing sanction and the target countries. In sum, evaluation of the utility and efﬁcacy of sanctions based on their impact on altering state behaviour, must consider the triad of systemic, domestic and sanction characteristics that create unique ‘sanc- tion settlement’. These include states’ economic circumstances, such as its access to domestic ﬁnancial resources, international ﬁnancial networks; their external relationships and ability of manoeuvring the ever-changing landscape of global politics and internal conﬁguration and dynamics of political power. All these elements collectively shape the success or failure of a sanction. References Aldrich, W.W. 1967. The Suez Crisis - A Footnote to History. Foreign Affairs 45 (3): 541–552. Allen, S.H. 2008.
