CHAPTER 10 Leverage in Energy Security: Weaponising For Good and Bad Cauvery Ganapathy “Those engaged in trade with each other Do not shoot at one another” (Wintour 2022)* The combination of stresses in the international environment that coalesced to form the permacrisis of this decade, has ampliﬁed concerns over the politics of energy security. The geopolitics of energy has been a permanent ﬁxture in international relations predating the last century, so, this is not to suggest a return, much less a revival, of the subject. The value of energy resources, and easy access to them, as a currency of strategic power, has been consistent over two centuries now. What has not, however, remained the same is the nature of this cur rency, and the changing signiﬁcance of state and non-state actors who *German Chancellor Helmut Schmidt speaking to President Jimmy Carter, March 5th, 1980 C. Ganapathy (B) Dubai, UAE e-mail: ganapathy.cauvery@gmail.com © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_10 171

172 C. GANAPATHY transact in it, whether as sellers, buyers or intermediates. What ascribes novelty to the concerns over the subject in the era of permacrisis is that it stands today at an unprecedented intersection of having to grapple with record high consumption of fossil fuels despite having to countenance the persistent and ever-mutating problems of these old forms of energy, while simultaneously struggling to push forward the clean energy tran- sition, through the deployment of renewables largely, in a manner that would cut the cords with the old ways as much as possible. 2023 could be considered an inﬂection point by virtue of the fact that the world’s consumption of fossil fuels has been the highest ever recorded,1 while at the same time having the highest number of dramati- cally ambitious clean energy goal commitments by the maximum number of countries being made within the same timeline. While the prognosis of devastating climate change, and the impact and direct contribution the use of fossil fuels has on it, should have been the catalyst, it is the perma- crisis that have greatly aggregated and accelerated, if not induced in some measure, a fundamental shift in global energy security dynamics. Despite the dichotomies in the pursuit of more ambitious clean energy targets at the same time as increased fossil fuel consumption, the net result of the permacrisis has been the creation of a signiﬁcant, even if unintended, alignment between security and environmental concerns where energy security is concerned, and in so doing, it has engineered a paradigmatic shift in the global energy landscape. The purpose of the chapter is to draw out the broad contours of just such overarching impacts that the permacrisis have had on the ﬁeld of energy geopolitics, while taking cognisance of the inherent dichotomies, and potential for a repetition of old errors in the domain. The chapter intends to do this by focusing on three broad areas of concern. First, a possible weaponisation of energy resources, both enhanced and compounded by increasing interdependence in the age of permacrisis; second, consider how the fact of such weaponisation is likely to change or may already have changed geopolitical dynamics or reinforced existing conﬁgurations in international relations; third, how likely it is that this state of play from fossil fuels will be passed on as a legacy issue into 1 Given the COVID-19 lockdowns and the suppression of demand and reduced economic activity and output, the previous data recorded to be referenced would ideally be that of 2019; the resultant pent-up demand post the easing of COVID-19 restrictions must also be accounted for.

10 LEVERAGE IN ENERGY SECURITY: WEAPONISING … 173 the pathways of new energy transitions through monopolies of resource access, supply, transit, technological dominance or processing capabilities. It would be prudent to limit the scope of this chapter to the pandemic’s timeline or the outbreak of the war in Ukraine and the fallouts most easily attributable to it. Yet there is very little in international relations that operate through such neat silos or in along a simple linear pathway. This is much less in the case of a timeline that has experienced a global pandemic that left no country, domain or industry unaffected. As such, major global issues such as the sheer inability of international organisations to reform in time or the global ﬁnancial crisis of 2008, remain as relevant to the points of origin and scale of the permacrisis of 2022–23 as do the repercussions of a dogged global pandemic or a return of war to Europe with signiﬁcant ramiﬁcations across continents. The upheavals in the traditional moor- ings of international geopolitics and geoeconomics, triggered through a coincidence of multiple developments, many of which could be traced to the enduring but insidious impacts of partially resolved or unresolved legacy complexities of the past, have contributed towards compounding an already mendacious maze of international relations. Further compli- cating that maze is a most perseverant retinue of global issues ranging from a fast deteriorating security situation in Asia and the wider Indo- Paciﬁc, a vividly perceptible realisation that climate change and its impacts are an existential problem here and now, instead of being a long-term policy consideration/projection alone, to the depressing consistence of the internecine conﬂicts in Syria, Yemen and now parts of Africa leading to concerning regularity and levels of human migration, all the while grap- pling with the growing tendency to try and fortify national boundaries while faced with the truism of unavoidable interdependence. Energy poli- tics of the last decade needs to be contextualised in the midst of this cacophony of varied crises. Weaponisation of Energy Resources and Access Barriers Energy geopolitics of states has been directly impacted by one or more elements of what may be identiﬁed as the permacrisis spanning the last decade, and none more so than the Russian invasion of Ukraine just as the world was recovering from the most devastating global pandemic. Russia’s invasion of Ukraine has had wide-ranging ramiﬁcations on global food, energy and security calculations. For all intents, along with the

174 C. GANAPATHY lasting impact of the pandemic, this particular act by Moscow could be singled out as among the chief trigger-points of what we today recognise as the permacrisis. While no reference to its abundant fossil fuel resources has been made by Moscow itself in the purported reasons for this act of aggression, energy can be reasonably identiﬁed as being central to the calculations that were made in both, the lead-up to the war, as well as Russia’s projections for the counter-response. The value of those abun- dant energy resources would be more relevant still to Moscow’s ability to stand its ground for a much longer-term regardless of any tactical victories in the war itself. Now, energy is a strategic commodity (Mu 2020). By that token alone, the leeway that countries can make for disruptions or deliberate attacks on other imported goods, do not apply to energy. Fulﬁlment of energy requirements, if not guaranteed sufﬁciency in it, lies at the nub of a state’s food security, economic security and by extension, national security matrix. The counter-response to any tendency or act to weaponise such a strategic commodity, then, is expectedly and understandably stringent and signiﬁcant. The weaponisation of energy is not a new phenomenon. The efforts to weaponise its control over hydrocarbon resources by a state which is rich in it have several examples with the Iranian oil embargo of 1979 being the one with perhaps the greatest long-lasting impact in terms of the geopolitical changes it triggered. The weaponisation of it by Russia, though, appears poised to be one of equal, if not much greater, signiﬁ- cance in the long term. Expectations that the Russians would weaponise their leverage by virtue of energy trade, over Western Europe is more than four decades old now (Mark Davis, 2016). And, yet, when the most robust wielding of this weapon came, Europe was as unprepared as it appears to have been naively sanguine with the arrangement four decades ago (Wintour 2022). The efforts to weaponise its control over hydro- carbon resources by a state which is rich in it, has typically led to an immediate search for supplanting of that actor by ﬁnding alternatives to them, in addition to the imposition of sanctions to limit their presence in the interdependent international milieu. This has happened in the cases of Iran, Venezuela as well as Saudi Arabia at times, in some instances more easily than in others. This option, however, becomes that much more challenging when the actor to be replaced is the world’s largest oil and natural gas exporter, and far more crucially, the state with the largest

10 LEVERAGE IN ENERGY SECURITY: WEAPONISING … 175 stockpile of tactical nuclear weapons in the world (Arms Control Associ- ation 2023), and with a land-border congruous with more than half of Europe. Given this background, Europe’s willingness to become part of an interdependent matrix with Russia to the degree it did in the energy sector was a perplexing strategic miscalculation of mammoth proportions. Winston Churchill’s prescription about the safety and certainty of energy supplies lying in the diversiﬁcation of source alone (Yergin 2006) appears to have fallen by the wayside in Europe’s willingness to tie its fortunes, and more importantly its national security, to an overarching dependence on Russia. Prior to February, 2022, Europe procured nearly 50% of its gas imports (accounting for 40% of its total gas supplies) and 25% of its oil from Russia (Bloomberg News 2021). Even more glaring is the fact that this dependence on the Russians was for the form of energy which constituted EU’s principal baseload capacity generator. The reasoning behind this may have been an oversimpliﬁcation of the value of interdependence. The EU’s rationale for this, apart from the simple math of cheaper logistics, a comfortable pricing mechanism (Bloomberg News 2021) and the reduction of reliance on the seemingly more volatile Middle Eastern and OPEC sources, could most understand- ably have been the consideration that forging interdependence situates a country in the economic and national security calculus of the other, and this, in turn, induces a degree of rational behaviour by establishing a stake in the welfare of the other. The expectation, then, is that this would inoc- ulate both countries from an irrational act or aggressive motivations on the part of the other. What this calculation/assumption does not make an adequate correction for is that the other actor may be counting on this very same interdependence to inhibit the ability of the recipient state to mount a counter-response. That interdependence regulates relations between states and reduces the value of aggression (Keohane 1990) while prioritising trade and cooperation is a tenet which needs multiple quali- ﬁers to apply to the Realist mode of states, and the case of Russia is no different. Ralph Norman Angell in his Nobel prize winning work ‘The Great Illusions’ (Angell 1933) had laid out the fundamental precept upon which Helmut Schmidt’s conviction that when nation-states become econom- ically interdependent, the cost–beneﬁt analysis of going to war would establish it as being a wholly undesirable exercise. What Angell did not, however, account for are elements of strategic calculations based on

176 C. GANAPATHY ideologies, differing worldviews and readings of history, domestic polit- ical concerns and the baggage of unresolved issues from the past, which do not immediately appeal to a rational explanation. Interdependence affects world politics but as Keohane and Nye point out in their seminal work, it is governments that decide on those patterns of interdependence (Keohane and Nye 2012), which in turn opens that decision-making agency to multiple unquantiﬁable considerations, not all of which may be rational. To think of economic interdependence as benign (Keohane and Nye 2012) when underlying ideological issues fester alongside funda- mentally differing readings of history and conﬂicting aspirations for the future, would tantamount usually then, to an expensive folly. The Russians had, in fact, reduced their energy supplies to Europe prior to February 2022 in what could be interpreted as an effort at exerting leverage. Russia not sending enough gas to Europe in 2021 may have been a way to restrict supplies such that Europe’s response to any aggressive intent on Moscow’s part (Sheppard et al. 2022) would be muted. Given that there is evidence now to suggest that by March 2021 (Kramer 2021), President Putin had made up his mind regarding the invasion of Ukraine in the coming spring, the decision by the Russians to reduce the supply of gas to Europe, could well account for a case of energy blackmail. The very fact that the sanctions regime put in place post the Ukraine invasion, to a large degree manoeuvred to keep Russian oil and gas out of the fold, is testament to the power of energy as a resource and to a validation that Russia’s gamble based on the strength of its status as an energy superpower paid off, even if in part. The very nature of sanctions and the counter-responses it triggers is central to how acts of the weaponisation of energy resources have been launched and conducted (Mark Davis 2016). While the Russian invasion of Ukraine may have had as some part of its calculation the consideration that it already enjoyed sufﬁcient leverage over the EU which would insulate it from the consequences of its aggres- sive intent, the very foundational motivations of the NATO should have been enough to disregard this being a serious consideration. Further- more, Vladimir Putin’s own contentions (Sullivan 2022) suggest that, the potential expansion of NATO was a motivating impulse above and beyond any economic calculation he may have made on the question of the value of Russia’s status as an energy superpower suffering, in the lead- up to the invasion of Ukraine. There is, though, some value in pointing out that EU and NATO’s reaction to the takeover of the Crimea in 2014

10 LEVERAGE IN ENERGY SECURITY: WEAPONISING … 177 may well have led the Russians to believe they had greater currency of inﬂuence over Europe by virtue of their energy dependence on Russia. Furthermore, Putin’s violation of the Budapest Memorandum of Security Assurances, 1994, by which Russia promised to never use military force or economic coercion against Ukraine, Kazakhstan and Belarus, itself could be seen as the ﬁrst shot ﬁred in this disagreement between the two sides, which has now snowballed into the crisis over Ukraine. The weaponisation of energy in the context of the Russian invasion of Ukraine is not restricted to Moscow’s calculations of the leverage it enjoyed over the EU, but in fact found an equally, if not more, disturbing manifestation in the blowing up of the Nord Stream pipeline, arguably the poster-child of erstwhile energy cooperation between the Russians and their European neighbours. In the very existence of the Nord Stream pipeline was inherent an assumption of cooperative security. The attack on the Nord Stream is a classic example of how vulnerable energy coop- eration can be to changes in strategic calculations despite the indisputable value of it to national security and sustenance, more so now that evidence regarding the involvement of a pro-Ukraine group in the attack on what effectively was the energy lifeline of the country’s European allies in this war against the Russians, has been discovered (The Economist 2023). In addition to the sabotage of the Nord Stream pipeline, the targeted Russian onslaught against the energy infrastructure of Ukraine is testa- ment to the centrality of energy security in a conﬂict, regardless of whether it began with or is aimed at energy per se. No European energy system, including the ones damaged in the First and Second World Wars, has seen the kind of targeted destruction that the Ukrainian system has borne since February 2022 (Task Force April 2023). Within a very short time of launching the war, Russia systematically destroyed nearly 50% of Ukraine’s installed power capacity and decimated its oil reﬁning industry, in addition to effectively uprooting the country’s laid energy infrastruc- ture in the form of transmission lines and distribution networks (Task Force May 2023). The attacks on Ukraine’s energy infrastructure came in just as evidence of Russian involvement in the outages of the Caspian Pipeline Consortium began to show. This targeting of energy infrastruc- ture, is part of a long dubious practice among state and non-state actors to disrupt and gain strategic advantage at the cost of a country’s energy systems, and by that token, its national security. In attacking Europe’s energy infrastructure, whether through physical destruction such as in the case of Ukraine, or through disruptions as in the case of the EU,

178 C. GANAPATHY Russia partakes in a list of notoriety among petrostates which weaponise not just their energy resources but also their points of access to those resources once procured by a buyer or even one independently owned by another state (Wintour and Borger 2019). Iran’s signiﬁcance to the international market, for instance, has as much to do with its undeniable clout of control over the Strait of Hormuz through which a substantial part of international trade passes, as it does to the natural resource wealth Tehran sits on. It is a leverage that Iran holds and values, and one that every country that has its energy supplies passing through the Hormuz chokepoint remains vulnerable to. Whether Russia has been able to achieve the goals it set out to achieve through the use of energy as a weapon in the case of the Ukraine inva- sion would beneﬁt from an ex-post analysis. On the face of it, though, the Russians, it would appear, have exhausted the most prized weapon in their arsenal of political and strategic leverage over the Europeans for the moment. Russia’s actions having come at substantial economic costs, ties in with Mearsheimer’s contention that regardless of the degree of relative security and prosperity born of the interdependence of the status quo, ‘the rational desire to achieve regional hegemony in an anarchic inter- national environment’ (Mearsheimer, 1990), is a natural instinct among states, despite how irrational the calculation may appear from the outside. That the energy insecurity of a partner or an adversary has a real impact on the national security of a country, and therefore matters leading to such a state of affairs must not be allowed to transpire, much less be precipitated, then, is an expectation that does not hold much water within the inherently anarchical international system. Russia’s obvious economic losses and the kind of devastation that the Russian economy, society and general international status has suffered as a result of the war, is a throw- back to Alfred Mahan’s counter-response to Angell (Rühle 2019)i nt h a t there are seemingly irrational and unquantiﬁable factors that cause leaders and states to act on the impulse of going to war despite the considerable costs of it or the absence of any obvious or lasting beneﬁts from it. As with any strategic commodity, the weaponisation of energy has been a two-way street. The demand generators/buyers hold as much of a sway as the producers/suppliers. Although the desperation for energy resources means that the buyers are the ones that would suffer more typically in the absence of trade, it is equally true that many of the petrostates that have the energy resources as the mainstay of their economies cannot stay aﬂoat in the absence of such trade, so, while they are energy-abundant, they

10 LEVERAGE IN ENERGY SECURITY: WEAPONISING … 179 are not by a long measure self-sufﬁcient. The strategic nature of energy resources and its trade has led to its wielding often being conducted through proxy and through apparently disconnected steps. The energy production and pricing disagreement between the Russians and the Saudi Arabians that began in March 2020, for instance, in a complete rebuttal of the understanding the two had reached in 2016 to control the price and market share of their most lucrative exports, is indicative of the kind of impasse that can build over geopolitical calculations that have little to do with the economics of the energy market itself. Sanctions are a particularly complicated part of the toolkit in the gamut of counter-response to an act of weaponisation of energy resources and trade. The exemptions of the sanctions regime in the case of the Ukraine war and the fact that countries like the US and the UK, as primary leviers of those sanctions, could still access processed fuel that was being bought off the Russians by the Indians at considerably subsidised rates, for one, is evidence of how grey these areas of operation can be. The strategic nature of energy as a scarce commodity means that consensus on withholding trade in it is a rare phenomenon. Oftentimes, sanctions on an energy supplier may be made for an entirely different concern, far removed from the ideological or even hard security matters that seem to be the main triggers for the act, and may in fact be based on more domestic economic considerations (Jensen-Eriksen 2017). This, in turn, translates into an omnipotent buyer and seller market with changes only in the participant list. This naturally compromises the ability of coun- tries to establish a fool-proof non-porous sanctions regime or lend such an act any effective credibility. If it does ever succeed, in the manner it was made possible brieﬂy in the case of Iran, then that would have a more reasonable impact on the country in question, to the extent of inﬂuencing its policy even if never really altering it. Absent such an effective compre- hensive sanction on the seller, the weaponising of energy by the buyer would not work, thereby resting the leverage safely with the producer/ seller more often than not. New Pathways to Counter the Weaponisation of Energy Wars and crises have a way of clarifying objectives, national, regional and global. Energy-related crises in the earlier decades typically saw a movement towards supplementing, if not supplanting, the problem- atic supplier, given that being held hostage to such weaponisation of

180 C. GANAPATHY a strategic resource can be powerful motivator. The permacrisis of this decade, however, has largely accelerated the movement towards replacing the very forms of energy that hold nation-states hostage. A fundamental shift has taken place in the patterns of energy demand and supply over the last decade. To be certain, this shift has been nearly three decades in the making, but the grand changes could more easily be traced to a more manageable timeline within the last decade, and could arguably be estab- lished as having accelerated post the pandemic and alongside the war in Ukraine. This repositioning within the energy security narrative is under- girded by three speciﬁc and largely inter-related developments—ﬁrst, the increasing commitments to green energy transitions by countries around the world having increased substantially over the last decade, with the most ambitious ones having been announced since the end of the pandemic and the outbreak of the war in Ukraine. To be fair, while the weaponisation of energy by Russia and the scarcities and supply chain disruptions born of it could be counted as contributing factors, they are only a part of a long litany of concerns that triggered this movement; second, the movement towards this new form of energy creates a new energy superpower in the form of China, supported by Russia and the countries of Africa which are rich in rare earths, metals and minerals. Finally, there is a signiﬁcant change in where the maximum demand for energy now emerges from, and this is a paradigm altering shift wherein the drivers that determine the demand and supply dynamics both, then, are naturally different from those of the present. This last development, effectively, changes the narrative on what quali- ﬁes as energy security. It expands the scope of the qualifying elements that decide on energy security being met by addressing the need for devel- oping countries to meet their emissions targets while addressing their economic growth and societal access issues in the most viable and afford- able manner. These countries routinely have to contend with the fact that what has security beneﬁts could also have inﬂationary tendencies, and that consideration alone can alter many of the calculations of the energy security debate. The main drivers of demand today are in Asia, a shift from the traditional bastions of demand generation of the last century. Further, countries that have traditionally been buyers of energy have become suppliers of oil for the ﬁrst time in nearly seven decades, for instance, with the US becoming a net exporter of oil for the ﬁrst time in 75 years by the end of 2018. While this change in the locus of demand

10 LEVERAGE IN ENERGY SECURITY: WEAPONISING … 181 generation has changed over the last decade, the architecture that under- writes the conduct of such energy trade, and all related interface between nation-states, has remained largely unchanged. The Ukraine war and the pandemic, in many ways, have helped with pushing forward the debate over the need for new international insti- tutional architectures that would be more representative and responsive, and this has been most pronounced in the spheres of climate change, debt restructuring and energy security. In so doing, the permacrisis has created an alignment of security and climate change needs as also the economic logic for a form of energy to be the most practical option. It is also a natural progression along a pathway that can trace its momentum to a few takeaways from the pandemic. The unfortunate combination of a pandemic and a war has led to three discoveries having emerged as a trifecta of assaults on the combination of national interest and secu- rity compulsions and the climate change redressal targets of countries. First, the struggle of the Global South for vaccines during the pandemic demonstrates the inability of the developing world to partake in what should have arguably been a universally accessible right, by virtue largely of not having a seat at the decision-making tables due to the very consti- tution of international decision-making infrastructure nor the ﬁnancial wherewithal to bypass international regulatory mandates; second, the supply chain bottlenecks of the time, which meant being able to afford did not necessarily translate into being able to access energy resources from outside the country; and, third, the invasion of Ukraine by the world’s largest exporter of oil and natural gas, which wreaked havoc on the energy and food security calculations of the Global South while upending the long-term clean energy transition blueprints of European countries. In concert, these three developments combined to create a national security dilemma for the Global South that exacerbated the environmental concerns that had already begun plaguing it—arguably with more imme- diate and noticeable ramiﬁcations than the countries of the Global North appeared to be facing. The one positive outcome of this insecurity matrix was a growth in the combined global vision that a move away from hydrocarbons was no longer a choice but a necessity, if only to insulate countries from the vagaries and political machinations of energy trade. The search for alternatives to the hydrocarbon resources whose unequal distribution entrenches monopolies, has undoubtedly been accelerated by the fallout from the Russian invasion of Ukraine. Trading routes,

182 C. GANAPATHY trading patterns and international investment ﬂows have also been dras- tically altered following both the pandemic and the Russian invasion of Ukraine. On the issue of energy security itself, in the short-term though, growing fears of scarcity and supply chain disruptions, has revived the preference for fossil fuels more, despite the movement towards clean energy commitments growing in many countries. The net result of this has not been just a rebound in fossil fuel consumption and unprecedented levels of demand spike, but also a considerable degree of urgency in the procurement of these resources. Simultaneously, however, the urgent imperative of having to substitute what is now an unreliable supply of hydrocarbon resources from what was the world’s largest exporter of oil and natural gas prior to February 2022, has also meant a scramble for seeking hydrocarbon resources from alternative sources, at least in the short to medium term, in a manner that has increased the price advan- tage as well as political clout of other producers of oil and other fossil fuels. It has been a curious dichotomy of pushing forward with the most ambitious green energy transition targets while at the same time trying to scour every available point of fossil fuel import by countries that are dependent on fossil fuels. The return to fossil fuels has been desperation- driven in an environment where governments are trying to cushion the multiple lags in growth and inﬂation that were triggered by the long- term economic fallouts from the pandemic and the war in Ukraine. This, however, is expected to be a temporary adjustment, which signiﬁcantly will lead to the peak of energy production. The International Energy Agency had assessed that at the cost of economic growth, the pandemic would result in a 5% decline in energy demand in 2020 with a largely concomitant decline of 7% in global greenhouse gases (IEA 2020).2 2 These ﬁgures must be contextualised with the fact of the pandemic restrictions and that economies increased their growth with a resultant pressure brought to bear on the amount of energy being used and the greater amount of GHGs being released into the atmosphere. This does not, however, take away from the fact of markedly increased demand impulse based on increased economic activity.

10 LEVERAGE IN ENERGY SECURITY: WEAPONISING … 183 Old Follies in the New Pathways Although it is true that entrenched complications in national life and the international circumstance present an opportunity for visionary transfor- mations, it becomes an enormously challenging task to manoeuvre when the pulls on a state’s ﬁnite resources and largely well-entrenched strategic partnerships ﬁnd themselves at odds with each other. It is important to factor in that a lot of countries cannot afford to plan on the basis of long-term economics and that they need instead to plan based on their affordability index today. This has always been the case for countries navi- gating the geopolitics of energy, and never more so than in this age of seemingly unending permacrisis. Massive shocks can, however, trigger positive outcomes, even if they count for something only in the long term. There could be a push for innovation, useful employment generating indi- genisation, or even more open and efﬁcient government systems. What the spike in the prices of fossil fuels effectively does is make the most effective case for the search and consolidation of alternative forms of fuel which would be more cost-effective, and most importantly, make the case for a new energy architecture. The energy architecture of the future needs to remain vigilant against the inequities of the past, to begin with. The co-relations that can, and must, be drawn between the growing willingness among countries of the Global South to try and carve out an international agenda and objec- tive which differs from the older construct of the liberal international order and the failure of the international organisations to reform in time and become more agile in their responses to global inequities of access, agency and sheer power at a time when the circumstance of countries are markedly different from when these institutions were ﬁrst created, are signiﬁcant. The primary tests for the success of the clean energy transitions and in ensuring that it does not repeat the mistakes of the international fossil fuel economy would come down to two issues—the ﬁrst, a democratisation of the entire process and value chain which must begin at the level of funding, with International Climate Finance being placed at the centre of the agenda; and, second, actively ﬁghting against the tendencies of perpetuating monopolies in the value chain. A lag or failure on either of these two counts would negate any progress towards moving away from the cascading problems of the fossil

184 C. GANAPATHY fuel economy exempliﬁed by the war in Ukraine and the scarcities of the pandemic. First, on the issue of funding, because clean energy has to contend with discretionary demand and price sensitivity in a manner that fossil fuels have not had to, due to the lack of viable alternatives, pricing schemes and the ﬁnancing for the renewable energy projects play an oversized role in the deployment of renewable energy. It is pertinent, in this context, to highlight the role that the private sector can play in the ﬁeld of transition ﬁnance. Despite the very value of such investment emerging from the fact that it may be privately raised, and therefore carry with it lesser bureau- cratic obligations, it is equally true that private players would expectedly base their decisions on a regulatory structure that could incentivise such investment. So long as renewable energy remains tied to non-essential produc- tion, it will suffer, and it will remain so unless it is integrated into the behemoths of an economy such as the construction and fertiliser indus- tries—both, signiﬁcantly large chunks of the economy in the Global South. It becomes that much more important, then, to incentivise the acceleration of clean energy deployment and equally important that the energy architecture that is built this time maps out the issues of de-risking investment. As part of this effort, it is necessary to factor in the challenge of seeking the mobilisation of capital at a time of disquiet and ﬂux. Rudi- mentary risk assessments would call for a signiﬁcantly higher degree of caution at such a time. What becomes important then, is for insurance mechanisms, nationally and internationally, to assess and underwrite risk in a manner that would moderate the level of aversion to it, given the strategic importance of expanding these new energy pathways—some- thing, that appears unlikely to happen without deliberate government support mechanism and mandates. This will have to be done all the way through the value chain, namely in transmission, deployment and in storage. Furthermore, it would be useful to consider the levels of contribu- tion towards the international ﬁnance fund being more evenly distributed. There is a fundamental issue if countries that are looking at exploring more of the fossil fuels to fund their future growth are asked to bear a greater burden because that would be penalising them for a problem that was largely not caused by them. However, in order to incentivise the use of cleaner forms of energy, it may help to offer tax-subsidies to countries when they are seeking to import the inputs required, and

10 LEVERAGE IN ENERGY SECURITY: WEAPONISING … 185 make the transfer of technology an easier process. The differential in such an arrangement could then be borne by the climate fund that would be established under the auspices of this new energy architecture. A provision such as this could tie in neatly with the Just Energy Transition Partner- ships announced by Indonesia during the G20 in November 2022. In a signiﬁcant re-arrangement of traditional constructs of the energy security architecture, the oil-rich petrostates of the Gulf have begun an important transformation. The role that the Sovereign Wealth Funds of countries such as the UAE and Saudi Arabia are playing in underwriting the massive funding commitments towards the deployment of renewable energy, even as they continue to monetise their strategic fossil fuel resources, is the kind of parallel track that would enable the transformation of the energy architecture of the world in the most viable manner. For the developing countries of the Global South, what has compounded the dysfunctional dynamics of the oil and gas markets, marked by volatility, disruptions, price and value ﬂuctuations and a general lack of reliability, has been disjointed planning and a lack of well- spaced investment in clean energy projects even if the gestation periods are relatively long. It would be useful to consider the value of nuclear energy projects in this context. In addition to addressing all of the above listed concerns, nuclear energy has the beneﬁts of guaranteeing clean energy with a baseload capacity, a factor that is non-negotiable to the growth potentials of the developing countries. Beyond the implications for the GHG emissions and the health of the planet, a signiﬁcant fact that commends the growth of decentralised forms of renewable energy path- ways is that reducing a country’s dependence on hydrocarbons would to a large degree help insulate it from the vagaries of international energy politics. In addition to possessing these merits, it is the value of nuclear energy to cater to industrial production and manufacturing which could make the best case for its use (León 2023). It commends itself more due to its imperviousness to market ﬂuctuations and its promise of reliable grid-scale low-carbon energy which can be more decentralised. The second issue of concern in the hopes for a cleaner, democratic and more equitable system of energy security internationally, is to guard against the tendency towards monopoly creations. One of the principal verticals in the inherent shift in the global energy landscape is the unchal- lenged position it will accord China. By virtue of its control over rare earth minerals with 60% of production and nearly 85% of processing

186 C. GANAPATHY capacity, China is poised to single-handedly assume the combined rele- vance of all the petrostates and the OPEC countries to the traditional fossil fuel-powered economy of the previous century. Signiﬁcantly, nearly 55% of the world’s addition of renewable energy generation in the year 2022–2023 came from China alone (IEA 2023). Further, China enjoys a monopoly over the production of cheap membranes used in solar panels being purchased by most of the world. A combination of these three factors places China right at the centre of the clean energy transition 3 and the Climate Change agenda of the world. The concern with monopolies created in the previous energy systems should naturally apply to in this case too, for fear of this position being weaponised in the future, given the natural expectation of interdependence to exist in the new arrangement too. China has previously held dependent states hostage over critical minerals supplies, as it did with Japan (Bradsher 2010). It promises to do so again if the proclamations of the Regulations on Rare Earths Manage- ment 2021 (Shunshuke 2021) and the restrictions imposed on the export of gallium and germanium, announcements that were noticeably timed with US Treasury Secretary Janet Yellen’s visit to China in mid-2023, are any indication. In addition to China, it is Russia, with a control of nearly 17% of the world’s high-grade nickel production (Chin et al. 2022), a metal that is central to producing the batteries that stand at the heart of the EV revolution, which holds an important position in the ‘new energy economy’. Importantly, the role of African countries such as the DRC, Zimbabwe and Namibia which are endowed with substantial reserves of rare earths offers perhaps the best chance for a meaningful transfor- mation in the international energy architecture. Such a change would be conditional on the African countries cooperating to leverage their combined contributing capacity in the exploration, mining, production and trade of rare earths in a manner that could maximise value addition (Baskara, 2022) through the entire production chain, both upstream and downstream. It is essential that the mistakes of the past not be repeated in the clean energy transition. Yet, the fact that one country appears poised to be placed right at the centre of the emerging energy architecture, with overwhelming control over the domain, can reasonably be projected as a 3 The carbon footprint of the purported clean energy transition itself is worth a more incisive look.

10 LEVERAGE IN ENERGY SECURITY: WEAPONISING … 187 precursor to the new battle lines for the weaponisation of energy resources being drawn (Hammelehle 2023). The fruition of this possibility would see the fault lines of the past exacerbated, and the failure to guard against this repeat of history would nullify every other progress made under the new energy architecture. Coordinating and systematically integrating the efforts towards a sustainable transition across the different policy imperatives, such as the simultaneous but not always congruous commitments, that policy-makers would have towards clean energy as well as land and water use patterns or maintenance of forest cover, for instance, is essential to the enter- prise of clean energy transitions. Ensuring complementarity among these differing agendas needs to be an essential element in moving forward with a more systematic effort towards integrating clean energy options into any national energy policy. This is necessary due to concerns that the transition pathways that governments commit to, particularly when the commitments have been triggered by an external shock, tend to adversely impact national goals in areas of just energy distribution, sustainable food production and efforts to secure biodiversity (Tran and Egermann 2022). Unless synergies are established and the planning towards this transition is better integrated (Tran and Egermann 2022), the pulls and pressures in different, and often conﬂicting directions would continue. Although each of the pulls that governments are having to answer to in the process of transition, are all essential concerns, yet, the struggle between multiple commitments may dilute the original starting point of the effort to supplant or transition, particularly, given assessments that suggest a low Return on Investments (RoI) on renewable energy projects in many countries, particularly in the Global South. It is then, the task, of a society and polity to prioritise from among the varying agendas. It would be prudent to consider the qualitative elements that differen- tiate fossil fuels from the clean energy options in order to judge it more fairly and draw maximum beneﬁts from it. Among the primary draws of renewables and clean energy options is the kind of access it can bring to territorially remote and marginalised communities and populations, and this aspect alone should commend its value to any policy-making in the sphere, apart from the undeniable urgency and compulsions of Climate Change. Given that the permacrisis affect the marginalised more disproportion- ately, it will be necessary that the new energy architecture prioritise the issues of energy access, energy equity, environmental justice and climate

188 C. GANAPATHY change mitigation means. It is critical that the Global South’s struggle for equitable and reliable access to energy be positioned centrally in the international community’s quest for clean energy transitions and sustain- able emission targets. Locating the question of energy access, and working on realising this as a universal right would offer the most progressive agenda under this new energy architecture. Studies have estimated that getting about 1.2bn people presently bereft of it, equitable access to energy between 2023 and 2030, would cost $35bn/year (IRENA 2023), while getting access to clean cooking means for 3bn people living without it would cost $25bn/year (Culver 2022). These are fantastical sums of money, and such mobilisation of ﬁnance can only emanate from a place of recognising that everyone has a stake, albeit, some more than others. There is a fundamental equitability in the new energy pathways simply by virtue of them are not being dependent on the random distribution of natural resources. Lessened asymmetry in resource distribution does not, however, automatically translate into a veritable equitability in access to those resources. While comparative advantages (Kyriakopoulou et al. 2023) will continue to play an inordinate role in where the chips fall in this new energy landscape, the attendant reconﬁguration under this new architecture offers the Global South its best chance yet at redeﬁning parameters of energy security that work for them.
