CHAPTER 11 International Security and Irregular Cross-border Migration in Times of Permacrisis Sabahat Ambreen Contemporary international security spans a broad spectrum of inter- connected concerns and threats that pose challenges for both national and human security. The major challenge for nation-states, international organisations, and other stakeholders is to reduce such threats in order to secure the safe existence of humanity. Human security encompasses more than conventional concepts of security and places signiﬁcant emphasis on the advancement and safeguarding of human rights. Moreover, the dynamics of the balance of power system have shifted from solely relying on military power to a more nuanced combination of economic and mili- tary factors. In this new paradigm, economic inﬂuence often carries more weight than military might. Furthermore, various economic tools such as trade policies, sanctions, cyber-attacks, and foreign aid are also employed S. Ambreen (B) Milli Al-Ameen College, University of Calcutta, Kolkata, India e-mail: suﬁsaba75@gmail.com © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_11 191

192 S. AMBREEN to achieve geopolitical objectives. While the Universal Declaration of Human Rights (UDHR) has contributed to establishing the foundation for human security paradigm, it took over ﬁve decades for human security to emerge as a clearly deﬁned alternative to the state security model. A Historical Perspective on Security In 1982, the Independent Commission on Disarmament and Security Issues led by Swedish politician Olof Palme introduced the concept of ‘common security’ as a means to explore different perspectives on peace and security. Later on, there was a transition in the concept of security, shifting from a narrow focus on military threats and national security to a more expansive and contextual aspect. It was acknowledged that although the industrialised world was primarily concerned with military issues, the security of third world countries was jeopardised by poverty, hardship, and economic disparity. Hence, it was imperative for the developed world, namely, to address these non-military security concerns in a deliberate and purposeful manner. The approach towards human security became apparent with the publication of the 1994 Human Development Report: New Dimensions of Human Security by the United Nations Development Programme (UNDP) (Timothy 2004). Expanding upon the theoretical advancements in security throughout the 1980s, the Report highlighted the fact that social and economic uncertainty posed a risk to global stability. It opened with the statement that: For too long, nations have sought arms to protect their security. For most people today, a feeling of insecurity arises more from worries about daily life than from the dread of a cataclysmic world event. Most people instinc- tively understand what security means. It means safety from the constant threats of hunger, disease, crime and repression. It also means protec- tions from sudden and hurtful disruptions in the patterns of our daily life (Human Development Report 1994). The 1994 UNDP study showed that human security has two inter- related components: ‘freedom from fear’ (such as dangers arising from war, conﬂict, and state-sanctioned violence) and ‘freedom from want’ including avoidable illnesses, economic adversity, poverty, and develop- mental issues (Human Development Report, 1994). Despite on-going

11 INTERNATIONAL SECURITY AND IRREGULAR … 193 debates among experts and scholars on the importance of conceptual- ising human security in this manner, it is crucial to acknowledge that the freedom from fear and want are closely linked. In September 2000, Koﬁ Annan, the Secretary-General of the United Nations, reiterated the need for the international community to effectively accomplish the dual objec- tives of achieving freedom from fear and freedom from want. In response to the emerging issues, the Japanese government formed the Commis- sion on Human Security. This commission functioned as an independent body while maintaining strong afﬁliations with the United Nations. The Commission aimed to broaden the conventional understanding of security and shift the focus from the state to encompass ‘non-traditional’ threats. It posited that, Human security means protecting fundamental freedoms—freedoms that are the essence of life. It means protecting people from critical (severe) and pervasive (widespread) threats and situations. It means using processes that build on people’s strengths and aspirations. It means creating polit- ical, social, environmental, economic, military and cultural systems that together give people the building blocks of survival, livelihood and dignity (Commission on Human Security 2003). Precisely, the UNDP and the Commission approached human security from a human-centric perspective that emphasised a holistic comprehen- sion of development, rather than just focusing on the economic dimen- sions of an individual’s or community’s existence. They advocated for the creation of political systems that upheld individual rights, economic insti- tutions that generated employment opportunities, and inclusive cultures that rejected discrimination based on race, religion, gender, or any other arbitrary categorisation. As the Commission noted: Human security policies must consider the ﬁght against poverty as a major challenge for the international community. It is imperative to develop global strategies supporting growth and sustainable development while at the same time implementing policies of economic development and social protection at the national level (Human Development Report, 1994).

194 S. AMBREEN Irregular Migration as a Non-T raditional Security T hreat One of the most notable instances of non-traditional security concerns that the world has been encountering is irregular cross-border migration. It is widely known that international migration disregards national bound- aries and has direct implications for international security, peace-building, and developmental objectives (Van Der Linj 2019). Theoretically, irreg- ular migration refers to movement of people that occurs outside the rules, regulations, or international treaties that regulate arrival into or departure from the country of origin or destination. However, irregular migration is inherently a multifaceted problem. Irregular migration, from the view- point of transit or destination nations, pertains to the act of a country accepting, accommodating, or employing an individual who does not possess the necessary permits or documentation required by immigra- tion and labour laws. From the perspective of the countries of origin, irregularity occurs when an individual crosses an international border without a passport or any other legitimate travel document, or when they fail to meet the administrative conditions for leaving the country. There are situations in which migrants ﬁrst enter a country legally, but then become undocumented by exceeding their authorised stay or engaging in unauthorised employment. Certain individuals migrate into and depart from their country through unauthorised means. Others exhibit a state of being ‘semi-compliant’: they strive to adhere to national laws and regu- lations, but are unable to do so completely (Ozkul et al. 2012). Another complexity that arises due to irregular movement pertains to the fact that large migratory ﬂows are increasingly made up of people with ‘mixed motivations’ for travel. They may be pursuing economic advancement or escaping to avoid war, religious strife or lack of rights in the country of origin. In addition to being embroiled in conﬂicts, nations with poor governance and lack of services are more likely to ‘push’ migration for economic reasons too. Migrants’ individual motivations also inﬂuence their choice of destination. Migration both results from and can lead to human security prob- lems. Push factors like disruptions in the economic lives of individuals and their families, displacement due to environmental disasters, and polit- ical and religious persecution constitute threats to human security that drive migration on a mass scale. But the perception of migration as a threat to state/national security has certainly heightened in recent years,

11 INTERNATIONAL SECURITY AND IRREGULAR … 195 partly as the security agenda has become more prevalent across many aspects of policy, and partly in response to the rapid rise in the number of international migrants, especially ‘irregular’ migrants. The securitisa- tion of migration has become a prominent topic among academicians, policymakers, and international players, especially after the 9/11 terror incident, resulting in greater control of states over individuals and in a broader understanding of what constitutes a security challenge. Experts also argue that migration poses a threat to both human security and state sovereignty (Ullah et al. 2020). Consequently, countries are implementing a series of regulatory measures and stricter policies for documentation and its veriﬁcation. These measures affect the migrant populations disproportionately. The assumption behind such approach is that ﬂawed documentation systems may allow the free movement of terrorists and criminals, along with economic migrants who enter a state with legal documents. The imple- mentation of stricter border controls and more stringent visa regulations has led to unauthorised migrants adopting more clandestine methods while seeking ways to their intended destinations. However, the imple- mentation of stronger visa regulations frequently accelerates the growing trend of migrants resorting to unlawful pathways (Ahsan 2013). Security-Migration Nexus Extensive research has been conducted in recent decades, particularly in the area of security studies, resulting in the predominant perception of the migration-security nexus being seen primarily from national security perspective. The correlation between population dynamics and security studies is rapidly gaining attention, bolstered by the emergence of polit- ical demography as a distinct area of research in the ﬁelds of international relations and political science. Migration may have signiﬁcant implica- tions for several aspects, including state sovereignty, the distribution of power among nations, and the character of disputes in the international system. Moreover, the increase in human mobility has been linked to the formation of urban clusters for migrants, challenges with regard to a state’s ability to regulate entry into their territories, and disparities in the ethnic-religious makeup of populations. If immigrants are not assimilated into host communities, especially if they originate from a vastly different cultural milieu, there is an increased likelihood of religious and ethnic conﬂicts. This necessitates additional governmental initiatives to integrate

196 S. AMBREEN ethnic minorities into a national fabric. Based on this area of research, it has been identiﬁed that there can possibly be potential hazards for the society that hosts immigrants (Estevens 2019). According to a publication by the International Organization for Migration (IOM), in order to understand the integral link between migra- tion and security, it is important to focus on four things—security sector reform, Transnational Organised Crime (TOC), conﬂict prevention and stabilisation. An efﬁcient security sector enhances regional stability and encourages opportunities for international cooperation. It is comprehen- sive and reﬂective of the entire populace, encompassing migrants as well. It operates with openness and responsibility, which are core characteristics of good governance. Security sector reform can enhance the capacity of migrants to access justice and exercise their rights without facing bias or apprehension. The primary focus of security sector reform should be to emphasise the incorporation of safety and security measures for migrants, as well as ensuring for them easy access to the court system. Both elements should be analysed together in order to create cohesive and coordinated solutions. Hence, security forces and law enforcement organisations must prioritise safeguarding the rights of migrants and granting them access to inclusive judicial and legal systems. A comprehensive and migrant- aware approach to law enforcement, resulting from the restructuring of the security sector, may also aid in crime prevention and the fostering of social cohesion (IOM 2021). In contemporary times, international migration has become expensive and risky, as it deliberately restricts an individual’s legal access to the preferred destination. Moreover, the advantages of globalisation, such as enhanced and expedited communication, seamless ﬂow of ﬁnances, and unrestricted worldwide travel, have facilitated the growth, diversiﬁcation, and expansion of transnational and organised crime syndicates. The entire business of smuggling and trafﬁcking of migrants has emerged as a lucra- tive form of criminal activity as it deals with people’s illegal movement across borders in order to make a proﬁt. The smuggling of migrants is deﬁned as, The procurement, in order to obtain, directly or indirectly a ﬁnancial or other material beneﬁt, of the illegal entry of a person into a state Party of which the person is not a national or a permanent resident (Sharpe 2018).

11 INTERNATIONAL SECURITY AND IRREGULAR … 197 Unstable societies, lack of regular migration pathways and poor border management can create conducive environment for transnational crimes and the risks to which migrants are exposed (UNNM 2021). Trafﬁcking of human beings is deﬁned as, the recruitment, transportation, transfer, harbouring or receipt of persons, by means of the threat, or use of force or other forms of coercion, of abduction, of fraud, of deception, of the abuse of power or of a position of vulnerability or of the giving or receiving of payments or beneﬁts to achieve the consent of a person having control over another person, for the purpose of exploitation (UNODC 2000). Human trafﬁcking is both a cause for and consequence of viola- tions of human rights as well as global inequality, gender discrimination, marginalisation, and poverty. Migrants, especially irregular migrants are at a huge risk of becoming susceptible to human trafﬁcking, particularly in situations where labour regulations are relaxed and terrible working conditions are accepted in some areas related to domestic employment. This is exacerbated when migrants are separated from community and family support systems, lack access to lawful economic opportunities, legal recognition, and social safeguards, or engage in undocumented migration (IOM 2021). This global network of organised criminal activity poses a growing threat to national and international security, with negative consequence for public safety, health, functioning of democratic institu- tions, and economic stability throughout the globe. Criminal networks are not only expanding, but also broadening their operations, which has led to the convergence of threats that were formerly separate but are now having explosive and destabilising impacts. The thriving industry of migrant smuggling and human trafﬁcking may provide a signiﬁcant risk to law enforcement, particularly when they are connected to organised crime and interact with the transportation of illegal items, such as ﬁrearms and drugs. In this situation, the individuals who exploit migrants are the ones who are engaging in unlawful criminal activities, not the migrants themselves (IOM 2021). Armed conﬂicts frequently arise due to a multifaceted interplay of issues like inequality, weak governance, restricted economic prospects, and rivalry for resources. These factors are further intensiﬁed by the impacts of climate change. An often cited worldwide security problem is associated with the process of radicalisation and the manifestation of

198 S. AMBREEN violent extremism. Migrants may be escaping from violence instigated by violent extremist organisations in their home areas, but they may also be subjected to forceful, deceitful, or exploitative recruiting into these violent extremist organisations. Risk factors for recruitment include lack of education, unemployment, and restricted mobility. Additionally, inability to integrate into host communities owing to social isolation and marginalisation has also been cited as a contributing factor. Conﬂict prevention may address the many and diverse factors that contribute to violence and instability, which frequently lead to displacement. Preventive measures, including as demobilisation, disarmament, and reintegration (DDR), are typically most successful when implemented using a compre- hensive community-based strategy. Community policing is an additional method for preventing crime and resolving conﬂicts that should be taken into account (IOM 2021). Stabilisation endeavours might effectively avert, alleviate, and diminish the catalysts and adverse consequences of migration, including displace- ment, within vulnerable environments. The process entails efforts to provide favourable circumstances for the reinstatement of regular social, economic, and political activities. This is achieved by aiding in the restora- tion of fundamental rights and security, as well as by fostering societal unity, functional governance structures, and efﬁcient provision of liveli- hoods and services. Community-centred stabilisation involves the inclu- sion of vulnerable and marginalised groups, such as internally displaced persons (IDPs), refugees, former combatants, women, and youth. It also engages local communities, local authorities, and members of civil society in the process of making decisions. The objective of stabilisation is to provide a fundamental degree of stability that serves as a foundation for sustainable development and the establishment of security. Recon- struction, such as the restoration of power and water, is a signiﬁcant component of stabilisation endeavours, in which migrants may have a pivotal impact. Transnational networks can enable the ﬂow of ﬁnan- cial remittances, new skills, and capacities to boost rebuilding efforts. Additionally, displaced individuals and returnees can take the lead in reha- bilitating infrastructure in their communities. Furthermore, adopting a comprehensive, community-centred strategy for stabilisation can enhance social unity by uniting communities in the pursuit of common goals and aid in the prevention and reduction of conﬂicts, particularly those that arise from or contribute to violent extremism (IOM 2021).

11 INTERNATIONAL SECURITY AND IRREGULAR … 199 Irregular Migration and State Security The provisions of the international refugee law prevent states from deporting any individual with valid asylum claims by providing the right to seek and enjoy asylum from persecution. The same refugee law in all its treaties and legal documents has also put prime emphasis on protecting ‘state sovereignty’. The majority who support advancing refugee rights appear to believe that international refugee law (underpinned by the 1951 Refugee Convention) at least provides a break from states’ arbitrary treat- ment of asylum seekers and thus calls for a solid foundation for extending rights and protection to those seeking refuge across borders. However, Simon Behrman in his seminal work also makes a valid point by arguing that, International refugee law has evolved as a means of control over the refugee. The ﬁrst principles on which it has been built place the rights of the state above those of the refugee. Insofar as there is such a thing as a ‘right of asylum’, it is a right vested in the state rather than the refugee. For most people forcibly displaced around the world, refugee law is at best an irrelevance and at worst a barrier to protection (Berhman 2019). Historical evidence demonstrates that migration had a signiﬁcant role in driving worldwide socio-political changes during the twentieth century, alongside the two world wars, advancements in technology, and restruc- turing of the global economy. It has been a matter of security concern since the 1980s. Currently, international migration is a highly debated and controversial topic in the realm of security. The public discourse surrounding the nexus between migration and security mostly concen- trates around issues that involve safeguarding national security and the welfare of the citizens, and where irregular migration is widely debated as and is pre-conceived to be a signiﬁcant threat to the security of a nation as well as its citizens. Threat is an antithesis to security. It ‘induces anxiety and fear of varying intensity, ranging from fright or petriﬁcation to an impulse or action to counteract’ (Koser 2011). According to Khalid Koser, the notion that migration poses a threat to national security has intensiﬁed due to the rise in the number of ‘irregular’ migrants (Koser 2005). However, the manner in which these migrants can become a threat to national security has not been substantiated by well- grounded research. Other terms are often used in political and media

200 S. AMBREEN discourses, such as ‘illegal’, ‘undocumented’, ‘unauthorised’, or ‘clandes- tine’ migration to bolster the argument. Public perceptions of irregular migration have also become highly politicised. Such politicisation has caused the media and right-wing politicians to portray ‘uncontrolled migration’ as a threat to security. There are two common misconceptions regarding irregular migrants: ﬁrst, that they engage in unlawful activities and second, they are linked to the transmission of infectious diseases, notably HIV/AIDS. Both of these assumptions are broad generalisations. Within this context, it becomes important to shed some light on the manifestation of irreg- ular migrants as a major security threat during COVID-19. Nations which earlier had open border policies were forced to shut their doors to immigrants and ramp up vigilance in fear of spread of coronavirus. The worldwide lockdown and other travel restrictions had aggravated the global migration crisis. Unskilled and low-paid migrant workers lost their jobs as repercussions to the lockdown. Moreover, inability to maintain social distancing, lack of proper access to healthcare facilities, xenophobia, limited knowledge and awareness of the rules and regulations, all these took a toll on the overall well-being of migrants, particularly undocu- mented migrants. Even the principle of non-refoulement 1 was abrogated during the pandemic. Another beﬁtting example of side-stepping individual concerns for the sake of national security would be the case of Europe. The Euro- pean Union established the Frontex agency in 2005 to coordinate with member states and undertake risk studies in the ﬁeld of immigration control, amidst a hostile atmosphere of ‘defending Europe’. Over time, its responsibilities expanded to encompass the coordination of coast guard activities and addressing the routes for migration. The current EU system for managing and controlling migration, which has developed over the last four decades, is currently referred to as the ‘Area of Freedom, Security, and Justice’ (Koser 2005). The more recent policy of ‘externalisation’ also depicts a clear example of policies that aim to disregard human as well as national security concerns. The policy emerged as a key strategy in the European Union’s 1 Article 33(1) forbids nations from repatriating persons to a country where they would face a signiﬁcant danger of abuse of human rights. This principle ensures that individuals should not be sent back to a nation where they would be subjected to torture, cruel, inhuman, or humiliating treatment or punishment, as well as other irreversible harm.

11 INTERNATIONAL SECURITY AND IRREGULAR … 201 approach to managing irregular migration ﬂows. This strategy involves the delegation of migration management and border control responsibilities to regions and countries outside the EU. By outsourcing them, including the processing of refugees and asylum seekers, the EU aims to prevent irregular arrivals and maintain control over its borders. Externalisation has become a central component of the EU’s overall response to the chal- lenges posed by migration ﬂows. The implementation of certain measures has the potential to enhance the capacity for protection in both transit countries and countries of ﬁrst arrival. However, it has been observed that the implementation of the EU’s externalisation policy frequently results in the infringement upon the rights of the individuals. The phenomenon under consideration pertains to the obstruction of an individual’s funda- mental entitlement to freely exit a nation, be it their own or a foreign country. This obstruction often results in individuals becoming embroiled in circumstances characterised by abuse and exploitation. The practice in question undermines the fundamental right to seek asylum, as it compels individuals to pursue protection in nations where asylum systems are not adequately operational. It aggravates instances of human rights viola- tions that serve as a catalyst for migration rather than alleviating them. This exacerbation occurs through the provision of assistance to secu- rity or border forces that engage in abusive practices, as well as through the suppression of human rights diplomacy with third countries in the pursuit of collaborative efforts on migration-related matters (Human Rights Watch 2018). The EU member states have curtailed or eliminated legal migration pathways, particularly for low-income labourers, as part of their efforts to enhance security measures and safeguard their citizens. The decrease in authorised channels for migration, coupled with the on-going need for foreign labourers, has resulted in adverse outcomes. Initially, it resulted in an increase in irregular migration which involves the use of clan- destine migratory routes, hence promoting smuggling and occasionally leading to human trafﬁcking. This has caused fear over the inﬁltration of undesirable migrants and has led to a cycle of distrust between migrants and the European political community. Furthermore, it has established a clear division between ‘desirable migrants’ and ‘undesirable migrants’ that required the implementation of a system capable of distinguishing between them (Bilgic et al 2020). The current trend with regard to policy formulation and policy imple- mentation undoubtedly follows protecting state/national sovereignty and

202 S. AMBREEN the host population from apprehended immigration fallouts. The miscon- ception that migrants pose a danger to state sovereignty or security may be rectiﬁed by doing thorough and unbiased study and presenting the existing information. This should be accompanied by constructive conversation between governments, the media, and the general public. Few arguments in this regard need to be bolstered with more emphasis. Initially, numerical values hold signiﬁcance. The notion that irregular migration poses a challenge to state sovereignty and acts as a pressure on the national economy is based on the belief that governments are, or may be, overburdened by a large inﬂux of irregular migrants. The political importance of irregular migration often outweighs its numerical impor- tance. Furthermore, irregular migrants are frequently attributed with questionable motives without any supporting evidence (Koser 2005). Irregular Migration and Human Security The notion of human security redirects political and analytical focus from nation-states to the individuals, the primary subjects whose secu- rity should be given priority. Human security integrates the concepts of security, development, and human rights. This human-centric ideology presents an alternative strategy to address large-scale irregular migra- tion, necessitating a comprehensive intervention and collaboration among nations, global organisations, and non-governmental organisa- tions. Nevertheless, the lack of a well-deﬁned structure makes the concept of human security somewhat ambiguous, necessitating a deeper compre- hension of its current meaning in a vastly transformed global landscape compared to the late twentieth century, when the concept emerged with remarkable vigour in the ﬁeld of international relations. Furthermore, there is a need for additional advancements in operational tools and realistic action plans to ensure the effective execution of human security. One aspect that is commonly neglected in conversations about irreg- ular migration is the adverse effect it has on migrants themselves. Typi- cally, those who fall victim to trafﬁcking, particularly women and children, are frequently forced to exploitation in domestic work or the commercial sex industry. Some scholars have drawn comparisons between the contem- porary issue of human trafﬁcking and past occurrences of the slave trade due to the signiﬁcant level of human rights abuses involved. A criticism of the UN Protocols on Smuggling and Trafﬁcking is that they place greater emphasis on drawing attention to the human rights violations associated

11 INTERNATIONAL SECURITY AND IRREGULAR … 203 with trafﬁcking, while minimising the possible instances of abuse that may arise from migrant smuggling (Koser 2001). Studies speciﬁcally focused on the illicit transportation of asylum seekers have revealed that smug- gling can also subject migrants to many forms of economic, social, and political susceptibility. Migrants are not only at risk due to their irregular movement, but also because of their undocumented status. Irregular migrants frequently engage in unstable and hazardous employment, lacking access to health- care, education, and other social welfare beneﬁts. Additionally, they are vulnerable to exploitation within the housing market. In his work, Ramesh Thakur contends that a signiﬁcant number of individuals perceive security concerns as originating mostly from internal disputes, sickness, starvation, environmental degradation, street crime, and interpersonal violence (Thakur 2003). The acknowledgement of these variables forms the basis of the core principles of the human security approach, namely the concepts of ‘freedom from want’ and ‘freedom from fear’. When exam- ining large-scale migration from a human security standpoint, it involves analysing the social, political, and economic variables that serve as push factors, as well as assessing the circumstances of migrants in their new places of residence (Mawadza 2008). Mass irregular migration may be effectively managed and controlled by prioritising the enhancement of human security, since it is a signif- icant push factor that drives movement. Most political actors should recognise that individuals affected by civil wars and conﬂict, political/ ethnic/religious persecution, economic deprivation, famine, or migration under extremely poor health conditions are experiencing human inse- curity. The well-being and progress of individuals are often jeopardised when they migrate or reside irregularly inside a host or transit nation. The prevailing mental representation of mass irregular migration now vividly illustrates the connection between human insecurity and both the origins and consequences of such movement. If irregular migration is driven by human insecurity in various regions, it is crucial to consider the safety of migrants who encounter numerous inhumane conditions in detention centres, refugee camps, or when they fall victim to crim- inal networks that smuggle migrants and trafﬁc humans. An approach to human security which is grounded in various forms of social justice would view national security and human security as interdependent. Upon irreg- ular arrival in any nation, migrants are often associated with instances of illegal employment, exploitation in labour, engagement in prostitution,

204 S. AMBREEN and participation in networks involving human organ trafﬁcking. Human insecurity arises not just from the nations of origin or the illegal migration journey, but also from the circumstances that many immigrants encounter inside host cultures. Referring to the pandemic period once again, irregular migrants were already suffering from a myriad of difﬁculties before the onset of the pandemic. From being exploited at the hands of the ruthless smugglers to staying in overcrowded detention camps, the migrants easily became victims of various kinds of diseases and mental illness. Lack of food and nutrition, source of income, proper and safe place to reside, and an overall uncertainty about their future, all these factors caused high levels of anxiety among the migrants. An added misery in the form of a deadly and highly contagious virus simply led to a crisis within a crisis. Adam Coutts, a public health specialist at Cambridge University mentioned speciﬁcally about refugees being more vulnerable to COVID-19 or any other diseases due to factors such as ‘high geographical mobility, instability, living in overcrowded conditions, lack of sanitation and WASH (waters, sanita- tion and hygiene) facilities, and lack of access to decent healthcare or vaccination programmes in host communities’ (Reidy 2020). The fear of deportation too made undocumented migrants and asylum seekers reluc- tant to share vital information about their health, and even ask for basic medical assistance during the pandemic. Furthermore, strict lockdown prevented many migrants, including migrant workers from reuniting with their families. Labelling any problem as a security concern carries substantial conse- quences in terms of the laws, norms, policies, and procedures that are deemed appropriate in response. Within the realm of migration, the term ‘label’ has been employed to rationalise heightened levels of monitoring, conﬁnement, expulsion, and laws that impose stricter limitations. These responses can have adverse effects on the migrants, such as restricting asylum seekers from reaching secure locations, forcing migrants to rely on human smugglers and trafﬁckers, and fuelling an increasing anti- immigrant sentiment among the general public, the media, and in political discussions in several countries. Typical reactions to this include the notion that migration can serve as a means of importing terrorists and criminals, or as a way of disseminating contagious illnesses. These impres- sions are highly deceptive, yet they are widely held. There is scarce

11 INTERNATIONAL SECURITY AND IRREGULAR … 205 global data to suggest that migrant communities have a higher propor- tion of terrorists, prospective terrorists, or criminals when compared to indigenous populations (Koser 2011). When analysed through a different perspective, irregular migration can be seen as a threat to national sovereignty, as every state has the lawful authority to regulate the entry and stay of individuals within its bound- aries. It is noteworthy that the majority of irregular migrants globally have not entered a country without permission, but instead choose to stay or work without permit. However, the governments are judged quite strictly on the basis of their ability to handle migration. Public perception of a government’s handling of migrants is an important contributing factor in the responses of the governments in this regard. Irregular migration presents genuine challenges for nations, but migrants are also exposed to danger and vulnerability. The majority of nations have, however, been unsuccessful in efﬁciently managing or controlling irregular migration. Hence, there is a need for novel, efﬁcient, and consistent strategies to tackle the problem of irregular migration, which take into account the concerns of governments and the imperative to safeguard the rights of irregular migrants. Simultaneously, it is crucial to maintain a realistic perspective about expectations. Irregular migration is expected to persist in the foreseeable future, but it may be feasible to diminish its magnitude. One factor is the inﬂuential variables that shape the extent of interna- tional migration, such as the increasing differences in income and human security across various civilisations. However, the capacity to alter these forces is severely restricted (Crisp and Dessalegne 2002). Furthermore, despite the existence of some progress towards liberalisation in some regions, such as addressing the demographic deﬁcit, there will not be a signiﬁcant expansion of regular migration pathways to meet the overall need for movement. We must also recognise the restricted potential for innovative and revolutionary methods. Nations possess the authority to govern their borders, and in speciﬁc regions of the globe, decisions about irregular migration are inﬂuenced by the belief (regardless of its accu- racy) that countries face the possibility of being excessively burdened by a signiﬁcant inﬂux of illegal migrants, who are perceived as posing a threat to both the nation and its society. It is quite improbable that states will completely remove the control mechanisms they have implemented. However, nations are becoming more open to recognising that existing policy methods have achieved very limited success, and many are prepared to at least contemplate newer possibilities.

206 S. AMBREEN Concluding Observations The phrase ‘permacrisis’ chosen as the word of the year 2022 by Collins Dictionary, accurately describes the present global situation. Currently, the world is grappled in numerous crisis situations that appear to lack easy or durable solutions. Some notable examples are the Russia-Ukraine war, Israel-Palestine conﬂict, the Syrian conﬂict, the recent pandemic, and the on-going migrant issue. While recent policy measures have partially reduced the adverse effects of migration, the overall situation involving cross-border irregular migration continues to be a manifestation of prej- udice and a potential danger to national security. Even in case of refugees who are recognised by international law and protected under the 1951 Refugee Convention, Protracted Refuge Situations (PRS) depict a sign of political failures, carelessness, and poor resource allocation. Refugees in these situations are frequently locked in limbo: although it is not safe for them to return home, they have also not been granted perma- nent residency to stay in another nation. This situation contemplates an important fact—that the global refugee regime has basically been unsuc- cessful in providing concrete solutions to the refugee problem and to assist displaced persons regain a degree of normalcy and rebuild their lives. Iran and Pakistan, for example, are ‘one of the longest protracted situa- tions as they have hosted approximately 2.4 million Afghan refugees for the past 40 years’ (UNHCR 2020). PRS has been a daunting factor for nations as well as for organisa- tions dealing with refugees. It also highlights the challenges that the UNHCR has been facing in providing solutions to the on-going refugee crisis. The circumstances in the country of origin, the asylum coun- try’s governmental responses, and a variety of other actors’ inadequate engagement—all contribute to long-term displacement. Refugees and IDPs are unable to return home since the problems in their country of origin is never being addressed. Failure to communicate with the host nation only serves to promote the notion that refugees are an incon- venience and a security risk, which encourages encampments or seeking refuge in already crowded urban areas, preventing the development of regional solutions. Therefore, the problem of refugee and irregular migra- tion (including all their negative consequences) very aptly portray the situation of permacrisis. While irregular migration is a perceived threat to national and human security, it will not be wise to undermine its positive aspect. Through

11 INTERNATIONAL SECURITY AND IRREGULAR … 207 several policy measures, it is pushing states towards cooperation, coor- dination and exchange of information, especially at the regional level. These processes can contribute towards regional conﬁdence building and stability over time, which can in turn contribute towards stronger and a more balanced international system. Through the implementation of international cooperation, the destabilising implications of international migration must be addressed so that movements of persons across borders will no longer be a source of insecurity but rather a factor contributing to harmonious inter-state relations and to the development of the countries of origin, transit, and destination in all aspects. CHAPTER 12 International Management of Complex Humanitarian Emergencies: Challenges Ahead Omprakash Mishra and Amartya Ray Nothing better represents the multiplicity of crises facing the world on a permanent basis past about four decades than the phenomenon of ‘complex humanitarian emergency’ involving state failure and the imper- ative of reaching necessary assistance and relief to millions of civilians trapped in ever-increasing number of armed conﬂict in different parts of the world. Even while the state borders have become fragile, armed conﬂict of different hues involving incessant disorder in the domestic sphere with concomitant spill-over in the neighbouring countries have imperilled the life and security of an unprecedented number of human O. Mishra (B) Jadavpur University, Kolkata, India e-mail: professor.mishra@gmail.com A. Ray Thakur Panchanan Mahila Mahavidyalaya, Coochbehar Panchanan Barma University , Cooch Behar, India © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_12 209

210 O. MISHRA AND A. RAY beings, forced out of the country or forcibly displaced within their country. Rising expectations fuelled by the forces of globalisation and propelled by increased awareness of the interdependent effects of mass human displacement and consequences thereof have necessitated an inter- national response system in the state-centric Westphalian political order in the world. Even while the state-centric political order rules the world, there are unmistakable signs of the internationalisation of the prob- lems and crises facing the nation-states. Moreover, there is an increasing demand for an appropriate response mechanism to address the adverse effects of such problems. The various attempts to reﬂect the international concern towards the violent conﬂict raging in different parts of the world and the urgent ameliorative measures needed to contain the consequent humanitarian emergency have necessitated an increasingly elaborate and institutionalised system. Centrally, at the global level various arrangements spawned by the United Nations (UN) system, the International Red Cross Committee (ICRC) and some of the prominent International Non- Government Organisations (INGOs) are in the forefront of addressing the challenges involved. State Failure and Violent Conflicts Volatile international politics, and as a result transition in socio-political landscapes within the boundaries of nation-states, tend to create varying degrees of disquiet and tension which may give rise to violent conﬂicts. This was the situation around the globe from the mid-1980s when the end of the Cold War was imminent and change in not only governments but also in models of government was seen across continents. Accord- ingly, this phase came to be dominated with intra-state conﬂicts among groups with different claimed identities who were ﬁghting to take hold of the fragile state machinery. The number of such conﬂicts averaged at 20–25 per year till the early part of 1980s, but eventually it rose to 60– 65 per year, as per estimates by various studies conducted and this trend continued till the middle of 1990s (Klugman1999). Conﬂicts of this kind were, naturally, mostly armed and consequently these resulted in human catastrophes in terms of hunger, dislocation, homelessness, physical abuse and sometimes death. According to estimates of the International Red Cross Society, the number of vulnerable persons, due to such conﬂicts, rose dramatically during that period, adding almost ten million people to the count annually (Klugman 1999). The world was used to see wars

12 INTERNATIONAL MANAGEMENT OF COMPLEX … 211 between or among states and there were some, at least on paper, prin- ciples related to such wars. States tend to oblige to some norms and methods during their armed conﬂict with another. But this new kind of wars, mostly waged by a state against its own population or waged inside the national boundaries by factions of the population creating a humanitarian disaster had remained outside of legal, moral or theoret- ical frameworks except for the provisions of Common Article 3 in the Geneva Conventions, 1949. Thus, there emerged a need to look into these conﬂicts with a new lens, something different than what we used in conventional warfare, and set some principles to mitigate and regu- late them. Also, there was a need to deﬁne these new wars, without which it was not possible to analyse, interpret and explain them, averting humanitarian crises resulting from such wars. D e ﬁ n i n gt h e s e n e ww a r si st r i c k i e rt h a na si ts e e m st ob e , b e c a u s e conﬂicts are of so many varieties, encompassing a range of issues like ethnic, religious, political and social or a combination of them, or some- times simply avenging an old wrong is the motive. And in almost all the cases, the brunt of these conﬂicts is primarily and heavily borne by non-combatant civilians. Therefore, these conﬂicts are less of a war in conventional sense but more of a humanitarian crisis; and the extremely complex nature of these crises, due to intersection of a lot of issues and causes, these are termed as Complex Humanitarian Emergencies (CHE) . Simply put, the term ‘complex humanitarian emergency’ has been adopted by the United Nations to deﬁne conditions where armed conﬂict and the absence of an effective government and law and order have culminated in human disasters, symbolised by intense social tension and disruption. These emergencies generally crop up in places where the state institutions are either underdeveloped or have been worn out over time. Sometimes, relatively stronger states also fall prey to such emergen- cies if it gets overwhelmed by the extent, expanse and magnitude of the conﬂict. Restoration and maintenance of governance is the most impor- tant prerequisite to avert or at least mitigate such human tragedies and for that, sometimes, outside action in the form of intervention becomes necessary.

212 O. MISHRA AND A. RAY CHE: Meaning, Evolution and Characteristics Before starting the discussion, it needs to be remembered that human- itarian emergencies arising out of political conﬂicts are not a new phenomenon discovered in the late 1980s or early 1990s. Small-scale, intra-state violence perpetuated with the aim of capturing the state apparatus was pretty commonplace since the advent of the modern West- phalian state system. Also, political differences taking an armed dimension during the Cold War period was quite common where two superpowers or their regional partners took sides to champion their political and strategic agenda. These conﬂicts were often termed as Low-Intensity Conﬂicts (LICs) which took in its fold a range of violent instruments starting from civil wars to military coups. There had been other conceptual build-ups as well to deﬁne and describe conﬂicts limited to within the boundaries of a state and where civilians were the primary target, like new wars, etc. But none of those ideas or concepts could precisely represent what was happening from the 1980s in different parts of the world, in terms of its human dimension. These new conﬂicts were much different from the old ones; here warring factions were focusing on inﬂicting damages to the non-combatant general population and civilian infrastructure rather than the military assets of each other; and main purpose of this tactic was to spread fear and insecurity among the population as it was assumed that that would be less expensive but more effective in gaining control over territories. As a result of this territorial aspect of the conﬂict, naturally, these produced large numbers of Internally Displaced Persons (IDPs) and refugees in and around the conﬂict zone. This shifting focus to civilians and emphasis on territory in place of each other’s forces and assets called for a new conceptual framework which had to be broader than the existing ones and at the same time it had to be human-centric and this was the crossroads where the concept of the Complex Humanitarian Emergency emerged. The term originated in the 1980s and it was ﬁrst used to describe the upheavals in Mozambique. During this time international humanitarian organisations realised the needs of aid and assistance which were trig- gered by the ongoing internal armed conﬂict (OECD 1999). The term was later adopted by various United Nations agencies and also people in academia since the early 1990s. Under Secretary General and Emergency Relief Coordinator (ERC) of the UN explained in his brieﬁng to the UN Security Council in 1999 that ‘contemporary armed conﬂicts are rarely

12 INTERNATIONAL MANAGEMENT OF COMPLEX … 213 conducted on a clearly deﬁned battleﬁeld, by conventional armies that confront one another. Instead warfare nowadays are regularly conducted within cities and villages, where civilians are the preferred targets, terror is spread intentionally, and the total physical eradication or displacement of certain classes of the population is the principal strategy’ (OCHA 1999). These conﬂicts are marked by rampant violation of human rights as well as humanitarian laws, resulting in mutilation, rape, forced starvation, attacks on medical facilities, etc. which were hitherto unforeseen in conventional wars. These violations, in fact, have become a part of the strategy of each side. This is why, these days, even local conﬂicts lead to tremendous human sufferings and call for international attention. A CHE generally involves a multiplicity of actors from different origins, with varied motives and speciﬁc instruments of choice for engagement, thus making the situation quite complicated. Mary Kaldor had identiﬁed ﬁve different types of actors: ﬁrstly, the regular armed forces; secondly, paramilitary groups; thirdly, self-defence units; fourthly, foreign merce- naries and lastly, the regular troops with the backing of international humanitarian regime (Kaldor 2007). We may add local mercenaries to this list as well. Moreover, though it bears the word ‘emergency’ in its nomenclature, the complex emergencies are more often than not actually long-lasting. It is therefore important to keep in mind that these situa- tions, as it is frequently depicted by the media or the policymakers, are not a series of unforeseen events leading towards a human catastrophe but a combination of causes that were brewing to become a threat to humanity over a long course of time. The situation is more complex in these cases as international treaties, covenants or the UN principles and directives do not apply to such developments and scope of international community’s response is also very restricted. Presently, complex emergencies essentially mark the most deﬁnitive way of state disruption. The most basic characteristic of such conﬂicts is the destroying of citizen’s ability to protect their life and sustain liveli- hood. High degree of violence leads to a situation where just saving life for another day becomes the primary goal of the affected popula- tion. As constitutional arrangements break down, the warring factions gather more strength and momentum. Generally, this phase of dimin- ishing government control is preceded by strong social strife manifested in the form of rampant corruption in the institutions of the state and criminalisation of politics. Usually an emergency-like situation builds up

214 O. MISHRA AND A. RAY with activities at three levels, namely—international, national and commu- nity levels. It is also necessary to take note that an emergency may or may not engulf the whole of a nation. For example, in African states like Sudan, the conﬂict zone was nation-wide and eventually South Sudan was carved out of it. But in the case of Myanmar, the conﬂict zone is mainly restricted to Rakhine province; or as in the case of Sri Lanka, it was the north-eastern Jaffna peninsula. In some cases, emergencies were brought under control with political or military intervention from inside or out, quite quickly, like that in Eastern Europe or in the Balkans in the 1990s, whereas some conﬂicts continue with tremendous human suffering and violation of basic human rights, like in Syria, Afghanistan and Myanmar. To be precise, the following characteristics may be attributed to a CHE: 1. It is essentially intra-state or domestic affair, not inter-state, 2. Though it is an intra-state affair, it has spiralling effect in the neighbourhood, 3. It is very difﬁcult to separate combatants and civilians, 4. Civilians and civil structures are primary target of violence, 5. Illegal, shadow economy grows with this kind of emergencies and 6. It generally has several actors of different hues with often overlap- ping interests. Humanitarianism and Government Though there is no strictly agreed-upon deﬁnition of humanitarianism, it broadly refers to dedication and belief in the fundamental value of human life—this central ethical consideration is accepted worldwide. This term also makes a more speciﬁc reference to the crisis response that has evolved from the founding of the International Red Cross Society and the ﬁrst Geneva Convention over 150 years ago. As a systemic response to crisis, humanitarianism involves the needs of affected people trapped in a conﬂict (PHAP 2021). When a conﬂict occurs, need of security and humanitarian aid heightens. Victims of the conﬂict require both; but the government has to make a choice; and more frequently security takes priority over aid. A government which is under threat to its authority, primary objective is to bring the situation under control and restore law and order in the

12 INTERNATIONAL MANAGEMENT OF COMPLEX … 215 conﬂict zone. But the other objective of rebuilding the destroyed phys- ical and institutional structures and addressing the urgent need of aid in the form of food, medicine, etc. cannot be overlooked either. Thus, the affected government’s ability is stretched to limits and it tends to prioritise while at the same time looking for international assistance. Internationalisation of Humanitarian Crises Humanitarian issues dominating the discourse of international politics is nothing new. In the ﬁrst few decades of the twentieth century, these issues played an important role in shaping and reshaping of opinion of the masses on wars, especially the two world wars. In the following decades humanitarian concerns largely dominated the discussions on the Vietnam War, the Spanish civil war or the liberation movement of Bangladesh and so on. However, the way and in what frequency these concerns related to humanitarianism and human rights laws came to limelight during various crises of the 1980s and 1990s, were unprecedented (ICRC 1999). The UN General Assembly’s Resolution 46/182 established the Guiding Principles for the world community’s response to humanitarian crises and this was also instrumental in the establishment of the Ofﬁce of the Emergency Relief Coordinator (ERC) and the Inter-Agency Standing Committee (ISAC). The ISAC takes within its fold various important humanitarian players both within and outside the ambit of the UN and it principally focuses on data sharing, analysis and collective decision-making with regard to an emerging humanitarian crisis. The primary responsibility for the maintenance of international peace and security lies with UN Security Council under Article 24 of the UN Charter and it has the authority to determine whether a situation poses threat to international peace and security under Article 39, Chapter 7. The Council may opt for coercive action in the form of collective use of force (Article 42) or the imposition of sanctions (Article 41). Moreover, though it has not been formally mentioned in the Charter, the Council is authorised to deploy ‘robust’ Peacekeeping Operations, the mandate of which under Chapter 7 includes use of force. In last half of the previous century, the Security Council following its given mandate has deployed peacekeeping operations or sometimes carried out military intervention to mitigate humanitarian crises in different parts of the globe. However, it has been frequently criticised for being selective, and sometimes prejudiced, in its approach. It has

216 O. MISHRA AND A. RAY remained highly sensitive to conﬂicts involving European powers or situ- ations developing on European soil, whereas its approach to situations in Africa, Latin America or Asia has often been marked by either haste or overdoing or simply gross negligence. For instance, the world community has remained kind of spectator in Russia’s aggression in Ukraine twice in recent times; on the contrary it is charged with unnecessary and avoidable damages in Libya. In the case of Afghanistan, the Western mission left the scene without erecting an effective ﬁrewall against the very menace they fought and dislodge from power, leaving millions of Afghans to fend for themselves. It is argued that decisions regarding intervention or to be remaining on the side-lines, or sometimes leaving a situation half-baked, in a conﬂict is mainly determined by the powerful few and their perceived interests. Consequently, the term ‘world community’ is reduced to a set of few powerful states, especially the veto-yielding permanent ﬁve. In any case, internationalisation of crisis management has become the norm of the day nonetheless. It is argued that this new develop- ment, i.e. internationalisation of humanitarian emergencies has basically stemmed from the current discourse of international interdependence. It is presumed that walking alone in international politics was never possible but it has become even more impossible in this age of interconnected- ness due to technological advancement and globalisation as such in terms of ﬁnancial, social and cultural inﬂuence. Maddison points out, ‘Inter- national trade is taken to be an indicator of interdependence and its high and, with some interruptions, rapidly growing values are accepted as evidence of the increasing interdependence of nations. For instance, between 1820 and 1992, world population increased ﬁvefold, income per head eightfold, world income 40-fold, and world trade 540-fold’ (Maddison 2003). CHE and Displacement Fatalities as a consequence of a complex humanitarian crisis are mostly indirect in the sense that more people go through long-term suffering not as a direct result of the ensuing war but as displaced people living without almost no human facilities and protection, as IDPs (Internally Displaced Persons) or as refugees. Since the 1990s, the vulnerability and helpless- ness of the people who are displaced within the state boundaries have engaged both the academia and the policy community. Persistent effort from some international humanitarian agencies, human rights advocacy

12 INTERNATIONAL MANAGEMENT OF COMPLEX … 217 groups or INGOs has been able to make the voices of these people heard and also helped in developing a framework much needed for protection and dignity of human lives entangled in such internal conﬂicts. If people escaping a conﬂict are denied access to neighbouring country, they would deﬁnitely try to settle down in other parts of their own country which they consider safer. Thus, in most of the modern conﬂicts, we can see ﬂow of both IDPs and refugees—having different needs and concerns. But the trend indicates that the IDPs are outnumbering refugees in conﬂict zones in recent intra-state or civil wars. As per the records of the UNHCR, the number of IDPs stood at 62.5 million, whereas the number of refugees was 34.6 million during the middle of the current year (UNHCR 2023). These disturbing numbers are mainly due to excessive mushrooming of ANSAs (Armed Non-State Actors) in conﬂict zones across the globe; as they do not care to adhere to the norms and principles laid down in international law with regard to the conduct of war and violate human rights rampantly too. Internal conﬂicts and thereby internal displacements are not easily resolved. Protracted displacement is a reality for most of the IDPs. Afghanistan, Syria, DR Congo, Yemen, Ethiopia, etc. are some striking examples of such long-term displacement, having been there for decades. A systematic approach for understanding the critical situation of the IDPs and their needs began with the appointment of Francis M. Deng as the Special Representative of the UN Secretary General on IDPs. The Special Representative and his team were assigned the task of drafting a document which could be used as the ‘Guiding Principles on Internal Displacement’ aiming at the international response and national action for the IDPs. It was believed that international human rights laws (IHRL), international humanitarian laws (IHL) and international refugee laws were insufﬁcient in providing a safety net to the IDPs; and the Guiding Principles was basically a comprehensive document which consisted of both but went further ahead in its scope. This was necessary and eventu- ally became instrumental in protection of rights of the IDPs. For refugees, there has been a legal and ethical framework in place since the World War II with speciﬁc reference to their rights and safety; but this idea of IDP is a product of the new age intra-state wars or internal violent conﬂicts and that is why it requires a new set of guidelines. In this way, the Guiding Principles on Internal Displacement represents a milestone in the evolu- tion and norm-setting for the protection of internally displaced persons

218 O. MISHRA AND A. RAY around the world. The 30 principles included cover almost each and every aspect of assistance and protection for the displaced people. International Response to Humanitarian Emergencies Till a few decades ago, international involvement in internal crisis of a state used to be limited to humanitarian aid and assistance in recon- struction. But the number of crises and consequent devastation of such intra-state conﬂicts went beyond all previous parameters from the early 1980s. Conceptions of human rights and humanitarian assistance were also undergoing change during the same time and internationally it was generally accepted that certain rights of humans are inviolable under whatsoever circumstances. This caught the international community in a moral dilemma as to whether intervene in such a crisis or not. On strict legal terms, it was difﬁcult to establish logic for encroaching upon a state’s sovereign sphere; at the same time, it was impossible to sit quietly in the face of immense human suffering and brutality. The UN Secu- rity Council Resolution 688 of 5th April, 1991 was a watershed moment in this debate. The US, the UK and France led a hitherto unforeseen relief operation to protect the minority Kurds facing state violence from Saddam Hussein’s Iraq. This is probably the decisive moment when the ongoing debate over meaning and scope of sovereignty was put to rest. In the post-Cold War world, sovereignty was more frequently projected as a responsibility of state than its absolute rights over its citizens. Francis M. Deng and his committee’s submission of sovereignty as responsibility had found traction both in international legal and moral parlance. The traditional notion of sovereignty rests heavily on the criterion of popular support, as they say; therefore, if that support from its own people is withdrawn, then popular sovereignty loses its ground and the interna- tional community is free to act to protect the citizens from the state they belong to. It is a fact that standards of accepted behaviour of states have under- gone change over time. Genocide, ethnic cleansing, etc. provoke a very different and sharp reaction around the world than it was used to generate 3–4 decades ago. Due to advancement in communication technology and its affordability, people are more aware of what is happening around them and their reactions to events have changed accordingly. In this changed backdrop, government’s reaction and management of a crisis have seen

12 INTERNATIONAL MANAGEMENT OF COMPLEX … 219 major changes too. Governments today are expected to be both respon- sible and responsive. Globalisation and its forces have increased outside scrutiny manifold over how a government maintains its relation with its citizens. International Commission on Intervention and State Sovereignty (ICISS), a Canada-backed project, proposed some deﬁnitive solutions to the debate on intervention in its report published in 2001. The very title of the report, ‘Responsibility to Protect’ (R2P), is signiﬁcant and instructive of what it may contain. This report became the basis of the agreement reached by world leaders in the World Summit Outcome held in 2005 to address the issues of atrocity crimes of genocide, war crimes, ethnic cleansing and war against humanity. The leaders agreed that these particular four types of grave crimes which result in complex humani- tarian emergencies may be and should be dealt with under the doctrine of Responsibility to Protect (R2P). Both the UN Security Council and General Assembly endorsed the R2P doctrine and it has been at the centre of quite a few actions since then. The report highlights that the plethora of armed conﬂicts made easy by cheap and lethal weapons of destruc- tion in a ‘convulsive process of state fragmentation and state formation’ (ICISS 2001) in many parts of the world, necessitates a rethinking on approaches to security and the role of states. However, it needs to be kept in mind that intervention does not necessarily mean military inter- vention; and intervention aimed at protecting the victims might go wrong and there are examples of that. Dilemmas for International Community There are three dilemmas we encounter with regard to international management of Complex Humanitarian Emergencies (CHE) around the world. These are: First, it is debatable whether to provide humanitarian assistance in a particular CHE or not. At the outset this may sound extremely odd. When men are going through catastrophic situation due to intra-state warfare or extensive conﬂict and violence caused by failure of the state machinery, one would be expected to lend a helping hand. Complex emergencies usually bring along starvation, lack of medical care, rooﬂess life, etc. In general, undigniﬁed life needs immediate attention. Interna- tional community tries to respond to such situations with emergency relief through humanitarian agencies. But the problem lies with delivery of such

220 O. MISHRA AND A. RAY relief. Quite often this relief reaches the perpetrators of violence instead of the victims who actually need it. In a situation of complex emergency, law and order breaks down and local control is taken over by one or the other of the belligerent sides in different parts of the state. It becomes easy for them to forcefully divert the route of those reliefs to their choice of desti- nation. In fact, not allowing reliefs to the victims is also a part of the war plan. Thus, while trying to help the victims, to be precise non-combatant civilians, the world community may add fuel to the ﬁre. It may unwittingly prolong the warfare by providing non-military assistance to the party they intend to bring down. Moreover, there are also cases of misappropriation of resources by local stakeholders who are an integral part of the relief delivery mechanism. Foreign donors or the agencies are not familiar with the terrain or the demography of the area they plan to serve. As a result, they must take help from the local community leaders or the local NGOs which sometimes turn out to be corrupt or established during the conﬂict with the sole aim of diverting the resources for private gain. The multi- donor evaluation of the Rwandan crisis, for example, could not trace a third of the 170 registered NGOs, and around $120 million of funding could not be accounted for (World Disasters Report 1997). Second, there is dilemma relating to taking military support in relief distribution. Complex political emergencies create such a condition of hostility and lawlessness that sometimes it becomes impossible for the humanitarian organisations to reach the turf of hostility on their own. These agencies are essentially neutral, unarmed and profess non- discriminatory distribution of relief. But the belligerents more often than not perceive them as an agent of the opposition. Thus, they do not allow the aid workers to go to hinterlands under their control or block the roads to the conﬂict zones. In Somalia, Al-Shabab used to exact money from the aid agencies to work inside their land, and eventually went up to asking for unequal allotment of aid to them, which paved the way for exit of the ICRC which has always remained committed to neutrality in aid distribution (Jackson n.d.). In some cases, the national army, if not a party to the ensuing conﬂict makes the passage for the aid workers. On other times, when the national government itself is a party to the conﬂict, the situation becomes more complicated. Still the governments are, at least to some extent, bound by some rules and principles, and aid agencies can negotiate a way to work with them. The condition is totally unfavourable when the government is non-existent and the warring factions do not adhere to the rules and conventions of warfare. Attack on aid workers is

12 INTERNATIONAL MANAGEMENT OF COMPLEX … 221 on the rise and that is a testament to this claim. In 2021, 461 aid workers were victims of major attacks, and 141 were killed, 203 got wounded and 117 were kidnapped across the globe (AWSD 2022). Following the September 11 attacks, political rhetoric about human- itarian organisations focused on their role in the war on terror. Former U.S. Secretary of State Colin Powell, in 2001, mentioned of NGOs as a ‘force multiplier for the United States and a part of the U.S. combat team’ (Powell 2001). Humanitarian organisations condemned these comments, as they disregarded the principles of neutrality, impartiality and indepen- dence (Burnett 2004). According to the Red Cross Movement, stringent counter-terrorism laws coupled with greater dependence on military are the latest obstacle in provision of aid. This hobnobbing with military sometimes portrays an idea of integrated front in the ﬁght against terror which includes NGOs. This has led to immense loss for the relief commu- nity as it seriously hampers their credibility and capability to negotiate adverse situations. For example, armed groups in Pakistan had refused to allow the ICRC and Pakistani Red Crescent Societies to provide aid, believing that they were actually running a search and security operations on behalf of the Western powers. It is largely believed that Osama Bin- Laden was found by the health staff administering polio vaccines to the children in a door-to-door campaign organised by the government, and this perception among people led to a halt in that campaign and it left grassroots-level health workers in Pakistan exposed to life risk. The third and most important dilemma is regarding intervention, espe- cially a military one. Legally, the answer to this question of military or otherwise intervention is a straight no. Sovereignty of states within its boundary is the foundational principle upon which the modern state system is standing. Under the UN framework principles, no power, for whatsoever reason, is entitled to intervene in another state. But this textual interpretation is often challenged by contextual reading. Article 2(7) dictates that the United Nations has no authority to intervene in matters which are apparently well within the domestic jurisdiction of a state. At the same time, it was also readily seen that plea of domestic jurisdiction may serve as an excuse for UN scrutiny or action neces- sary to serve the cause of international peace and security. Therefore, the framers of the Charter kept a backdoor for UN action in the shape of Chapter 7 as constituting an exception to the otherwise general rule of non-interference. Since the 1990s, the interventions by the United Nations in an internal or international situation of armed conﬂict or to

222 O. MISHRA AND A. RAY quell civic disturbances at a large scale or to mitigate the sufferings of the civilians caught in humanitarian emergency situations have been under- taken under Chapter 7. Moreover, the UN when consensus could be reached has taken an expansive view of the requirement under Chapter 7 for enforcement ‘action with respect to threats to peace, breaches of peace and acts of aggression’. Thus, the UN has intervened and/or even authorised intervention in a large number of situations involving violent armed conﬂict endangering civilians, forcible displacement of people across the country (IDP) or beyond the border (refugees) and in other manifestations of complex emergencies within a country. As per the Common Article 3 of the Geneva Conventions, states are also required to provide consent to pass relief operations through the territory under their control. So technically, there is no need for inter- vention, humanitarian in name and military in nature, to any country as the disagreements can be resolved within the UN framework. But reality is far from this ideal state of affairs. Let us take the case of East Pakistan where India had to intervene and help the emergence of an indepen- dent Bangladesh in the face of unprecedented human rights violation and abuse of human dignity which had a spill-over effect on India in terms of millions of refugees. Pakistani authorities had blockaded relief works in East Pakistan in the ﬁnal phase of the assault and censored media, thus practically cutting the land and its people from the rest of the world. It was clearly a political or humanitarian emergency and complex in nature. It became a question of justice over order or order over justice for India and it chose the ﬁrst line. New Delhi was heavily criticised by the US and its Western allies for its action but it went ahead anyway. When Vietnam invaded Cambodia in 1978 to uproot the inhuman Pol Pot regime, it was condemned too by the UNSC, it stopped just short of taking coercive action against them. In fact, France launched a scathing criticism citing the inviolability of sovereign rights of a state, ignoring the butchery that was going on at that time in Cambodia. Ironically France was an active participant in NATO action in Kosovo in 1999, which even bypassed the UN to launch a quick, deﬁnitive strike. So, there is nothing in black and white as to whether a state or a group of states, within or outside the UN auspices, can intervene into another in case of a complex humani- tarian emergency, citing human rights violation and/or state failure. The West did launch such offensives time and again, against Iraq, Libya and Afghanistan, etc. and sometimes failed to act where it was much needed, like that in Rwanda or Syria.

12 INTERNATIONAL MANAGEMENT OF COMPLEX … 223 Conclusion The base and the basis for the emergence, ﬂourishing as well as the suste- nance of the nation-state system for the past few centuries has been the imperative of affording security at the individual and community level to the people and the ability of the states to thwart external aggres- sion and intervention. The expansion of connectivity and interdependence in the recent decades in the world together with the felt necessity of centring human beings and their security and welfare as the goal of the national development and international cooperation has necessitated a strong urge for avoiding conﬂict and war and mitigate and redress the consequences thereof. We therefore are witness to repeated occurrences of violent conﬂict and at the same time unceasing efforts to contain these at the international level. However, the inventiveness and innovation at the global level through the legal instrumentalities and conﬂict resolution mechanisms as well as political-military interventions have weakened but hardly deterred the complex violent conﬂict and war raging in various parts of the world. The resultant consequences have destabilised main- tenance of political order within the borders of the nation-states and exacerbated international crises including in the form of the return of international war in the last couple of years. Consequently, an effective international management of violent armed conﬂict and war continue to delude attainment of peace and security in the world. Acceptance of the centrality of human life and rights in the political narrative of the world often stands defeated in the face of the weaknesses of the international human rights and humanitarian regime and the deﬁciencies in consensus making processes at the political level in the UN system. The interna- tional management of CHEs therefore is more often than not is a casualty that must be addressed by fresh approaches and international consensus building.
