PART III Regional Variations and Implications of Permacrisis

CHAPTER 13 China’s Transition from ‘Peaceful Rise’ to ‘Chinese Dream’: A Critical Assessment of China’s Changing Narrative under Xi Jinping Priyadarshini Ghosh Widespread apprehension both within the academic circles and among the political elites surrounding China’s rise has characterised the global political landscape in recent years. With its entry into the World Trade Organization in 2001, Chinese economy has undergone rapid trans- formation so much so that China became the global leader in leading technologies from just being once the low-cost ‘factory to the world’. Thus, emboldened by its economic might, China leveraged its inter- national diplomacy to become a leading trading partner of emerging economies across the world. As a rising power, China became more vocal in its power ambitions—namely to emerge as the next global hegemon. P . Ghosh (B) Maynaguri College, University of North Bengal, West Bengal, India e-mail: ghoshpriyadarshini1@gmail.com © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_13 227

228 P . GHOSH China’s strategic posturing especially in the aftermath of the ‘unraveling of American power’ has given credence to the idea that the ‘pivot’ of power in international politics has gradually shifted from the Atlantic to the Paciﬁc (Cooley and Daniel, 2020). Beijing’s military modernisation programme together with its assertive international posturing, particu- larly with regard to China’s territorial disputes in the South China Sea, have been read as signals for China’s great power ambitions which have sent political ripples across the international waters. Such moves exacer- bated global apprehensions and heightened the threat perception of other major powers in its immediate neighbourhood and also the United States. Once worried that negative branding of China’s image could impede its continued economic growth as well as its image as a whole, Beijing sought to attach itself to the slogan of “peaceful development” (China’s Peaceful Development, 2011) in an attempt to reassure not only the developing countries but also its own neighbours that its newfound strength was benign. Hu Jintao after assuming Presidency in 2003 advocated for a ‘harmonious world’ and China’s ‘peaceful rise’ (heping jueqi), both important ideological pillars of China’s soft power policy. However, the term ‘peaceful rise’ has been consigned to the dustbin of history once Xi Jinping became the supreme leader. Contemporary Chinese political literature and Xi Jinping’s vision statements clearly capture the dramatic transformation that Chinese foreign policy has undergone, wherein China no longer hesitates to shed off its ambiguity and project its worldview and power overtly. The Xi Jinping era has been the fulcrum of this transfor- mation. The objective of this chapter is to understand and analyse the context behind this transformation. Accordingly, this chapter endeavours to examine how China’s global ‘outreach’ became ‘overreach’ under Xi Jinping’s stewardship. This writing has been structured into four sections. The ﬁrst part is devoted to the understanding of the concept of peaceful rise, followed by the transition to the Xi Jinping era and his idea of national rejuvenation or the ‘Chinese dream’. In this context, taking a cue from Fareed Zakaria’s logic of neoclassical realism, the third part shall try to capture how the internal political transition in China had its repercus- sions on China’s foreign policy. In the ﬁnal part, we shall focus on three different issue areas of Chinese foreign policy in the Xi Jinping era which might have global strategic implications for the decades ahead.

13 CHINA’S TRANSITION FROM ‘PEACEFUL RISE’ TO ‘CHINESE … 229 China’s ‘Peaceful Rise’ under Hu Jintao It was in President Hu Jintao’s tenure that China’s political leadership came up with the idea of China’s “peaceful rise”. The ‘peaceful rise’ theory was ﬁrst introduced by Zheng Bijian in November 2003 at the China-sponsored Boao Forum for Asia. Zheng Bijian was a former vice president of the Communist Party of China (CPC) Central Party School as well as a former chairman of the China Reform Forum, a representa- tive semi-governmental organisation for China’s diplomacy. Zheng also served as President Hu Jintao’s senior foreign policy advisor. In 2013, in his speech titled ‘China’s Road of Peaceful Development and the Building of Communities of Interests’, at the 5th World Forum on China Studies, in Shanghai, Zheng Bijian clariﬁed what he meant by ‘peaceful rise’. He said, “As an observer and scholar, since I proposed the concept of “China’s peaceful rise”, I have always stressed that the word ‘peaceful’ is directed against ‘China threat’ argument and the word ‘rise’ is directed against ‘China collapse’ views, both spread by some international opin- ions” (Bijian 2013). Zheng conﬁgured that China’s peaceful rise discourse could act as an effective counter-narrative to the China-threat theory and pacify the sound of alarm associated with China’s rise in some Western corners of the world. At the Center for Strategic and International Studies of the United States on December 9, 2002, Zheng said, China will have a totally different path of development from the path of rise of all major powers in the world since modern history. This will be a brand new path of development. I want to emphasize one point here. Our path is different from both the paths of Germany in World War I and Germany and Japan in World War II, when they tried to overhaul the world political landscape by way of aggressive wars. Our path is also different from that of the former U.S.S.R. during the reign of Brezhnev, which relied on a 5 military bloc and arms race in order to compete with the United States for world supremacy. Our brand new path relies upon the following factors, namely: relying on our own development; relying on the opening up of markets; relying on institutional innovation; relying on getting connected with economic globalization instead of being isolated from it; and relying on reciprocity and mutual beneﬁt with other countries for the purpose of win-win relations (Bijian 2005). After Zheng’s introduction of the ‘peaceful rise’ theory at the Boao Forum in 2003, the theory gained greater visibility, having been

230 P . GHOSH mentioned at a Harvard lecture by Premier Wen Jiabao in December 2003 and also referred to by Hu Jintao at an event celebrating the 110th birth anniversary of Mao Zedong. However, since April 2004, both Pres- ident Hu Jintao and Premier Wen Jiabao replaced the use of ‘peaceful rise’ with the term ‘peaceful development’. A case in point was a lecture delivered at the Boao Forum in April 2004, where Hu Jintao explained China’s foreign policy with the concept of peaceful development rather than peaceful rise. This change in terminology was triggered by a deci- sion reached by the Chinese government not to use ‘peaceful rise’ as an ofﬁcial term. The main trigger for the peaceful rise theory came from the discourses on ‘China as a threat’. This viewpoint put forward by some Western academics, who regarded China’s emergence as a ‘threat’, became an intense topic of discussion and debate (Kurlantzick 2007: 65). Such a view garnered a negative publicity for an emerging power like China and according to Chinese scholars and political leaders appeared to have engendered a negative branding for China’s image abroad. The peaceful rise theory sought to counter this negative image of China (Pathak 2015). However, simply understanding the peaceful rise theory as a response to the China-threat theory would be too naive. According to Li Mingjiang ( 2008), the peaceful rise theory was proposed “to declare China’s expan- sion of national power and its presumed forthcoming rise to global power status, and to gain acceptance of this declaration from neigh- bouring Asian countries” (Mingjiang 2008). What is interesting to note in this respect is that China’s proposal of the term ‘rise’ itself has great signiﬁcance attached to it vis-à-vis its foreign policy. The proposal of the peaceful rise implicates the transformation of China’s foreign policy in three ways: ﬁrst, there is a “switch from America-centric diplomacy to neighbour centric diplomacy”; second, “from diplomatic objectives focused on gaining admission into the international society to the goal of undertaking global leadership role”; and third is “the transformation from a diplomatic strategy focused on China’s economic development to one centered on increasing its comprehensive national power” (Mingjiang 2008). Put it simply, peaceful rise theory symbolically declared the trans- formation in China’s global strategy from being inward looking to the one focused on carving out for itself a global role. This line of thinking as calibrated by Zheng during the Hu Jintao era reciprocated in many ways, the spirit of the earlier decades. China’s leaders adopted a restrained approach to foreign policy for almost three

13 CHINA’S TRANSITION FROM ‘PEACEFUL RISE’ TO ‘CHINESE … 231 decades after Mao’s death in 1976. They were of the view that any threat to the power of the CPC and its leaders came from within and not from abroad. This notion was cemented by the 1989 Tiananmen crisis. Accord- ingly, to pave the way for the country’s “inexorable economic ascendance, and to prevent a backlash, they reassured the outside world of China’s peaceful intentions” (Shirk 2022: 4). Then, something changed, as Susan Shirk ( 2022) observed in her very recent and extremely illuminating, disturbing and utterly persuasive book, Overreach: How China Derailed its Peaceful Rise. She observed that from a ‘fragile superpower’ China marched forward to become a “global heavyweight, threatening Taiwan as well as its neighbours in the South China Sea, tightening its grip on Hong Kong and openly challenging the United States for preeminence not just economically and technologically but militarily” (Shirk 2022: 12). China began to ‘overreach’ and thus began its phase of transition to the Xi Jinping era. What is the ‘Chinese Dream’? Xi Jinping completed a decade in ofﬁce and began an unprecedented third term at the Communist Party’s 20th Party Congress held in October 2022. Ever since, Xi’s taking over, his views on China’s place in the world have begun to make its way into the Chinese political literature. In China ‘Qiang guo’ or strong country is the much popular axiom that has found its place now. Phraseology of the earlier eras like that of China’s ‘peaceful rise’ (Hu Jintao era) and ‘biding time, hiding brightness’ (Deng’s era) lay buried in the sands of time. The thrust of Xi’s argument is that China’s time has come. During his ‘Road to Rejuvenation’ speech on November 29, 2012, Xi spelt out, brieﬂy, his ideas of the ‘Chinese Dream’. He said: In the future, the Chinese nation will ‘forge ahead like a gigantic ship breaking through strong winds and heavy waves’…Everyone has an ideal, ambition and dream. We are now all talking about the Chinese Dream. In my opinion, achieving the rejuvenation of the Chinese nation has been the greatest dream of the Chinese people since the advent of modern times… Empty talk harms the country, while hard work makes it ﬂourish. Our generation of Communists should draw on past progress and chart a new course for the future. We should strengthen Party building, rally all the sons and daughters of the Chinese nation around us in a common effort to build our country and develop our nation, and continue to boldly advance towards the goal of the rejuvenation of the Chinese nation” (Jinping 2012).

232 P . GHOSH At the Communist Party’s Congress in 2017, he declared China was “moving to the centre stage of the world”. In a world witnessing ‘changes unseen in a century’, Xi has declared many a time that ‘the West was declining and the East was rising’. At this conjecture in history, Xi believes that there are opportunities for China—taking the “centre stage”, but at the same time, the global order for Xi represents turmoil or even chaos. And it is amidst this chaos that the Communist Party of China under his leadership which will act as China’s defence and as leading what he calls the country’s ‘great rejuvenation’ or the Chinese dream (Krishnan 2022a). During Xi Jinping’s coronation in 2012 at the 18th Congress of the CPC, the Communist Party newspapers in China were ﬂooded with vigorous coverage of a debate raging between the ‘Left’ and ‘Right’ factions of the party. Both these factions of the Communist Party vehe- mently argued for treading different paths for the country’s future politics. Surprisingly, after a decade later, such debates have become a thing of the past, because no discernible Left or Right factions exist anymore in China. For both editorials and the average Chinese, the past decade also synonymously called as the ‘new era’ (or ‘xin shidai’) stands for one thing: it means the Xi Jinping era and it began in 2012. Plagued by the existential crisis, the Communist Party elders during the transi- tion in 2012 gave Xi Jinping, the mandate to clean the rot. However, in hindsight it can be said that the former leaders got much more than they bargained for. Strong Leader, Makes a Strong Country? According to the theory of Realism, which happens to be one the most dominant theories of international politics, it is the centrality of power that determines states behaviour. States seek power to preserve their exis- tence. There are variations within the realist school on why states seek power. For Classical Realism and Modern Realism, the source lies within human nature (unit-level factor). For Structural Realists (also called neorealists) like Kenneth Waltz, it is the anarchical nature of the inter- national system (structural factor) that propels state to seek power. There is yet another group of scholars within the realist paradigm who seek to bridge the gap between the structural and unit level factors. Gideon Rose categorised these scholars as ‘neoclassical realists’. According to Walt ( 2002), the causal logic of neoclassical realism places domestic politics

13 CHINA’S TRANSITION FROM ‘PEACEFUL RISE’ TO ‘CHINESE … 233 as an intervening variable between distribution of power and foreign policy behaviour (Walt 2002: 11). One important intervening variable is leaders themselves, namely how they perceive the international distribu- tion of power. There is no impartial reading of the distribution of power. Instead, what becomes signiﬁcant is how this distribution of power shapes the perceptions of the state leaders. According to neoclassical realists like Schweller ( 1996) not only do states differ in terms of their interests, but also differ in terms of their ability to extract and direct resources from the society that they rule. Fareed Zakaria ( 1998) introduced the variable of state strength into this version of Realism. State strength is deﬁned as the capability of a state to channelise resources that are available in order to attain certain objectives (Zakaria 1998: 26). Hence, according to the neoclassical theory of Realism, unit level factors like how power is perceived and how leadership is exercised plays a crucial determinant in a state’s foreign policy. It is possible to catalogue Xi Jinping’s perception of power and his political inﬂuence on Chinese domestic politics through this lens of neoclassical realism. Immediately after Xi Jinping took over, anti- corruption campaign was launched to get rid of the massive political and corruption scandals that plagued the party’s ranks. It was this anti- corruption campaign that readied the deck to catapult Xi to a position of strength within the party itself. This move was welcomed by the public who had been wearied by CPC ofﬁcials’ rampant corruption and amassing of disproportionate wealth. The anti-corruption campaign laid down stringent rules for Party members. At the same time, in the garb of ﬁghting and uprooting corruption from the Party ranks, through this campaign, Xi very dexterously purged his political rivals within the Party. Thus gaining a much needed political space which was now rid of the occasional critics, Xi embarked on a ‘massive restructuring of the Party- State’, which he completed in 2018, when a completely new structure of governance was laid out by the CPC that, for the ﬁrst time in decades, ‘brought Party organs out of the shadows’ and positioned them in the secured charge of the State bureaucracy (Krishnan 2022a). The ‘collective leadership’1 model put in place after Deng Xiaoping that saw a division of responsibilities in the top Politburo Standing Committee was also given 1 For a detailed understanding of the collective leadership model followed in China since the Deng Xiaoping era, please see Krishnan. 2020. India’s China Challenge, Noida: HarperCollins, pp.40–42.

234 P . GHOSH a quiet burial. The Premier was no longer at the helm of affairs of the economy. His role was reduced to just presiding over a diminished State bureaucracy. With an objective to end the ‘ﬁefdoms’ that emerged in the Jiang Zemin and Hu Jintao era, and to place the party’s “core” leader in charge of all domains, Xi Jinping diluted the division between the Party and the State. Accordingly, his 2018 reforms declared that the “Party is the highest force for political leadership” (Krishnan 2022a). Xi did not rest at this. The overhaul of the People’s Liberation Army (PLA) was on Xi Jinping’s reform agenda as well. The reform of the PLA was an ambitious project since after Deng Xiaoping, none of the Chinese Presidents have been able to successfully undertake or complete this project. And here too, Xi very adeptly played the anti-corruption campaign card, which struck fear in the Generals after Xi purged the PLA’s two highest-ranking Generals who had been accused of running a ‘pay for post’ military. Commenting on this stupendous task, the PLA Daily described it as ‘the largest scale military reform since the 1950s’. According to Ananth Krishnan, The Hindu’s China correspondent and an well-known China expert, “…the reforms ended the Soviet-style General Staff Departments, disbanding four vast bureaucracies handling staff, poli- tics, logistics and armaments and bringing them under the direct control of the Central Military Commission. Xi’s remaking of the Party-State brought him ﬁrm unchallenged control and removed any and all space for dissent” (Krishnan 2022a). China’s Global ‘Overreach’ Imagine a leader unchallenged, supremely powerful, almost Leviathan like, it is but natural that his foreign policy would reverberate of his might. A part of Xi Jinping’s ‘Chinese dream’ of national rejuvenation is also a qiang guo or a strong country with a strong foreign policy. However, Xi’s worldview suffers from an inherent contradiction. For him, China would be leading the world order but the world itself is apparently full of external threats. It is this contradiction that has determined the trajec- tory of Chinese diplomacy in the past decade. China’s foreign policy appears to be caught between, two opposing spectrum. On the one hand, China strives to present itself as ‘the savior of an UN centred world order’ and on the other, China continues to aggressively and assertively pursue its core interests ever more, regardless of the consequences. This has been aptly demonstrated in Chinese assertiveness ranging from Ladakh

13 CHINA’S TRANSITION FROM ‘PEACEFUL RISE’ TO ‘CHINESE … 235 to the South China Sea to most recently, the waters around Taiwan. This has been dubbed as the ‘wolf warrior’ approach (Krishnan 2022b). It is pertinent here to highlight three issue areas where China’s so-called over- reach under Xi Jinping has been most pronounced having probable global ramiﬁcations and a cascading impact on India per se. B e l t a n dR o a dI n i t i a t i v e Launched in 2013, the Belt and Road Initiative (BRI) also referred to as the New Silk Road was President Xi Jinping’s signature foreign policy initiative and a platform to stake China’s claim to global domi- nance. Originally, BRI was conceptualised to be two-pronged: Silk Road Economic Belt stretching over land frontiers and the Maritime Silk Road. BRI is popularly known in China as ‘One Belt One Road’ initiative. The geographical outreach of BRI also signiﬁcantly expanded from a ‘regional to a near-global initiative’, in both of its components. China emphasised that bereft of hegemonic pulls and pressures, the BRI was a new model for trade, partnership and integration and that the principles of multi- lateralism, environment and sustainability were ﬁrmly rooted in the BRI (Luthra 2023). Even though the BRI was envisioned as a vast conglomeration of devel- opment and investment initiatives devised originally for linking (through physical infrastructures) East Asia and Europe, the BRI actually has been used to further a narrative of China’s inevitable global hegemony. As of now, a total of 147 countries—accounting for 40% of the total GDP of the world and two-thirds of the world’s population have inked agree- ments for projects or shown intent in doing so. However, many analysts have criticised China’s lending through the BRI as ‘debt trap diplomacy’ designed to continue China’s economic domination and manipulative strategies against these countries with unsustainable loans and using the debt to gain leverage over those governments and even seize their infras- tructure. As a result, ten years later, today the BRI stands as an ‘inﬂection point’ because it still remains an “amorphous and hard-to-deﬁne initia- tive”, sometimes labelled by political commentators as “a Trojan horse for China-led regional development and military expansion” (Krishnan 2022b; McBride et al., 2023). The rising debt levels in many partner countries are pointers to the long-term unsustainability of these loans. A case in point is that of Sri Lanka, which fell behind in its payment in 2017 over its Hambantota

236 P . GHOSH port. In order to renegotiate the deal, China obtained a 99-year lease on the property. Not only Sri Lanka, Argentina, Malaysia, Pakistan, Kenya, Tanzania and Montenegro are some of the other countries which have found themselves burdened with huge debt overhangs and their debt-to- GDP ratios have reached unsustainable results and have led to balance-of- payments crisis (Bennon and Fukuyama 2023). Many of these countries have been forced to turn towards the International Monetary Fund to ﬁnance these loans, which these countries have incurred as a result of the BRI projects that they entered into. According to a study published by the National Bureau of Economic Research (Cambridge), approximately half of China’s loans to developing countries are ‘hidden’ meaning that they are not included in the ofﬁcial statistics. Such hidden debts have resulted in a series of ‘hidden defaults’ (Horn et al., 2019). However, it is well known that China had other objectives while funding for the BRI projects. And these objectives were not just economic but also political and strategic, including gaining political inﬂuence and access to strategic resources. Criticism aside, not all BRI projects have underperformed. Greece’s Piraeus port project has delivered the outcomes for both the partner countries and examples like these have only bolstered Beijing’s successful overreach. The fact that indebted partners have only returned to China for more assistance underscores the reality of China’s politico-economic muscle. The cases of both Pakistan and Sri Lanka drive home this truth. Both these countries have received more than $26 billion from China in the past ﬁve years, and dealing with ﬁnancial crises, are once again turning not only to the IMF but also to Beijing. China-US Rivalry With the Obama administration in October 2011 announcing its strategic pivot to Asia, it has been widely argued that a major driver for this US rebalancing has been the rise of China. One of the prominent highlights of the trajectory of China’s foreign policy under Xi Jinping has been the rising China-US rivalry and the deepening ties with Russia. Relations with Washington reached its nadir during the Donald Trump administration. During the COVID-19 pandemic, which was thought to be the next ‘Suez moment’ for the United States, China positioned itself in trying to ﬁll the power vacuum created by the crisis in leadership in Wash- ington (Campbell and Rush, 2020). China tried to project itself as the

13 CHINA’S TRANSITION FROM ‘PEACEFUL RISE’ TO ‘CHINESE … 237 saviour of an UN centred world order which in its views Donald Trump and US foreign policy in general was trying to topple. In the race to be the new global hegemon, during the pandemic, through its well crafted and deftly manoeuvred strategies of providing material assistance to the affected countries, Beijing was ﬁrmly placed at a vantage point of being a serious contender for the United States in the competition for global leadership. This was especially highlighted by Xi Jinping in many of his recent speeches, wherein he has time and again referred to a world facing two paths—one of chaos caused by “small cliques”, as China refers to the United States and its allies, primarily hinting at the Quad (short form for the Quadrilateral Security Dialogue that involves United States, Japan, Australia and India) and the other his ‘community of shared destiny’ (Krishnan 2022b). For Beijing, on the other side of US-China rivalry has been the newfound bonhomie with Russia. China’s present relations with Russia have been described by the Russian Foreign Minister Sergey Lavrov as being ‘the best in history’, as well as a declaration in February 2021, on the eve of Russia’s invasion of Ukraine (which has yet not been condemned by China), of a relationship with ‘no limits’ (Krishnan 2022c). With an objective to blunt the criticism of Chinese assertive claims in the South China Sea and its aggressive posturing in the Indo- Paciﬁc region, China has tried to warm up to its Southeast Asian neigh- bours through its economic diplomacy in the region. However, the recent military manoeuvres against Taiwan in the past few months have under- scored Beijing’s renewed emphasis on protecting China’s ‘sovereignty’ and its core interests. Such moves have undercut China’s broader geopo- litical ambitions. India has been long considered by Beijing as a crucially signiﬁcant ‘swing power’, but a country with which ties have plummeted since April 2020 after the Doklam crisis. India-China Relationship The Galwan valley stand-off between India and China marked an “impla- cable decline in India-China ties” (Gokhale 2021). The bilateral relations have plunged to the lowest levels since the normalisation of ties in the 1980s during the Rajiv Gandhi era. Multiple transgressions by the PLA at the Sino-Indian border in the western sector have triggered tensions in the recent past between the two sides which still continues, unabated. India’s relations with China during Xi Jinping’s tenure can at best be

238 P . GHOSH characterised as ‘see-sawing’, wherein from the highs of two ‘informal summits’ in Wuhan in 2018 and Mamallapuram in 2019 it straightway nosedived after the Galwan valley clash in June 2020. A notable feature of Chinese foreign policy during the presidency of Xi Jinping has been that territorial problems with neighbours are now seen as ‘threats to China’s ‘sovereignty’ and not as ‘disputes’ that could be mutually resolved. This diminishes both the scope and space for ﬁnding political solutions. With India getting close to Washington in the past few years, Beijing’s equations with New Delhi have been viewed through the lens of the great power rivalry between China and the United States. The most recent case in point has been Xi Jinping’s conscpicuous absence from the G-20 summit in New Delhi in September 2023. Even though China’s chair was not left vacant, it was the Chinese Premier Li Qiang and not President Xi who occupied the seat at the G-20 New Delhi summit. China has usually placed special emphasis on G-20, viewing it as an important platform to shape the global order and exert its rising clout. However, many political observers were of the opinion that China’s absence without providing any acceptable reason was a deliberate signal to India and the West and was a ‘clearly well thought out’decision and not a ‘routine’ one (Krishnan 2023). Scholars have forecasted gloomy days ahead for Sino-Indian relations, wherein under Xi, the coming ﬁve years are more likely to be charac- terised by an ‘ever-sharper period of tensions with the West’, the tremors of which are most likely to be felt in China’s immediate neighbourhood and in its relations with India (Krishnan 2022b). Anti-western feelings ran high when the Chinese Foreign Ministry criticised the United States for visit of the US House Speaker Nancy Pelosi to Taiwan in August 2022. The Chinese Foreign Ministry said, “They clearly believe they live in the time of 120 years ago…today’s China is not the old China of 100 years ago that was humiliated and bullied”. The ministry even went to the extent of drawing a parallel between the G7 and the alliance of eight nations that invaded China in 1900 and marked a “century of humil- iation” (Foreign Ministry, People’s Republic of China, 2022). China through its project of ‘national rejuvenation’ or the Chinese dream will seek to bury these memories and that is a bold assertion that Xi Jinping is all determined to achieve during his reign.

13 CHINA’S TRANSITION FROM ‘PEACEFUL RISE’ TO ‘CHINESE … 239 Conclusion In the latest issue (September/October 2023) of Foreign Affairs, Johnson (2023) in his very interestingly titled article ‘Xi’s Age of Stagna- tion: The Great Walling Off of China’ has observed that over the past one year the Chinese government under Xi Jinping has lurched from crisis to crisis. A continuous period of slow economic growth together with the public anger and the resulting anti-government protests in Guangzhou, Chengdu, Shanghai and Beijing against Xi Jinping’s grossly unpopular zero-COVID-19 policy have brought out in full public glare the unpopu- larity of Xi Jinping in China. Although Xi Jinping won an unprecedented third term as Chinese President, suddenly the public mistrust which is higher than ever before, has turned Xi’s ‘Chinese dream’ into a night- mare. Consequently, as many analysts have argued, it was expected that Xi would loosen his tight grip over the Chinese society and economy. But none of that actually happened. Although COVID restrictions are gone, strict government intervention still continues. Ian Johnson seeks to explain this phenomenon as ‘the new national statis’ or what Chinese call ‘neijuan’. According to Johnson, neijuan can be deﬁned as life twisting inwards without real progress. He argues that this walling-off now perme- ates every aspect of the Chinese life under Xi Jinping, leaving the country more isolated and stagnant than any period since Deng launched his reforms in the late 1970s (Johnson 2023). However, in the past, every time the party faced a crisis, it survived, as it was quick to adapt and experiment. In the aftermath of Mao’s death, the party faced with a leadership crisis relaxed its grip on society and China opened up. During the Tiananmen massacre too, the party was quick to understand that crises such as these could only be averted by economic progress. This so-called adaptative authoritarianism best characterised the leaders of the erstwhile generation who believed that the country was prone to large-scale crisis and needed course correction every time it faced such challenges. But Xi Jinping’s China is of a different breed. Self-critical reﬂection is a thing of the past in China now. In China’s inward turn, many Chinese scholars have already started seeing a replication of the constrained atmosphere that characterised the countries of the Eastern bloc during the Cold War (Johnson 2023). It cannot be said for certain if Beijing’s bet to turn inward and succeed on its own in order to withstand the pressure of an uncertain world will pay off. However, the thing that can be said with certainty is that if

240 P . GHOSH the party’s successes in dealing with these challenges may, as its media reminds, rest on Xi, so will its failures in future. Both the domestic and the international environments that Xi currently faces are signiﬁcantly worse than in 2012 when he took ofﬁce as the Party’s general secre- tary. China is undergoing an economic slowdown which has resulted in faltering conﬁdence on the Chinese economic growth story. On the other hand, the looming debt and China’s geostrategic competition with the United States and its allies is jeopardizing the future of China’s economic growth and also its technological progression. As a result, policymaking is becoming increasingly volatile, as China’s increasing challenges is making it harder for Xi to strike that ﬁne balance between the Party’s ideolog- ical agenda and the reality of replicating the golden past of unbridled economic growth. In order to rein in this volatility, China needs to focus on three balancing acts: growth with security in economic policy; the political and diplomatic tussle against the United States for global lead- ership along with avoiding ‘economic decoupling’ from the West; and within the domestic sphere balancing the competing elite factions. In conclusion, going by the Fareed Zakarian logic of state strength being a determining factor in a state’s foreign policy, how much of that ability to mobilise and direct resources at his disposal in the pursuit of the ‘Chinese dream’ Xi Jinping will be able to demonstrate, remains a pertinent question. Xi has his task cut out and will have a tough road to tread in the coming days. These looming challenges in the garb of slowing economic growth, uncertainty in governance and an ever- increasing hostile international environment, if left uncontrolled, are more likely to make the ‘Chinese dream’ of national rejuvenation the Chinese nightmare of national stagnation. CHAPTER 14 China’s Geoeconomic Manoeuvrings in Southeast Asia: Security Challenges and Implications Tilottama Mukherjee It is indeed a reality that China has the ‘economic centrality’ to the Southeast Asian region. However, the survey report entitled ‘The State of Southeast Asia 2022’ conducted by the Singapore’s ISEAS-Yusof Ishak Institute revealed that despite China being the leading economic power in the region: Japan remains the most trusted major power among Southeast Asians (54.2%), followed by the US (52.8%) and the European Union (48.5%). An appalling 58.1% expressed distrust towards China, and 49.6% of those who distrust China fear that China could use economic and military power to threaten the country’s interest and sovereignty. This was despite the fact that the overwhelming majority in the survey concluded that China is the most inﬂuential economic and political power. This survey was T. Mukherjee (B) Syamaprasad College, University of Calcutta, Kolkata, India e-mail: tilottamamukherjee@yahoo.com © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_14 243

244 T. MUKHERJEE done on 1677 respondents from across the 10 ASEAN member countries (ISEAS-YusofIshak Institute Survey Report 2022). The question that arises here is that if there is any possible way to understand if the mistrust for China has increased over the years or the increasing levels of economic engagement is helping China to improve its image and inﬂuence among the Southeast Asians. Well, China has enjoyed relative popularity as a rising Asian country in the 1990s till the ﬁrst two decades of the new millennium. However, China under Presi- dent Xi Jinping has been following a diplomatic style vis-à-vis the smaller countries, popularly known as the ‘wolf warrior’ style of diplomacy. It is perceived to be assertive, confrontational, combative and coercive. It is in sharp contrast to what President Deng Xiaoping believed in. His style of leadership was calm observance, steadfastly coping with China’s international affairs by hiding its intent and being good at maintaining a low proﬁle. Deng propagated the dictum taoguang yang hui. It roughly means ‘keeping a low proﬁle and biding time, while getting something accomplished’ (Youyi 2011). But, the China under President Xi is “more conﬁdent and more capable than ever before” (Kurlantzick 2006: 270–276). During the Cold War years, the Southeast Asian countries were suspicious of China as Maoist China was supporting communists in Thailand, Malaysia and most signiﬁcantly the Khmer regime in Cambodia. The Association of Southeast Asian Nations (ASEAN) was established in 1967 to thwart the advance of communism in the region. However, after analysing the avail- able literature of the post-Cold War years it can be understood that since the late 1990s, perceptions of China in Southeast Asia had started to shift signiﬁcantly in favour of China. As a plausible explanation, it can be said that, China had beneﬁted from missteps by the US and Japan, ranging from the 1997 Asian ﬁnancial crisis to Washington’s post- 9/11 focus on counter- terrorism in Southeast Asia. But the transformation also stems from a rise in China’s soft power in Southeast Asia, that is, China’s ability to inﬂuence Southeast Asian countries by persuasion, rather than coercion. China was extending its soft power to the region through various means, such as culture, diplomacy, business actions, and participation in multilat- eral organisations and portraying its economic strength which will extend the gravitational pull to other neighbouring countries (Kurlantzick 2006: 270–276).

14 CHINA’S GEOECONOMIC MANOEUVRINGS IN SOUTHEAST … 245 T he South China Sea Imbroglio: High Stakes for Southeast Asia However, the President Xi-led China has been far more assertive in accomplishing what it feels would be in their best national interest. This is why some of the activities of China are being construed as ‘threats’ by other smaller countries. For instance, China’s sweeping claims over the South China Sea. The South China Sea is a moderately enclosed sea, about 3.5 million square kilometres in area, extending from the Singa- pore Strait to the Taiwan Strait. Geo-strategically located between the Paciﬁc Ocean and the Indian Ocean, about one-third of the world’s shipping takes place through its waters. It is situated in the South of China and has the islands of Taiwan and the Philippines in its east. It has Vietnam and Cambodia towards its west and Singapore, Malaysia and Brunei in its south (Chakraborti and Bhattacharya 2012: 2). The South China Sea issue has been ﬂaring up at regular intervals from the latter half of the previous century because eight states have been claiming sovereignty to these islands. Singapore and Malaysia have been disputing sovereignty claims over Pisang Island and Pulau Batu Puteh. China, Taiwan and Vietnam contest each other’s claims to sovereignty over the Paracels, a group of ﬁfteen islets and several reefs and shoals scattered over a 200 km area in the northern South China Sea. Today China is in possession of all the islands in the Paracels as it had captured those from South Vietnam in 1974. Taiwan also contests China’s claims to Pratas Island and Macclesﬁeld Bank. Seven states assert their claims on the Spratly archipelago. Whereas China, Taiwan and Vietnam claim the entire archipelago, Indonesia, Philippines, Malaysia and Brunei claim sovereignty over portions of it. Except Brunei and Indonesia, all the others have established a military presence in the Spratlys (Puri and Sehgal 2011: 438). In fact, the People’s Republic of China has been claiming complete sovereignty over the entire South China Sea region, citing the presence of historical documents that apparently prove Chinese interaction with this region from time immemorial and China has put forward the concept of ‘nine dash/dotted lines’ extending over the entire South China Sea region to back up its claims of sovereignty. Vietnam and China already had two armed skirmishes with each other in 1974 and 1988 on the South China Sea issue and continue to be embroiled in regular scufﬂes on this issue. Though these countries are bound by the United Nations Convention

246 T. MUKHERJEE on the Law of the Sea (UNCLOS III), 1982 and ASEAN’s Declaration on the Conduct of Parties in the South China Sea (DOC), 1992 based on the principles of peace and tranquillity in the region as per the Treaty of Amity and Cooperation (TAC), there have been frequent untoward incidents leading to even loss of lives, owing to contested sovereignty claims in the region. Time and again, Chinese ‘aggressive’ postures have been reported such as, the Chinese PLA-N building artiﬁcial island on the Mischief Reef in the Spratlys, deploying Romeo-class conventional submarines in the disputed region; Chinese ships ﬁring on Vietnamese ﬁshing boats in the South China Sea; Chinese plans to begin tourist cruises to Paracels; China placing boundary markers on the Paracels; China creating a country-level city called Sansha in the Hainan province to administer its claims in the South China Sea; China cutting the exploration cables of Binh Minh 02 seismic vessel of Vietnam National Oil and Gas, and so on (Thayer 2010: 401–404). China has fully militarised at least three of the several islands it built in the disputed South China Sea, arming them with anti-ship and anti-aircraft missile systems, laser and jamming equipment and ﬁghter jets (The Guardian 2022). The Philippines, in February 2023, accused China of using a powerful laser against one of its ships on a resupply mission at Second Thomas Shoal which reportedly has blinded its crew (Al Jazeera 2023). This is despite the fact that in 2016, the International Court of Justice (ICJ) ruled that there was no legal basis for China to claim historical rights to resources within the sea areas falling in the proposed nine-dash line. Beijing refused to accept such a ruling and subsequently rejected it. An international ruling seems to be of no effect in containing China’s belligerent posture in the region. Besides this, with Laos, China claims large parts of its territory, appar- ently based on historical reasons, presumably dating back to the Yuan Dynasty. Furthermore, China and Myanmar share over 2000 km-long border, not least, Myanmar has often accused China of creating trouble on the border and instigating insurgent groups. So, pondering over the question as to why China suffers from trust deﬁcit among its neighbours in the region—besides these maritime and territorial disputes which seem to be the obvious reason—there is also the opinion that China has been failing to accept the fact that Southeast Asians want their region to remain as an open region. The Southeast Asian countries, consequently, do not want to willingly fall into the sphere of inﬂuence of any one particular regional hegemon, be it an Asian state

14 CHINA’S GEOECONOMIC MANOEUVRINGS IN SOUTHEAST … 247 or otherwise. Centuries of struggle against colonial powers have made sovereignty sacrosanct to them, and they would not barter their political autonomy for securing economic beneﬁts (Jaknanihan 2022). If we look at some crude statistics, we will see that China’s trade volume with the ten members of the ASEAN has nearly doubled from $443 billion in 2013 to a whopping $878 billion in 2021. During the same period of time, however, Beijing has also built over 3200 acres of artiﬁcial landmass and fully militarised at least three islands in the South China Sea (Business Standard 2022). Arguably, had China not exhibited such a heavy-handed attitude, it may have been able to utilise its economic inﬂuence as the source of trust. ‘China Dream’ and the ‘Debt-T rap’ in Southeast Asia Over the past decades, as a result of its unparalleled economic success and mammoth connectivity projects, China is becoming increasingly central in world politics. In the October 2017 Report to the Chinese Commu- nist Party’s 19th Congress, President Xi Jinping stated that by 2050, China will have “become a global leader in terms of composite national strength and international inﬂuence” (Xinhua 2017). China’s activities have given birth to worldwide apprehensions regarding its strategy of expansionism through lending or investments in other countries. There are concerns that China’s capital transfers to these countries are actu- ally tools or efforts to ensnare these countries in debt-traps so that their vulnerability could be exploited and their resources be brought under Beijing’s control. An example may sufﬁce: at the time when Myanmar was reeling under the military Junta regime and was under signiﬁcant western sanctions, China’s clout in the country continued to grow phenomenally. In 2009, China ﬁnalised and ﬁnanced the construction of the Myitsone Dam on the Irrawaddy River in the Kachin State. However, after much protest from the citizens of Myanmar over a number of issues such as environmental consequences, ﬂooding and unacceptably unfair sharing of electricity output between the two countries, the project was ﬁnally stalled by the Thein Sein administration, in September 2011. It was an estimated $3.6 billion project and since Myanmar could not repay China’s loans for the aborted project, the latter imposed upon the former a repayment regime in the form of equity in all the new dams in the country. It has helped China to consolidate its foothold over Naypyidaw.

248 T. MUKHERJEE A mention, in this context must, also be made of Laos. Laos is a country for which China remains the largest source of development assis- tance and investment. As part of the Belt and Road Initiative (BRI), China and Laos made known their plans of constructing a high speed railway track through Laos. China is going overboard in projecting that the proposed railway track, once put into operation, will be of huge beneﬁt to the host country as it will turn Laos from a landlocked to a land-linked country. The railway track will originate at the Yunnan Province and will terminate at Singapore after running through Laos. Despite the popular narrative, the actual fact remains that it is China which will actually greatly beneﬁt by this railway track as it will link China’s Yunnan Province directly to Laos, Thailand, Malaysia and Singapore, allowing Chinese goods to ﬂood their markets very easily and quickly. This railway project costs $6 billion. Laos, only for this one project, has over $1.5 billion debt to the EXIM Bank of China. The total debt Laos owes to China is estimated to be around 45% of its GDP (Freeman 2019). The developing countries are in perpetual need of investments for their infrastructural development. In this situation, China appears to them as a quick and easy source of investment. There is a limit to how much a country will be provided with loans, as long as it deals with agencies like the IMF. Besides, the IMF has stringent conditions for sanctioning loans, such as, it checks for a country’s credit worthiness, or its ability to repay loans. Here, China seems to be a more lenient donor. But, the problem reportedly starts when a country defaults, a phenomenon which has been accentuated since the BRI was announced. We must note here that 42 developing countries including 4 in Southeast Asia, namely, Laos, Brunei, Cambodia and Myanmar, have public debt exposure to China in excess of 10% of their GDPs (Davidson 2021). Belt and Road: Some Implications for Southeast Asia China has been going overdrive in trying to build a global economic system with itself as the core of such a system. Accordingly, China is trying to revive the ‘maritime silk route’ which is the maritime portion of the historic Silk Route. This idea was formally announced for the ﬁrst time by Chinese President Xi Jinping in his speech when he visited Central Asia and Southeast Asia in September and October 2013, in which he raised the initiative of jointly building the Silk Road Economic Belt and

14 CHINA’S GEOECONOMIC MANOEUVRINGS IN SOUTHEAST … 249 the 21st-Century Maritime Silk Road (Belt and Road) in the Indone- sian Parliament. Once again at the China-ASEAN Expo in 2013, Premier Le Keqiang highlighted the necessity to build the Maritime Silk Road directed towards the ASEAN and to generate deliberate propellers for neighbourhood development (Ministry of Foreign Affairs, China 2015). The proposed Maritime Silk Route will run through and connect China’s Fujian Province, Guangdong, Guangxi, Hainan, the Malacca Strait, Kula Lumpur, Kolkata, Colombia; passing through the rest of Indian Ocean it will connect Nairobi, Horn of Africa, Red Sea, the Mediterranean and Venice and then will ﬁnally connect with the land segment of the Silk Route (Chaturvedy 2017). The Silk Road Economic Belt focuses on bringing together China, Central Asia, Russia and Europe by linking China with the Persian Gulf and the Mediterranean Sea through Central Asia and West Asia, not least, connecting China with Southeast Asia, South Asia and the Indian Ocean. It will connect China with Europe through the South China Sea and the Indian Ocean in one way, and in another direction, it will connect China with the South China Sea and the South Paciﬁc (Ministry of Foreign Affairs, China 2015). Thus, it is an extremely ambitious project joining several countries and continents with China’s provinces. China has been trying its best to make this project proﬁtable to host coun- tries and has thus, termed this initiative to be ‘harmonious and lucrative’, fostering ‘a multi polar world’ with ‘open regional cooperation’. It has been actively promoting this proposal by visiting countries and garnering support in this regard so that the concept of ‘peaceful rise’ of China does not take a beating (The Economic Times 2018). For furthering China’s economic growth story, it needs a constant inﬂow of energy supplies and the BRI purports to establish an energy corridor for China. One such project within the BRI is the Myanmar-China crude oil pipeline, which was operationalised in 2017 with the objective of enabling China to import crude oil from West Asia and Africa without having to ship its supplies through the Strait of Malacca and the disputed South China Sea region. The pipeline measures 30 metres in width and covers a distance of 793 km. The Myanmar-China Pipeline Watch Committee—the only civil society organisation which has been monitoring the social impact of this Project—is of the opinion that huge stretches of lush green agricultural lands have been taken away from the farmers in Myanmar for this project, and without adhering to an adequate and transparent compensation policy. Moreover, there is rampant soil erosion along the pipeline resulting

250 T. MUKHERJEE in damage to water resources, which may subsequently cause further envi- ronmental degradation (Project Report of the Myanmar-China Pipeline Watch Committee 2015). Foreign investments in Southeast Asia origi- nating from China have grown 20-fold from 2005 to 2019 (Goh and Nan, 2021) but it is equally true that Southeast Asia’s growing economic linkages with China have been generating strategic concerns in equal measure. Analysts and scholars have often pointed out how Vietnam has been following a ‘hedging strategy’ vis-à-vis China as far as the BRI is concerned: in so far as avoiding opposition with the PRC, avoiding dependence on it, agreeing to and defying PRC as and when the situa- tion demands, and simultaneously diversifying relations with other major powers. On a number of projects, Vietnam has started distancing itself from China. For example, reportedly, Hanoi has denied Chinese funding for the Van Don-Mong Cai highway, owing to national security concerns as Mong Cai is a city near Vietnam’s border with China. Furthermore, Vietnam has opted out Chinese Huawei involvement in its 5G infras- tructural development. Instead, Vietnam is now endeavouring to put in place its own 5G model (Trinh and Hui, 2023). President Tran Dai Quang, while welcoming the BRI in the First Belt and Road Forum in Beijing in May 2017, emphasised that the BRI must ensure “sus- tainability, effectiveness and inclusiveness, openness, mutual respect and beneﬁts, and compliance with the UN Charter and international law” (Ao 2019). It is because of the thorny issues in Sino-Vietnam relations and the lack of political trust that it became necessary for President Quang to reiterate the principles of cooperation expected from China during the implementation of the BRI. It has also been reported that the govern- ment of Philippines led by President Ferdinand Marcos has cancelled the rail projects that were concluded with China by the previous adminis- tration under President Rodrigo Duterte, and the Marcos government has made its plans of renegotiating the terms with China about ﬁnancing those cancelled projects (Preston 2022). Similarly, in Malaysia, Presi- dent Mahathir Mohammad, after assuming ofﬁce in 2019, announced the cancellation of some of the key BRI projects and put pressure on the Chinese leadership to meet at the negotiating table for reworking on the terms of ﬁnancing and for slashing the cost of the projects, in case Beijing wants to revive those stalled projects. For instance, Malaysia cancelled the 688 km-long East Coast Rail Link and also a natural gas pipeline in the eastern State of Sabah. The administration cancelled or deferred some

14 CHINA’S GEOECONOMIC MANOEUVRINGS IN SOUTHEAST … 251 US$23 billion worth of projects awarded to Chinese companies by the previous government (Ao 2019). The implementation of the BRI projects by the Chinese companies is also exacerbating the existing ethnic cleavages in the Southeast Asian societies. For instance, the Morowali Industrial Park (IMIP) project in Indonesia is noteworthy in this context. It is a 5000-hectare fully inte- grated smelting and Manufacturing Park in the Central Sulawesi regency. The Torajas, a Southern Sulawesi ethnic group, have been brought to work in the smelters. But IMIP hired other ethnic groups that do not get along with the Torajas, so that the Indonesian workers cannot collectively mobilise against the Chinese IMIP (Ismail and Camba 2022). China’s Hydro-politics in Southeast Asia A mention must also be made of how Southeast Asia is paying the price for China’s dam-building frenzy to meet its own energy requirements necessary to propel its economic growth. Also, it is a fact that one way of wielding power is by controlling resources needed by other coun- tries. This is one critical issue which is emerging to be very important in China’s relations with its smaller neighbours, in the new millennium. The Mekong River originates in China and passes through lower riparian states like Myanmar, Laos, Thailand, Cambodia and ultimately Vietnam, before it joins the South China Sea. Therefore, any activity by China as the upper riparian state will have direct spill over effect on these Southeast Asian states. China completed its ﬁrst hydro-electric dam on the Mekong, the Manwan Dam, in 1955. Over the years, China has built not less than 11 hydropower dam projects in the upper Mekong basin, and many more dams have been planned to be built by China in the near future. It has built hundreds of dams on the tributaries of the Mekong. This dam-building frenzy has caused severe crisis in Vietnam’s water reserve as the Mekong has always been its lifeline. These mainstream dams have created decreased water ﬂows from the upstream, thereby aggravating the salinity of the Mekong River in Vietnam and have been generating anxiety in the Vietnamese government to provide adequate fresh water supplies to its population. Apart from difﬁculties in providing freshwater, the government is also very much worried about the ecological diversity, the agricultural output and the ecological balance in the Mekong Delta. It therefore does not come as a surprise that in the year 2016, Vietnam

252 T. MUKHERJEE had to face a major drought situation. About 80 per cent of Vietnam’s total rice produced for export comes from the Mekong Delta. As China has pledged to achieve net-zero carbon emissions by 2060, making arrangements for hydro-electricity by building more and more dams on the Mekong and its tributaries remains one of the most viable options before China to meet its goal of providing clean energy for the country. The numbers and varieties of ﬁsh have decreased drastically over the years. The ﬂood seasons aren’t following the same pattern anymore; about 20 per cent of the world’s freshwater ﬁsh used to be supplied from Mekong, which has been badly hit today. A study from the US- based climate consultant Eyes on Earth informs us that for six months in 2019, China’s dams blocked an unprecedented amount of water from entering the lower Mekong. The amount of water withheld was so large that, for the ﬁrst time since modern records have been kept; there was no monsoon-driven rise in water levels just over the Chinese border in Chiang Saen, Thailand. China has been restricting more and more water over time—particularly during the monsoon seasons—since its behemoth Nuozhadu Dam was institutionalised. Reportedly, the level of the mainstream is falling so steeply that irri- gation pumps are not reaching to that level, which has thereby, badly hit the irrigation sector. The Thai government, for instance, had to conduct relief efforts by deploying its army because of this. This may snowball into a massive food crisis in Southeast Asia (Eyler 2020). As per the research conducted by the Mekong Region Futures Institute and the International Rivers Hydro Scorecard, around 55% of all Chinese over- seas dam investments are in Asia; of that, 55% i.e. around 73% are in Southeast Asia. Within the Mekong Basin alone, 81% of overseas Chinese dams are in Laos. According to POWERCHINA, which is China’s leading hydropower developer, planned dams in Laos are expressly marketed and designed to power the construction of China-Laos Railway Project, which is an integral part of the BRI (Guerreiro 2021). So, the socio-economic effects of these dams will be borne by these lower riparian states, the host countries, to apparently fuel the ‘China Dream’ of President Xi. As a result of deep-seated political distrust for China, several South- east Asian countries have been improving relations with other countries, such as Japan, India, Australia, France and the USA, who can possibly act as re-balancers in the region. For instance, Japan has challenged China’s sovereignty claims in the Senkaku/Diaoyu Islands. The People’s Liberation Army’s (PLA) announcement, in November 2013, of an Air

14 CHINA’S GEOECONOMIC MANOEUVRINGS IN SOUTHEAST … 253 Defence Identiﬁcation Zone (ADIZ) over the East China Sea region that overlapped the Japanese, the South Korean and Taiwanese zones was a momentous development in this context which angered Japan signiﬁ- cantly. Since 2007 and particularly since the time of announcement of the Maritime Silk Road project, the USA, Japan, India and Australia have been holding talks to collaborate, for coming together on a single platform and they could ﬁnally do so by forming the ‘Quad’ or the ‘Quadrilateral Security Dialogue’. Interestingly, the Vietnamese Navy has participated in the Malabar joint naval exercises of these countries, which are normally held in the Malabar Coast, off Bay of Bengal, much to the chagrin of China. China has even labelled Vietnam as a ‘shadow member’ of the Quad. With the aim to curb China’s growing military inﬂuence in Southeast Asia, India and select ASEAN countries have begun holding joint military exer- cises. The Indian Navy has been regularly conducting joint military and naval exercises not only with Vietnam but also with Singapore, Japan, South Korea, Indonesia and Malaysia to ensure the safety of the strategic waterways in the region such as Straits of Malacca and Isthmus Kra. India and Vietnam have also engaged in the PASSEX Naval exer- cises that include signal drills, surface manoeuvres and communications, thereby inviting much wrath of China as it regards the Indo-Paciﬁc region as vital to its security. Vietnam has welcomed greater American involve- ment in the South China Sea, while Philippines, Indonesia, Malaysia, Taiwan and Brunei have subscribed to a similar stance. On 16 April 2013, China released its eighth white paper on defence. Among other things pertinent to this discussion, the most crucial feature of this document was the declaration that China will play a greater role in the global affairs. It also critiqued the American rebalancing postures in the Indo-Paciﬁc. Moreover, not only did it term the South China Sea as its ‘core inter- est’—which eventually led to 12 out of 27 countries criticising the move in the ASEAN Regional Forum—it also hailed and lauded the trailing incidents of USS Impeccable in 2009 and USS Cowpens in 2013 by the Chinese Navy in the South China Sea (Kondapalli 2008,3 – 6 ) . Not surprisingly, even the Chinese Confucius Institutes have received mixed reactions from the common Southeast Asian people. These insti- tutes are bodies that promote Chinese language and culture in other countries. Beijing has been pushing these institutes as part of development of its bilateral relations with other countries. Ofﬁcial Chinese government estimates inform us that more than 500 such institutes have been opened

254 T. MUKHERJEE in 140 countries. In Southeast Asia, till the beginning of 2017, China had set up 31 Confucius Institutes as well as four Chinese Cultural Centres in Thailand, Singapore, Laos and Cambodia (Parameswaran 2017). Why would these countries need to open branches of such institutes for the promotion of Chinese language, culture and way of life on their soil is not known with certainty, and therefore, the reception has been mixed with some iota of suspicion surely present. Conclusion In sum, then, it can be said that China led by President Xi Jinping is being criticised for following a style of diplomacy which is too ‘aggressive’ in matters which Beijing construes to be in its ‘core interest’. Even though it insists on its ‘peaceful rise’, the PLA hawks have been regularly rattling their sabres and China has been engaging in covert and overt manoeuvres in South China Sea, to further its own interest, turning a deaf ear to the complaints of its neighbouring countries and blatantly ﬂouting interna- tional norms. The Southeast Asian countries have found themselves to be at the receiving ends of how the ‘China Dream’ is being played out. Even though, these countries overwhelmingly believe that China is a crucial economic and political player in the region, there is much apprehension about the dubious nature of economic partnerships with China, since it is in a position to dictate the terms of partnership with these smaller countries. China-funded developmental projects in these neighbouring countries are often proving to be debt-traps in which these countries are ﬁnding themselves ensnared, which is why, several projects are now being cancelled or their terms are being re-negotiated. Many of these countries have also intensiﬁed their political relations and defence ties with countries like the USA, India, Japan, Australia and France who too have publicly criticised China for its ‘excesses’ in several sectors. These countries can maintain the balance of power in the region. Therefore, how China can use its excellent economic linkages with the region to build political trust will determine, to a great extent, the future trajectory of its rise. Not least, it shall also determine how peacefully China can achieve its objective of becoming a global leader in terms of national strength and international inﬂuence.
