CHAPTER 15 Neoliberalism, Geoeconomics and the Chinese Reality: The BRI Experience in Southeast Asia Ishani Naskar The world is a fast-changing one, albeit complex. From the second half of the past century, neoliberal economics and accompanying political values have largely underscored international politics. However, into 1920s, it is very evident that the neoliberal order has undergone considerable changes, putting to question the supremacy of the US-led global order. A recent work traces the rise and fall of the neoliberal order that has led to the attrition of the American Dream and the Washington Consensus (Gerstle 2022). The ﬁnancial recession in 2008 seemed to have laid bare the inﬁrmities of the open economy. It justiﬁed the need for the return of nationalistic fervour and populism both at the domestic and interna- tional levels. The seemingly quixotic decisions of the Trump presidency to dissociate from the Trans-Paciﬁc Partnership can be contextualised thus. I. Naskar (B) Jadavpur University, Kolkata, India e-mail: akiksha@gmail.com © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_15 257

258 I. NASKAR A notable aspect of the contemporary liberal and neoliberal order is the rising signiﬁcance of regionalism. Regionalism developed in the Cold War period, but what remains noteworthy is that it is still devoid of any universal formatting, template or attributes. The evolution of regionalism ought to be contextualised in the fact that in the recent international relations, globalisation can be dissociated from the dominance of the US. There are now plural world views of neoliberal economy and order. For instance, there has been an attempt to create a pan-Asian economic space in Asia. Two trends underpin this observation. First is the growing salience of the mega trade pacts that have become symptomatic of the failure of the Doha Rounds of global trade negotiations—the Regional Comprehensive Economic Partnership (RCEP) is an example thereof. The second trend is the remarkable transition of the Chinese economy enabling it to act as the binding force that has boosted inter-regional trade and strengthened the East Asian regional architecture. Whereas ASEAN occupies the driver’s seat as the primary regional mechanism, albeit rooted in Southeast Asia, two other pan-Asian regional mechanisms have become integral to the regional dynamics of the region—the ASEAN Plus Three grouping and the East Asian Summit. This in turn is supplemented by the currency swap arrangement of the region—the Chiang Mai Initiative and the Asian Bond Initiative, with the Asian Bond Market taking care of the domestic and regional bond interdependencies (Ravenhill 2010). Some scholars have described this as East Asian Regionalism but this also has been described as the New Asian Regionalism. An expert argues that the New Asian Regionalism is witness to the steady ﬂow of global investments attracting the interests of govern- ments and business communities; this has been made possible for two reasons. First, the hub of economic activity has shifted from the West to Asia energising the commercial vibrancy of the region. Second, Asia is on a path of integration riding a ten-fold increase in Free Trade Agreements (FTAs) (Hsieh 2021). The ASEAN-led mechanisms involve economies of the Asia–Paciﬁc creating a larger legal framework that underlines the New Asian Regionalism. This in turn is strengthening the New Regional Economic Order (NREO), set to underline the new multi-polar economic order. This new ordering will see a rise in the scope of South-South Cooperation and the challenge to both the Washington Consensus and the Brussels Effect. The Chinese have proposed an alternative in the form of the Beijing Consensus that rejects the Western standards of GDP and per capita income as important markers for understanding economic

15 NEOLIBERALISM, GEOECONOMICS AND THE CHINESE … 259 growth. However, the Beijing Consensus has its own set of challenges in terms of acceptability and the larger question of Chinese geopolitical manoeuvrings vis-à-vis the international order. Therefore, the pivot of the new emerging economic multilateralism is ASEAN and the ASEAN plus mechanisms. The ASEAN Consensus as it is now being called is best suited for the geoeconomics of the region. It attempts to maintain both equidistance and ﬁne balance between the Washington Consensus and the Beijing Consensus; however, the geopol- itics of the region is dominated by the US narrative of the Indo-Paciﬁc. As a region, Southeast Asia is an example of collective bargaining of the Asian powers. However, China is not a part of the ASEAN ingenuity because China has its own geoeconomic and geopolitical objectives that are not essentially in sync with that of this neighbourhood. In fact, the focus of the rest of the discussion is to highlight how China’s Belt and Road Initiative has found an easy way into the sub-regional frameworks of the ASEAN region. The chapter argues that China is free-riding the process of integration of the region, especially in terms of connectivity and furthering the objectives of the BRI. We shall also try to read into the nature of the BRI, looking at it from a non-western perspective and see whether China’s forays into Southeast Asia is an example of how actually geoeconomics can help attain geopolitical goals, or, whether geopolitics is a priority in its own rights. The rest of the discussion shall be divided into three parts. The next section will look at the sub-regional framework in the ASEAN region—the Greater Mekong Sub-region. The following section will see how the BRI has found its way into the GMS region by providing support to the infrastructural activities of the region apart from trade linkages. The ﬁnal section will be an analysis of how the BRI is building up an alternative narrative of regionalism akin to the Asian context. The concluding section will try to argue how the BRI is a story of the interplay between geoeconomics and geopolitics. T he Greater Mekong Sub-Region- - -Steps Towards Partnership In the early 1990s, the countries around the Mekong Basin were plagued by several challenges that ranged from poor economic growth to conﬂicts and ethnic issues and painful histories. The countries include Laos, Cambodia, Vietnam, Myanmar, Thailand and the two Chinese provinces of Yunnan and Guangxi. Further, the region was the least connected in

260 I. NASKAR terms of physical connectivity making the possibility of any kind of inte- gration bleak at that point in time. In 1992, for the ﬁrst time, the ofﬁcial representatives of the Mekong region met in Manila at a meeting organ- ised by the then director general of the Asian Development Bank (ADB). The meeting was a stepping stone for establishing physical connectivity, encouraging a free ﬂow of both goods and people across the borders of these countries, and improving trade competitiveness. The idea was to address the issue of peace through economic development and recover the war-torn systems of Indo-China. That was made possible by the changing nature of the economies of the region—China, Vietnam, Laos and Cambodia. Morita recalled this ﬁrst meeting, “When we ﬁrst met, we did not even have a name for the group. This was discussed only in a subsequent meeting when it was decided to call it the Greater Mekong Sub-region.” He further describes, “We focused on our common values of modesty, spirit of cooperation, kindness and avoidance of conﬂict” (Morita 2012:4 ) . Pivotal to the GMS are the ‘economic corridors’ that were proposed at the Eighth Ministerial Meeting of the GMS in 1998. In the subse- quent economic ministerial meeting in 2000, three economic corridors were designated. . The East–West Economic Corridor (EWEC) runs from Vietnam to Myanmar. It is the only direct and continuous land route across mainland Southeast Asia. . The North–South Economic Corridor (NSEC) runs from southern China to the ports of Southeast Asia—there are three sub- corridors—Kunming–Bangkok via the Lao PDR or Myanmar; Kunming–Ha Noi–Hai Phong; and Nanning–Ha Noi. It is almost complete. . the Southern Economic Corridor (SEC) spans from Vietnam to Myanmar but at the lower latitudes. The corridor runs through southern Thailand, Cambodia, and southern Vietnam and includes the Phnom Penh–Ho Chi Minh City Highway Project. Yet, some parts of the corridors are still to be completed; for instance, the section from Bangkok to Dawei in Myanmar, newly added as a part of the SEC, is still unpaved. Or, the border between Meng La in Myanmar and Daluo in Yunnan along the NSEC does not allow passing by people of

15 NEOLIBERALISM, GEOECONOMICS AND THE CHINESE … 261 the third countries (Ishida 2019: 186). Apart from these corridors, trans- port projects include upgradation of the inland water navigation, seaports and railway network(s). There are plans to upgrade the two-lane roads to four-lane roads and, subsequently expressways. Improvements in connectivity have gone a long way to reduce the cost of transportation, reduced travel times and costs and are effec- tive in enhancing the potential of trade throughout the region. The economic corridors are helping in boosting trade and overall development converting towns and settlements in trading hubs (Asian Development Bank 2023). The sectors of cooperation that have been identiﬁed by GMS are— agriculture, energy, environment, human resource development, telecom- munication, tourism, trade and investment and transport. Into the third decade, the GMS strategy indicates a quantum shift in multisectoral investments—targeting urban and rural integrated development along the corridors mentioned above. It requires institutional reforms towards better transport and trade facilitation, power generation and agreements, focus on sustainable development and management of the environment. Most important the strategy is to promote the GMS as an integrated tourist destination that could ensure improved use of human resources (Hsieh 2021: 6–7). Thus, the ASEAN Mekong Basin Development Cooperation (AMBDC) is important in this context. The AMBDC is not only an economic framework for cooperation but also has a railway connectivity project amongst which the Singapore—Kunming Project is a ﬂagship project. The Project was proposed at the Fifth ASEAN Summit in December 2015. The Singapore-Kunming Rail Link (SKRL) was devel- oped to connect several ASEAN countries and China. It is being projected as a pan-Asian rail link(s) that includes three main routes from Kunming, China, to Bangkok, Thailand. The eastern link will be established through Vietnam and Cambodia, the central line will be laid through Laos and the western linkage is planned to pass through Myanmar (SKRL 2023). The railway link is one of the critical components of the Master Plan on ASEAN Connectivity, yet the progress is really slow because the orig- inal plan was to complete this mega project way back in 2015. There are many missing links in train connectivity (Chankosal 2013: 11). However, the major reason for the inability to complete the project is the cost of

262 I. NASKAR this project which was put at more than a billion dollars. 1 While the story of AMBDC is yet to ﬂourish, there are underlying cross-currents between Vietnam and Thailand over inﬂuence in the Indo-China region and vying for China’s attention (Yoshimatsu 2010: 7–11; Narine 2002). One can see Thailand projecting its Ayeyawady-Chao Phraya-Mekong Economic Cooperation Strategy (ACMECS) 2 against Vietnam’s East–West Corridor and CLV Triangle Development (Vietnam Investment Review June 17, 2019). On the other hand, Malaysia has always been supporting the AMBDC. In the meanwhile, it was realised that China has started to make forays into the region. However, before discussing how China’s BRI has made inroads into this region, the following section will be a concise discussion of the Belt and Road Initiative. Belt and Road Initiative: A ‘Policy Envelope’ One should begin with the understanding that for the Chinese Commu- nist Party, history and heritage is a huge repository of political capital that the leadership (across generations) has been drawing from to legit- imise policies. Under Xi Jinping, the idea is rejuvenated under the larger rubric of spiritual rejuvenation of the Chinese nation (Wang 2021). His recent urge to advance the study of Chinese civilisation to strengthen the cultural conﬁdence of the country is important (Global Times May 28, 2022). Placed in this context, one can recollect that under the Han Dynasty (C. 206–220 BCE) trade routes were established, mostly conti- nental that reached to Central Asia and West Asia and conducted trade 1 Under the AMBDC, ﬁfty-ﬁve projects were organised by 2005, covering a number of sectors including infrastructure, trade and investment, industry, minerals, agriculture, forestry and tourism. Of these projects, thirty-one secured funding while the remaining twenty-four required roughly $87.5 million of funding support. Therefore, the major cause of this slow progress is the lack of sufﬁcient ﬁnancial resources, particularly after the Asian ﬁnancial crisis in 1997–1998. For details see ASEAN Annual Report 2005–2006: ASEAN at the Centre, Jakarta, ASEAN Secretariat, July 2006,p .2 7 . 2 The Ayeyawady-Chao Phraya-Mekong Economic Collaboration Strategy, or ACMECS, is a framework for collaboration between Cambodia, Lao People’s Democratic Republic, Myanmar, Thailand, and Vietnam that intends to leverage member countries’ distinct assets and promote balanced development in the subregion. Thailand’s Prime Minister Thaksin- Shinawatra initiated the establishment of this framework of cooperation in April 2003. Transportation, as well as trade and investment facilitation, are examples of collaborative sectors.

15 NEOLIBERALISM, GEOECONOMICS AND THE CHINESE … 263 with the Ottoman Empire until it was shut down in 1453. In 2013, Pres- ident Xi Jinping declared China’s regional policies. He referred to the ancient trade and cultural contacts between China and countries around; he was only connecting the dots drawing from historical capital. The two routes thus mentioned, the ‘21st Century Maritime Silk Road’ initiative and its complementary Silk Road were only about resuscitating century- old contact that the Middle Kingdom had with Asia and Europe. The whole initiative is brought under the single framework of One Belt One Road (OBOR). One of the discourses on the OBOR is that it projects the image of China as a civilisational power that draws heavily from its history of imperial (and not imperialist) relations with the neighbourhood both far and near. In his 2017 speech, President Xi Jinping made exten- sive reference to the historical roots of and civilisational links of the BRI (Xinhua May 16, 2017). Building on this discourse, Chinese reconnect is not driven only towards its particularistic advantage but driven more for the larger beneﬁt of all those who join the BRI, which ﬁnds expression in ‘win–win cooperation’. There is no clear-cut ofﬁcial deﬁnition of the BRI. The closest to that can be found in the document ‘Visions and Actions on Jointly Building Silk Road Economic Belt and the 21st Century Maritime Silk Road’ jointly published by the National Development Reform Commis- sion, Ministry of External Affairs and the Ministry of Commerce with due permission from the State Council of the People’s Republic of China. The route map of connectivity was thus laid as: The Belt and Road runs through the continents of Asia, Europe and Africa, connecting the vibrant East Asia economic circle at one end and the devel- oped European economic circle at the other, and encompassing countries with huge potential for economic development. The Silk Road Economic Belt focuses on bringing together China, Central Asia, Russia and Europe (the Baltic); linking China with the Persian Gulf and the Mediterranean Sea through Central Asia and West Asia; and connecting China with Southeast Asia, South Asia and the Indian Ocean. The 21st-Century Maritime Silk Road is designed to go from China’s coast to Europe through the South China Sea and the Indian Ocean in one route, and from China’s coast through the South China Sea to the South Paciﬁc in the other (National Development Reform Commission March 28, 2015). It further mentions that,

264 I. NASKAR On land, the Initiative will focus on jointly building a new Eurasian Land Bridge and developing China-Mongolia-Russia, China-Central Asia-West Asia and China-Indochina Peninsula economic corridors by taking advan- tage of international transport routes, relying on core cities along the Belt and Road and using key economic industrial parks as cooperation plat- forms. At sea, the Initiative will focus on jointly building smooth, secure and efﬁcient transport routes connecting major sea ports along the Belt and Road. The China-Pakistan Economic Corridor and the Bangladesh- China-India-Myanmar Economic Corridor are closely related to the Belt and Road Initiative and therefore require closer cooperation and greater progress (National Development Reform Commission March 28, 2015). It is noteworthy that, although the BRI seeks to connect well beyond the immediate neighbourhood into Africa, its primary scope of connec- tivity is the neighbourhood and Europe, i.e. Eurasia and East Asia. To put it in a nutshell, the BRI has ﬁve geographical regions—Southeast Asia, South Asia, Central Asia, West Asia, Central and East Europe. This is supplemented by the Maritime Silk Route (MSR) that links, East Asia and the Chinese coast through the South China Sea, Indian Ocean and the Bay of Bengal to Southeast Asia and South Asia, and further to West Asia and Europe through the Persian Gulf and Mediterranean Sea (Palit 2017). The MSR is a single channel of connectivity linking China with East Asia, South and Southeast Asia and West Asia (Garlick 2020: 10). Likewise, it has been observed that the maritime arm of the BRI is bolstering economic inter-connectivity between China and the maritime states of Southeast Asia, South Asia and the Middle East (Clarke 2018: 91). However, one must make a mental note of the fact that regions as ‘constructs’ are largely associated with the Western discourse; they are underpinned by geoeconomic and geopolitical interests. China’s BRI therefore need not and does not follow the same regional constructs. As an example, one can read Qian’s work which gives the impression that BRI treats Pakistan within the larger framework of West Asia and is singled ‘out of’ the South Asian framework. According to him, ‘the proposal of the Belt and Road’ initiative, construction of the ‘Pakistan energy corridor’ and the ‘Gulf Pearl Chain’ will cover the Arabian Penin- sula, and in turn, promote the joint-building of ‘the Belt and Road’ (Qian 2016: 28). There is thus a deliberate geographical ambiguity as one of the deﬁning features of the BRI. Moreover, BRI is evolving through newer dimensions; there is already a BRI 2.0 in the ofﬁng. Thus,

15 NEOLIBERALISM, GEOECONOMICS AND THE CHINESE … 265 it is not only about geographical imprecision but also about modula- tions and modiﬁcations in the policy approach of the BRI. Therefore, one can conceive of the BRI as a ‘policy envelope’ (Garlick 2019:4 ; Zen 2019), that can ‘ﬁt in’ as well as ‘keep out’ according to evolving demands or situations or both. However, contemporary critical literature gives an idea of how BRI’s policy envelope feature allows for a plurality of interpretations to the extent that it can cause tensions within various domestic constituencies for resources and power (Jones and Zen 2019: 1415–1439). Yet, as far as China’s regional engagements and activities are concerned, the BRI has also become one of the main channels and avenues for Chinese organisations and associations to promote trade and business. An example could be the China and the Central and East Euro- pean 17 + 1 Forum. This mechanism which began in 2012 was designed to promote business and trade relations with the countries of the region. 3 Similarly, for the Arab states, there is the China-Arab States Cooperation Forum that will facilitate the cooperation between China and the Arab countries. The Arab Policy Paper published in 2016 envisions the kind of relationship China is seeking with the region, which is supplemented by Xi’s proposal for a 1 + 2 + 3 pattern of cooperation (Shiwei 2022). China’s relations with the Central Asian countries have been institu- tionalised through the Shanghai Cooperation Organisation (SCO) since its formation back in 2000. The SCO is a forum that provides China with the scope to develop a regional framework involving critical neighbours of Central Asia, Russia and India according to the Chinese conception of regional engagement. In addition, in 2022, China’s energy-rich province of Shandong hosted the First China-Central Asia Industrial and Invest- ment Cooperation Forum. Zhu Yongbiao, executive director of Lanzhou University’s Belt and Road Research Centre, made the observation that the forum is another building block for the BRI. He opines that “[F]rom a practical perspective, the forum will promote cooperation between China and the ﬁve Central Asian countries in energy, food, climate change 3 In 2021 Lithuania followed by Latvia and Estonia left the CEE 17 + 1 Forum making in 16 + 1. Again in 2023 in context of China’s support to Ukraine, the Czech Republic declared that they are no longer ‘an active member’ of the mechanism. See, Start Lau, “China’s club for talking to Czechs is dead Czechs say”, Politico, May 4, 2023, available at, https://www.politico.eu/article/czech-slam-china-xi-jinping-pointless-club-for-central- europe/.

266 I. NASKAR and other areas under the Belt and Road Initiative (BRI)” (Mingyang and Cong 2023). In South Asia, however, China has not been able to bring up either any equivalent of SCO or any alternative to the regional organisation of the region—SAARC. Instead, China has taken a more customised approach with the individual countries ﬂooding them with funds and building infrastructure such as the CPEC in Pakistan, the Hambantota Port Project in Sri Lanka, the Podda Setu in Bangladesh, dam construc- tions and Pokhara Airport in Nepal and the expansion of Velena Airport and Sinamale Bridge in Maldives. A Carnegie Foundation Research paper mentions that during the pandemic, China made diplomatic inroads through medical assistance by providing badly needed medical equip- ment, biomedical expertise, and funds to deal with the coronavirus (Pal 2021). During COVID times China created more functional initiatives like the South Asian COVID Initiative. Research in the post-COVID period reveals that China has used the pandemic as an opportunity to make further forays into South Asia—like Bangladesh, Nepal, Sri Lanka, and Pakistan. 4 In the post-COVID period, these countries have seen a rise in tourism with a rise in footfalls of the tourists from China. This has helped the countries to recharge their sliding economy to a certain extent. Besides, there is a rise in the number of Confucius Institutes that have been established in these countries; alongside, scholars and students are given full scholarship offers from China, and a section of the local media, both print and digital actively promote the BRI in their respective coun- tries (Attanayake and Haiqi, 2021). To put all this in perspective, in South Asia, China’s BRI has manifested in the form of bilateral mollycoddling, targeting vulnerable economies and free-riding the individual insecurities some countries of the region have vis-à-vis India. The presence of India in 4 Since 2020 initiated and China continued virtual dialogues with South Asian countries focusing on strengthening cooperation in ﬁghting the COVID-19 pandemic. Proposals were made to extend the CPEC to Afghanistan and China also place a proposal to build the Trans-Himalayan Economic Corridor with Nepal jointly. China also made a proposal to organise a China-South Asia E-commerce Cooperation Forum on Poverty Alleviation in Rural Areas. Important to note that the participants agreed to continue their cooperation through regular communication and consultations. 15 NEOLIBERALISM, GEOECONOMICS AND THE CHINESE … 267 South Asia will prevent China from either designing or instrumentalising any regional grouping to enmesh with the broader scope of the BRI. Compared to South Asia, China has made a noticeable foray into Southeast Asia with a special focus on the GMS region. For instance, “The AMBDC’s ﬂagship project Singapore-Kunming Rail Link (SKRL) made little headway and headline for two decades until China made a splash in recent years with several high-speed train projects under its Belt and Road Initiative (BRI)” (Ha and Seth 2021: 3). The National Develop- ment and Reform Commission, the Ministry of Foreign Affairs of China, and the Ministry of Commerce of the People’s Republic of China issued the ‘Vision and Actions on Jointly Building the Silk Road Economic Belt and the 21st-Century Maritime Silk Road’ in 2015, with the authorisa- tion of the State Council. It underlined the importance of focusing on the China-Indo-China Peninsula Economic Corridors by utilising inter- national transportation networks, depending on core cities along the Belt and Road, and employing signiﬁcant economic industrial parks as cooper- ation platforms (National Development and Reform Commission 2015). Thus, the mandate of the BRI is to penetrate the economic corridors and connectivity of the GMS region. Two points are important here in the concluding part of this section. First, China is aware of the fact that many of the infrastructural projects that China has been investing in under the BRI are white elephants that will be giving little or no value to the recipients and will burn a hole in China’s pockets. The shape of the projects has also changed over time as ground realities and circumstances have changed especially after the pandemic. The much-hyped China-Pakistan Economic Corridor is an ideal example. To begin with, the experts and decision-makers who originally laid the blueprint of the CPEC were quite aware of the tech- nical difﬁculties and unfeasibility of the project. Thus, modiﬁcations of the original plan went to the extent that it almost resembled no corridor as was originally conceived (Small 2020: 20). Subsequently, in the post- COVID period, with changing strategic conﬁgurations both at the global and regional levels, with Pakistan’s domestic woes and its shrinking strategic leverage, with the emerging complications of the Sino-Pakistan relationship and with China’s economic challenges, its pitching for CPEC has changed. Now it has become more modest. 5 5 Even under BRI 2.0 CPEC’s position is not very bright. A look into the ofﬁcial website of the CPEC from the Pakistani side will show that until now only four projects

268 I. NASKAR Not only in the case of CPEC, various issues and accompanying controversies have emerged with other projects of the BRI in different parts of the world—Asia and Africa in particular questioning the viability, and sustainability of the BRI projects both from the donor’s and recipi- ent’s ends. 6 Yet the BRI is set to continue and a major rationale behind it is the geopolitical dividends it will fetch for China. But this is not new; in this context, one is reminded of Chinese President Jiang Zemin’s response to the criticism against the proposed Tibet Qinghai railway in 2001 (New York Times 2001). It is further understood that mega projects like the BRI are not about mere materialistic, economic goalposts, but a larger goal of normative intent that can project China as a potential global leader. This takes us to the second point. The BRI underscores China’s attempt at creating an ideational backdrop for the implementation and success of the project itself. This is more important in the context of the growing perception that China actually would not be able to deliver international public goods that are expected of it in case China wishes to provide alternative leadership to the West, particularly the US. One such argument is the Kindleberger Trap that projects China as a weak power rather than a strong contender to the US in assuming international responsibility. 7 As a counterargument, the BRI narrative speaks about under the CPEC have been completed. Xi Jinping’s proposal of an alternative form of international relations, his master narrative of the ‘China Dream’ of being resurrected to the global centre stage ﬁnds expression in the BRI. Materialistically this ﬁnds expres- sion in China’s grand display of its engineering prowess and ability to establish economic outposts worldwide, undertake major overseas infras- tructural projects, make huge energy investments and provide credit lines. When this is read in conjunction with the Chinese role in BRICS and the New Development Bank, it makes sense that the BRI also seeks to create an alternative reading of regional ordering. T he BRI and an Alternative Expression of Regionalism Before discussing the BRI as an alternative expression of regionalism, there is a need to understand how regionalism has become a critical component of Chinese foreign policy. The argument here is that China has developed its own comprehensive sense of security that understands the symbiotic relationship between economic development and security (Freeman 2018: 82). Thus, China believes that regional economic devel- opment is a way of achieving comprehensive security. Economic uplift of the larger neighbourhood could ensure security that would in turn assure China of fewer domestic threats, and less disturbed borders. It is a well- known fact that China faces domestic threats in Xinjiang. China believes that by promoting its own version of comprehensive security, Chinese foreign policy would be in a position to address both domestic challenges and quell adverse dynamics that threaten its neighbourhood. There is a background to this. Right from the time of President Hu Jintao, China had underlined the principles of ‘peace, development and cooperation’ that directed Chinese participation in multilateral institutions like the UN and also encouraged positive bilateral relations with individual coun- tries. Further, Jintao’s proposal of a ‘harmonious world order’ bore the message that China would remain mindful of the interests of other nations and China, Springer, 2023, pp.161–165. For the four types of debt traps read, Yanzhong Huang, “The Four Traps China May Fall Into”, Council on Foreign Relations, October 30, 2017, available at, https://www.cfr.org/blog/four-traps-china-may-fall.

270 I. NASKAR and would interpret foreign policy in a non-military, non-combative way (Mederois 2009: 45–60). At present, Xi Jinping, in his idea of ‘Compre- hensive National Security’, has underlined the need to diversify Chinese foreign policy towards economic, technological and geopolitical leader- ship. Therefore, contemporary Chinese foreign policy is looking forward to delivering public goods and harnessing the potential of the regional mechanisms of the region. Now when one looks at Southeast Asian regionalism like the ASEAN and sub-regional frameworks like the GMS, an interesting plethora of literature connects regionalism with a state’s foreign policy to achieve security in a larger sense, towards comprehensive security. Since the Asian economic meltdown, states have resorted to multilateral mechanisms to promote the idea of comprehensive security, which needs both collective will and collective responsibility. At the foreign policy level, it’s an attempt on their part to address economic insecurities that they cannot address individually. Thus, multilateralism in this part of the world has ensured what Pempel described as ‘institutional buffering’ against global ﬁnan- cial distress and its impacts. It explains to a certain extent why there has been a surge in multilateralism in this region (Pempel 2010: 209–238). At this point, one could try to juxtapose (or connect) the Southeast Asian idea of comprehensive security with the Chinese one. China’s interest in comprehensive security that proposes collective economic development underscores Chinese FTA with ASEAN or master connectivity plans in the GMS region. Therefore, both China and the countries of Southeast Asia share a common approach in promoting regionalism and regional engagement as a part of their foreign policy. For China, connecting with regionalism is both an economic and strategic goal that is facilitated through the BRI. Perhaps no single theoretical approach is adequate to explain how BRI takes regionalism as a part of its policy. It implies two things. First, it implies, that in promoting an alternative normative ordering, the BRI necessitates a different approach to regionalism. In this context, Kavalski has explained elaborately how the Chinese understanding of norms is based on relationships and not on uniformity or appropriateness (Kavalski 2023: 247–267). This takes us to the second implication—the Chinese approach to regionalism encourages the idea of ‘co-constitution’ or ‘mutualisation’ of practices, values and norms (Garlik 2020:9 ) . I n this manner, the ultimate objective of this normative mandate of the BRI is to persuade member countries of the region to appreciate the

15 NEOLIBERALISM, GEOECONOMICS AND THE CHINESE … 271 Chinese approach towards regionalism. This would allow Southeast Asian countries to accept the Chinese infrastructural and economic forays into the region. Thus, terms like ‘win–win cooperation’ were one amongst the lexicon that would underscore the Chinese materialistic approach to regionalism as much as BRI’s ideational foray into Southeast Asia. Conclusion At a discursive level, the dominant understanding of the BRI in South- east Asia is an interface between geoeconomics and geopolitics. As Small ( 2020: 9) observed, BRI projects are impossible to dissociate from geopo- litical dividends. Wigell makes a strong case for the growing importance of geoeconomics over geopolitics, and how regional powers are using economic policies as a tool of their foreign policy to achieve strategic goals. He underlines that geoeconomic calculations are a serious compo- nent of regional powers’ geo-strategy and identiﬁes China as one of those regional powers (others being Brazil, Germany, India, Japan, Russia, and South Africa). He further identiﬁes four manifestations of these geoe- conomic strategies—neo-imperialism, neo-mercantilism, hegemony, and liberal institutionalism (Wigell 2016: 135–151). Herein many may differ about China’s regional status, but it is better to think of China as a rising regional power with geoeconomic strategies that impact its regional neighbourhood and then beyond. China is an aspiring global power but not a global one. In terms of its military prowess, it is still behind the United States. However, it is in the economic domain that China is creating the global space for itself. In this context, the work by Bethany Allen throws light on how China has weaponised its economic poli- cies and activities to gradually change the shape of the world order in the future. She has argued how China is systematically using its compa- nies and businesses to neutralise competitors and threats—both economic and political (Allen, 2023). China does not believe in the neo-liberal ordering propagated by the West that also promotes liberal values. In this context, the way China’s BRI is designing an alternative form of economic integration with the neighbouring areas of the GMS sub-region, it is symptomatic of a new avatar of neo-liberal economics. One can also argue that China’s BRI-led projects in the GMS region indicate the triumph of Chinese geoeconomics free-riding the neo-liberal idea of economic integration. Time will tell what shape it takes in the future.

CHAPTER 16 India’s Role in the Indo-Paciﬁc Region: An Overview of Strategic Geopolitical Convergences Sayantani Sen Mazumdar Taking cue from historical evidences it can be stated that sea power was never of considerable relevance to either India or China. In fact, the geopolitical strategies of the two great Asian civilisations were steered more by the precept of continentalism (Raja Mohan 2013: 36). However, the process of globalisation largely galvanised these two Asian neighbours to accord impetus to extensive maritime strategy along with territorial defence from the mid-1950s onwards. Crucial initiatives of Den Xiaoping in the political arena like reintegration of China into the world economy, greater emphasis on bolstering equations with the major powers and a bid to establish a peaceful environment outlined the groundwork of addressing the perils in its borders from the early 1980s. As China began to undertake productive stances for the stabilisation of internal security S. S. Mazumdar (B) Diamond Harbour Women’s University, Sarisha, India e-mail: senmazumdarmimi@gmail.com © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_16 275

276 S. S. MAZUMDAR architecture through resolution of border disputes, it provided greater room for substantial nurturing of maritime paradigms. From the 1980s under the leadership of Prime Minister Rajiv Gandhi, India adopted outward-looking approach and began to open up. Since the early 1990s, India has been yielding the fruits of globalisation and got inclined to an active maritime strategy thereby being the harbinger of an expansive national maritime power (Raja Mohan 2013: 39). Geopolitics and Asia–Pacific Region The study of geopolitics underlines that Mahan’s perception of the expan- sion of Russian power and its urge of reinforcing control over the seas of all directions in Asia undoubtedly accredited his successors with the ability to decipher the popular geopolitical conceptions of ‘Eurasia’ and the unﬂinching struggle between ‘sea power’ and ‘land power’. Halford Mackinder in his arguments emphasised on the contestation between the ‘pivot’ area that primarily included Russia, Eastern Europe and Central Asia and the rest of the ‘marginal’ regions, which are placed around the Eurasian landmass. Mackinder asserted that Asia would be domi- nated by the one, who dominated the pivot area(Mahan 2003: 66). While Mackinder focused on the signiﬁcance of land power, Nicholas Spykman gave prominence on the coalition of maritime powers because of the ability to defeat the dominant forces in the heartland through the means of controlling the marginal regions in Eurasia(Spykman 1994). Conse- quently, there is the onset of a persisting geopolitical tension between the ‘heartland’ and ‘rimland’ in Eurasia. The credibility of the United States as a great power at the turn of the twentieth century and its potential to administer both the Atlantic and the Paciﬁc Oceans helped in developing the strategic representation of Eurasia and the relevance of the United States as the adjudicator among the many powers of that vast space. The concept or impression of heartland in Eurasia gained momentum more particularly after the disintegration of the Soviet Union and the contes- tation among the major powers for the expansion of inﬂuences in the newly independent republics in Central Asia. Crucial facets like Russia’s efforts to reclaim its eminence in the so-called near abroad in the ﬁrst decade of the twenty-ﬁrst century, its collaboration with China in Central Asia as well as Beijing’s paramount advocacy of the Shanghai Coopera- tion Organisation (SCO) have imparted renewed vigour to the notion of Eurasia. But it is to be borne in mind that the perception of Eurasia’s

16 INDIA’S ROLE IN THE INDO-PACIFIC REGION … 277 centrality in our geopolitical understanding of the world may not be in tandem with the emergence of Asian neighbours like China and India and their increasing maritime capabilities. Unlike Mahan and Mackinder, Spykman, having written after more than three decades, predominantly focused on the growth of large territorial units and on the possibilities of China and India becoming part of the great-power constellation in the near future. The steady rise of China and its joining the East Asian miracle in the early 1990s have been indicative of the fact that the world’s economic gravity has started shifting from the Euro-Atlantic to the Asia– Paciﬁc region. The increasing growth of India posed as a challenge to the expanding supremacy of China at the turn of the twenty-ﬁrst century. The implications of the rise of the two Asian giants made way for the impression of a permanent confrontation between the Eurasian rimlands and heartland to become less reasonable (Calder 1996). The prime focus of the Indo-Paciﬁc region is centred on the ocean, as it is the cord that conjoins its vast stretch and India does play a vital strategic role in the Indian Ocean. The terms ‘Indo-Paciﬁc’ and ‘Asia– Paciﬁc’ are often used interchangeably in geostrategic discourses but it is pertinent to underscore that these two terms are markedly different from each other. By ‘Asia–Paciﬁc’ the area of Asia lying in the Paciﬁc Ocean is denoted. It predominantly entails the coherence and common interests by the Asia’s Paciﬁc powers shared in their common region. The stretch of Asia–Paciﬁc into three parts comprising North-East Asia, Southeast Asia and Oceania highlights that territorially India is not a part of this Asia–Paciﬁc region, which gains credence more due to economic reasons rather than security ones. The multilateral institution, APEC (Asia–Paciﬁc Economic Cooperation Forum), underlines the increasing signiﬁcance of the Asia–Paciﬁc region, of which India is not a member (Javaid 2021). On the other hand, the Indo-Paciﬁc region portrays that expanse, which connects the Indian Ocean and the Paciﬁc Ocean as well as the ambit of the land surrounding them. The Indo-Paciﬁc region primarily projects the shift of relevance to the East from the West and the conjoining of the important sea lines of communication comprising the littorals of the two Oceans has rendered intense signiﬁcance both in strategic and economic terms. India is chieﬂy a maritime nation and there is substantive refer- ence of the practice of maritime trade in the annals of its international equations with other countries. India is connected with its major trade partners primarily through the Indian Ocean. It is estimated that nearly 70 per cent of India’s merchandise trade per annum is conducted via

278 S. S. MAZUMDAR sea route. India has been experiencing large-scale growth in commercial and subsistence activities at sea, which are imperative for its economic advancement as the maritime pursuits related to trade and transportation, resource exploitation as well as the leisure industry accrue better oppor- tunities for livelihood. Given the economic prospect of the Indo-Paciﬁc region, a number of countries are investing ample ﬁnancial, technological and human capital to expand maritime economies and are endeavouring to harness their unique strengths. Large volume of world’s oil shipments, nearly over 60 per cent traverse to China, Japan and other economies from the Middle East’s oil ﬁeld through the Indian Ocean and about 70% of the container trafﬁc passes through this Ocean to and from the indus- trial countries of Asia and other regions of the world. All these attributes render intense strategic relevance to the Indian Ocean thereby attracting the attention of China’s leaders and policy-makers. It is pertinent to underscore that the trafﬁc or trade transaction across the Atlantic has decreased considerably and that across the Paciﬁc is not actively mobile but conduction of trade across the Indian Ocean has escalated signiﬁ- cantly. But the Indian Ocean faces myriad security threats and certain parts especially the west around the Horn of Africa and next to the Strait of Malacca in the east are vulnerable to the alarming activities of the pirates and the terrorists, who are posing fundamental geopolitical threats to the stability of the region. The Ocean has also been a centre stage of confrontation among the major players on a number of occa- sions. For instance, in the case of India it started with the border conﬂicts ultimately culminating in the 1962 War with China, and Japan has for decades been involved in a dispute with China on the issue of control- ling of a group of islands in the East China Sea, referred to as Senkaku in Japanese and Diaoyutai in Chinese (Lintner 2018: 272). China’s keen- ness in opening a corridor down to the Indian Ocean was ﬁrst expressed in September 1985 as the country was advancing towards a market-oriented economy from rigid socialism. China in fact outlined the feasibility of ﬁnding an outlet for trade transactions for China’s landlocked south- western provinces of Yunnan, Sichuan and Guizhou with a combined population of 160 million people through Myanmar down to the Indian Ocean (Qi 1985). In addition to the tension along and across the border, the Indian Ocean emerges as the cause of animosity between the two neighbours, India and China. The Chinese are making forays into what India has always considered as its own arena and that might lead to a conﬂicting scenario. China is overtly vigilant and keeps a close watch on

16 INDIA’S ROLE IN THE INDO-PACIFIC REGION … 279 the sea lanes used by its suppliers of oil in the Middle East, a move that is challenging India’s primacy in the Indian Ocean. Recent collaborative naval exercises between India and the United States along with Japan’s concerns are indicative of the plausibility of a new ‘Cold War’ like situ- ation but this time China not the Soviet Union shall act as the main adversary(Lintner 2018: 24). Indo-Paciﬁc as a Realm of Strategic Dynamics It is asserted that the intricacies of the equations between China and India in the Indian and the Paciﬁc Oceans will be ordained not only by the rationale of the Sino-Indian bilateral relations but will be determined by the kind of policies that the other regional powers along with the United States as the leading maritime power in the Indo-Paciﬁc might endorse. The transition or development in the position of world lead- ership is intermittently connected with the shifts in allocation of the Seapower (Modelski and Thompson 1988: 18). A considerable boost in the volume of external trade of both India and China was visible in the last quarter of the twentieth century due to the fallout of globalisation on their respective economies. The purview of integration of China in 1978 and India in 1991 with the world economy expanded widely with the eradication of their voluntarily chosen economic isolation. Measures like less dependence on foreign trade, more emphasis on import substi- tution and according signiﬁcance to exports were adopted by both the neighbours in order to curb restrictions on imports thereby booming up the growth strategy. As a consequence, both the countries experienced growth in trade to a large extent. For example, the seaborne trade in China and India has touched upon 90% of total trade and the vast expanse of their economies, rapid rate of economic growth, growing interde- pendence and interconnectedness with the world economies mandate investments in naval modernisation by both the countries. Since India’s economic progress is directly related to the growth of its overseas trade and commercial transactions most of its trade is of maritime nature. India is steered to maintain parity, economic prosperity and naval capability, a prerequisite for protection of the nation’s vast and varied maritime concerns. It is often being cited that the American dispatch of the aircraft carrier USS Enterprise during India’s war with Pakistan in 1971 under- scored India’s imperative for a powerful navy (Tellis 1987: 185–219). China, too, being conscious about interconnectedness between national

280 S. S. MAZUMDAR economic development and sea power in this era of globalisation, has been consistently focusing on the building of a strong and modern navy (Raja Mohan 2013: 41). The US decision to send aircraft carriers into the Taiwan Strait to prevent Beijing’s intimidation of Taiwan in 1996 might have politically fuelled up the development of Chinese capacities for considerable reorientation of its maritime strategy and to thwart potential American interventions(Scobell 2000: 227–246). The prevalence of marked differences between the naval conﬁdence building measures and the conﬁdence building initiatives of the land- based armed forces is very much apparent. The naval forces are not separated or demarcated by national boundaries and while operating in international waters they exhibit distinctive facets like mobility, ﬂexibility, long-range and potentially global outreach. And unlike armies on the border that are mostly controlled tightly by the headquarters, the naval forces leave greater scope for initiative and leadership by the commander at sea. Navies, also as a matter of fact, are liable to intersect with commer- cial rules as well as law and order directives at sea thereby accruing greater opportunities for contact and communication between the navies. However, the distinct characteristics of the naval forces at times might be a cause of a series of difﬁculties. The intractable obstacles, variations on size, technical and operational abilities, and movement demand trade- offs between a range of parameters on both sides are not always easy to regulate. Factors like port calls, high-level naval exchanges and activities do improve equations between the naval establishments and render valu- able channels of communication. However, it needs to be underscored that they do not provide the political space for discussion and analysis of the conﬂict dynamics and the viable mechanism to address mutual inter- ests and misconceptions. While the political leadership concedes that the Asian neighbours, India and China are not a threat to each other and Asia has the credence to accommodate their objectives and simultaneous rises, the strategic communities on both sides are not so optimistic and have fathomed adversarial images of each other. It is a concerning factor for many Chinese scholars that India might cooperate with the US Navy to block Beijing’s energy lifeline at the Strait of Malacca. To address the increasing volume of misperceptions about each other’s strategic inten- tions in the Indo-Paciﬁc, India and China sought to launch a maritime security dialogue in April 2012, when the foreign minister of China, Yang Jiechi, was in India (Tuteja in Raja Mohan 2013: 203).

16 INDIA’S ROLE IN THE INDO-PACIFIC REGION … 281 The proliferation of China’s dominance more particularly in the ﬁeld of economic and commercial development has opened up a plethora of challenges to the United States, which is encountering an economic slow- down. The persistent rise of Chinese military strength and its increasing political leverage on its Asian periphery have raised fundamental ques- tions about the prospect of US primacy in Asia and the tenability of its regional alliances. The perception of a collaborative venture between India and the United States in response to China’s continued rise is also buttressed by the shared democratic values between New Delhi and Washington, which is in sharp contrast to Beijing’s authoritarian polit- ical system. Herein China stands as the primary challenge to the United States and India is a potential strategic partner. Tellis argues that the present American approach towards China is largely being deﬁned by the threat of the possible power transition and with China’s expanding supremacy, this American attitude might get all the more competitive and hostile. In this context, the possibility of India to become a signiﬁcant partner of the United States gets increased. Keeping in mind the existing juxtaposition between the two Asian neighbours it may be asserted that even before exerting inﬂuence on the US preponderance, China’s growth would directly act as an impediment to India’s elevation as a global player (Tellis et al. 2012). At this critical juncture, it is a matter of deep concern to decipher the role of the United States especially the manner in which it can address the Chinese preponderance and increasing naval power of India. The two countries possess the capacity to inﬂuence world’s maritime politics as they lead about two billion people to the seas. The changing dynamics are bound to exert major implications on all dimensions of the Indo- Paciﬁc, including the balance of power system in the region, exploration of opportunities on its littoral and the seabed as well as the safeguarding of peace and order including the maintenance of sea lines of communi- cation. The emergence of China and India and their expanding overtures on the Indo-Paciﬁc region call for the restructuring of the US maritime policies. The naval undertakings of China and India will recast the nitty–gritty of Sino-Indian relations as well. The outcomes of the Sino-Indian contes- tation in the Indo-Paciﬁc region are to a large extent conditioned by the reverberation of the United States to the simultaneous rise of China and India perhaps leading to the abrupt dawning of a ‘strategic triangle’ among Washington, Beijing and New Delhi. A strategic triangle implies

282 S. S. MAZUMDAR a condition in which the policies of one have far-reaching impact on the collaboration between the other two on a sustained basis in time and space. The Bush administration’s emphasis on the balance of power in Asia, which promotes freedom, has intensely induced the growth of schol- arly and public interest in a triangular dynamic among the United States, China and India (Ayres and Raja Mohan 2009). It can be stated that the changing power equations in the Indo- Paciﬁc region have made the US jittery and it has started vacillating between different policies. For instance, the foreign policy perspective under George W. Bush used to be inclined more towards India for the cause of restraining the Chinese power in Asia (Friedberg 2011: 245–263). The US policy to sustain American primacy in Asia involved a number of new initiatives like the strengthening of US alliances in Asia, rebalancing the global military disposition towards the Asia–Paciﬁc region and focusing on a new viable approach in order to endure the expansion of the Chinese power. The United States sought to expand its military partnerships and outreach in the arc extending from the Western Paciﬁc and East Asia into the Indian Ocean region and South Asia wherein defence cooperation with India is the strategic crux. India has been accorded central position by the United States as it is one of the largest and most dynamic countries in this region with paramount military capabilities. India emphasises on a set of principles upholding international security and prosperity and the US Defence Secretary Leon Panetta described India as the ‘lynchpin’ of the geopolitical strategies highly discernible in this vast stretch(Raja Mohan 2013: 241). Though Obama’s ‘pivot to Asia’ largely signiﬁed the strategic relevance of India New Delhi is keenly concerned about the shifting power balance between Washington and Beijing. India is quite apprehensive about the foreseeable US-China bilateral strategic understanding (Raja Mohan 2023: 241). Chinese construction projects, its attempts to turn tiny shoals and rocks into sovereign-territory islands in the South China Sea and the move- ment of warships have made the South China Sea one of the world’s most closely watched regions, with spy satellites from various countries zooming in on the region since any unusual development in this geopo- litical hotbed may adversely impact the balance of power. In many ways, China has won the battle for the Spratly Islands as other players like the United States, the Philippines, Vietnam, Malaysia, Brunei and Taiwan, avoid conﬂict that would have wide-ranging disastrous consequences.

16 INDIA’S ROLE IN THE INDO-PACIFIC REGION … 283 China’s motive behind the launching of the BRI cannot be properly assessed. Apparently it seems to be a platform wherein there is efﬁcient allocation of resources and integration of markets for the beneﬁt of all the participants but actually it is a well synchronised plan to exert Chinese dominance over much of the developing nations. Japan has offered infras- tructure development projects in the region as ‘quality infrastructure’ compared to China’s initiatives of ‘construction schemes’ as an alternative to the BRI (Lintner 2019: 217–235). Japan and India have embarked upon a number of endeavours like the Quad to bolster their coopera- tion. Although regional naval cooperation actually commenced in 2007 with annual collaborative exercises, called Exercise Malabar, by India, Japan, Australia, Singapore and the United States, they were initially conducted with relatively informal arrangements in the seas of Japan. But in 2015 Exercise Malabar was developed with more participants after an agreement between Prime Minister Modi and President Obama and the organisation of a joint naval activity by the United States, India and Japan in the Bay of Bengal. India’s ﬂeet is active in the region with substantial aircraft carriers, submarines and over 90 other kinds of ships. The Indian Navy is into a modernisation drive to promote its submarines and other vessels especially those that can detect foreign submarines. Meanwhile, China has also invested in various port projects in the region such as Kyaukphyu in Myanmar, Chittagong in Bangladesh, Hambantota in Sri Lanka and Gwadar in Pakistan. China is primarily a huge inland empire with a relatively short coastline for a country of its size and its landlocked inland provinces have to depend on outlets and channels through neigh- bouring countries for trade with the outside world. The deep seaport in Gwadar has immense strategic importance as it, combined with the Karakoram Highway, which was opened in 1979, links northern Pakistan with Xinjiang thereby rendering western China a direct outlet to the Indian Ocean. Kyaukphyu in Myanmar was constructed to realise the goal of development for Yunnan and other south-western Chinese provinces, which are distantly located from China’s own ports. Chinese plans to open a trade route from Tibet through Sikkim down to the port of Kolkata have been met with acute scepticism in India (Lintner 2018: 273).

284 S. S. MAZUMDAR India’s Role in the Indo-Pacific Region There is a growing realisation that a stable and secure maritime envi- ronment in India’s neighbourhood is a prerequisite for development and prosperity of the country. Efﬁcient and improved maritime connectivity is of utmost importance for strengthening of India’s regional and global trade. Greater maritime connectivity between India and its trade part- ners across the world is one of the bases for the integration of the economies of the Indo-Paciﬁc region. In his Keynote Address at the Shangri La Dialogue on 1 June 2018 at Singapore, PM Modi under- lined India’s key role in bolstering of maritime security and cooperation in the Indo-Paciﬁc region. A robust approach to rejuvenate India’s maritime connectivity with its neighbouring countries would not only accelerate India’s merchandise trade but also unravel opportunities for industrial development as well as highlight trade potential in the Indo- Paciﬁc region. For the sustenance of the facets of maritime commons like connectivity, security and cooperation, the Indo-Paciﬁc countries need to embark upon crucial initiatives leading to better facilitation of the warning or relief systems. For example, proper coordination and synergy among the Early Warning Centres, including for tsunami warnings, could co-exist with the Humanitarian Assistance and Disaster Relief (HADR) Exercises and SOPs (Standard Operating Procedures) for HADR for the capacity building measures in this area prone to natural disasters. There- fore, a priority of maritime cooperation in the Indo-Paciﬁc region is to achieve a secure, viable and effective multimodal transport system in the region. In this connection, certain propositions may be put forward like the organisation of a coordinated and collaborative maritime strategy of India with its immediate and extended neighbours. Initiatives like Sagar- mala may be referred to in this context. India has adopted substantial stances to build infrastructure facilities and capacity building measures at all the major ports under the Sagarmala Project, more particularly on the eastern seaboard. Collaboration with ports located in South- east Asian neighbouring countries like Myanmar, Thailand, Indonesia, Malaysia, Singapore and Vietnam can turn Indian ports conducive gate- ways to ASEAN countries and vice-versa. Moreover, the furtherance of multimodal interfaces between land and sea transportation needs to be encouraged. The expansion of interfaces with land, rail, water and pipelines is required since ports are the gateways and avenues to devel- opment. The protection of the maritime environment necessitates the

16 INDIA’S ROLE IN THE INDO-PACIFIC REGION … 285 proper and effective utilisation of the available resources (Economic Times 2023). Most of the Indo-Paciﬁc countries are signatories to the World Trade Organization (WTO) Trade Facilitation Agreement (TFA), apart from other pertinent bilateral or regional agreements on trade facilitation. Taking into account certain trade facilitation prerogatives, implemen- tation of regional trade facilitation layout is desirable. Training and capacity building measures should be the focus of the trade facilita- tion programme. Learning from each other’s strengths and weaknesses can contribute to regional integration in an effective way. It is impera- tive for the Indo-Paciﬁc countries to adhere to regulatory convergence in cases of transportation. For the purpose of sharing of information, generation of inter-operability of customs single windows as well as for dispute settlement they should abide by the customs cooperation mechanism. For the simpliﬁcation of trade procedures and promotion of value chains, they may opt for a paperless trading system. For the pioneering of the connectivity agendas, the countries may form working groups and conduct joint studies in order to formulate a vision and an Indo-Paciﬁc connectivity master plan. Increased maritime coopera- tion among the concerned stakeholders would not only enable them to deal with maritime threats and challenges but also facilitate coordina- tion through the prism of maritime multilateralism. It is necessary for the countries to accelerate technical cooperation, capacity building measures, interdependence of experience, knowledge and expertise through various international and national forums. Emphasis should be imparted for the implementation of connectivity projects like Project Mausam for favourable outcome. Greater maritime connectivity in the Indo-Paciﬁc region is ardently required to form greater markets and for the strength- ening of the security system. This will accrue not only to the increase of efﬁciency of maritime services but also lead to the proliferation of trade and investment in the Indo-Paciﬁc region. Discussion, interaction and most importantly dialogue will undoubtedly foster the essence of better mutual understanding and reconciliation. It is pertinent to under- score that the cooperative multilateralism in the Indo-Paciﬁc is directly related to the increase of the connectivity facilities and the decrease of transportation costs. India has modiﬁed its foreign policy approach from the traditional strategy of balancing power to an encouraging approach to balance threat. The balance of threat theory is premised on the notion that the states

286 S. S. MAZUMDAR collaborate to balance against threat dynamics rather than power alone (Walt 1990: 5). In this lieu, factors like geographical congruities, potential and intent of the source of the threat are the crucial variables (Walt 1990: 5). For instance, keeping in mind China’s geographical proximity and its aggressive military capabilities vis-à-vis India, China may be perceived as a potential threat to India’s national security. It is mention worthy that under the multialignment policy, India is cultivating its relations with China’s competitors such as the United States and Japan. The momentum is undoubtedly gaining ground and India’s collaborative endeavours with the United States after ‘pivot to Asia’ policy and the United States’s extension of support to India’s membership in a number of multilat- eral institutions is generating an impression, most particularly among the Chinese population that the United States and Japan are aligning with India to balance China in Asia. India’s collaborative military exercises with the United States, Japan and other contenders of China have further solidiﬁed the perception (Chandra 2017: 107). China has pivotal interests in the region, as like India and Japan, China’s energy security is intermit- tently connected to the safety of the sea lanes of communication in the Indian Ocean. The impediments to energy ﬂows in the Indian Ocean do not come only from the contenders, who have a shared interest in keeping these sea lanes working but also from the local instabilities and hazards in the choke points and certain littorals, particularly the Strait of Hormuz and the Horn of Africa. These problems cannot be solved simply by military enforcement. During the visit of Chinese Premier Wen Jibao to New Delhi in December 2010, India and China professed their shared interests in the Indian Ocean and had consented to cooperate in countering piracy in the Gulf of Aden. It was probably the ﬁrst time that New Delhi and Beijing spoke jointly about maritime security cooperation (Raja Mohan 2013: 208). China and other states may attempt to espouse a strategy leading to the solution rather than breeding the problem. It is to be discerned that the ﬂeets or naval forces of the United States, Japan and India in a combined manner inevitably constitute overwhelming pres- sure on China’s oil supply. It is judicious for the Asian neighbours to have substantive discussions between their security establishments since there is an ongoing advancement of their respective economic interests in the seas as well as naval capabilities. China’s developmental agenda or the endeavour of stimulating the essence of maritime community based on mutual interests and shared future among the Indian Ocean countries are propelled by crucial facets.

16 INDIA’S ROLE IN THE INDO-PACIFIC REGION … 287 China would like to discourage scepticism about its intrusive advances in the region. It would like to underline its credibility as a development partner for economic growth, blue economy and pursuit of the Global Development Initiative in the Indian Ocean region. However, China’s moves in the region are seen as attempt at resisting Indian initiatives like Indo-Paciﬁc Oceans Initiative (IPOI). The IPIO underscores the strengthening of conﬁdence building measures and synergies among the countries on maritime and strategic cooperation. Being apprehensive of increasing Chinese forays in the Indian Ocean region, India has sought to stabilise IPOI and encouraged other countries and organisations like the ASEAN to get associated with the same in order to counter aggressive Chinese stances in the Indian Ocean (Sakhuja 2023). Contemporary intellectual deliberations and the public discourses are marked more by the attributes of hostility between the two neighbours, like the Indian scepticism of Chinese encirclement and Beijing’s appre- hensions of India collaborating with the United States in a naval blockade of Chinese resources. There is little scope for cooperation between the two countries in these analyses. India and China, however, must not undertake any measure that might threaten the security of the Indian Ocean region and it should in no way seem that they are hegemonic in their neighbourhood. Any initiative by either New Delhi or Beijing to form an exclusive sphere of inﬂuence in the waters adjacent to them is bound to create suspicion among the signiﬁcant players, which may propel them to form a coalition to restrain those ambitious overtures. The nurturing of any misperception about each other’s interests is uncalled for and there should be manifestation of some empathy to the concerns of the other. It is to be borne in mind that the maritime dynamics of China and India will not be preconditioned primarily by the overtures of New Delhi and Beijing directed at the other. Rather the naval policy parameters and actions are likely to be streamlined by the larger strategic environment in the Indo-Paciﬁc region. Both China and India need to be able to exert their relevance in their wider maritime neighbourhood. The other countries, their allies and various independent actors will respond robustly to the growing inﬂuence of China and India. It is this interde- pendent gesture that will shape the prospective stability in Asia and its waters. The propensities for the Chinese naval outreach to the Indian Ocean and the ramiﬁcations of India’s emerging proﬁle in the western Paciﬁc are likely to harmonise the Indo-Paciﬁc into a single geopolitical theatre (Raja Mohan 2013: 212).

288 S. S. MAZUMDAR Conclusion Historical inferences have emphasised on consultations and negotiations as the most pragmatic and effective strategy to resolve maritime disputes. In order to achieve the objective of win–win cooperation, it is imperative to manage differences through rules and devising parameters to establish regional peace and stability (Changlin 2017: 37–39). New strategic chal- lenges have arisen in the Indo-Paciﬁc region due to a number of factors like unresolved territorial disputes, the rise of terrorism, non-traditional threats like piracy, transnational crime, etc. Moreover, the existing struc- tures are not competent enough in confronting many current conﬂict situations like those arising from provocations or disturbances in the South China Sea. The emergence of international organisations like the ASEAN is signiﬁcant factor in geopolitical dynamics in the Indo-Paciﬁc region as India and the ASEAN share enduring cultural and historical connects and many political as well as economic lineages. Both sides promote attributes such as the rule of law, international norms and the respect for state sovereignty and multilateral cooperation besides several institutional similarities. The stakeholders in the region need to decipher each other’s concerns and priorities, and it is pertinent to underscore that connectivity can mitigate a number of challenges. Connectivity should uphold solidarity and be based on conﬁdence and trust. To promote connectivity, respect should be paid to sovereignty and territorial integrity of other countries. Due emphasis should be put on the attributes of consultation, good governance, certainty and clarity, vitality as well as sustainability, which are inclined to empower nations and safeguard them from debt burden. Promotion of trade and infrastructure is also needed. For the cause of peace and stability in the Indo-Paciﬁc region, it appears that the policy- makers in both China and India will be keen to venture all three ideas such as cooperative security, a dialogue and discussion process and balance of power. Their respective emergence and relevance have made them more open to regional cooperative mechanisms. But the Asian neighbours will not pursue multilateralism at the cost of their own autonomy. Beijing and New Delhi have exhibited profound interest in more formal dialogue and discussion on maritime security issues with other great powers and at the same time, they have actively followed the policy of balancing each other in the Indo-Paciﬁc region. The vast expanse of the Indo- Paciﬁc strategic space with its distinctive priorities, as the Paciﬁc for China

16 INDIA’S ROLE IN THE INDO-PACIFIC REGION … 289 and the Indian Ocean for India, can spur the emergence of subregional orders. The relative decline of the United States has not subdued its dominance in controlling both the oceans’ spaces as well as depicting the overriding security framework for the Indo-Paciﬁc stretch. The trajectory of the US equations with China and India and the evolving dynamics between Beijing and New Delhi are likely to be the basic contours of the prospective security architecture in the Indo-Paciﬁc region. Though other countries have a role in the political evolution of the littoral, it is the United States that exerts the most vital inﬂuence on the emerging Sino-Indian incongruities in the Indo-Paciﬁc region (Raja Mohan 2013: 243). It is interesting to perceive that the countries of the region, having recognised that there is much to be gained through cooperation rather than through rivalry, have exhibited keenness to collaborate with each other in economic matters. There is indeed much more progress that can be achieved through cooperative endeavours as the region has abundant commonalities that may be optimally harnessed for mutual beneﬁts. India has remained a valuable and important player in this region and has been prioritising connectivity and quality infrastructure through collaborative ventures. Regional harmony is crucial for India’s own geopolitical inter- ests and the strategies are also part of its commitment to implementation of long-term good friendship and cooperation among the regional players in order to attain alignment in common areas of interest and economic well-being of the region.
