CHAPTER 17 Analysing Opportunities for India in Africa’s Economic Integration in the Context of the African Continental Free Trade Area Abhishek Mishra The African continent and India share a unique, historical, and inti- mate relationship. They are both connected by ancient maritime links across the waters of the Indian Ocean and an endured common struggle against colonialism and apartheid. Over the years, this historical associa- tion has transformed into a multi-faceted partnership with an emphasis on economic cooperation and development partnership under the rubric of South-South Cooperation (SSC). A qualitative shift in India and Africa’s long-standing ties can be discerned in more recent time. Even during the COVID-19 pandemic, India stood in solidarity with Africa and gifted over 150 tonnes of medical aid to 25 African countries, despite facing many developmental challenges of its own (Beri 2021). Under India’s ‘Vaccine Maitri’ initiative, India supplied 24.7 million doses of ‘Made in India’ A. Mishra (B) MP-Institute for Defence Studies and Analyses, New Delhi, India e-mail: a.mishrapolsc@gmail.com © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_17 291

292 A. MISHRA COVID-19 vaccines en gratis, as well as commercially, which included COVAX supplies to 40 countries in Africa (Chakrabarty 2018). However, the disruptions associated with the pandemic are causing tectonic shifts beneath the global economic landscape. These shifts could eventually bring about signiﬁcant realignment in international relations and the manner in which nation-states would interact in future. Both India and African countries have stood by each other through the most trying times and the new economic realignments at the global level may open up new opportunities for mutually beneﬁcial partnerships between them. This will not only beneﬁt the combined 2.5 billion people residing in both the regions, but could also render greater stability to the rather frayed and fractured global economy. As two vibrant and development-oriented economies and growth poles, India and Africa continue to engage constructively and make efforts to strengthen their mutually beneﬁcial development partnership. Given that both sides are well endowed with natural resources, have access to technology, skilled manpower, entrepreneurs, and ﬁnancing, provides a good rationale for increasing two-way ﬂows in trade and investment. Over the years, India has put in together various important policy frameworks and institutional measures so as to create an enabling trade and business environment with countries from Africa. India’s policy-level focus on Africa was initiated with the launch of the ‘Focus Africa’ programme in March 2002 whose main objective was to increase interactions between the two regions by identifying the areas of bilateral trade and investment (PIB, GoI 2022). The programme initially focused on just 7 targeted countries: South Africa, Ghana, Tanzania, Mauritius, Nigeria, Kenya, and Ethiopia. At present, this programme has been extended to cover the entire African continent. There have been many other important initiatives like the Pan-African e-Network project (PAeNP) which was conceived by former President of India Dr A.P .J. Abdul Kalam and was formally launched on February 26, 2009. This was operationalised in 48 African countries which connected Indian educational institutions and super-speciality medical hospitals with their African countries through a satellite and ﬁbre optic network (Ministry of External Affairs, GoI 2013). This initiative was well-received by Africans and this became Indian government’s ﬂagship projects in Africa. In October 2019, India also launched a network known as the e-VidyaBharati and e-ArogyaBharati (e-VBAB) projects (Business Stan- dard 2018). The pan-Africa e-Network project was based on technology

17 ANALYSING OPPORTUNITIES FOR INDIA IN AFRICA’S … 293 connected to satellite. The e-VBAB has established web-based portals for tele-education and tele-medicine. So far, 17 African countries have joined this e-VBAB initiative. India had also launched the Duty-Free Tariff Preference Scheme (DFTP) for Least Developing Countries (LDCs) in August 2008 (Ministry of Commerce, GoI 2017). This scheme has beneﬁtted 38 African nations. Duty free access to 98.2% of India’s total tariff lines has been extended under the scheme. However, exclusion list includes fruits and vegetables, cashew nuts, tobacco, coffee, tea, spices, wine, and spirits, e t c . w h i c ha r e m u c hi nd e m a n di nA f r i c a . I n d i aw o u l db ee x p e c t e dt o include products that African countries are more interested in exporting to India. Overview of Africa in Recent Years Africa is a transitioning continent with huge trade and investment poten- tial. For a long time, the African continent used to make news for its conﬂict, poverty, or exoticism. Foreign Direct Investment (FDI) in Africa was almost exclusively focused on the extraction and export of natural resources. However, over the last couple of decades, Africa has embarked on a process of transformation and growth and has made remarkable progress in a range of economic and developmental areas. The continent is home to nearly 17% of the world’s population, 9.6% of global oil output, 90% of the global platinum and cobalt supply, half of the world’s gold supply, two-thirds of global manganese supply, 35% of the world’s uranium, 75% of world’s coltan, and 54 votes in the United Nations General Assembly (UNGA). Every year, 12 million young people enter the job market while only 3 million formal jobs are created, according to the African Development Bank Group (AfDB) (ADB Group 2016). With a median age of 25 years old, the African continent is the youngest in the world and its population is expected to more than double to 2.2 billion people by 2050. African economies have sustained unprecedented rates of growth, driven mainly by strong domestic demand, improved macroeconomic management, a growing middle class, and increasing political stability. The continent’s GDP is dominated by its commodity-dependent regions, largest being North Africa, followed by West Africa and Southern Africa (UNCTAD World Investment Report 2022). Tourism is a critical source of revenues for many African economies.

294 A. MISHRA However, the COVID-19 pandemic and the Russo-Ukrainian war are signiﬁcantly delaying convergence of African economies with the rest of the world. This year witnessed the continent growing at only 3.6% due to a worldwide slowdown, tighter global ﬁnancial conditions, and a dramatic pickup in global inﬂation (IMF Regional Economic Outlook 2022). Rising food and energy prices are impacting the continent’s most vulnerable, and public debt and inﬂation are at an all-time high. The continent faces an infrastructure ﬁnancing gap of anywhere between US$ 68–108 billion annually (ADB Group 2018). Ultimately, Africa’s safety and prosperity will depend upon tackling inﬂation while supporting growth, building a more food-secure continent, managing the uncertainty in oil prices and energy transition, and using digital currency innovation to deliver ﬁnancial services. It is estimated that by 2030, “Africa will be home to nearly 1.7 billion people and an estimated US$ 6.7 trillion worth of consumer and busi- ness spending” (Signe 2018). Increased political stability in recent years and improving regional integration through initiatives, like the African Continental Free Trade Area (AfCFTA), are making market access easier. The current economic growth and poverty-alleviation efforts mean that more than 43% of the continent’s people are expected to reach middle or upper-class status by 2030 (Signe 2020). According to UNCTAD’s World Investment Report 2022, investment ﬂows to Africa reached a record US$ 83 billion in 2021, rebounding strongly after the disrup- tion caused by the COVID-19 pandemic (UNCTAD World Investment Report 2022). Investments in the continent are moving beyond tradi- tional sectors like the extractive and commodity sectors, towards newer sectors like clean energy, data centres, and the construction of power plants and industrial complexes (Table 17.1). African countries were badly hit by the pandemic and the subsequent slowdown of the global economy, leading to a decline in manufacturing output, trade and investment volumes, tourism, etc. However, several African countries seized the opportunity of the crisis to embark on various macroeconomic and structural reforms. Such reforms are crucial for ensuring an inclusive growth over the long run. Some of these reforms include the uniﬁcation of the Sudanese Pound exchange rate to allow for the determination of currency exchange rates without the interference from the Central Bank of Sudan (Dabanga 2022); fuel subsidy removal reforms in Nigeria (Onuah 2022), etc., among others.

In 2020, the continent’s “total merchan- dise exports declined by 17% to US$ 394.6 billion, whereas imports contracted by 12.1% to US$ 505.2 billion, which subsequently led to widening of the trade deﬁcit” (Export–Import Bank of India 2022:2 ) . Thes continent’s recovery is dependent on the recovery of production and trade with its major partners, like China and the European Union (EU). In 2020, intra-African merchandise trade accounted for 16.2% of the total merchandise trade of Africa, lowest among the regions after Latin America and Oceania (Graph 17.1). In terms of commodity patterns, mineral fuels, oil and its prod- ucts continue to be the largest export item from Africa, whereas its import basket is relatively diversiﬁed compared to its exports. Mineral fuels, mainly dominated by petroleum oil, not crude, and machinery and mechanical appliances, are Africa’s two-largest import items. Other items include electronic and electrical equipment, vehicles, cereals, plastics, among others. In 2020, due to the low productivity and disruptions in value chains induced by the pandemic in United States as well as in Asia and Europe, there was a contraction in both the value and quantities demanded of exports from Africa. China was the largest market for Africa’s exports, accounting for 14.2% of Africa’s total exports. The UAE has replaced India as the second-largest destination for Africa during 2020 largely due to a decline in Indian import of petroleum oil during the same period. As regards imports, the share of traditional markets like France, the USA, Italy, Germany, as Africa’s key import sources has been declining in recent years. China was the largest source for Africa’s imports, accounting for 19.7% of Africa’s total imports in 2020. However, the continent’s share in China’s global exports has declined from 2019 to 2020. In 2021, China was the major export destination for African countries, followed by India, accounting for 6.5% of Africa’s total exports in 2021 (Export– Import Bank of India 2022: 39). India and Africa’sT r a d e R e l a t i o n s India’s relations with Africa are based on the principles of equality, mutual respect, and beneﬁt and are in line with Africa-led and Africa- owned development. There are four main pillars that deﬁnes India and Africa’s relations, namely; development partnership, trade and investment, defence and maritime security, and robust people-to-people contacts. In a post-pandemic world, the narratives of a rising India and a resilient Africa

17 ANALYSING OPPORTUNITIES FOR INDIA IN AFRICA’S … 297 present abundant prospects for a shared future and interconnected aspira- tions. The operationalisation of the landmark AfCFTA, which came into effect on 1 January 2021, is set to give further boost and lay the ground for deeper integration between India and African economies (Mishra 2018). Broadly speaking, trends in India and Africa’s trade relations show an upward movement. India’s bilateral trade with Africa has remained above US$ 60 billion except during the commodity price slowdown in 2016 and later due to the COVID-19 pandemic in 2020. According to data from the EXIM Bank, “trade between the two sides grew from US$ 68.6 billion in 2011–12 to US$ 89.6 in 2021–22, the highest ever level recorded by both the regions” (CII 2022). With the onset of the pandemic and in line with global developments, trade between the two regions moderated and slowed down during 2020–21 and rebounded in 2021–22, growing by nearly 60%. According to a study by the Confed- eration of Indian Industry (CII), “India’s exports to Africa accounted for 9.5% share in India’s total exports while Indian imports from Africa accounted for 8.1% of total Indian imports” (CII 2022: 12). Countries, like South Africa, Nigeria, Egypt, Togo and Kenya, have been India’s largest export destinations in Africa. On the other hand, South Africa, Nigeria, Guinea, Egypt, and Angola have been India’s top 5 import part- ners from Africa. Not only have Indian exports to Africa grown, but “there has also been a simultaneous increase in African exports to India by around US$5 billion during the same period”. Currently, “Africa’s market share stands at a healthy 8%in India’s overall import basket, while India contributes roughly 9% of Africa’s total imports from outside the continent” (Table 17.2). Major Export Partners—in 2020, South Africa was the leading destina- tion for India’s exports to Africa, accounting for around 13.4% of India’s exports to the region in 2018–19, followed by Nigeria, Egypt, Kenya, Mozambique, and Tanzania (Export–Import Bank of India 2022: 62). Due to the pandemic, most of Indian exports to the region have decreased with few exceptions like Kenya (increased exports of petroleum oil) and Togo (increased exports of cereals, pharmaceutical products, articles of iron and steel, etc.) (Graph 17.2). Major Import Partners—as regards India’s imports from Africa, South Africa has recently replaced Nigeria as India’s top import source due to higher imports of ships, boats, and ﬂoating structures, and copper and

Source The Ministry of Commerce and Industry (MOCI), Government of India: and EXIM Bank Analysis India’s Duty-Free Tariff Preference Scheme for Least Developed Countries (DFTP-LDC) India’s DFTP-LDC came into effect in August 2008 with tariff pref- erences spread over ﬁve years. The scheme was welcomed by African partners when it was fully operationalised in October 2012, with 85% of India’s total tariff lines made duty-free, 9% of tariff lines enjoying a ‘Margin of Preference’ ranging from 10 per cent to 100% and only 6 per cent of total tariff lines retained in the ‘Exclusion List’ with no duty pref- erences, for the exports from LDCs. However, since then, this scheme has been expanded from April 1, 2014, and now provides full preferential market access on about 98.2% of India’s total tariff lines. As of 2020, it is estimated that 34 out of 47 LDCs have become beneﬁciaries to the scheme, which include 26 African countries (Ministry of Commerce, GoI 2017). The African Continental Free Trade Area and Opportunities for India in Africa’s Economic Integration The African Continental Free Trade Area (AfCFTA), which came into effect on 1 January 2021, is the biggest free trade agreement in the world, given the number of participating countries. Only the World Trade

302 A. MISHRA Organization established in 1994 had a greater number of participating countries. The AfCFTA agreement proposes to progressively eliminate tariffs and remove non-tariff barriers on intra-African trade. While tariff on 10% of items would be eliminated over a period of time, 90 per cent of items would be under a non-tariff regime. Should the participating coun- tries sincerely implement the terms of the agreement, there would be a marked improvement in the volume of trade within the African conti- nent. Since there are a number of non-tariff barriers, their gradual lifting would further contribute to the intra-African trade (UNCTAD 2021). It is remarkable that as of October 2022, 44 of the 54 African signato- ries have deposited their instruments of AfCFTA ratiﬁcation (TRALAC 2022). India, being Africa’s third-largest trading partner and ﬁfth-largest investor, has critical stakes in AfCFTA agreement. Its success can open up the continent for the Indian enterprises like never before. India has been much encouraging of efforts at regional economic integration and the AfCFTA agreement can propel Africa in positive direction with bene- ﬁcial impact on its trade and connectivity within Africa. The AfCFTA, serving 1.3 billion people across 54 African countries with a combined GDP valued at US$3.4 trillion, could provide signiﬁcant impetus to India’s economic engagement with African countries, and help diversify the India-Africa bilateral trade ﬂows (The World Bank 2022). India’s strategy to enhance its trade and investment relations with African countries mostly entails an integrated approach, comprising, inter alia, integrating the continent to the Global Value Chain (GVC), strengthening Africa’s infrastructure and connectivity, and facilitating trade ﬁnance in Africa, among others. In a broad sense, the AfCFTA agreement can run a full course when Africa industrialises and the coun- tries are able to increase productive capabilities in a competitive landscape. Value addition of African products will help to integrate Africa to the GVC and will give the continent an added edge. So far, Indian investment in Africa has concentrated in pharmaceuticals, textiles, or steel industry with a focus on a particular country or with the motive of exporting to the United States or European Union (Afreximbank and EXIM India 2018). However, the investment paradigm needs to shift to the point that the products could be exported in all the parts of the continent, rather than simply exporting outside the African continent. India is placed favourably to help these countries process primary commodities like gold, diamond,

17 ANALYSING OPPORTUNITIES FOR INDIA IN AFRICA’S … 303 raw cocoa, and add value to them. India can certainly prove to be a crucial partner for helping in marketing of these resources. At the 16th edition of the CII-EXIM Bank Conclave on India-Africa Project Partnership held in virtual format in 2021, various opportunities and challenges for India’s relations with African countries in the context of the AfCFTA were discussed by the participants, particularly in the session “Aligning India Africa bilateral trade with AfCFTA” (CII 2021). The resultant inputs and suggestions offered by the participants are useful and relevant to our current discussion. In his address, Dr. Albert G. Zeufack, Chief Economist at the World Bank for Africa Region, suggested three things that government and business community in India and Africa may need to emphasise so as to beneﬁt from the AfCFTA. According to him, there is an inherent need on the Indian side to fully understand the megatrends that are driving Africa’s future and factor these considerations in its decision on policy and investment proposals. The COVID-19 pandemic has brought major disruption to Africa’s economic activity by reducing commodity prices, disrupting global supply chains, and interrupting African coun- tries’ growth trajectories. In 2020, sub-Saharan Africa entered into its ﬁrst recession in 25 years which caused major losses in ﬁnancial resource and viability of mega-development and infrastructure projects (The World Bank 2020). The industrial fabric in many countries is threatened and investments have begun to decline. Therefore, the Indian private sector needs to cultivate a holistic understanding of the trends that will inform Africa in the post-pandemic period. The second point is that both the sides should not be focusing solely on trade balance, whether it is in favour of Africa or India. Due to India’s high domestic demand and energy requirements, India imports much more than it exports to Africa. As per some estimate, Africa will have the largest labour force in the world by 2050, with a youthful and enterprising working-age population. Focus on job creation, therefore, becomes a priority. Therefore, going forward, the Indian private sector in Africa should prioritise targeted investments in sectors which have the potential to create job opportunities. This would help in reduction in poverty. The third consideration that Dr.Zeufack emphasises is the need for Africa and India to agree on the right metrics to measure each other’s success. Both sides should clearly deﬁne and artic- ulate precisely the targets, aims, and objectives. Is it just the volume of trade? Or should it also include the quantum of investments and number

304 A. MISHRA of jobs created? These are crucial considerations that Indian and African business entities need to pay due attention to. Sachin Chaturvedi, Director General, Research and Information System for Developing Countries (RIS), has stressed on the importance of working towards reducing the cost of transaction which is quite high. This would facilitate the banking and ﬁnancial infrastructure to enter Africa. Banking and ﬁnance can help enhance trade and investments. However, from an Indian perspective, India’s banking presence and coverage in Africa is very limited. There has been a reduction in the number of Indian bank branches across Africa. Indian Banks are only present in Mauritius (9), Kenya (5), Botswana (2), and one each in Uganda, South Africa, and Tanzania. Apart from this, the Bank of Baroda has entered into joint ventures in Zambia and Nigeria. Therefore, there is an urgent need for India to increase its banking presence in the continent by not only opening new branches but also buying more equity in African banks and regional development organisations (Mazumdar 2018). Another issue relates to the question of facilitation of trade between the two sides. This is important because it accrues beneﬁt to all coun- tries by allowing better access for businesses, to production inputs from abroad, and by supporting greater participation in the global value chains. This is why at the third edition of the India-Africa Forum Summit (IAFS) in New Delhi, both “India and Africa agreed to work towards creating a conducive environment for trade facilitation in accordance with World Trade Organization’s (WTO) Bali Trade Facilitation Agreement” (Ministry of External Affairs, GoI 2015). There are some lessons to be drawn from countries that have beneﬁtted due to efﬁcient port infrastructure, logistics networks, and dedicated sea lanes in their cooperative arrangement with other countries (Mumin and Schramm 2018). It is well documented that over 90% of goods traded by countries in the African continent is conducted by seas. In certain African countries, the lack of infrastructure, especially in road and railway networks, hampers the transportation. Even for landlocked African countries, maritime gateways are essential. The other transporta- tion modes are expensive and relatively inefﬁcient. In contrast, the sea would provide cheap and easy transportation of goods and freight. As such, African countries are expected to prioritise developing their maritime transport infrastructure. Ports and logistics network would be crucial in this respect. This is exactly what India is trying to accom- plish through the ‘SAGARMALA’ project. Essentially the focus here is on

17 ANALYSING OPPORTUNITIES FOR INDIA IN AFRICA’S … 305 ‘port-led’ holistic development. Under this, India will launch around 41 ports with dedicated facilities that will aim to “reduce the logistics costs for both domestic and export–import cargo through optimised infras- tructure investments” (Ministry of Ports, Shipping and Waterways, GoI 2023). This is exactly what India and Japan, under their joint initiative known as the Asia Africa Growth Corridor (AAGC), are trying to repli- cate in the port of Mombasa in Kenya. Without any doubt, the value chains that will be created and product fragmentation at various levels will require the focused and dedicated attention from the private sector to come in. The development of ports under AfCFTA will be very impor- tant as they will be the vital hubs along which most of the trade and investments will take place, and industrial zones will be set up. While the AfCFTA is expected to help Indian private sector to enter markets in Africa, there are certain on-the-ground, practical chal- lenges that Indian companies would need to consider before making any policy decisions or investment. Considerations related to location where Indian companies will invest and the preferred sector—whether resource-dependent sectors (metals, mining, oil and gas, petrochemicals) or consumer-oriented industries (food, agro-processing, pharmaceuticals, machinery). Indian companies will need to choose better organised and connected countries for new foreign direct investments. These challenges are some key constraints in enhancing Indian engagements in Africa. These constraints include the lack of access to inputs and ﬁnance, high tariff rates, availability of technology and skilled labour, political stability and regulatory framework, level of investor protection, and high cost of labour (Afreximbank and EXIM India 2018: 65). Lack of knowledge of Indian investors about the diversity of African markets is counted as one of the obstacles to right and efﬁcient investment. Unfortunately, knowl- edge asymmetry continues to be one big area of concern which requires to be properly addressed. This aspect possibly led Indian Trade and Economic Council and the India-Africa Trade Council to jointly organise an Indian Economic Dialogue in the AfCFTA which marked the beginning of consultations between the AfCFTA and representatives from India (Business Stan- dard 2021). Presently, India’s dealings with AfCFTA Secretariat in Accra, Ghana, are at a nascent stage. AfCFTA’s own focus is currently on encour- aging more ratiﬁcation from African Union (AU) member states and

306 A. MISHRA addressing Rules of Origin (RoO) details. Ultimately, the direction that AfCFTA takes in the coming years will inform many decisions by Indian business enterprises in this respect. Indian Investments in the African Region Indian investments in Africa have shown a remarkable increase over the last few decades. However, before the 1990s, India was handi- capped as to the lack of sufﬁcient foreign reserve. It was not possible to release necessary foreign exchange. Gradually, with a slew of economic reforms undertaken by Indian government over last two decades, the overall investment ceilings on outward investments have been abolished. Subsequently, because of India’s high growth rate and growing energy demands, India began investing in gas and oil ventures abroad. Histor- ically, India’s business interests and investment decision in Africa were focused on small and medium enterprises. Now bigger Indian companies are in play. Most of these companies have gone to the extractive sector as there is high demand for energy and raw materials in India. India’s own growing proﬁle since 2000 has coincided with a number of countries in Africa also achieving economic growth and development. Africa emerged as an attractive investment destination because of accel- erated growth, burgeoning middle class, abundance of natural resources, especially energy resources. The ﬁrst Indian investment in Africa was in the 1960s. Birla Group had established a textile mill in Ethiopia. Since then, Indian investments have diversiﬁed in both geographical and secto- rial terms, from the traditional destination of East and Southern Africa— comprising of mostly English-speaking Anglophone countries—to North, West, and Central Africa. According to data from the Ministry of Finance, Government of India, and the Reserve Bank of India (RBI): “approved India’s cumulative investments in Africa during April 1996 to March 2022 amounted to US$ 73.9 billion” (Export–Import Bank of India 2022: 10). Mauritius, Mozambique, Sudan, Egypt, and South Africa were the top destinations of India’s investment, making the country the ﬁfth-largest investor in the African continent (Table 17.3). Some of the major Indian companies investing in Africa countries are Oil and Natural Gas Corporation—Videsh Limited (Sudan, Libya, Demo- cratic Republic of Congo, Cote d’Ivoire, and Mozambique), Reliance Industries Limited (Egypt), Varun Beverages (Morocco), Bharti Airtel

308 A. MISHRA (South Africa, Kenya), Essar Group, TATA Group (Zambia, South Africa, Mozambique, and Cote d’Ivoire), and Gujarat State Petrol Corporation (Egypt). For Indian investors, the most preferred method of investment is by setting up a Wholly Owned Subsidiary (WOS) or by buying and acquiring shares in an existing company. Other method includes investing through joint ventures, mainly to serve regional and local markets. Africa’s manu- facturing sector, followed by ﬁnancial, insurance, real estate and business services, and wholesale and retail trade, restaurants and hotels are the targets for investment. Mauritius accounts for the vast majority of both inward and outward Foreign Direct Investments (FDI) movements between India and Africa. This is because of the favourable and advantageous tax conditions and suitable ﬁnancial facilities in Mauritius. Between 2008 and 2016, “Indian FDI outﬂows to Mauritius totalled US$47.6 billion” (Chakrabarty 2018). This is not a one-way process. Mauritius accounts for a majority share of India’s overall FDI inﬂows, followed by Mozambique, South Africa, Seychelles, Morocco, and Kenya. In April 2021, India signed the Comprehensive Economic Cooperation and Partnership Agreement (CECPA) with Mauritius after years of nego- tiations (Ministry of Commerce, GoI 2021). Mauritius is the ﬁrst African country with which India has signed such an agreement. The agreement covers 310 export items for India including foodstuff and beverages, agri- cultural products, textile and textile articles, base metals, electrical, and electronic items, among others. Mauritius will beneﬁt from preferential market access into India for 615 products including frozen ﬁsh, sugar, biscuits, fresh fruits, mineral water, beer, medical and surgical equip- ment, soaps, etc. As regards trade in services, Indian service providers are likely to have access to around 115 subsectors from the 11 broad service sectors like professional services, research and development, higher education, environmental, ﬁnancial, tourism, telecommunication, among others. However, for the Indian exporters to avail preferential beneﬁts under the India-Mauritius CEPCA, they are required to obtain a Certiﬁ- cate of Origin (CoO) from authorised Indian agencies (India-Mauritius CECPA 2021). Overall, “Indian investments in Africa can be characterised by large- scale investments in oil, gas, and mining sector by public sector enter- prises” (Chakrabarty 2018: 25). However, on a point of caution, the record of these companies is not bright and they have been criticised

17 ANALYSING OPPORTUNITIES FOR INDIA IN AFRICA’S … 309 widely. Many times, these public sector enterprises have had to give up on their acquired blocks due to problems related to availability of skill, unvi- able prospects and at times due to the failure to live up to the obligations under the contract. Investments from private companies have focused on the manufac- turing sector, in hotels, and petrochemicals. However, only few of the countries in Africa have attracted such investment from Indian compa- nies. The investments have been motivated by “access to raw materials and market-entry strategies” (Chakrabarty 2018: 26). Moreover, these big private companies are constantly competing with SMEs who have also invested in manufacturing sector and are likely to have a stronger impact on African development by creating new jobs. Lack of access to inputs and adequate ﬁnance are the key constraints in doing business in Africa. Issues such as political stability/regulatory framework, availability of technology, skilled labour, cost of labour, level of investor protection, and tariff rates are somewhat moderate to large constraints to successfully do business in Africa. Stakeholders need to identify and rectify these bottlenecks and challenges. Therefore, India is not only looking to expand its export basket to include a whole range of products according to African needs, but is also aiming to diver- sify its investments in new sectors other than the traditional sectors of investment. Without any doubt, economic cooperation between the two sides has opened up varied opportunities, including a particular focus on development cooperation. Establishing V alue Chains for Utilisation of AfCFTA The AfCFTA is expected to have transformational impact on the African economy. The single, uniﬁed, liberalised, and continental market that African countries are attempting to establish could unlock the continent’s manufacturing potential, facilitate industrialisation, and drive sustainable growth and jobs. India, being one of Africa’s oldest trading partners, is well positioned to leverage the AfCFTA and its impact on bolstering Regional Production Networks (RPNs) to broad-base Indian companies’ operations in different regions of Africa. The continent’s large working population, its growing middle class, and the signiﬁcant share of services are all conducive factors for enhancing India’s trade and investments in Africa. Consumer-driven goods related to agribusiness, pharmaceuticals,

310 A. MISHRA apparel and clothing, and automotive components are opportunities for increasing Indian FDI where Africa, through the AfCFTA is putting in place the appropriate Rules of Origin (RoO) and progressively eliminating both tariff and non-tariff barriers. Currently, Africa’s exports to India are dominated by 5 African coun- tries which account for over two-thirds of total African exports to India. Therefore, there is a need for India to explore and diversify its import basket to include other sources from Africa. Similarly, Indian exports to Africa are also heavily concentrated in a few large economies with the top 5 destinations of South Africa, Nigeria, Egypt, Togo, and Kenya accounting for nearly half the total exports to Africa. Subsequently, Indian companies are attempting to expand their exports to top African economies, based on their GDP size and population, and also expand imports from these top African economies. Sourcing more goods from other African economies would be beneﬁcial for the trade partnership. Going forward, Indian businesses and companies would look to invest in African economies with a view to exporting to India and serving local markets and people. It is important for Indian investors to view Africa not just as a destination for short-term returns, but also as a partner for medium- and long-term economic growth. A recent study by the CII, by using data from the International Trade Centre (ITC), uses the Export Specialization Index (ES Index) to deter- mine potential export products that have potential to bolster bilateral trade from India to Africa and vice versa. The Table 17.4 provides the example of 4 African countries which has been identiﬁed by CII for targeted trade promotion between India and Africa (CII 2022: 16). Conclusion Trade and investment linkages between India and Africa have witnessed an uptick in recent years, and are likely to grow even more, especially with growth opportunities expected following the operationalisation of the AfCFTA. Numerous opportunities exist for Indian companies to establish value chains and Regional Production Networks across various sectors like agriculture and food processing, healthcare, pharmaceuticals, ﬁnancing clean energy, digital technologies, exploring alternate solutions for infras- tructure ﬁnancing, increasing access to trade ﬁnance in Africa, among others. One of the long-term goals of Indian private and business enter- prises’ is to scale up India-Africa bilateral trade volume to US$ 200 billion

Ultimately for India, as this author has argued elsewhere, ‘the idea is to take the manufacturing base closer to African markets and come up with sub-regional and targeted strategies’, in accordance with African needs and priorities as envisaged in the Agenda 2063, so as to create products to serve local needs and generate employment. CHAPTER 18 (Re) Positioning Myanmar in the India–China Congagement Politics Sampurna Goswami International Relations are centred on ‘interactions’. These ‘interactions’ broadly include cooperation, competition and conﬂict. Ever since, global politics has been deﬁned by ‘ﬂows’ rather than ‘spaces’, ‘interactions’ have taken courses beyond these three dominant types. Until the 1990s, nation-states emphasised military capabilities as a means to project power. Realists believed that international order is governed by anarchy and this constraint states from cooperating and compels them to calibrate mili- tary power to counter major threats. This ‘power’ interpretation of world order brought ‘geography’ into ‘politics’ more than ever before. This interpretation which is often termed as ‘geopolitics’—a strategy of coun- tering and containing through military might, continued to dominate IR for decades until the European Community came into existence. The idea of integration between nations placed adjacent to each other in a S. Goswami (B) Sukanta College, University of Calcutta, Kolkata, India e-mail: sg_plsc@sukantacollege.org © The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Singapor e Pte Ltd. 2024 O. Mishra and S. Sen (eds.), Global Political Economy, Geopolitics and International Security , https://doi.org/10.1007/978-981-97-2231-0_18 317

318 S. GOSWAMI contiguous geographical territory gave rise to a new discourse within IR— Regionalism. This discourse as advocated by the liberals did not reject the meta-geographical notions and interpretations of the world order. The advocates stressed the idea that anarchy as the dominant global order does not impede states’ willingness to cooperate. In fact, in situations of abso- lute anarchy, states cooperate and integrate into regions to maximise their absolute gains (Greico 1988: 485). Liberal Institutionalists further argued that states are less concerned about power and security but are more interested in maximising gains through integration. However, scholars of regional integration could not fully reject the idea that power play and balance of power strategies dominate a country’s foreign policy objec- tives and any cooperation or concerted initiative on the part of states as rational actors are deﬁned by power and security consideration (Greico 1988: 485–507). Geography and International Relations are intertwined with each other in such a way that even in the era of globalism, scholars tend to conﬁne themselves to the territorial analysis of global politics. This is inevitable because, even though nation-states desire to avoid conﬂict and contesta- tion, the scarcity of resources and a country’s quest to acquire power over other countries make conﬂict a ‘constant’ in the discourse of international relations. However, conﬂict need not, and is not always projected through violent wars. With the beginning of the Cold War and the emergence of regions, countries realised that wars were costly affairs. The drainage of wealth in wars far outweighs the advantages the warring countries acquire. Even the country that wins and gets placed in a higher position in the power hierarchy suffers from a mammoth economic crisis. With the end of the Cold War, therefore, the importance of military power is grad- ually waning. Moreover, the emergence of the United States (US) as a hegemonic power and the swell of US-led economic order augmenting through the International Monetary Fund and World Trade Organization pushed nation-states to adhere to foreign policies that necessarily opti- mised their economic interest. Edward N. Luttwak ( 1990: 17) argued that intersecting web of power relations was shrugging off the military defence and was taking up commerce and economic relations as a means to counter other nation-states (rivals mostly). According to Luttwak, the ‘grammar of commerce’ was replacing the ‘logic of conﬂict’. However, this grammar of commerce, as pointed out by Luttwak ( 1990: 17) ‘can at times be competitive and at times may end up in forming novel alliances,

18 (RE) POSITIONING MYANMAR IN THE INDIA–CHINA … 319 free trade areas and vertical integrations…[w]hich in turn would co- produce, co-develop and co-market goods and services but whatever be the result, all such commercial engagements would unfold with regards to frontiers’. Even though commercial relations result in a variable sum game unlike military conﬂicts that lead to zero-sum results, the statist notion of teasing out the greatest beneﬁt dominates. In other words, transnational economic transactions and engagements seem to promote all-pervasive development of the region but in reality, it maximises outcomes within the sovereign boundaries of one nation while minimising for the other. Thus, the grammar of commerce is just a veil that hides the ‘realpolitik’ interests of nation-states of outdoing each other. As put forth by Luttwak ( 1990: 18–19)—‘states act ‘Geoeconomically’ simply because of what they are: spatially deﬁned entities structured to outdo each other on the world scene’. Against this backdrop, therefore, it can be stated that the conven- tional ‘interactions’—cooperation, competition and conﬂict have taken courses where rivals are cooperating in certain areas while contesting in other sectors. This dualism in interaction is premised to reap the greatest beneﬁt. Rivals engage with each other only in those sectors where such engagement would result in a win–win situation and ﬁght in those where outmanoeuvring each other would result in a variable sum if not a zero-sum outcome. This kind of dual interaction is most palpable in regions. This is obvious because geoeconomics as a strategy of containing and countering through cooperation and engagement is applied mostly to integrations—regions or multilateral forums. This, however, is most visible in the Global South, where integration was adopted as the most viable approach to development. Most of the nation-states of the Global South adhered to the idea that opening backward areas to the forces of the market and connecting them to the comparatively developed areas (at times the economic centres and capitals) of the adjacent countries would generate dividends in the form of improvement of infrastructure, industrialisation, employment, improvement of trade relations, people-to- people connectivity and much more. However, primarily due to the highly competitive and interdependent global and national political environ- ment, integration is becoming more complex. As such, provinces within a particular nation are attempting to integrate with provinces of adja- cent nation-states, thereby creating several micro-regions within a broader region, all interacting to generate outcomes that add to development within and beyond the territorial borders of the participating sovereign

320 S. GOSWAMI nation-states. One such micro-region has been created by India’s North- east, Myanmar and China’s southwest. Mostly based on secondary data, we seek to delve deep into the India’s and China’s engagement with Myanmar. The ﬁrst part of the chapter tries to understand the cartography and how a micro-region is emerging around this geographical space. The second section of the chapter shall include three sub-sections. These sub- sections will brieﬂy deal with the sectors where both India and China have invested. These sub-sections will also try to tease out how India’s and China’s cooperation with Myanmar is culminating in contestation with each other. This section shall be followed by a conclusion to the chapter. India–Myanmar–China: T he Cartography South Asian politics is carved largely by ethnic conﬂicts, political confrontations, illegal immigration and insurgency. Besides these, the presence of China, and the ever present contestation and conﬂict between India and Pakistan have often posed a challenge to the overall regional setting. However, India along with her neighbours has engaged in several regional and sub-regional concerted initiatives, for instance, the South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation (SAARC) or the Bay of Bengal Initiative for Multi-Sectoral Technical and Economic Cooperation (BIMSTEC) with the agenda of promoting all-pervasive development and addressing the existing challenges through joint mechanisms. China’s presence in South Asia, however, is driven by two separate, but interre- lated objectives. One; to exact compliance from weaker nation-states of the South Asian Region to contain India and two; to expand its naval presence in the Indian Ocean to counter-hegemonic US and emerging India in the Indian Ocean Region. To make its presence overtly palpable, China has connected its Historical Silk Road with its newly conceived 21st-Century Maritime Silk Road—together termed as the Belt and Road Initiative (BRI). The BRI has also helped China to connect the two adjacent and overlapping regions—South Asia and the Indian Ocean. However, the Chinese attempt to invest in the infrastructural devel- opment of South Asian littorals, especially in ports, has also further encouraged New Delhi to ingress more deeply into the South Asian littoral countries through infrastructural and human resource develop- ment. This has given rise to what David Scott considers ‘The New Great Power Great Game of India and China’ (Scott 2008: 1–26).

18 (RE) POSITIONING MYANMAR IN THE INDIA–CHINA … 321 This ‘great game’, of late, has become more visible in micro-regions within South Asia and the Indian Ocean where both India and China are engaged to outrun each other. One such micro-region is centred on Myanmar. Since the 1990s, a new political cartography consisting of India’s Northeast, Myanmar and China’s Southwest has been emerging in Asia. Several factors are responsible for the emergence of this cartog- raphy— . First, Myanmar is equidistant to Delhi-Mumbai and Shanghai-Hong Kong. Furthermore, Myanmar is positioned right above the junc- ture of the Bay of Bengal, the Andaman Sea and the Indian Ocean. This geographical position of Myanmar has made the country immensely strategic as any transnational concerted initiative centred on Myanmar provides the participating nation-states (landlocked border provinces of India, China and Thailand) access to the Indian Ocean (Yhome 2014:2 – 3 ) . . Second, Myanmar’s isolation ensuing from more than ﬁfty years of military rule and a series of Western sanctions that have been imposed from time to time made it inevitable for the country to engage with its neighbours for economic and diplomatic support. This opened an avenue for both India and China—already engaged in the game of outrunning each other, to bolster their footprints in Myanmar through investments, soft loans and initiating cooperation in areas of connectivity and development. . Third, until 1989, India remained one of the launching pads from where pro-democracy student leaders of Myanmar organised campaigns. India provided ﬁnancial and logistic support to oppo- sition party leaders Aung San Syu Kyi and U Nu. New Delhi also provided intelligence support to the Kachin Independence Army and Karen National Union who were ﬁghting against the ruling mili- tary regime under Ne Win (Routray 2011: 299–301). New Delhi’s engagement with Myanmar’s military regime through the adoption of India’s ambitious Look East Policy in 1991 was driven by several factors; 1. China’s ingression into Myanmar. 2. India’s ambition to connect Northeast with Southeast Asian coun- tries through Myanmar.

322 S. GOSWAMI 3. New Delhi’s efforts to address the issues of illegal drug and arms trade. 4. To counter the insurgent outﬁts active along the India–Myanmar border. 5. To meet the rising demand of hydrocarbons by exploiting Myan- mar’s untapped natural reserves (Routray 2011: 312–317). . Fourth, China’s interest in Myanmar can be traced back to 1987, when Deng Xiaoping adhered to the policy of ‘reform and opening to the outside world’ also known as China’s ‘second revolution’ (Cheng and Zhang 1999: 91). The idea behind designing such a policy was premised on two important parameters, one; to preserve China’s independence, sovereignty and territorial integrity and two, to create a favourable international environment that is conducive to reforms, modernisation and opening up (China Balance Sheet 2020). Under this regime, integration with its neighbours to develop and modernise its landlocked south-western province of Yunnan became a priority. Furthermore, the end of the Cold War and the rise of the US, India’s growing inﬂuence in Myanmar, Myan- mar’s ASEAN membership, Beijing’s strategic concerns to access the Indian Ocean through Myanmar ports to bypass the Malacca choke- point, development of Yunnan and addressing the non-traditional security issues (narcotics, aids, etc.) were some of the other impor- tant driving forces behind China’s Myanmar policy (Steinberg and Fan, 2012: 155). The last two factors bring to the fore two conspicuous facets—one, the state-centric notion of security has been replaced by transnational ﬂows of contrabands, migrants, criminals, arms, diseases and environmental threats and therefore the ‘ﬂowmation of threat has itself blurred the borders’ (Ó Tuathail 1997: 49). This facet is quite evident in this partic- ular cartography and these issues have been one of the driving forces behind India’s and China’s policy vis-à-vis Myanmar. Two, both India and China crave to capture markets and escalate their economic advantages through state-led investment, transnational infrastructural development, cross-border facilitation of goods and services to exact greater proﬁts

18 (RE) POSITIONING MYANMAR IN THE INDIA–CHINA … 323 and augment relatively higher development within the nation-states rein- stating the spatiality, territory and border in their external relations with Myanmar. Therefore, India’s and China’s attempts to facilitate cross- border integration with Myanmar through de-territorialisation are, in reality culminating in re-territorialism. Th e I n d i a–China Interaction in Myanmar: Contested Cooperation? The existing literature that deals with all inter-state interactions surrounding this cartography has centred on the study at the bilateral level. Most of the literature is either about India-Myanmar or Myanmar– China relations. However, the nuances of this cartography lie in the simultaneous engagement of both India and China in Myanmar. The factors that drove India and China into Myanmar, as mentioned in the preceding section, highlight a pattern that is pertinent for both India and China—countering each other’s footprints and attempts to bolster their presence. Even though China entered Myanmar much earlier (1980), India being a late entrant (1990) has tried to make similar investments as undertaken by China. These investments will be dealt with brieﬂy in the succeeding sections. However, the great race between India and China that has ensued from the similarity in both the countries’ approach towards integration with Myanmar is unfolding a competitive engage- ment. Therefore, any analysis of the India-Myanmar or Myanmar-China relations at a bilateral level will only limit the understanding of this cartography as a whole. Furthermore, Myanmar, over the years, has become a theatre of geopo- litical competition and rivalry as several Asian economies are engaging in interaction, cooperative schemes and investments. These investments are aimed towards the promotion of growth and development of Myanmar but in doing so, the geoeconomic considerations are unfolding in a way that shows how these nation-states are trying to outdo each other in the country. Thus, to understand the actual pattern of interaction that surrounds this cartography, the notion of integration and region- alism must be considered as the theoretical premise. Indian and Chinese investments in Myanmar can be studied under three major heads— Connectivity, Sea Ports and Hydrocarbon and Development Cooperation.

324 S. GOSWAMI Connectivity The idea of connecting backward areas and provinces of a country with the economic growth centres (nodes and hubs) of the adjacent countries makes connectivity the ﬁrst and the preliminary step towards regional integration. In this particular cartography, ‘regionalism as a process’— where features of regionalism, for instance, cross-border connectivity, investment, infrastructure and trade, are deliberately undertaken (does not result from ‘spill over’) as a means to promote development. As a result, connectivity has been stressed by both India and China as the foremost step towards integration with Myanmar. India’s connectivity initiatives in Myanmar fall within the ambit of its Neighbourhood First Policy and the Act East Policy (earlier Look East Policy). Both these policies undertaken by New Delhi are targeted essentially towards consol- idating its re-integrationist foreign policy designed against the backdrop of China’s ambitious BRI, of which most of India’s neighbours are beneﬁciary (Summers 2019: 6). For India, connectivity deﬁned as “the cornerstone” of international cooperation is also a vital element for India’s own development, essentially at the intra-state level (Pant and Yhome 2020: 33–34). Off late, New Delhi is trying to create an “alternative infrastructural development model” (Pant and Yhome 2020, 37) based on shared prosperity through consultative, non-reciprocal and outcome- oriented foreign and integrationist policy” (Pulipaka et al., 2017: 12), for its neighbours at a time when there is a growing scepticism against China’s aggressive infrastructural initiatives. India’s two most ambitious connectivity projects in Myanmar include the India-Myanmar-Thailand Trilateral Highway and the Kaladan Multi- modal Transit Transport Project. Apart from these, there are several other proposed projects which include the Rhi-Tiddim Road, Stilwell Road, Chennai-Dawei Corridor and the Jiribam-Kalay Rail Link. The India– Myanmar–Thailand Trilateral Highway, conceived in 2005, is still under construction. The construction work of most of the road is completed and the roadway is operational in sections. The Kaladan Multimodal Transit Transport Project can be divided into three sub-sectors—the Sittwe Port, the inland waterway through the Kaladan River and the motorway connecting Paletwa in Myanmar to Zorinpui in India. This project is also under construction. However, India has completed the Sittwe Port and the inland waterway from Paletwa to Sittwe is operational (Shesadri 2014: 20). Other projects have been

18 (RE) POSITIONING MYANMAR IN THE INDIA–CHINA … 325 proposed but not much progress has been achieved yet. Two facets are to be considered here—one, India has been a late entrant in this sector. India has begun to invest in Myanmar’s connectivity sector only since 2005. Two, bureaucratic complexities and India’s failure to fast-track the projects in Myanmar have been responsible for the prolonged delay of these projects. China, on the contrary, entered the connectivity sector long back in 1980 with its sole cross-border connectivity project; the Irrawaddy Corridor. This corridor comprises three separate sectors—the Burma Road that connects Kunming in China with Mandalay in Myanmar, Inland Waterway along the Irrawaddy River that connects Bhamo in upper Irrawaddy with Minbu in the middle Irrawaddy and Railway connectivity proposed to connect Kunming with Kyaukphyu (Garver 2006: 1–22). Although most sections of this connectivity project are oper- ational, save the railway sector, China is facing signiﬁcant difﬁculties in the connectivity sector. China’s connectivity projects in Myanmar aim to provide Yunnan with an access to the Indian Ocean. This realpolitik interest has not been accepted widely by the population of Myanmar. Furthermore, Beijing’s attempts to revive suspended projects under BRI have made the subsequent ruling regimes (both civil and military) even more apprehensive about China’s aggressive ambitions. Besides these, the rising xenophobia amongst the population has compelled Naypyidaw to suspend most of the Chinese projects including several sections of the Irrawaddy Corridor. In spite of repeated attempts by Beijing to add most of its delayed or suspended connectivity projects under its ambi- tious China-Myanmar Economic Corridor (CMEC), the government in Naypyidaw has turned a deaf ear. However, Beijing has succeeded in concluding several MOUs during President Xi Jinping’s visit to Myanmar in 2020 and has been able to revive a few projects under its CMEC but this has only deepened Myanmar’s apprehensions about China’s debt trap diplomacy (Shesadri 2020). The India–China connectivity race that has unravelled in Myanmar since 2000 is bringing about variable gains for both India and China. Even though India entered late and has delayed the implementation and operationalisation of its projects, it enjoys a snug position vis-à-vis China. This is because of India’s welfare posture and benevolent approach that calls for all-pervasive development of both countries through cross-border connectivity. However, to what extent India can augment development in the Northeast, similar to what China has achieved in Yunnan, depends on

326 S. GOSWAMI New Delhi’s capability to operationalise the projects. However, besides the constant struggle to outdo each other in Myanmar, both India and China agreed to cooperate on a quadrilateral economic corridor involving Myanmar and Bangladesh in 1999. This quadrilateral connec- tivity venture termed the Kunming Initiative-Bangladesh, China, India, Myanmar-Economic Corridor (BCIM EC) failed in the face of India’s presentiments about China’s interests in the Northeast. Moreover, the forum called for an over-arching dominance and leadership of Beijing that was unacceptable to India (Uberoi 2014). These, coupled with the lack of clarity about the nature of the forum as to whether it is an Economic Corridor, or a Growth Quadrangle, the participating countries failed to reach any conclusive agreement. Seaports and Hydrocarbons Ever since economy became the frontiers of war and contestation— geographical advantage, strategic opportunities and corresponding mili- tary capabilities are measured in terms of a country’s maritime presence. Several nineteenth-century IR scholars including British naval strategist, Julian Corbett advocated that protection of Sea Lanes of Commu- nications(SLOCs) by actual physical destruction or capture of enemy warships, or through naval blockades is a strategy to weaken an enemy both at land and sea (Smith 2018). Against the backdrop of the contested and conﬂictual international politics of who gets what, when and how— sea power is not merely what happens at sea, it is more than that. It is about geopolitics, geoeconomic and geo-culture (Iliopoulas 2009:5 ) . For both India and China, the Indian Ocean remains one of the most strategic frontiers of expanding inﬂuence and bolstering naval capabilities. Almost 80% of India’s and China’s exports and imports (most importantly energy import) pass through the Indian Ocean. Thus, the protection of these SLOCs has, over the years, compelled both New Delhi and Beijing to proliferate their presence in this maritime domain. India’s reasser- tion into the Indian Ocean is driven mostly by China’s overt ingression into the theatres of naval and seaport diplomacy with the Indian Ocean littorals. For China, accessing the Indian Ocean through overseas port building is imperative because of the US presence in the Malacca Strait. Furthermore, the emergence of the Indo-Paciﬁc and US’ attempts to bring in other powers like Japan, India and Australia into the Indian Ocean through joint naval exercises has made China highly assertive.

18 (RE) POSITIONING MYANMAR IN THE INDIA–CHINA … 327 Myanmar’s maritime frontier along the junction of the Bay of Bengal, Andaman Sea and the Indian Ocean has provided immense opportunity for both its competing neighbours to outdo each other. India–China contestation in Myanmar’s rimland is palpable in three major sectors—seaport development, naval cooperation and hydrocarbon explo- ration. Both India and China have been able to build two grand seaports in the Rakhine province in Myanmar. India has built Sittwe (a part of the Kaladan Project) and China has built Kyaukphyu (part of a Special Economic Zone). The Kyaukphyu Deep Seaport and Special Economic Zone (SEZ) have been included under the CMEC as well. The SEZ includes two separate pipelines for oil and natural gas transportation from Kyaukphyu port to Kunming and an oil reﬁnery as well. Although India entered the hydrocarbon exploration sector in early 2000 it failed to import gas from Myanmar’s Shwe gas ﬁeld in the face of its inability to conclude an agreement with Bangladesh on an under- ground transnational gas pipeline. China, on the contrary, entered the sector in 2001 and was granted permission to explore natural gas four years later. China also won the bid to transport natural gas from Myan- mar’s Shwe ﬁelds directly through pipelines after India failed to transport the same. India’s failure provided Myanmar with an opportunity to sell the gas to China at greater proﬁt margins (Zhao 2008: 185). In the case of Naval Cooperation both India and China-made consid- erable inroads. However, India enjoys greater leverage vis-à-vis China. Since 1988 China has been the largest defence partner of Myanmar and has provided modern ﬁghter jets and naval ships including Hainan Class patrol vessels. China and Myanmar have also conducted joint naval exercises over the years, and more recently (2021) China transferred its old Ming-class diesel-electric submarine to Myanmar (Pandit 2021). However, China’s tacit association with the insurgent outﬁts in Myanmar, especially the Wa State Army and the poor quality of China-made artillery, encouraged Naypyidaw to consider India as a more trustworthy defence partner. India and Myanmar have successfully conducted joint naval exer- cises. The ﬁrst such Milan exercise was conducted in December 2005. India transferred BN-2 maritime MP aircraft, three HMS-X AMS sonar in 2013 and US$ 31 million worth of Shyena ASW torpedoes in 2017 (Peng 2019: 593). In October 2020, India gifted Myanmar 300 meters diesel-electric submarine INS Sindhuvir.

328 S. GOSWAMI Development Cooperation/Regionalism India and China have both proclaimed to cooperate in promoting a multi- lateral world order since 1955. At the Afro-Asian Bandung Conference in 1955, both India and China took the leadership in promoting an interna- tional order in which the rights and interests of the developing countries would be given sufﬁcient importance. Against this backdrop, both India (Middle Income Country) and China (Upper Middle Income Country) began to participate in development cooperation as an attempt to reduce the Global South’s dependence on the ﬁnances provided by the developed Global North (Chediek 2021: 3). Myanmar, a least-developed country and a country subjected to Western sanctions, provided an avenue for both India and China to make inroads into the theatre by providing development grants and assistance. However, India’s and China’s contri- bution in the development of Myanmar is more regional. According to Sloan (1971: 138–151), “nations which share common regional identity may be capable of helping one another to develop more rapidly than if a country pursued a solely nationalistic course”. India and China have often defended their Myanmar policy on the grounds of promoting all-pervasive development through cooperation and integration. Thus, development regionalism seems to be a better premise for understanding development- based integration in this cartography. Table 18.1 will provide a basic idea of the nature of investment and capital ﬂow that exists between India-Myanmar and Myanmar-China. The data provided in the table has been compiled by the author. Base data has been derived from several secondary sources. The competitive engagement of India and China is bringing in variable gains for both countries. None of the two Asian powers are net losers when measured in terms of the advantages accrued from integrating with Myanmar. China’s interest has been to provide Yunnan access to the Indian Ocean through the ports of Myanmar. China also aims to develop its landlocked south-western provinces through integration with neigh- bouring countries. To achieve that, China heavily invested in building border trade zones, special economic zones, cross-border connectivity, exploration of untapped natural resources of Myanmar, hydro-electric power projects and pipelines (oil and natural gas) that helped China to directly transport the oil imported from Persian Gulf countries through Myanmar. China has partially succeeded in achieving all of the above. However, ruling regimes in Myanmar have been compelled to adopt a

18 (RE) POSITIONING MYANMAR IN THE INDIA–CHINA … 331 hedging strategy against China since the 1980s. This is mainly because of China’s realpolitik ambition of developing Yunnan by exploiting the resources in Myanmar. Moreover, China maintained covert association with insurgent outﬁts of Myanmar and used them against the government in Naypyidaw whenever Myanmar tried for any sort of rapprochement with the US. Through such strategy, China indirectly interfered with Myanmar’s neutral foreign policy objective. Furthermore, Beijing recon- sidered most of the incomplete projects under its ambitious BRI that only resulted in exacerbating already existing anti-Chinese sentiments within the country. On the contrary, India’s approach and vision regarding its association and engagement with Myanmar have been benevolent and that of welfare. India’s ambitions behind its integration with Myanmar were also driven by its realpolitik interests of developing the Northeast, addressing the issue of insurgency and providing the landlocked Northeast access to ports in the Indian Ocean through Myanmar. Besides these, India also tried to inﬂuence the government in Myanmar in order to exact compliance against the aggression of China in the region and beyond. However, New Delhi never maintained any tacit association with any of the insurgent outﬁts in Myanmar since the 1990s and neither has it intervened in the domestic spheres of Myanmar, nor has India tried to achieve its objectives by exploiting the natural resources of Myanmar. This benevolent and trustworthy posture, in reality, provided India with greater leverage vis-à-vis China in this particular cartography. Myanmar, positioned in the centre of this competitive and contested engagement of both India and China, aims to accrue the largest share of develop- mental beneﬁts for itself. To achieve its interests, Myanmar, of late (before the February 1, 2021 coup), has begun to associate with other Asian economies including Japan, Singapore, Thailand, South Korea, Australia, the European Union and the US as well. Myanmar’s strategy of balancing India and China in a way that none can accrue absolute gains and achieve a position sufﬁcient to intervene in domestic policy-making has made this competitive engagement of India and China even stronger and discernible.

332 S. GOSWAMI Conclusion Integration, as a means to promote all-pervasive development of the cartography (India’s Northeast-Myanmar-China’s Southwest), carves the politics of India and China in Myanmar. Through such integration, however, both India and China aim to augment growth and development in their landlocked underdeveloped provinces of India’s Northeast and China’s Southwest. This integration with Myanmar is imperative for both the countries as the non-traditional security issues that have marred the India–Myanmar or the Myanmar-China border areas require joint efforts and cooperation at the political and security levels. Above all, integra- tion is unavoidable as both India and China crave to outmanoeuvre each other in several theatres of global politics. Geoeconomic being the new strategy of countering and containing, expanding inﬂuence and exacting compliance of weaker nation-states through ‘ﬂows’ (investments) provides immense opportunity for warring parties to secure and fulﬁl their national interest of calibrating power and outrunning their rival. Irrespective of the fact that India and China are engaging in several sectors in Myanmar (in the same sectors mostly), this cartography does not represent a region yet. However, the pattern of engagement, the approach and the outcome all represent characters of a region. The reason behind is that both India and China hesitate to cooperate with each other. Even though China tried to facilitate BCIM EC, India’s appre- hensions about Chinese interests and aggression in the Northeast have shut all possible avenues of cooperation. Nevertheless, studying India’s and China’s engagement with Myanmar as a bilateral association will only limit and narrow down the understanding of the cartography that repre- sents a micro-region as ‘power, market and community’ (Chatterjee 2007: 65–66). Therefore, a better terminology for this cartography would be a ‘micro-region under process’, where both India and China are engaging to contain each other and in the process trying to reap the greatest beneﬁt for themselves. Myanmar, on the contrary, is trying to balance both these contesting powers in a way that none can accrue absolute gains thereby steering the outcome of the ‘congagement’ as a variable sum for all three participating nation-states.
